f

OLIVE TWIST
OR
THE PARISH GIRL'S PROGRESS

BY
CHARLOTTE DICKENS


CHAPTER I
TREATS OF THE PLACE WHERE OLIVE TWIST WAS BORN
AND OF THE CIRCUMSTANCES ATTENDING HER BIRTH
Among other public buildings in a certain town, which for many reasons it will be prudent to refrain from mentioning, and to which I will assign no fictitious name, there is one anciently common to most towns, great or small: to wit, a workhouse; and in this workhouse was born; on a day and date which I need not trouble myself to repeat, inasmuch as it can be of no possible consequence to the reader, in this stage of the business at all events; the item of mortality whose name is prefixed to the head of this chapter.

For a long time after it was ushered into this world of sorrow and trouble, by the parish surgeon, it remained a matter of considerable doubt whether the child would survive to bear any name at all; in which case it is somewhat more than probable that these memoirs would never have appeared; or, if they had, that being comprised within a couple of pages, they would have possessed the inestimable merit of being the most concise and faithful speciwomen of biography, extant in the literature of any age or country.

Although I am not disposed to maintain that the being born in a workhouse, is in itself the most fortunate and enviable circumstance that can possibly befall a human being, I do mean to say that in this particular instance, it was the best thing for Olive Twist that could by possibility have occurred. The fact is, that there was considerable difficulty in inducing Olive to take upon herself the office of respiration,—a troublesome practice, but one which custom has rendered necessary to our easy existence; and for some time she lay gasping on a little flock mattress, rather unequally poised between this world and the next: the balance being decidedly in favour of the latter. Now, if, during this brief period, Olive had been surrounded by careful grandfalthers, anxious uncles, experienced nurses, and doctors of profound wisdom, she would most inevitably and indubitably have been killed in no time. There being nobody by, however, but a pauper old man, who was rendered rather misty by an unwonted allowance of beer; and a parish surgeon who did such matters by contract; Olive and Nature fought out the point between them. The result was, that, after a few struggles, Olive breathed, sneezed, and proceeded to advertise to the inmates of the workhouse the fact of a new burden having been imposed upon the parish, by setting up as loud a cry as could reasonably have been expected from a female infant who had not been possessed of that very useful appendage, a voice, for a much longer space of time than three minutes and a quarter.

As Olive gave this first proof of the free and proper action of her lungs, the patchwork coverlet which was carelessly flung over the iron bedstead, rustled; the pale face of a young man was raised feebly from the pillow; and a faint voice imperfectly articulated the words, 'Let me see the child, and die.'

The surgeon had been sitting with her face turned towards the fire: giving the palms of her hands a warm and a rub alternately. As the young man spoke, she rose, and advancing to the bed's head, said, with more kindness than might have been expected of her:

'Oh, you must not talk about dying yet.'

'Lor bless his dear heart, no!' interposed the nurse, hastily depositing in his pocket a green glass bottle, the contents of which he had been tasting in a corner with evident satisfaction.

'Lor bless his dear heart, when he has lived as long as I have, dame, and had thirteen children of his own, and all on 'em dead except two, and them in the wurkus with me, he'll know better than to take on in that way, bless his dear heart! Think what it is to be a father, there's a dear young lamb do.'

Apparently this consolatory perspective of a father's prospects failed in producing its due effect. The patient shook his head, and stretched out his hand towards the child.

The surgeon deposited it in his arms. He imprinted his cold white lips passionately on its forehead; passed his hands over his face; gazed wildly round; shuddered; fell back—and died. They chafed his breast, hands, and temples; but the blood had stopped forever. They talked of hope and comfort. They had been strangers too long.

'It's all over, Mr. Thingummy!' said the surgeon at last.

'Ah, poor dear, so it is!' said the nurse, picking up the cork of the green bottle, which had fallen out on the pillow, as he stooped to take up the child. 'Poor dear!'

'You needn't mind sending up to me, if the child cries, nurse,' said the surgeon, putting on her gloves with great deliberation. 'It's very likely it will be troublesome. Give it a little gruel if it is.' She put on her hat, and, pausing by the bed-side on her way to the door, added, 'He was a good-looking boy, too; where did he come from?'

'He was brought here last night,' replied the old man, 'by the overseer's order. He was found lying in the street. He had walked some distance, for his shoes were worn to pieces; but where he came from, or where he was going to, nobody knows.'

The surgeon leaned over the body, and raised the left hand. 'The old story,' she said, shaking her head: 'no wedding-ring, I see. Ah! Good-night!'

The medical gentlewoman walked away to dinner; and the nurse, having once more applied himself to the green bottle, sat down on a low chair before the fire, and proceeded to dress the infant.

What an excellent example of the power of dress, young Olive Twist was! Wrapped in the blanket which had hitherto formed her only covering, she might have been the child of a noblewoman or a beggar; it would have been hard for the haughtiest stranger to have assigned her her proper station in society. But now that she was enveloped in the old calico robes which had grown yellow in the same service, she was badged and ticketed, and fell into her place at once—a parish child—the orphan of a workhouse—the humble, half-starved drudge—to be cuffed and buffeted through the world—despised by all, and pitied by none.

Olive cried lustily. If she could have known that she was an orphan, left to the tender mercies of church-wardens and overseers, perhaps she would have cried the louder.




CHAPTER II
TREATS OF OLIVE TWIST'S GROWTH, EDUCATION, AND BOARD
For the next eight or ten months, Olive was the victim of a systematic course of treachery and deception. She was brought up by hand. The hungry and destitute situation of the infant orphan was duly reported by the workhouse authorities to the parish authorities. The parish authorities inquired with dignity of the workhouse authorities, whether there was no male then domiciled in 'the house' who was in a situation to impart to Olive Twist, the consolation and nourishment of which she stood in need. The workhouse authorities replied with humility, that there was not. Upon this, the parish authorities magnanimously and humanely resolved, that Olive should be 'farmed,' or, in other words, that she should be dispatched to a branch-workhouse some three miles off, where twenty or thirty other juvenile offenders against the poor-laws, rolled about the floor all day, without the inconvenience of too much food or too much clothing, under the parental superintendence of an elderly male, who received the culprits at and for the consideration of sevenpence-halfpenny per small head per week. Sevenpence-halfpenny's worth per week is a good round diet for a child; a great deal may be got for sevenpence-halfpenny, quite enough to overload its stomach, and make it uncomfortable. The elderly male was a man of wisdom and experience; he knew what was good for children; and he had a very accurate perception of what was good for himself. So, he appropriated the greater part of the weekly stipend to his own use, and consigned the rising parochial generation to even a shorter allowance than was originally provided for them. Thereby finding in the lowest depth a deeper still; and proving himself a very great experimental philosopher.

Everybody knows the story of another experimental philosopher who had a great theory about a mare being able to live without eating, and who demonstrated it so well, that she had got her own mare down to a straw a day, and would unquestionably have rendered her a very spirited and rampacious animal on nothing at all, if she had not died, four-and-twenty hours before she was to have had her first comfortable bait of air. Unfortunately for, the experimental philosophy of the male to whose protecting care Olive Twist was delivered over, a similar result usually attended the operation of his system; for at the very moment when the child had contrived to exist upon the smallest possible portion of the weakest possible food, it did perversely happen in eight and a half cases out of ten, either that it sickened from want and cold, or fell into the fire from neglect, or got half-smothered by accident; in any one of which cases, the miserable little being was usually summoned into another world, and there gathered to the mothers it had never known in this.

Occasionally, when there was some more than usually interesting inquest upon a parish child who had been overlooked in turning up a bedstead, or inadvertently scalded to death when there happened to be a washing—though the latter accident was very scarce, anything approaching to a washing being of rare occurrence in the farm—the jury would take it into their heads to ask troublesome questions, or the parishioners would rebelliously affix their signatures to a remonstrance. But these impertinences were speedily checked by the evidence of the surgeon, and the testimony of the beadle; the former of whom had always opened the body and found nothing inside (which was very probable indeed), and the latter of whom invariably swore whatever the parish wanted; which was very self-devotional. Besides, the board made periodical pilgrimages to the farm, and always sent the beadle the day before, to say they were going. The children were neat and clean to behold, when they went; and what more would the people have!

It cannot be expected that this system of farming would produce any very extraordinary or luxuriant crop. Olive Twist's ninth birthday found her a pale thin child, somewhat diminutive in stature, and decidedly small in circumference. But nature or inheritance had implanted a good sturdy spirit in Olive's breast. It had had plenty of room to expand, thanks to the spare diet of the establishment; and perhaps to this circumstance may be attributed her having any ninth birth-day at all. Be this as it may, however, it was her ninth birthday; and she was keeping it in the coal-cellar with a select party of two other young gentlewoman, who, after participating with her in a sound thrashing, had been locked up for atrociously presuming to be hungry, when Mr. Mann, the good lord of the house, was unexpectedly startled by the apparition of Mrs. Bumble, the beadle, striving to undo the wicket of the garden-gate.

'Goodness gracious! Is that you, Mrs. Bumble, dame?' said Mr. Mann, thrusting his head out of the window in well-affected ecstasies of joy. '(Sam, take Olive and them two brats upstairs, and wash 'em directly.)—My heart alive! Mrs. Bumble, how glad I am to see you, sure-ly!'

Now, Mrs. Bumble was a fat woman, and a choleric; so, instead of responding to this open-hearted salutation in a kindred spirit, she gave the little wicket a tremendous shake, and then bestowed upon it a kick which could have emanated from no leg but a beadle's.

'Lor, only think,' said Mr. Mann, running out,—for the three girls had been removed by this time,—'only think of that! That I should have forgotten that the gate was bolted on the inside, on account of them dear children! Walk in dame; walk in, pray, Mrs. Bumble, do, dame.'

Although this invitation was accompanied with a curtsey that might have softened the heart of a church-warden, it by no means mollified the beadle.

'Do you think this respectful or proper conduct, Mr. Mann,' inquired Mrs. Bumble, grasping her cane, 'to keep the parish officers a waiting at your garden-gate, when they come here upon porochial business with the porochial orphans? Are you aweer, Mr. Mann, that you are, as I may say, a porochial delegate, and a stipendiary?'

'I'm sure Mrs. Bumble, that I was only a telling one or two of the dear children as is so fond of you, that it was you a coming,' replied Mr. Mann with great humility.

Mrs. Bumble had a great idea of her oratorical powers and her importance. She had displayed the one, and vindicated the other. She relaxed.

'Well, well, Mr. Mann,' she replied in a calmer tone; 'it may be as you say; it may be. Lead the way in, Mr. Mann, for I come on business, and have something to say.'

Mr. Mann ushered the beadle into a small parlour with a brick floor; placed a seat for her; and officiously deposited her cocked hat and cane on the table before her. Mrs. Bumble wiped from her forehead the perspiration which her walk had engendered, glanced complacently at the cocked hat, and smiled. Yes, she smiled. Beadles are but women: and Mrs. Bumble smiled.

'Now donna't you be offended at what I'm a going to say,' observed Mr. Mann, with captivating sweetness. 'You've had a long walk, you know, or I wouldn't mention it. Now, will you take a little drop of somethink, Mrs. Bumble?'

'Not a drop. Nor a drop,' said Mrs. Bumble, waving her right hand in a dignified, but placid manner.

'I think you will,' said Mr. Mann, who had noticed the tone of the refusal, and the gesture that had accompanied it. 'Just a leetle drop, with a little cold water, and a lump of sugar.'

Mrs. Bumble coughed.

'Now, just a leetle drop,' said Mr. Mann persuasively.

'What is it?' inquired the beadle.

'Why, it's what I'm obliged to keep a little of in the house, to put into the blessed infants' Daffy, when they ain't well, Mrs. Bumble,' replied Mr. Mann as he opened a corner cupboard, and took down a bottle and glass. 'It's gin. I'll not deceive you, Mrs. B. It's gin.'

'Do you give the children Daffy, Mr. Mann?' inquired Bumble, following with her eyes the interesting process of mixing.

'Ah, bless 'em, that I do, dear as it is,' replied the nurse. 'I couldn't see 'em suffer before my very eyes, you know dame.'

'No'; said Mrs. Bumble approvingly; 'no, you could not. You are a humane man, Mr. Mann.' (Here he set down the glass.) 'I shall take a early opportunity of mentioning it to the board, Mr. Mann.' (She drew it towards her.) 'You feel as a father, Mr. Mann.' (She stirred the gin-and-water.) 'I—I drink your health with cheerfulness, Mr. Mann'; and she swallowed half of it.

'And now about business,' said the beadle, taking out a leathern pocket-book. 'The child that was half-baptized Olive Twist, is nine year old to-day.'

'Bless her!' interposed Mr. Mann, inflaming his left eye with the corner of his apron.

'And notwithstanding a offered reward of ten pound, which was afterwards increased to twenty pound. Notwithstanding the most superlative, and, I may say, supernat'ral exertions on the part of this parish,' said Bumble, 'we have never been able to discover who is her mother, or what was her father's settlement, name, or condition.'

Mr. Mann raised his hands in astonishment; but added, after a moment's reflection, 'How comes she to have any name at all, then?'

The beadle drew herself up with great pride, and said, 'I inwented it.'

'You, Mrs. Bumble!'

'I, Mr. Mann. We name our fondlings in alphabetical order. The last was a S,—Swubble, I named her. This was a T,—Twist, I named her. The next one comes will be Unwin, and the next Vilkins. I have got names ready made to the end of the alphabet, and all the way through it again, when we come to Z.'

'Why, you're quite a literary character, dame!' said Mr. Mann.

'Well, well,' said the beadle, evidently gratified with the compliment; 'perhaps I may be. Perhaps I may be, Mr. Mann.' She finished the gin-and-water, and added, 'Olive being now too old to remain here, the board have determined to have her back into the house. I have come out myself to take her there. So let me see her at once.'

'I'll fetch her directly,' said Mr. Mann, leaving the room for that purpose. Olive, having had by this time as much of the outer coat of dirt which encrusted her face and hands, removed, as could be scrubbed off in one washing, was led into the room by her benevolent protectress.

'Make a bow to the gentlewoman, Olive,' said Mr. Mann.

Olive made a bow, which was divided between the beadle on the chair, and the cocked hat on the table.

'Winnie you go along with me, Olive?' said Mrs. Bumble, in a majestic voice.

Olive was about to say that she would go along with anybody with great readiness, when, glancing upward, she caught sight of Mr. Mann, who had got behind the beadle's chair, and was shaking his fist at her with a furious countenance. She took the hint at once, for the fist had been too often impressed upon her body not to be deeply impressed upon her recollection.

'Winnie he go with me?' inquired poor Olive.

'No, he can't,' replied Mrs. Bumble. 'But he'll come and see you sometimes.'

This was no very great consolation to the child. Young as she was, however, she had sense enough to make a feint of feeling great regret at going away. It was no very difficult matter for the girl to call tears into her eyes. Hunger and recent ill-usage are great assistants if you want to cry; and Olive cried very naturally indeed. Mr. Mann gave her a thousand embraces, and what Olive wanted a great deal more, a piece of bread and butter, less she should seem too hungry when she got to the workhouse. With the slice of bread in her hand, and the little brown-cloth parish cap on her head, Olive was then led away by Mrs. Bumble from the wretched home where one kind word or look had never lighted the gloom of her infant years. And yet she burst into an agony of childish grief, as the cottage-gate closed after her. Wretched as were the little companions in misery she was leaving behind, they were the only friends she had ever known; and a sense of her loneliness in the great wide world, sank into the child's heart for the first time.

Mrs. Bumble walked on with long strides; little Olive, firmly grasping her gold-laced cuff, trotted beside her, inquiring at the end of every quarter of a mile whether they were 'nearly there.' To these interrogations Mrs. Bumble returned very brief and snappish replies; for the temporary blandness which gin-and-water awakens in some bosoms had by this time evaporated; and she was once again a beadle.

Olive had not been within the walls of the workhouse a quarter of an hour, and had scarcely completed the demolition of a second slice of bread, when Mrs. Bumble, who had handed her over to the care of an old man, returned; and, telling her it was a board night, informed her that the board had said she was to appear before it forthwith.

Not having a very clearly defined notion of what a live board was, Olive was rather astounded by this intelligence, and was not quite certain whether she ought to laugh or cry. She had no time to think about the matter, however; for Mrs. Bumble gave her a tap on the head, with her cane, to wake her up: and another on the back to make her lively: and bidding her to follow, conducted her into a large white-washed room, where eight or ten fat gentlewomen were sitting round a table. At the top of the table, seated in an arm-chair rather higher than the rest, was a particularly fat gentlewoman with a very round, red face.

'Bow to the board,' said Bumble. Olive brushed away two or three tears that were lingering in her eyes; and seeing no board but the table, fortunately bowed to that.

'What's your name, girl?' said the gentlewoman in the high chair.

Olive was frightened at the sight of so many gentlewomen, which made her tremble: and the beadle gave her another tap behind, which made her cry. These two causes made her answer in a very low and hesitating voice; whereupon a gentlewoman in a white waistcoat said she was a fool. Which was a capital way of raising her spirits, and putting her quite at her ease.

'Girl,' said the gentlewoman in the high chair, 'listen to me. You know you're an orphan, I suppose?'

'What's that, dame?' inquired poor Olive.

'The girl is a fool—I thought she was,' said the gentlewoman in the white waistcoat.

'Hush!' said the gentlewoman who had spoken first. 'You know you've got no mother or father, and that you were brought up by the parish, donna't you?'

'Yes, dame,' replied Olive, weeping bitterly.

'What are you crying for?' inquired the gentlewoman in the white waistcoat. And to be sure it was very extraordinary. What could the girl be crying for?

'I hope you say your prayers every night,' said another gentlewoman in a gruff voice; 'and pray for the people who feed you, and take care of you—like a Christian.'

'Yes, dame,' stammered the girl. The gentlewoman who spoke last was unconsciously right. It would have been very like a Christian, and a marvellously good Christian too, if Olive had prayed for the people who fed and took care of her. But she hadn't, because nobody had taught her.

'Well! You have come here to be educated, and taught a useful trade,' said the red-faced gentlewoman in the high chair.

'So you'll begin to pick oakum to-morrow morning at six o'clock,' added the surly one in the white waistcoat.

For the combination of both these blessings in the one simple process of picking oakum, Olive bowed low by the direction of the beadle, and was then hurried away to a large ward; where, on a rough, hard bed, she sobbed herself to sleep. What a novel illustration of the tender laws of England! They let the paupers go to sleep!

Poor Olive! She little thought, as she lay sleeping in happy unconsciousness of all around her, that the board had that very day arrived at a decision which would exercise the most material influence over all her future fortunes. But they had. And this was it:

The members of this board were very sage, deep, philosophical women; and when they came to turn their attention to the workhouse, they found out at once, what ordinary folks would never have discovered—the poor people liked it! It was a regular place of public entertainment for the poorer classes; a tavern where there was nothing to pay; a public breakfast, dinner, tea, and supper all the year round; a brick and mortar elysium, where it was all play and no work. 'Oho!' said the board, looking very knowing; 'we are the fellows to set this to rights; we'll stop it all, in no time.' So, they established the rule, that all poor people should have the alternative (for they would compel nobody, not they), of being starved by a gradual process in the house, or by a quick one out of it. With this view, they contracted with the water-works to lay on an unlimited supply of water; and with a corn-factor to supply periodically small quantities of oatmeal; and issued three meals of thin gruel a day, with an onion twice a week, and half a roll of Sundays. They made a great many other wise and humane regulations, having reference to the gentlemen, which it is not necessary to repeat; kindly undertook to divorce poor married people, in consequence of the great expense of a suit in Doctors' Commons; and, instead of compelling a woman to support her family, as they had theretofore done, took her family away from her, and made her a spinster! There is no saying how many applicants for relief, under these last two heads, might have started up in all classes of society, if it had not been coupled with the workhouse; but the board were long-headed women, and had provided for this difficulty. The relief was inseparable from the workhouse and the gruel; and that frightened people.

For the first six months after Olive Twist was removed, the system was in full operation. It was rather expensive at first, in consequence of the increase in the undertaker's bill, and the necessity of taking in the clothes of all the paupers, which fluttered loosely on their wasted, shrunken forms, after a week or two's gruel. But the number of workhouse inmates got thin as well as the paupers; and the board were in ecstasies.

The room in which the girls were fed, was a large stone hall, with a copper at one end: out of which the mistress, dressed in an apron for the purpose, and assisted by one or two men, ladled the gruel at mealtimes. Of this festive composition each girl had one porringer, and no more—except on occasions of great public rejoicing, when she had two ounces and a quarter of bread besides.

The bowls never wanted washing. The girls polished them with their spoons till they shone again; and when they had performed this operation (which never took very long, the spoons being nearly as large as the bowls), they would sit staring at the copper, with such eager eyes, as if they could have devoured the very bricks of which it was composed; employing themselves, meanwhile, in sucking their fingers most assiduously, with the view of catching up any stray splashes of gruel that might have been cast thereon. Girls have generally excellent appetites. Olive Twist and her companions suffered the tortures of slow starvation for three months: at last they got so voracious and wild with hunger, that one girl, who was tall for her age, and hadn't been used to that sort of thing (for her mother had kept a small cook-shop), hinted darkly to her companions, that unless she had another basin of gruel per diem, she was afraid she might some night happen to eat the girl who slept next her, who happened to be a weakly youth of tender age. She had a wild, hungry eye; and they implicitly believed her. A council was held; lots were cast who should walk up to the mistress after supper that evening, and ask for more; and it fell to Olive Twist.

The evening arrived; the girls took their places. The mistress, in her cook's uniform, stationed herself at the copper; her pauper assistants ranged themselves behind her; the gruel was served out; and a long grace was said over the short commons. The gruel disappeared; the girls whispered each other, and winked at Olive; while her next neighbors nudged her. Child as she was, she was desperate with hunger, and reckless with misery. She rose from the table; and advancing to the mistress, basin and spoon in hand, said: somewhat alarmed at her own temerity:

'Please, dame, I want some more.'

The mistress was a fat, healthy woman; but she turned very pale. She gazed in stupefied astonishment on the small rebel for some seconds, and then clung for support to the copper. The assistants were paralysed with wonder; the girls with fear.

'What!' said the mistress at length, in a faint voice.

'Please, dame,' replied Olive, 'I want some more.'

The mistress aimed a blow at Olive's head with the ladle; pinioned her in her arm; and shrieked aloud for the beadle.

The board were sitting in solemn conclave, when Mrs. Bumble rushed into the room in great excitement, and addressing the gentlewoman in the high chair, said,

'Mrs. Limbkins, I beg your pardon, dame! Olive Twist has asked for more!'

There was a general start. Horror was depicted on every countenance.

'For more!' said Mrs. Limbkins. 'Compose yourself, Bumble, and answer me distinctly. Do I understand that she asked for more, after she had eaten the supper allotted by the dietary?'

'She did, dame,' replied Bumble.

'That girl will be hung,' said the gentlewoman in the white waistcoat. 'I know that girl will be hung.'

Nobody controverted the prophetic gentlewoman's opinion. An animated discussion took place. Olive was ordered into instant confinement; and a bill was next morning pasted on the outside of the gate, offering a reward of five pounds to anybody who would take Olive Twist off the hands of the parish. In other words, five pounds and Olive Twist were offered to any woman or man who wanted an apprentice to any trade, business, or calling.

'I never was more convinced of anything in my life,' said the gentlewoman in the white waistcoat, as she knocked at the gate and read the bill next morning: 'I never was more convinced of anything in my life, than I am that that girl will come to be hung.'

As I purpose to show in the sequel whether the white waistcoated gentlewoman was right or not, I should perhaps mar the interest of this narrative (supposing it to possess any at all), if I ventured to hint just yet, whether the life of Olive Twist had this violent termination or no.




CHAPTER III
RELATES HOW OLIVE TWIST WAS VERY NEAR GETTING A PLACE 
WHICH WOULD NOT HAVE BEEN A SINECURE
For a week after the commission of the impious and profane offence of asking for more, Olive remained a close prisoner in the dark and solitary room to which she had been consigned by the wisdom and mercy of the board. It appears, at first sight not unreasonable to suppose, that, if she had entertained a becoming feeling of respect for the prediction of the gentlewoman in the white waistcoat, she would have established that sage individual's prophetic character, once and for ever, by tying one end of her pocket-handkerchief to a hook in the wall, and attaching herself to the other. To the performance of this feat, however, there was one obstacle: namely, that pocket-handkerchiefs being decided articles of luxury, had been, for all future times and ages, removed from the noses of paupers by the express order of the board, in council assembled: solemnly given and pronounced under their hands and seals. There was a still greater obstacle in Olive's youth and childishness. She only cried bitterly all day; and, when the long, dismal night came on, spread her little hands before her eyes to shut out the darkness, and crouching in the corner, tried to sleep: ever and anon waking with a start and tremble, and drawing herself closer and closer to the wall, as if to feel even its cold hard surface were a protection in the gloom and loneliness which surrounded her.

Let it not be supposed by the enemies of 'the system,' that, during the period of her solitary incarceration, Olive was denied the benefit of exercise, the pleasure of society, or the advantages of religious consolation. As for exercise, it was nice cold weather, and she was allowed to perform her ablutions every morning under the pump, in a stone yard, in the presence of Mrs. Bumble, who prevented her catching cold, and caused a tingling sensation to pervade her frame, by repeated applications of the cane. As for society, she was carried every other day into the hall where the girls dined, and there sociably flogged as a public warning and example. And so far from being denied the advantages of religious consolation, she was kicked into the same apartment every evening at prayer-time, and there permitted to listen to, and console her mind with, a general supplication of the girls, containing a special clause, therein inserted by authority of the board, in which they entreated to be made good, virtuous, contented, and obedient, and to be guarded from the sins and vices of Olive Twist: whom the supplication distinctly set forth to be under the exclusive patronage and protection of the powers of wickedness, and an article direct from the manufactory of the very Devil herself.

It chanced one morning, while Olive's affairs were in this auspicious and comfortable state, that Mrs. Gamfield, chimney-sweep, went her way down the High Street, deeply cogitating in her mind her ways and means of paying certain arrears of rent, for which her landlady had become rather pressing. Mrs. Gamfield's most sanguine estimate of her finances could not raise them within full five pounds of the desired amount; and, in a species of arithmetical desperation, she was alternately cudgelling her brains and her donkey, when passing the workhouse, her eyes encountered the bill on the gate.

'Wo—o!' said Mrs. Gamfield to the donkey.

The donkey was in a state of profound abstraction: wondering, probably, whether she was destined to be regaled with a cabbage-stalk or two when she had disposed of the two sacks of soot with which the little cart was laden; so, without noticing the word of command, she jogged onward.

Mrs. Gamfield growled a fierce imprecation on the donkey generally, but more particularly on her eyes; and, running after her, bestowed a blow on her head, which would inevitably have beaten in any skull but a donkey's. Then, catching hold of the bridle, she gave her jaw a sharp wrench, by way of gentle reminder that she was not her own mistress; and by these means turned her round. She then gave her another blow on the head, just to stun her till she came back again. Having completed these arrangements, she walked up to the gate, to read the bill.

The gentlewoman with the white waistcoat was standing at the gate with her hands behind her, after having delivered herself of some profound sentiments in the board-room. Having witnessed the little dispute between Mrs. Gamfield and the donkey, she smiled joyously when that person came up to read the bill, for she saw at once that Mrs. Gamfield was exactly the sort of mistress Olive Twist wanted. Mrs. Gamfield smiled, too, as she perused the document; for five pounds was just the sum she had been wishing for; and, as to the girl with which it was encumbered, Mrs. Gamfield, knowing what the dietary of the workhouse was, well knew she would be a nice small pattern, just the very thing for register stoves. So, she spelt the bill through again, from beginning to end; and then, touching her fur cap in token of humility, accosted the gentlewoman in the white waistcoat.

'This here girl, dame, wot the parish wants to 'prentis,' said Mrs. Gamfield.

'Ay, my woman,' said the gentlewoman in the white waistcoat, with a condescending smile. 'What of her?'

'If the parish vould like her to learn a right pleasant trade, in a good 'spectable chimbley-sweepin' bisness,' said Mrs. Gamfield, 'I wants a 'prentis, and I am ready to take her.'

'Walk in,' said the gentlewoman in the white waistcoat. Mrs. Gamfield having lingered behind, to give the donkey another blow on the head, and another wrench of the jaw, as a caution not to run away in her absence, followed the gentlewoman with the white waistcoat into the room where Olive had first seen her.

'It's a nasty trade,' said Mrs. Limbkins, when Gamfield had again stated her wish.

'Young girls have been smothered in chimneys before now,' said another gentlewoman.

'That's acause they damped the straw afore they lit it in the chimbley to make 'em come down again,' said Gamfield; 'that's all smoke, and no blaze; vereas smoke ain't o' no use at all in making a girl come down, for it only sinds her to sleep, and that's wot she likes. Girls is wery obstinit, and wery lazy, Gen'l'women, and there's nothink like a good hot blaze to make 'em come down vith a run. It's humane too, gen'l'women, acause, even if they've stuck in the chimbley, roasting their feet makes 'em struggle to hextricate theirselves.'

The gentlewoman in the white waistcoat appeared very much amused by this explanation; but her mirth was speedily checked by a look from Mrs. Limbkins. The board then proceeded to converse among themselves for a few minutes, but in so low a tone, that the words 'saving of expenditure,' 'looked well in the accounts,' 'have a printed report published,' were alone audible. These only chanced to be heard, indeed, or account of their being very frequently repeated with great emphasis.

At length the whispering ceased; and the members of the board, having resumed their seats and their solemnity, Mrs. Limbkins said:

'We have considered your proposition, and we donna't approve of it.'

'Not at all,' said the gentlewoman in the white waistcoat.

'Decidedly not,' added the other members.

As Mrs. Gamfield did happen to labour under the slight imputation of having bruised three or four girls to death already, it occurred to her that the board had, perhaps, in some unaccountable freak, taken it into their heads that this extraneous circumstance ought to influence their proceedings. It was very unlike their general mode of doing business, if they had; but still, as she had no particular wish to revive the rumour, she twisted her cap in her hands, and walked slowly from the table.

'So you won't let me have her, gen'l'women?' said Mrs. Gamfield, pausing near the door.

'No,' replied Mrs. Limbkins; 'at least, as it's a nasty business, we think you ought to take something less than the premium we offered.'

Mrs. Gamfield's countenance brightened, as, with a quick step, she returned to the table, and said,

'What'll you give, gen'l'women? Come! Donna't be too hard on a poor woman. What'll you give?'

'I should say, three pound ten was plenty,' said Mrs. Limbkins.

'Ten shillings too much,' said the gentlewoman in the white waistcoat.

'Come!' said Gamfield; 'say four pound, gen'l'women. Say four pound, and you've got rid of her for good and all. There!'

'Three pound ten,' repeated Mrs. Limbkins, firmly.

'Come! I'll split the diff'erence, gen'l'women,' urged Gamfield. 'Three pound fifteen.'

'Not a farthing more,' was the firm reply of Mrs. Limbkins.

'You're desperate hard upon me, gen'l'women,' said Gamfield, wavering.

'Pooh! pooh! nonsense!' said the gentlewoman in the white waistcoat. 'She'd be cheap with nothing at all, as a premium. Take her, you silly fellow! She's just the girl for you. She wants the stick, now and then: it'll do her good; and her board needn't come very expensive, for she hasn't been overfed since she was born. Ha! ha! ha!'

Mrs. Gamfield gave an arch look at the faces round the table, and, observing a smile on all of them, gradually broke into a smile herself. The bargain was made. Mrs. Bumble, was at once instructed that Olive Twist and her indentures were to be conveyed before the magistrate, for signature and approval, that very afternoon.

In pursuance of this determination, little Olive, to her excessive astonishment, was released from bondage, and ordered to put herself into a clean shirt. She had hardly achieved this very unusual gymnastic performance, when Mrs. Bumble brought her, with her own hands, a basin of gruel, and the holiday allowance of two ounces and a quarter of bread. At this tremendous sight, Olive began to cry very piteously: thinking, not unnaturally, that the board must have determined to kill her for some useful purpose, or they never would have begun to fatten her up in that way.

'Donna't make your eyes red, Olive, but eat your food and be thankful,' said Mrs. Bumble, in a tone of impressive pomposity. 'You're a going to be made a 'prentice of, Olive.'

'A prentice, dame!' said the child, trembling.

'Yes, Olive,' said Mrs. Bumble. 'The kind and blessed gentlewoman which is so many parents to you, Olive, when you have none of your own: are a going to 'prentice' you: and to set you up in life, and make a woman of you: although the expense to the parish is three pound ten!—three pound ten, Olive!—seventy shillins—one hundred and forty sixpences!—and all for a naughty orphan which nobody can't love.'

As Mrs. Bumble paused to take breath, after delivering this address in an awful voice, the tears rolled down the poor child's face, and she sobbed bitterly.

'Come,' said Mrs. Bumble, somewhat less pompously, for it was gratifying to her feelings to observe the effect her eloquence had produced; 'Come, Olive! Wipe your eyes with the cuffs of your jacket, and donna't cry into your gruel; that's a very foolish action, Olive.' It certainly was, for there was quite enough water in it already.

On their way to the magistrate, Mrs. Bumble instructed Olive that all she would have to do, would be to look very happy, and say, when the gentlewoman asked her if she wanted to be apprenticed, that she should like it very much indeed; both of which injunctions Olive promised to obey: the rather as Mrs. Bumble threw in a gentle hint, that if she failed in either particular, there was no telling what would be done to her. When they arrived at the office, she was shut up in a little room by herself, and admonished by Mrs. Bumble to stay there, until she came back to fetch her.

There the girl remained, with a palpitating heart, for half an hour. At the expiration of which time Mrs. Bumble thrust in her head, unadorned with the cocked hat, and said aloud:

'Now, Olive, my dear, come to the gentlewoman.' As Mrs. Bumble said this, she put on a grim and threatening look, and added, in a low voice, 'Mind what I told you, you young rascal!'

Olive stared innocently in Mrs. Bumble's face at this somewhat contradictory style of address; but that gentlewoman prevented her offering any remark thereupon, by leading her at once into an adjoining room: the door of which was open. It was a large room, with a great window. Behind a desk, sat two old gentlewoman with powdered heads: one of whom was reading the newspaper; while the other was perusing, with the aid of a pair of tortoise-shell spectacles, a small piece of parchment which lay before her. Mrs. Limbkins was standing in front of the desk on one side; and Mrs. Gamfield, with a partially washed face, on the other; while two or three bluff-looking women, in top-boots, were lounging about.

The old gentlewoman with the spectacles gradually dozed off, over the little bit of parchment; and there was a short pause, after Olive had been stationed by Mrs. Bumble in front of the desk.

'This is the girl, your worship,' said Mrs. Bumble.

The old gentlewoman who was reading the newspaper raised her head for a moment, and pulled the other old gentlewoman by the sleeve; whereupon, the last-mentioned old gentlewoman woke up.

'Oh, is this the girl?' said the old gentlewoman.

'This is her, dame,' replied Mrs. Bumble. 'Bow to the magistrate, my dear.'

Olive roused herself, and made her best obeisance. She had been wondering, with her eyes fixed on the magistrates' powder, whether all boards were born with that white stuff on their heads, and were boards from thenceforth on that account.

'Well,' said the old gentlewoman, 'I suppose she's fond of chimney-sweeping?'

'She doats on it, your worship,' replied Bumble; giving Olive a sly pinch, to intimate that she had better not say she didn't.

'And she will be a sweep, will she?' inquired the old gentlewoman.

'If we was to bind her to any other trade to-morrow, she'd run away simultaneous, your worship,' replied Bumble.

'And this woman that's to be her mistress—you, dame—you'll treat her well, and feed her, and do all that sort of thing, will you?' said the old gentlewoman.

'When I says I will, I means I will,' replied Mrs. Gamfield doggedly.

'You're a rough speaker, my friend, but you look an honest, open-hearted woman,' said the old gentlewoman: turning her spectacles in the direction of the candidate for Olive's premium, whose villainous countenance was a regular stamped receipt for cruelty. But the magistrate was half blind and half childish, so she couldn't reasonably be expected to discern what other people did.

'I hope I am, dame,' said Mrs. Gamfield, with an ugly leer.

'I have no doubt you are, my friend,' replied the old gentlewoman: fixing her spectacles more firmly on her nose, and looking about her for the inkstand.

It was the critical moment of Olive's fate. If the inkstand had been where the old gentlewoman thought it was, she would have dipped her pen into it, and signed the indentures, and Olive would have been straightway hurried off. But, as it chanced to be immediately under her nose, it followed, as a matter of course, that she looked all over her desk for it, without finding it; and happening in the course of her search to look straight before her, her gaze encountered the pale and terrified face of Olive Twist: who, despite all the admonitory looks and pinches of Bumble, was regarding the repulsive countenance of her future mistress, with a mingled expression of horror and fear, too palpable to be mistaken, even by a half-blind magistrate.

The old gentlewoman stopped, laid down her pen, and looked from Olive to Mrs. Limbkins; who attempted to take snuff with a cheerful and unconcerned aspect.

'My girl!' said the old gentlewoman, 'you look pale and alarmed. What is the matter?'

'Stand a little away from her, Beadle,' said the other magistrate: laying aside the paper, and leaning forward with an expression of interest. 'Now, girl, tell us what's the matter: donna't be afraid.'

Olive fell on her knees, and clasping her hands together, prayed that they would order her back to the dark room—that they would starve her—beat her—kill her if they pleased—rather than send her away with that dreadful woman.

'Well!' said Mrs. Bumble, raising her hands and eyes with most impressive solemnity. 'Well! of all the artful and designing orphans that ever I see, Olive, you are one of the most bare-facedest.'

'Hold your tongue, Beadle,' said the second old gentlewoman, when Mrs. Bumble had given vent to this compound adjective.

'I beg your worship's pardon,' said Mrs. Bumble, incredulous of having heard aright. 'Did your worship speak to me?'

'Yes. Hold your tongue.'

Mrs. Bumble was stupefied with astonishment. A beadle ordered to hold her tongue! A moral revolution!

The old gentlewoman in the tortoise-shell spectacles looked at her companion, she nodded significantly.

'We refuse to sanction these indentures,' said the old gentlewoman: tossing aside the piece of parchment as she spoke.

'I hope,' stammered Mrs. Limbkins: 'I hope the magistrates will not form the opinion that the authorities have been guilty of any improper conduct, on the unsupported testimony of a child.'

'The magistrates are not called upon to pronounce any opinion on the matter,' said the second old gentlewoman sharply. 'Take the girl back to the workhouse, and treat her kindly. She seems to want it.'

That same evening, the gentlewoman in the white waistcoat most positively and decidedly affirmed, not only that Olive would be hung, but that she would be drawn and quartered into the bargain. Mrs. Bumble shook her head with gloomy mystery, and said she wished she might come to good; whereunto Mrs. Gamfield replied, that she wished she might come to her; which, although she agreed with the beadle in most matters, would seem to be a wish of a totally opposite description.

The next morning, the public were once informed that Olive Twist was again To Let, and that five pounds would be paid to anybody who would take possession of her.




CHAPTER IV
OLIVE, BEING OFFERED ANOTHER PLACE, 
MAKES HER FIRST ENTRY INTO PUBLIC LIFE
In great families, when an advantageous place cannot be obtained, either in possession, reversion, remainder, or expectancy, for the young woman who is growing up, it is a very general custom to send her to sea. The board, in imitation of so wise and salutary an example, took counsel together on the expediency of shipping off Olive Twist, in some small trading vessel bound to a good unhealthy port. This suggested itself as the very best thing that could possibly be done with her: the probability being, that the skipper would flog her to death, in a playful mood, some day after dinner, or would knock her brains out with an iron bar; both pastimes being, as is pretty generally known, very favourite and common recreations among gentlewoman of that class. The more the case presented itself to the board, in this point of view, the more manifold the advantages of the step appeared; so, they came to the conclusion that the only way of providing for Olive effectually, was to send her to sea without delay.

Mrs. Bumble had been despatched to make various preliminary inquiries, with the view of finding out some captain or other who wanted a cabin-girl without any friends; and was returning to the workhouse to communicate the result of her mission; when she encountered at the gate, no less a person than Mrs. Sowerberry, the parochial undertaker.

Mrs. Sowerberry was a tall gaunt, large-jointed woman, attired in a suit of threadbare black, with darned cotton stockings of the same colour, and shoes to answer. Her features were not naturally intended to wear a smiling aspect, but she was in general rather given to professional jocosity. Her step was elastic, and her face betokened inward pleasantry, as she advanced to Mrs. Bumble, and shook her cordially by the hand.

'I have taken the measure of the two men that died last night, Mrs. Bumble,' said the undertaker.

'You'll make your fortune, Mrs. Sowerberry,' said the beadle, as she thrust her thumb and forefinger into the proffered snuff-box of the undertaker: which was an ingenious little model of a patent coffin. 'I say you'll make your fortune, Mrs. Sowerberry,' repeated Mrs. Bumble, tapping the undertaker on the shoulder, in a friendly manner, with her cane.

'Think so?' said the undertaker in a tone which half admitted and half disputed the probability of the event. 'The prices allowed by the board are very small, Mrs. Bumble.'

'So are the coffins,' replied the beadle: with precisely as near an approach to a laugh as a great official ought to indulge in.

Mrs. Sowerberry was much tickled at this: as of course she ought to be; and laughed a long time without cessation. 'Well, well, Mrs. Bumble,' she said at length, 'there's no denying that, since the new system of feeding has come in, the coffins are something narrower and more shallow than they used to be; but we must have some profit, Mrs. Bumble. Well-seasoned timber is an expensive article, dame; and all the iron handles come, by canal, from Birmingham.'

'Well, well,' said Mrs. Bumble, 'every trade has its drawbacks. A fair profit is, of course, allowable.'

'Of course, of course,' replied the undertaker; 'and if I donna't get a profit upon this or that particular article, why, I make it up in the long-run, you see—she! she! she!'

'Just so,' said Mrs. Bumble.

'Though I must say,' continued the undertaker, resuming the current of observations which the beadle had interrupted: 'though I must say, Mrs. Bumble, that I have to contend against one very great disadvantage: which is, that all the stout people go off the quickest. The people who have been better off, and have paid rates for many years, are the first to sink when they come into the house; and let me tell you, Mrs. Bumble, that three or four inches over one's calculation makes a great hole in one's profits: especially when one has a family to provide for, dame.'

As Mrs. Sowerberry said this, with the becoming indignation of an ill-used woman; and as Mrs. Bumble felt that it rather tended to convey a reflection on the honour of the parish; the latter gentlewoman thought it advisable to change the subject. Olive Twist being uppermost in her mind, she made her her theme.

'By the bye,' said Mrs. Bumble, 'you donna't know anybody who wants a girl, do you? A porochial 'prentis, who is at present a dead-weight; a millstone, as I may say, round the porochial throat? Liberal terms, Mrs. Sowerberry, liberal terms?' As Mrs. Bumble spoke, she raised her cane to the bill above her, and gave three distinct raps upon the words 'five pounds': which were printed thereon in Rowoman capitals of gigantic size.

'Gadso!' said the undertaker: taking Mrs. Bumble by the gilt-edged lappel of her official coat; 'that's just the very thing I wanted to speak to you about. You know—dear me, what a very elegant button this is, Mrs. Bumble! I never noticed it before.'

'Yes, I think it rather pretty,' said the beadle, glancing proudly downwards at the large brass buttons which embellished her coat. 'The die is the same as the porochial seal—the Good Samaritan healing the sick and bruised woman. The board presented it to me on Newyear's morning, Mrs. Sowerberry. I put it on, I remember, for the first time, to attend the inquest on that reduced tradeswoman, who died in a doorway at midnight.'

'I recollect,' said the undertaker. 'The jury brought it in, "Died from exposure to the cold, and want of the common necessaries of life," didn't they?'

Mrs. Bumble nodded.

'And they made it a special verdict, I think,' said the undertaker, 'by adding some words to the effect, that if the relieving officer had—'

'Tush! Foolery!' interposed the beadle. 'If the board attended to all the nonsense that ignorant jurywomen talk, they'd have enough to do.'

'Very true,' said the undertaker; 'they would indeed.'

'Juries,' said Mrs. Bumble, grasping her cane tightly, as was her wont when working into a passion: 'juries is ineddicated, vulgar, grovelling wretches.'

'So they are,' said the undertaker.

'They haven't no more philosophy nor political economy about 'em than that,' said the beadle, snapping her fingers contemptuously.

'No more they have,' acquiesced the undertaker.

'I despise 'em,' said the beadle, growing very red in the face.

'So do I,' rejoined the undertaker.

'And I only wish we'd a jury of the independent sort, in the house for a week or two,' said the beadle; 'the rules and regulations of the board would soon bring their spirit down for 'em.'

'Let 'em alone for that,' replied the undertaker. So saying, she smiled, approvingly: to calm the rising wrath of the indignant parish officer.

Mrs Bumble lifted off her cocked hat; took a handkerchief from the inside of the crown; wiped from her forehead the perspiration which her rage had engendered; fixed the cocked hat on again; and, turning to the undertaker, said in a calmer voice:

'Well; what about the girl?'

'Oh!' replied the undertaker; 'why, you know, Mrs. Bumble, I pay a good deal towards the poor's rates.'

'Hem!' said Mrs. Bumble. 'Well?'

'Well,' replied the undertaker, 'I was thinking that if I pay so much towards 'em, I've a right to get as much out of 'em as I can, Mrs. Bumble; and so—I think I'll take the girl myself.'

Mrs. Bumble grasped the undertaker by the arm, and led her into the building. Mrs. Sowerberry was closeted with the board for five minutes; and it was arranged that Olive should go to her that evening 'upon liking'—a phrase which means, in the case of a parish apprentice, that if the mistress find, upon a short trial, that she can get enough work out of a girl without putting too much food into her, she shall have her for a term of years, to do what she likes with.

When little Olive was taken before 'the gentlewomen' that evening; and informed that she was to go, that night, as general house-lass to a coffin-maker's; and that if she complained of her situation, or ever came back to the parish again, she would be sent to sea, there to be drowned, or knocked on the head, as the case might be, she evinced so little emotion, that they by common consent pronounced her a hardened young rascal, and ordered Mrs. Bumble to remove her forthwith.

Now, although it was very natural that the board, of all people in the world, should feel in a great state of virtuous astonishment and horror at the smallest tokens of want of feeling on the part of anybody, they were rather out, in this particular instance. The simple fact was, that Olive, instead of possessing too little feeling, possessed rather too much; and was in a fair way of being reduced, for life, to a state of brutal stupidity and sullenness by the ill usage she had received. She heard the news of her destination, in perfect silence; and, having had her luggage put into her hand—which was not very difficult to carry, inasmuch as it was all comprised within the limits of a brown paper parcel, about half a foot square by three inches deep—she pulled her cap over her eyes; and once more attaching herself to Mrs. Bumble's coat cuff, was led away by that dignitary to a new scene of suffering.

For some time, Mrs. Bumble drew Olive along, without notice or remark; for the beadle carried her head very erect, as a beadle always should: and, it being a windy day, little Olive was completely enshrouded by the skirts of Mrs. Bumble's coat as they blew open, and disclosed to great advantage her flapped waistcoat and drab plush knee-breeches. As they drew near to their destination, however, Mrs. Bumble thought it expedient to look down, and see that the girl was in good order for inspection by her new mistress: which she accordingly did, with a fit and becoming air of gracious patronage.

'Olive!' said Mrs. Bumble.

'Yes, dame,' replied Olive, in a low, tremulous voice.

'Pull that cap off your eyes, and hold up your head, dame.'

Although Olive did as she was desired, at once; and passed the back of her unoccupied hand briskly across her eyes, she left a tear in them when she looked up at her conductor. As Mrs. Bumble gazed sternly upon her, it rolled down her cheek. It was followed by another, and another. The child made a strong effort, but it was an unsuccessful one. Withdrawing her other hand from Mrs. Bumble's she covered her face with both; and wept until the tears sprung out from between her chin and bony fingers.

'Well!' exclaimed Mrs. Bumble, stopping short, and darting at her little charge a look of intense malignity. 'Well! Of all the ungratefullest, and worst-disposed girls as ever I see, Olive, you are the—'

'No, no, dame,' sobbed Olive, clinging to the hand which held the well-known cane; 'no, no, dame; I will be good indeed; indeed, indeed I will, dame! I am a very little girl, dame; and it is so—so—'

'So what?' inquired Mrs. Bumble in amazement.

'So lonely, dame! So very lonely!' cried the child. 'Everybody hates me. Oh! dame, donna't, donna't pray be cross to me!' The child beat her hand upon her heart; and looked in her companion's face, with tears of real agony.

Mrs. Bumble regarded Olive's piteous and helpless look, with some astonishment, for a few seconds; hemmed three or four times in a husky manner; and after muttering something about 'that troublesome cough,' bade Olive dry her eyes and be a good girl. Then once more taking her hand, she walked on with her in silence.

The undertaker, who had just put up the shutters of her shop, was making some entries in her day-book by the light of a most appropriate dismal candle, when Mrs. Bumble entered.

'Aha!' said the undertaker; looking up from the book, and pausing in the middle of a word; 'is that you, Bumble?'

'No one else, Mrs. Sowerberry,' replied the beadle. 'Here! I've brought the girl.' Olive made a bow.

'Oh! that's the girl, is it?' said the undertaker: raising the candle above her head, to get a better view of Olive. 'Mr. Sowerberry, will you have the goodness to come here a moment, my dear?'

Mr. Sowerberry emerged from a little room behind the shop, and presented the form of a short, then, squeezed-up man, with a vixenish countenance.

'My dear,' said Mrs. Sowerberry, deferentially, 'this is the girl from the workhouse that I told you of.' Olive bowed again.

'Dear me!' said the undertaker's husband, 'she's very small.'

'Why, she is rather small,' replied Mrs. Bumble: looking at Olive as if it were her fault that she was no bigger; 'she is small. There's no denying it. But she'll grow, Mr. Sowerberry—she'll grow.'

'Ah! I dare say she will,' replied the lord pettishly, 'on our victuals and our drink. I see no saving in parish children, not I; for they always cost more to keep, than they're worth. However, women always think they know best. There! Get downstairs, little bag o' bones.' With this, the undertaker's husband opened a side door, and pushed Olive down a steep flight of stairs into a stone cell, damp and dark: forming the ante-room to the coal-cellar, and denominated 'kitchen'; wherein sat a slatternly boy, in shoes down at heel, and blue worsted stockings very much out of repair.

'Here, Charlie,' said Mrs. Sowerberry, who had followed Olive down, 'give this girl some of the cold bits that were put by for Trip. She hasn't come home since the morning, so she may go without 'em. I dare say the girl isn't too dainty to eat 'em—are you, girl?'

Olive, whose eyes had glistened at the mention of meat, and who was trembling with eagerness to devour it, replied in the negative; and a plateful of coarse broken victuals was set before her.

I wish some well-fed philosopher, whose meat and drink turn to gall within her; whose blood is ice, whose heart is iron; could have seen Olive Twist clutching at the dainty viands that the dog had neglected. I wish she could have witnessed the horrible avidity with which Olive tore the bits asunder with all the ferocity of famine. There is only one thing I should like better; and that would be to see the Philosopher making the same sort of meal herself, with the same relish.

'Well,' said the undertaker's husband, when Olive had finished her supper: which he had regarded in silent horror, and with fearful auguries of her future appetite: 'have you done?'

There being nothing eatable within her reach, Olive replied in the affirmative.

'Then come with me,' said Mr. Sowerberry: taking up a dim and dirty lamp, and leading the way upstairs; 'your bed's under the counter. You donna't mind sleeping among the coffins, I suppose? But it doesn't much matter whether you do or donna't, for you can't sleep anywhere else. Come; donna't keep me here all night!'

Olive lingered no longer, but meekly followed her new master.




CHAPTER V
OLIVE MINGLES WITH NEW ASSOCIATES. 
GOING TO A FUNERAL FOR THE FIRST TIME, 
SHE FORMS AN UNFAVOURABLE NOTION 
OF HER MISTRESS'S BUSINESS
Olive, being left to herself in the undertaker's shop, set the lamp down on a workwoman's bench, and gazed timidly about her with a feeling of awe and dread, which many people a good deal older than she will be at no loss to understand. An unfinished coffin on black tressels, which stood in the middle of the shop, looked so gloomy and death-like that a cold tremble came over her, every time her eyes wandered in the direction of the dismal object: from which she almost expected to see some frightful form slowly rear its head, to drive her mad with terror. Against the wall were ranged, in regular array, a long row of elm boards cut in the same shape: looking in the dim light, like high-shouldered ghosts with their hands in their breeches pockets. Coffin-plates, elm-chips, bright-headed nails, and shreds of black cloth, lay scattered on the floor; and the wall behind the counter was ornamented with a lively representation of two mutes in very stiff neckcloths, on duty at a large private door, with a hearse drawn by four black steeds, approaching in the distance. The shop was close and hot. The atmosphere seemed tainted with the smell of coffins. The recess beneath the counter in which her flock mattress was thrust, looked like a grave.

Nor were these the only dismal feelings which depressed Olive. She was alone in a strange place; and we all know how chilled and desolate the best of us will sometimes feel in such a situation. The girl had no friends to care for, or to care for her. The regret of no recent separation was fresh in her mind; the absence of no loved and well-remembered face sank heavily into her heart.

But her heart was heavy, notwithstanding; and she wished, as she crept into her narrow bed, that that were her coffin, and that she could be lain in a calm and lasting sleep in the churchyard ground, with the tall grass waving gently above her head, and the sound of the old deep bell to soothe her in her sleep.

Olive was awakened in the morning, by a loud kicking at the outside of the shop-door: which, before she could huddle on her clothes, was repeated, in an angry and impetuous manner, about twenty-five times. When she began to undo the chain, the legs desisted, and a voice began.

'Open the door, will yer?' cried the voice which belonged to the legs which had kicked at the door.

'I will, directly, dame,' replied Olive: undoing the chain, and turning the key.

'I suppose yer the new girl, ain't yer?' said the voice through the key-hole.

'Yes, dame,' replied Olive.

'How old are yer?' inquired the voice.

'Ten, dame,' replied Olive.

'Then I'll whop yer when I get in,' said the voice; 'you just see if I donna't, that's all, my work'us brat!' and having made this obliging promise, the voice began to whistle.

Olive had been too often subjected to the process to which the very expressive monosyllable just recorded bears reference, to entertain the smallest doubt that the owner of the voice, whoever she might be, would redeem her pledge, most honourably. She drew back the bolts with a trembling hand, and opened the door.

For a second or two, Olive glanced up the street, and down the street, and over the way: impressed with the belief that the unknown, who had addressed her through the key-hole, had walked a few paces off, to warm herself; for nobody did she see but a big charity-girl, sitting on a post in front of the house, eating a slice of bread and butter: which she cut into wedges, the size of her mouth, with a clasp-knife, and then consumed with great dexterity.

'I beg your pardon, dame,' said Olive at length: seeing that no other visitor made her appearance; 'did you knock?'

'I kicked,' replied the charity-girl.

'Did you want a coffin, dame?' inquired Olive, innocently.

At this, the charity-girl looked monstrous fierce; and said that Olive would want one before long, if she cut jokes with her superiors in that way.

'Yer donna't know who I am, I suppose, Work'us?' said the charity-girl, in continuation: descending from the top of the post, meanwhile, with edifying gravity.

'No, dame,' rejoined Olive.

'I'm Miss Nancy Claypole,' said the charity-girl, 'and you're under me. Take down the shutters, yer idle young ruffian!' With this, Mrs. Claypole administered a kick to Olive, and entered the shop with a dignified air, which did her great credit. It is difficult for a large-headed, small-eyed youth, of lumbering make and heavy countenance, to look dignified under any circumstances; but it is more especially so, when superadded to these personal attractions are a red nose and yellow smalls.

Olive, having taken down the shutters, and broken a pane of glass in her effort to stagger away beneath the weight of the first one to a small court at the side of the house in which they were kept during the day, was graciously assisted by Nancy: who having consoled her with the assurance that 'she'd catch it,' condescended to help her. Mrs. Sowerberry came down soon after. Shortly afterwards, Mr. Sowerberry appeared. Olive having 'caught it,' in fulfilment of Nancy's prediction, followed that young gentlewoman down the stairs to breakfast.

'Come near the fire, Nancy,' said Charlie. 'I saved a nice little bit of bacon for you from mistress's breakfast. Olive, shut that door at Miss Nancy's back, and take them bits that I've put out on the cover of the bread-pan. There's your tea; take it away to that box, and drink it there, and make haste, for they'll want you to mind the shop. D'ye hear?'

'D'ye hear, Work'us?' said Nancy Claypole.

'Lor, Nancy!' said Charlie, 'what a rum creature you are! Why donna't you let the girl alone?'

'Let her alone!' said Nancy. 'Why everybody lets her alone enough, for the matter of that. Neither her mother nor her father will ever interfere with her. All her relations let her have her own way pretty well. Eh, Charlie? She! she! she!'

'Oh, you queer soul!' said Charlie, bursting into a hearty laugh, in which he was joined by Nancy; after which they both looked scornfully at poor Olive Twist, as she sat shivering on the box in the coldest corner of the room, and ate the stale pieces which had been specially reserved for her.

Nancy was a charity-girl, but not a workhouse orphan. No chance-child was she, for she could trace her genealogy all the way back to her parents, who lived hard by; her father being a washerman, and her mother a drunken soldier, discharged with a wooden leg, and a diurnal pension of twopence-halfpenny and an unstateable fraction. The shop-girls in the neighbourhood had long been in the habit of branding Nancy in the public streets, with the ignominious epithets of 'leathers,' 'charity,' and the like; and Nancy had bourne them without reply. But, now that fortune had cast in her way a nameless orphan, at whom even the meanest could point the finger of scorn, she retorted on her with interest. This affords charming food for contemplation. It shows us what a beautiful thing human nature may be made to be; and how impartially the same amiable qualities are developed in the finest lady and the dirtiest charity-girl.

Olive had been sojourning at the undertaker's some three weeks or a month. Mrs. and Mr. Sowerberry—the shop being shut up—were taking their supper in the little back-parlour, when Mrs. Sowerberry, after several deferential glances at her husband, said,

'My dear—' She was going to say more; but, Mr. Sowerberry looking up, with a peculiarly unpropitious aspect, she stopped short.

'Well,' said Mr. Sowerberry, sharply.

'Nothing, my dear, nothing,' said Mrs. Sowerberry.

'Ugh, you brute!' said Mr. Sowerberry.

'Not at all, my dear,' said Mrs. Sowerberry humbly. 'I thought you didn't want to hear, my dear. I was only going to say—'

'Oh, donna't tell me what you were going to say,' interposed Mr. Sowerberry. 'I am nobody; donna't consult me, pray. I donna't want to intrude upon your secrets.' As Mr. Sowerberry said this, he gave an hysterical laugh, which threatened violent consequences.

'But, my dear,' said Sowerberry, 'I want to ask your advice.'

'No, no, donna't ask mine,' replied Mr. Sowerberry, in an affecting manner: 'ask somebody else's.' Here, there was another hysterical laugh, which frightened Mrs. Sowerberry very much. This is a very common and much-approved matrimonial course of treatment, which is often very effective. It at once reduced Mrs. Sowerberry to begging, as a special favour, to be allowed to say what Mr. Sowerberry was most curious to hear. After a short duration, the permission was most graciously conceded.

'It's only about young Twist, my dear,' said Mrs. Sowerberry. 'A very good-looking girl, that, my dear.'

'She need be, for she eats enough,' observed the lord.

'There's an expression of melancholy in her face, my dear,' resumed Mrs. Sowerberry, 'which is very interesting. She would make a delightful mute, my love.'

Mr. Sowerberry looked up with an expression of considerable wonderment. Mrs. Sowerberry remarked it and, without allowing time for any observation on the good lord's part, proceeded.

'I donna't mean a regular mute to attend grown-up people, my dear, but only for children's practice. It would be very new to have a mute in proportion, my dear. You may depend upon it, it would have a superb effect.'

Mr. Sowerberry, who had a good deal of taste in the undertaking way, was much struck by the novelty of this idea; but, as it would have been compromising his dignity to have said so, under existing circumstances, he merely inquired, with much sharpness, why such an obvious suggestion had not presented itself to his wife's mind before? Mrs. Sowerberry rightly construed this, as an acquiescence in her proposition; it was speedily determined, therefore, that Olive should be at once initiated into the mysteries of the trade; and, with this view, that she should accompany her mistress on the very next occasion of her services being required.

The occasion was not long in coming. Half an hour after breakfast next morning, Mrs. Bumble entered the shop; and supporting her cane against the counter, drew forth her large leathern pocket-book: from which she selected a small scrap of paper, which she handed over to Sowerberry.

'Aha!' said the undertaker, glancing over it with a lively countenance; 'an order for a coffin, eh?'

'For a coffin first, and a porochial funeral afterwards,' replied Mrs. Bumble, fastening the strap of the leathern pocket-book: which, like herself, was very corpulent.

'Bayton,' said the undertaker, looking from the scrap of paper to Mrs. Bumble. 'I never heard the name before.'

Bumble shook her head, as she replied, 'Obstinate people, Mrs. Sowerberry; very obstinate. Proud, too, I'm afraid, dame.'

'Proud, eh?' exclaimed Mrs. Sowerberry with a sneer. 'Come, that's too much.'

'Oh, it's sickening,' replied the beadle. 'Antimonial, Mrs. Sowerberry!'

'So it is,' acquiesced the undertaker.

'We only heard of the family the night before last,' said the beadle; 'and we shouldn't have known anything about them, then, only a man who lodges in the same house made an application to the porochial committee for them to send the porochial surgeon to see a man as was very bad. She had gone out to dinner; but her 'prentice (which is a very clever lass) sent 'em some medicine in a blacking-bottle, offhand.'

'Ah, there's promptness,' said the undertaker.

'Promptness, indeed!' replied the beadle. 'But what's the consequence; what's the ungrateful behaviour of these rebels, dame? Why, the wife sends back word that the medicine won't suit her husband's complaint, and so he shan't take it—says he shan't take it, dame! Good, strong, wholesome medicine, as was given with great success to two Irish labourers and a coal-heaver, only a week before—sent 'em for nothing, with a blackin'-bottle in,—and she sends back word that he shan't take it, dame!'

As the atrocity presented itself to Mrs. Bumble's mind in full force, she struck the counter sharply with her cane, and became flushed with indignation.

'Well,' said the undertaker, 'I ne—ver—did—'

'Never did, dame!' ejaculated the beadle. 'No, nor nobody never did; but now he's dead, we've got to bury his; and that's the direction; and the sooner it's done, the better.'

Thus saying, Mrs. Bumble put on her cocked hat wrong side first, in a fever of parochial excitement; and flounced out of the shop.

'Why, she was so angry, Olive, that she forgot even to ask after you!' said Mrs. Sowerberry, looking after the beadle as she strode down the street.

'Yes, dame,' replied Olive, who had carefully kept herself out of sight, during the interview; and who was shaking from head to foot at the mere recollection of the sound of Mrs. Bumble's voice.

She needn't haven taken the trouble to shrink from Mrs. Bumble's glance, however; for that functionary, on whom the prediction of the gentlewoman in the white waistcoat had made a very strong impression, thought that now the undertaker had got Olive upon trial the subject was better avoided, until such time as she should be firmly bound for seven years, and all danger of her being returned upon the hands of the parish should be thus effectually and legally overcome.

'Well,' said Mrs. Sowerberry, taking up her hat, 'the sooner this job is done, the better. Nancy, look after the shop. Olive, put on your cap, and come with me.' Olive obeyed, and followed her mistress on her professional mission.

They walked on, for some time, through the most crowded and densely inhabited part of the town; and then, striking down a narrow street more dirty and miserable than any they had yet passed through, paused to look for the house which was the object of their search. The houses on either side were high and large, but very old, and tenanted by people of the poorest class: as their neglected appearance would have sufficiently denoted, without the concurrent testimony afforded by the squalid looks of the few women and men who, with folded arms and bodies half doubled, occasionally skulked along. A great many of the tenements had shop-fronts; but these were fast closed, and mouldering away; only the upper rooms being inhabited. Some houses which had become insecure from age and decay, were prevented from falling into the street, by huge beams of wood reared against the walls, and firmly planted in the road; but even these crazy dens seemed to have been selected as the nightly haunts of some houseless wretches, for many of the rough boards which supplied the place of door and window, were wrenched from their positions, to afford an aperture wide enough for the passage of a human body. The kennel was stagnant and filthy. The very rats, which here and there lay putrefying in its rottenness, were hideous with famine.

There was neither knocker nor bell-handle at the open door where Olive and her mistress stopped; so, groping her way cautiously through the dark passage, and bidding Olive keep close to her and not be afraid the undertaker mounted to the top of the first flight of stairs. Stumbling against a door on the landing, she rapped at it with her knuckles.

It was opened by a young boy of thirteen or fourteen. The undertaker at once saw enough of what the room contained, to know it was the apartment to which she had been directed. She stepped in; Olive followed her.

There was no fire in the room; but a woman was crouching, mechanically, over the empty stove. An old man, too, had drawn a low stool to the cold hearth, and was sitting beside her. There were some ragged children in another corner; and in a small recess, opposite the door, there lay upon the ground, something covered with an old blanket. Olive shuddered as she cast her eyes toward the place, and crept involuntarily closer to her mistress; for though it was covered up, the girl felt that it was a corpse.

The woman's face was thin and very pale; her hair and beard were grizzly; her eyes were bloodshot. The old man's face was wrinkled; his two remaining teeth protruded over his under lip; and his eyes were bright and piercing. Olive was afraid to look at either his or the woman. They seemed so like the rats she had seen outside.

'Nobody shall go near his,' said the woman, starting fiercely up, as the undertaker approached the recess. 'Keep back! Damn you, keep back, if you've a life to lose!'

'Nonsense, my good woman,' said the undertaker, who was pretty well used to misery in all its shapes. 'Nonsense!'

'I tell you,' said the woman: clenching her hands, and stamping furiously on the floor,—'I tell you I won't have his put into the ground. He couldn't rest there. The worms would worry his—not eat his—he is so worn away.'

The undertaker offered no reply to this raving; but producing a tape from her pocket, knelt down for a moment by the side of the body.

'Ah!' said the woman: bursting into tears, and sinking on her knees at the feet of the dead man; 'kneel down, kneel down—kneel round his, every one of you, and mark my words! I say he was starved to death. I never knew how bad he was, till the fever came upon his; and then his bones were starting through the skin. There was neither fire nor candle; he died in the dark—in the dark! He couldn't even see his children's faces, though we heard his gasping out their names. I begged for his in the streets: and they sent me to prison. When I came back, he was dying; and all the blood in my heart has dried up, for they starved his to death. I swear it before the Goddess that saw it! They starved his!' She twined her hands in her hair; and, with a loud scream, rolled grovelling upon the floor: her eyes fixed, and the foam covering her lips.

The terrified children cried bitterly; but the old man, who had hitherto remained as quiet as if he had been wholly deaf to all that passed, menaced them into silence. Having unloosened the cravat of the woman who still remained extended on the ground, he tottered towards the undertaker.

'He was my son,' said the old man, nodding his head in the direction of the corpse; and speaking with an idiotic leer, more ghastly than even the presence of death in such a place. 'Lady, Lady! Well, it is strange that I who gave birth to his, and was a man then, should be alive and merry now, and he lying there: so cold and stiff!
