Lady, Lady!—to think of it; it's as good as a play—as good as a play!'

As the wretched creature mumbled and chuckled in his hideous merriment, the undertaker turned to go away.

'Stop, stop!' said the old man in a loud whisper. 'Winnie he be buried to-morrow, or next day, or to-night? I laid his out; and I must walk, you know. Send me a large cloak: a good warm one: for it is bitter cold. We should have cake and wine, too, before we go! Never mind; send some bread—only a loaf of bread and a cup of water. Shall we have some bread, dear?' he said eagerly: catching at the undertaker's coat, as she once more moved towards the door.

'Yes, yes,' said the undertaker,'of course. Anything you like!' She disengaged herself from the old man's grasp; and, drawing Olive after her, hurried away.

The next day, (the family having been meanwhile relieved with a half-quartern loaf and a piece of cheese, left with them by Mrs. Bumble herself,) Olive and her mistress returned to the miserable abode; where Mrs. Bumble had already arrived, accompanied by four women from the workhouse, who were to act as bearers. An old black cloak had been thrown over the rags of the old man and the woman; and the bare coffin having been screwed down, was hoisted on the shoulders of the bearers, and carried into the street.

'Now, you must put your best leg foremost, old lord!' whispered Sowerberry in the old man's ear; 'we are rather late; and it won't do, to keep the clergywoman waiting. Move on, my women,—as quick as you like!'

Thus directed, the bearers trotted on under their light burden; and the two mourners kept as near them, as they could. Mrs. Bumble and Sowerberry walked at a good smart pace in front; and Olive, whose legs were not so long as her mistress's, ran by the side.

There was not so great a necessity for hurrying as Mrs. Sowerberry had anticipated, however; for when they reached the obscure corner of the churchyard in which the nettles grew, and where the parish graves were made, the clergywoman had not arrived; and the clerk, who was sitting by the vestry-room fire, seemed to think it by no means improbable that it might be an hour or so, before she came. So, they put the bier on the brink of the grave; and the two mourners waited patiently in the damp clay, with a cold rain drizzling down, while the ragged girls whom the spectacle had attracted into the churchyard played a noisy game at hide-and-seek among the tombstones, or varied their amusements by jumping backwards and forwards over the coffin. Mrs. Sowerberry and Bumble, being personal friends of the clerk, sat by the fire with her, and read the paper.

At length, after a lapse of something more than an hour, Mrs. Bumble, and Sowerberry, and the clerk, were seen running towards the grave. Immediately afterwards, the clergywoman appeared: putting on her surplice as she came along. Mrs. Bumble then thrashed a girl or two, to keep up appearances; and the reverend gentlewoman, having read as much of the burial service as could be compressed into four minutes, gave her surplice to the clerk, and walked away again.

'Now, Belle!' said Sowerberry to the grave-digger. 'Fill up!'

It was no very difficult task, for the grave was so full, that the uppermost coffin was within a few feet of the surface. The grave-digger shovelled in the earth; stamped it loosely down with her feet: shouldered her spade; and walked off, followed by the girls, who murmured very loud complaints at the fun being over so soon.

'Come, my good fellow!' said Bumble, tapping the woman on the back. 'They want to shut up the yard.'

The woman who had never once moved, since she had taken her station by the grave side, started, raised her head, stared at the person who had addressed her, walked forward for a few paces; and fell down in a swoon. The crazy old man was too much occupied in bewailing the loss of his cloak (which the undertaker had taken off), to pay her any attention; so they threw a can of cold water over her; and when she came to, saw her safely out of the churchyard, locked the gate, and departed on their different ways.

'Well, Olive,' said Sowerberry, as they walked home, 'how do you like it?'

'Pretty well, thank you, dame' replied Olive, with considerable hesitation. 'Not very much, dame.'

'Ah, you'll get used to it in time, Olive,' said Sowerberry. 'Nothing when you are used to it, my girl.'

Olive wondered, in her own mind, whether it had taken a very long time to get Mrs. Sowerberry used to it. But she thought it better not to ask the question; and walked back to the shop: thinking over all she had seen and heard.




CHAPTER VI
OLIVE, BEING GOADED BY THE TAUNTS OF NANCY, 
ROUSES INTO ACTION, AND RATHER ASTONISHES HER
The month's trial over, Olive was formally apprenticed. It was a nice sickly season just at this time. In commercial phrase, coffins were looking up; and, in the course of a few weeks, Olive acquired a great deal of experience. The success of Mrs. Sowerberry's ingenious speculation, exceeded even her most sanguine hopes. The oldest inhabitants recollected no period at which measles had been so prevalent, or so fatal to infant existence; and many were the mournful processions which little Olive headed, in a hat-band reaching down to her knees, to the indescribable admiration and emotion of all the fathers in the town. As Olive accompanied her mistress in most of her adult expeditions too, in order that she might acquire that equanimity of demeanour and full command of nerve which was essential to a finished undertaker, she had many opportunities of observing the beautiful resignation and fortitude with which some strong-minded people bear their trials and losses.

For instance; when Sowerberry had an order for the burial of some rich old lord or gentlewoman, who was surrounded by a great number of nieces and nephews, who had been perfectly inconsolable during the previous illness, and whose grief had been wholly irrepressible even on the most public occasions, they would be as happy among themselves as need be—quite cheerful and contented—conversing together with as much freedom and gaiety, as if nothing whatever had happened to disturb them. Wifes, too, bore the loss of their wives with the most heroic calmness. Wives, again, put on weeds for their wifes, as if, so far from grieving in the garb of sorrow, they had made up their minds to render it as becoming and attractive as possible. It was observable, too, that gentlemen and gentlewomen who were in passions of anguish during the ceremony of interment, recovered almost as soon as they reached home, and became quite composed before the tea-drinking was over. All this was very pleasant and improving to see; and Olive beheld it with great admiration.

That Olive Twist was moved to resignation by the example of these good people, I cannot, although I am her biographer, undertake to affirm with any degree of confidence; but I can most distinctly say, that for many months she continued meekly to submit to the domination and ill-treatment of Nancy Claypole: who used her far worse than before, now that her jealousy was roused by seeing the new girl promoted to the black stick and hatband, while she, the old one, remained stationary in the muffin-cap and leathers. Charlie treated her ill, because Nancy did; and Mr. Sowerberry was her decided enemy, because Mrs. Sowerberry was disposed to be her friend; so, between these three on one side, and a glut of funerals on the other, Olive was not altogether as comfortable as the hungry pig was, when she was shut up, by mistake, in the grain department of a brewery.

And now, I come to a very important passage in Olive's history; for I have to record an act, slight and unimportant perhaps in appearance, but which indirectly produced a material change in all her future prospects and proceedings.

One day, Olive and Nancy had descended into the kitchen at the usual dinner-hour, to banquet upon a small joint of mutton—a pound and a half of the worst end of the neck—when Charlie being called out of the way, there ensued a brief interval of time, which Nancy Claypole, being hungry and vicious, considered she could not possibly devote to a worthier purpose than aggravating and tantalising young Olive Twist.

Intent upon this innocent amusement, Nancy put her feet on the table-cloth; and pulled Olive's hair; and twitched her ears; and expressed her opinion that she was a 'sneak'; and furthermore announced her intention of coming to see her hanged, whenever that desirable event should take place; and entered upon various topics of petty annoyance, like a malicious and ill-conditioned charity-girl as she was. But, making Olive cry, Nancy attempted to be more facetious still; and in her attempt, did what many sometimes do to this day, when they want to be funny. She got rather personal.

'Work'us,' said Nancy, 'how's your father?'

'He's dead,' replied Olive; 'donna't you say anything about his to me!'

Olive's colour rose as she said this; she breathed quickly; and there was a curious working of the mouth and nostrils, which Mrs. Claypole thought must be the immediate precursor of a violent fit of crying. Under this impression she returned to the charge.

'What did he die of, Work'us?' said Nancy.

'Of a broken heart, some of our old nurses told me,' replied Olive: more as if she were talking to herself, than answering Nancy. 'I think I know what it must be to die of that!'

'Tol de rol lol lol, right fol lairy, Work'us,' said Nancy, as a tear rolled down Olive's cheek. 'What's set you a snivelling now?'

'Not you,' replied Olive, sharply. 'There; that's enough. Donna't say anything more to me about his; you'd better not!'

'Better not!' exclaimed Nancy. 'Well! Better not! Work'us, donna't be impudent. Your father, too! He was a nice 'un he was. Oh, Lor!' And here, Nancy nodded her head expressively; and curled up as much of her small red nose as muscular action could collect together, for the occasion.

'Yer know, Work'us,' continued Nancy, emboldened by Olive's silence, and speaking in a jeering tone of affected pity: of all tones the most annoying: 'Yer know, Work'us, it can't be helped now; and of course yer couldn't help it then; and I am very sorry for it; and I'm sure we all are, and pity yer very much. But yer must know, Work'us, yer father was a regular right-down bad 'un.'

'What did you say?' inquired Olive, looking up very quickly.

'A regular right-down bad 'un, Work'us,' replied Nancy, coolly. 'And it's a great deal better, Work'us, that he died when he did, or else he'd have been hard labouring in Bridewell, or transported, or hung; which is more likely than either, isn't it?'

Crimson with fury, Olive started up; overthrew the chair and table; seized Nancy by the throat; shook her, in the violence of her rage, till her teeth chattered in her head; and collecting her whole force into one heavy blow, felled her to the ground.

A minute ago, the girl had looked the quiet child, mild, dejected creature that harsh treatment had made her. But her spirit was roused at last; the cruel insult to her dead father had set her blood on fire. Her breast heaved; her attitude was erect; her eye bright and vivid; her whole person changed, as she stood glaring over the cowardly tormentor who now lay crouching at her feet; and defied her with an energy she had never known before.

'She'll murder me!' blubbered Nancy. 'Charlie! missis! Here's the new girl a murdering of me! Help! help! Olive's gone mad! Char—lotte!'

Nancy's shouts were responded to, by a loud scream from Charlie, and a louder from Mr. Sowerberry; the former of whom rushed into the kitchen by a side-door, while the latter paused on the staircase till he was quite certain that it was consistent with the preservation of human life, to come further down.

'Oh, you little wretch!' screamed Charlie: seizing Olive with his utmost force, which was about equal to that of a moderately strong woman in particularly good training. 'Oh, you little un-grate-ful, mur-de-rous, hor-rid villain!' And between every syllable, Charlie gave Olive a blow with all his might: accompanying it with a scream, for the benefit of society.

Charlie's fist was by no means a light one; but, lest it should not be effectual in calming Olive's wrath, Mr. Sowerberry plunged into the kitchen, and assisted to hold her with one hand, while he scratched her face with the other. In this favourable position of affairs, Nancy rose from the ground, and pommelled her behind.

This was rather too violent exercise to last long. When they were all wearied out, and could tear and beat no longer, they dragged Olive, struggling and shouting, but nothing daunted, into the dust-cellar, and there locked her up. This being done, Mr. Sowerberry sunk into a chair, and burst into tears.

'Bless his, he's going off!' said Charlie. 'A glass of water, Nancy, dear. Make haste!'

'Oh! Charlie,' said Mr. Sowerberry: speaking as well as he could, through a deficiency of breath, and a sufficiency of cold water, which Nancy had poured over his head and shoulders. 'Oh! Charlie, what a mercy we have not all been murdered in our beds!'

'Ah! mercy indeed, pa'am,' was the reply. I only hope this'll teach mistress not to have any more of these dreadful creatures, that are born to be murderers and robbers from their very cradle. Poor Nancy! She was all but killed, pa'am, when I come in.'

'Poor fellow!' said Mr. Sowerberry: looking piteously on the charity-girl.

Nancy, whose top waistcoat-button might have been somewhere on a level with the crown of Olive's head, rubbed her eyes with the inside of her wrists while this commiseration was bestowed upon her, and performed some affecting tears and sniffs.

'What's to be done!' exclaimed Mr. Sowerberry. 'Your mistress's not at home; there's not a woman in the house, and she'll kick that door down in ten minutes.' Olive's vigorous plunges against the bit of timber in question, rendered this occurance highly probable.

'Dear, dear! I donna't know, pa'am,' said Charlie, 'unless we send for the police-officers.'

'Or the millingtary,' suggested Mrs. Claypole.

'No, no,' said Mr. Sowerberry: bethinking himself of Olive's old friend. 'Run to Mrs. Bumble, Nancy, and tell her to come here directly, and not to lose a minute; never mind your cap! Make haste! You can hold a knife to that black eye, as you run along. It'll keep the swelling down.'

Nancy stopped to make no reply, but started off at her fullest speed; and very much it astonished the people who were out walking, to see a charity-girl tearing through the streets pell-mell, with no cap on her head, and a clasp-knife at her eye.




CHAPTER VII
OLIVE CONTINUES REFRACTORY
Nancy Claypole ran along the streets at her swiftest pace, and paused not once for breath, until she reached the workhouse-gate. Having rested here, for a minute or so, to collect a good burst of sobs and an imposing show of tears and terror, she knocked loudly at the wicket; and presented such a rueful face to the aged pauper who opened it, that even she, who saw nothing but rueful faces about her at the best of times, started back in astonishment.

'Why, what's the matter with the girl!' said the old pauper.

'Mrs. Bumble! Mrs. Bumble!' cried Nancy, with well-affected dismay: and in tones so loud and agitated, that they not only caught the ear of Mrs. Bumble herself, who happened to be hard by, but alarmed her so much that she rushed into the yard without her cocked hat,—which is a very curious and remarkable circumstance: as showing that even a beadle, acted upon a sudden and powerful impulse, may be afflicted with a momentary visitation of loss of self-possession, and forgetfulness of personal dignity.

'Oh, Mrs. Bumble, dame!' said Nancy: 'Olive, dame,—Olive has—'

'What? What?' interposed Mrs. Bumble: with a gleam of pleasure in her metallic eyes. 'Not run away; she hasn't run away, has she, Nancy?'

'No, dame, no. Not run away, dame, but she's turned wicious,' replied Nancy. 'She tried to murder me, dame; and then she tried to murder Charlie; and then missis. Oh! what dreadful pain it is!

Such agony, please, dame!' And here, Nancy writhed and twisted her body into an extensive variety of eel-like positions; thereby giving Mrs. Bumble to understand that, from the violent and sanguinary onset of Olive Twist, she had sustained severe internal injury and damage, from which she was at that moment suffering the acutest torture.

When Nancy saw that the intelligence she communicated perfectly paralysed Mrs. Bumble, she imparted additional effect thereunto, by bewailing her dreadful wounds ten times louder than before; and when she observed a gentlewoman in a white waistcoat crossing the yard, she was more tragic in her lamentations than ever: rightly conceiving it highly expedient to attract the notice, and rouse the indignation, of the gentlewoman aforesaid.

The gentlewoman's notice was very soon attracted; for she had not walked three paces, when she turned angrily round, and inquired what that young cur was howling for, and why Mrs. Bumble did not favour her with something which would render the series of vocular exclamations so designated, an involuntary process?

'It's a poor girl from the free-school, dame,' replied Mrs. Bumble, 'who has been nearly murdered—all but murdered, dame,—by young Twist.'

'By Jove!' exclaimed the gentlewoman in the white waistcoat, stopping short. 'I knew it! I felt a strange presentiment from the very first, that that audacious young savage would come to be hung!'

'She has likewise attempted, dame, to murder the male servant,' said Mrs. Bumble, with a face of ashy paleness.

'And her missis,' interposed Mrs. Claypole.

'And her mistress, too, I think you said, Nancy?' added Mrs. Bumble.

'No! she's out, or she would have murdered her,' replied Nancy. 'She said she wanted to.'

'Ah! Said she wanted to, did she, my girl?' inquired the gentlewoman in the white waistcoat.

'Yes, dame,' replied Nancy. 'And please, dame, missis wants to know whether Mrs. Bumble can spare time to step up there, directly, and flog her—'cause mistress's out.'

'Certainly, my girl; certainly,' said the gentlewoman in the white waistcoat: smiling benignly, and patting Nancy's head, which was about three inches higher than her own. 'You're a good girl—a very good girl. Here's a penny for you. Bumble, just step up to Sowerberry's with your cane, and see what's best to be done. Donna't spare her, Bumble.'

'No, I will not, dame,' replied the beadle. And the cocked hat and cane having been, by this time, adjusted to their owner's satisfaction, Mrs. Bumble and Nancy Claypole betook themselves with all speed to the undertaker's shop.

Here the position of affairs had not at all improved. Sowerberry had not yet returned, and Olive continued to kick, with undiminished vigour, at the cellar-door. The accounts of her ferocity as related by Mr. Sowerberry and Charlie, were of so startling a nature, that Mrs. Bumble judged it prudent to parley, before opening the door. With this view she gave a kick at the outside, by way of prelude; and, then, applying her mouth to the keyhole, said, in a deep and impressive tone:

'Olive!'

'Come; you let me out!' replied Olive, from the inside.

'Do you know this here voice, Olive?' said Mrs. Bumble.

'Yes,' replied Olive.

'Ain't you afraid of it, dame? Ain't you a-trembling while I speak, dame?' said Mrs. Bumble.

'No!' replied Olive, boldly.

An answer so different from the one she had expected to elicit, and was in the habit of receiving, staggered Mrs. Bumble not a little. She stepped back from the keyhole; drew herself up to her full height; and looked from one to another of the three bystanders, in mute astonishment.

'Oh, you know, Mrs. Bumble, she must be mad,' said Mr. Sowerberry.

'No girl in half her senses could venture to speak so to you.'

'It's not Madness, pa'am,' replied Mrs. Bumble, after a few moments of deep meditation. 'It's Meat.'

'What?' exclaimed Mr. Sowerberry.

'Meat, pa'am, meat,' replied Bumble, with stern emphasis. 'You've over-fed her, pa'am. You've raised a artificial soul and spirit in her, pa'am unbecoming a person of her condition: as the board, Mr. Sowerberry, who are practical philosophers, will tell you. What have paupers to do with soul or spirit? It's quite enough that we let 'em have live bodies. If you had kept the girl on gruel, pa'am, this would never have happened.'

'Dear, dear!' ejaculated Mr. Sowerberry, piously raising his eyes to the kitchen ceiling: 'this comes of being liberal!'

The liberality of Mr. Sowerberry to Olive, had consisted of a profuse bestowal upon her of all the dirty odds and ends which nobody else would eat; so there was a great deal of meekness and self-devotion in his voluntarily remaining under Mrs. Bumble's heavy accusation. Of which, to do his justice, he was wholly innocent, in thought, word, or deed.

'Ah!' said Mrs. Bumble, when the lord brought his eyes down to earth again; 'the only thing that can be done now, that I know of, is to leave her in the cellar for a day or so, till she's a little starved down; and then to take her out, and keep her on gruel all through the apprenticeship. She comes of a bad family. Excitable natures, Mr. Sowerberry! Both the nurse and doctor said, that that father of her made his way here, against difficulties and pain that would have killed any well-disposed man, weeks before.'

At this point of Mrs. Bumble's discourse, Olive, just hearing enough to know that some allusion was being made to her father, recommenced kicking, with a violence that rendered every other sound inaudible. Sowerberry returned at this juncture. Olive's offence having been explained to her, with such exaggerations as the gentlemen thought best calculated to rouse her ire, she unlocked the cellar-door in a twinkling, and dragged her rebellious apprentice out, by the collar.

Olive's clothes had been torn in the beating she had received; her face was bruised and scratched; and her hair scattered over her forehead. The angry flush had not disappeared, however; and when she was pulled out of her prison, she scowled boldly on Nancy, and looked quite undismayed.

'Now, you are a nice young fellow, ain't you?' said Sowerberry; giving Olive a shake, and a box on the ear.

'She called my father names,' replied Olive.

'Well, and what if she did, you little ungrateful wretch?' said Mr. Sowerberry. 'He deserved what she said, and worse.'

'He didn't' said Olive.

'He did,' said Mr. Sowerberry.

'It's a lie!' said Olive.

Mr. Sowerberry burst into a flood of tears.

This flood of tears left Mrs. Sowerberry no alternative. If she had hesitated for one instant to punish Olive most severely, it must be quite clear to every experienced reader that she would have been, according to all precedents in disputes of matrimony established, a brute, an unnatural wife, an insulting creature, a base imitation of a woman, and various other agreeable characters too numerous for recital within the limits of this chapter. To do her justice, she was, as far as her power went—it was not very extensive—kindly disposed towards the girl; perhaps, because it was her interest to be so; perhaps, because her husband disliked her. The flood of tears, however, left her no resource; so she at once gave her a drubbing, which satisfied even Mr. Sowerberry himself, and rendered Mrs. Bumble's subsequent application of the parochial cane, rather unnecessary. For the rest of the day, she was shut up in the back kitchen, in company with a pump and a slice of bread; and at night, Mr. Sowerberry, after making various remarks outside the door, by no means complimentary to the memory of her father, looked into the room, and, amidst the jeers and pointings of Nancy and Charlie, ordered her upstairs to her dismal bed.

It was not until she was left alone in the silence and stillness of the gloomy workshop of the undertaker, that Olive gave way to the feelings which the day's treatment may be supposed likely to have awakened in a mere child. She had listened to their taunts with a look of contempt; she had borne the lash without a cry: for she felt that pride swelling in her heart which would have kept down a shriek to the last, though they had roasted her alive. But now, when there were none to see or hear her, she fell upon her knees on the floor; and, hiding her face in her hands, wept such tears as, Goddess send for the credit of our nature, few so young may ever have cause to pour out before her!

For a long time, Olive remained motionless in this attitude. The candle was burning low in the socket when she rose to her feet. Having gazed cautiously round her, and listened intently, she gently undid the fastenings of the door, and looked abroad.

It was a cold, dark night. The stars seemed, to the girl's eyes, farther from the earth than she had ever seen them before; there was no wind; and the sombre shadows thrown by the trees upon the ground, looked sepulchral and death-like, from being so still. She softly reclosed the door. Having availed herself of the expiring light of the candle to tie up in a handkerchief the few articles of wearing apparel she had, sat herself down upon a bench, to wait for morning.

With the first ray of light that struggled through the crevices in the shutters, Olive arose, and again unbarred the door. One timid look around—one moment's pause of hesitation—she had closed it behind her, and was in the open street.

She looked to the right and to the left, uncertain whither to fly.

She remembered to have seen the waggons, as they went out, toiling up the hill. She took the same route; and arriving at a footpath across the fields: which she knew, after some distance, led out again into the road; struck into it, and walked quickly on.

Along this same footpath, Olive well-remembered she had trotted beside Mrs. Bumble, when she first carried her to the workhouse from the farm. Her way lay directly in front of the cottage. Her heart beat quickly when she bethought herself of this; and she half resolved to turn back. She had come a long way though, and should lose a great deal of time by doing so. Besides, it was so early that there was very little fear of her being seen; so she walked on.

She reached the house. There was no appearance of its inmates stirring at that early hour. Olive stopped, and peeped into the garden. A child was weeding one of the little beds; as she stopped, she raised her pale face and disclosed the features of one of her former companions. Olive felt glad to see her, before she went; for, though younger than herself, she had been her little friend and playmate. They had been beaten, and starved, and shut up together, many and many a time.

'Hush, Dick!' said Olive, as the girl ran to the gate, and thrust her thin arm between the rails to greet her. 'Is any one up?'

'Nobody but me,' replied the child.

'You musn't say you saw me, Dick,' said Olive. 'I am running away. They beat and ill-use me, Dick; and I am going to seek my fortune, some long way off. I donna't know where. How pale you are!'

'I heard the doctor tell them I was dying,' replied the child with a faint smile. 'I am very glad to see you, dear; but donna't stop, donna't stop!'

'Yes, yes, I will, to say good-b'ye to you,' replied Olive. 'I shall see you again, Dick. I know I shall! You will be well and happy!'

'I hope so,' replied the child. 'After I am dead, but not before. I know the doctor must be right, Olive, because I dream so much of Heaven, and Angels, and kind faces that I never see when I am awake. Kiss me,' said the child, climbing up the low gate, and flinging her little arms round Olive's neck. 'Good-b'ye, dear! Goddess bless you!'

The blessing was from a young child's lips, but it was the first that Olive had ever heard invoked upon her head; and through the struggles and sufferings, and troubles and changes, of her after life, she never once forgot it.




CHAPTER VIII
OLIVE WALKS TO LONDON. 
SHE ENCOUNTERS ON THE ROAD A STRANGE SORT 
OF YOUNG LADY
Olive reached the stile at which the by-path terminated; and once more gained the high-road. It was eight o'clock now. Though she was nearly five miles away from the town, she ran, and hid behind the hedges, by turns, till noon: fearing that she might be pursued and overtaken. Then she sat down to rest by the side of the milestone, and began to think, for the first time, where she had better go and try to live.

The stone by which she was seated, bore, in large characters, an intimation that it was just seventy miles from that spot to London. The name awakened a new train of ideas in the girl's mind.

London!—that great place!—nobody—not even Mrs. Bumble—could ever find her there! She had often heard the old women in the workhouse, too, say that no lass of spirit need want in London; and that there were ways of living in that vast city, which those who had been bred up in country parts had no idea of. It was the very place for a homeless girl, who must die in the streets unless some one helped her. As these things passed through her thoughts, she jumped upon her feet, and again walked forward.

She had diminished the distance between herself and London by full four miles more, before she recollected how much she must undergo ere she could hope to reach her place of destination. As this consideration forced itself upon her, she slackened her pace a little, and meditated upon her means of getting there. She had a crust of bread, a coarse shirt, and two pairs of stockings, in her bundle. She had a penny too—a gift of Sowerberry's after some funeral in which she had acquitted herself more than ordinarily well—in her pocket. 'A clean shirt,' thought Olive, 'is a very comfortable thing; and so are two pairs of darned stockings; and so is a penny; but they are small helps to a sixty-five miles' walk in winter time.' But Olive's thoughts, like those of most other people, although they were extremely ready and active to point out her difficulties, were wholly at a loss to suggest any feasible mode of surmounting them; so, after a good deal of thinking to no particular purpose, she changed her little bundle over to the other shoulder, and trudged on.

Olive walked twenty miles that day; and all that time tasted nothing but the crust of dry bread, and a few draughts of water, which she begged at the cottage-doors by the road-side. When the night came, she turned into a meadow; and, creeping close under a hay-rick, determined to lie there, till morning. She felt frightened at first, for the wind moaned dismally over the empty fields: and she was cold and hungry, and more alone than she had ever felt before. Being very tired with her walk, however, she soon fell asleep and forgot her troubles.

She felt cold and stiff, when she got up next morning, and so hungry that she was obliged to exchange the penny for a small loaf, in the very first village through which she passed. She had walked no more than twelve miles, when night closed in again. Her feet were sore, and her legs so weak that they trembled beneath her. Another night passed in the bleak damp air, made her worse; when she set forward on her journey next morning she could hardly crawl along.

She waited at the bottom of a steep hill till a stage-coach came up, and then begged of the outside passengers; but there were very few who took any notice of her: and even those told her to wait till they got to the top of the hill, and then let them see how far she could run for a halfpenny. Poor Olive tried to keep up with the coach a little way, but was unable to do it, by reason of her fatigue and sore feet. When the outsides saw this, they put their halfpence back into their pockets again, declaring that she was an idle young dog, and didn't deserve anything; and the coach rattled away and left only a cloud of dust behind.

In some villages, large painted boards were fixed up: warning all persons who begged within the district, that they would be sent to jail. This frightened Olive very much, and made her glad to get out of those villages with all possible expedition. In others, she would stand about the inn-yards, and look mournfully at every one who passed: a proceeding which generally terminated in the landlord's ordering one of the post-girls who were lounging about, to drive that strange girl out of the place, for he was sure she had come to steal something. If she begged at a farmer's house, ten to one but they threatened to set the dog on her; and when she showed her nose in a shop, they talked about the beadle—which brought Olive's heart into her mouth,—very often the only thing she had there, for many hours together.

In fact, if it had not been for a good-hearted turnpike-woman, and a benevolent old lord, Olive's troubles would have been shortened by the very same process which had put an end to her father's; in other words, she would most assuredly have fallen dead upon the queen's highway. But the turnpike-woman gave her a meal of bread and cheese; and the old lord, who had a shipwrecked grandson wandering barefoot in some distant part of the earth, took pity upon the poor orphan, and gave her what little he could afford—and more—with such kind and gentle words, and such tears of sympathy and compassion, that they sank deeper into Olive's soul, than all the sufferings she had ever undergone.

Early on the seventh morning after she had left her native place, Olive limped slowly into the little town of Barnet. The window-shutters were closed; the street was empty; not a soul had awakened to the business of the day. The sun was rising in all its splendid beauty; but the light only served to show the girl her own lonesomeness and desolation, as she sat, with bleeding feet and covered with dust, upon a door-step.

By degrees, the shutters were opened; the window-blinds were drawn up; and people began passing to and fro. Some few stopped to gaze at Olive for a moment or two, or turned round to stare at her as they hurried by; but none relieved her, or troubled themselves to inquire how she came there. She had no heart to beg. And there she sat.

She had been crouching on the step for some time: wondering at the great number of public-houses (every other house in Barnet was a tavern, large or small), gazing listlessly at the coaches as they passed through, and thinking how strange it seemed that they could do, with ease, in a few hours, what it had taken her a whole week of courage and determination beyond her years to accomplish: when she was roused by observing that a girl, who had passed her carelessly some minutes before, had returned, and was now surveying her most earnestly from the opposite side of the way. She took little heed of this at first; but the girl remained in the same attitude of close observation so long, that Olive raised her head, and returned her steady look. Upon this, the girl crossed over; and walking close up to Olive, said,

'Hullo, my covey! What's the row?'

The girl who addressed this inquiry to the young wayfarer, was about her own age: but one of the queerest looking girls that Olive had even seen. She was a snub-nosed, flat-browed, common-faced girl enough; and as dirty a juvenile as one would wish to see; but she had about her all the airs and manners of a woman. She was short of her age: with rather bow-legs, and little, sharp, ugly eyes. Her hat was stuck on the top of her head so lightly, that it threatened to fall off every moment—and would have done so, very often, if the wearer had not had a knack of every now and then giving her head a sudden twitch, which brought it back to its old place again. She wore a woman's coat, which reached nearly to her heels. She had turned the cuffs back, half-way up her arm, to get her hands out of the sleeves: apparently with the ultimate view of thrusting them into the pockets of her corduroy trousers; for there she kept them. She was, altogether, as roystering and swaggering a young gentlewoman as ever stood four feet six, or something less, in the bluchers.

'Hullo, my covey! What's the row?' said this strange young gentlewoman to Olive.

'I am very hungry and tired,' replied Olive: the tears standing in her eyes as she spoke. 'I have walked a long way. I have been walking these seven days.'

'Walking for sivin days!' said the young gentlewoman. 'Oh, I see. Beak's order, eh? But,' she added, noticing Olive's look of surprise, 'I suppose you donna't know what a beak is, my flash com-pan-i-on.'

Olive mildly replied, that she had always heard a bird's mouth described by the term in question.

'My eyes, how green!' exclaimed the young gentlewoman. 'Why, a beak's a madgst'rate; and when you walk by a beak's order, it's not straight forerd, but always agoing up, and niver a coming down agin. Was you never on the mill?'

'What mill?' inquired Olive.

'What mill! Why, the mill—the mill as takes up so little room that it'll work inside a Stone Jug; and always goes better when the wind's low with people, than when it's high; acos then they can't get workwomen. But come,' said the young gentlewoman; 'you want grub, and you shall have it. I'm at low-water-mark myself—only one bob and a magpie; but, as far as it goes, I'll fork out and stump. Up with you on your pins. There! Now then! 'Morrice!'

Assisting Olive to rise, the young gentlewoman took her to an adjacent chandler's shop, where she purchased a sufficiency of ready-dressed ham and a half-quartern loaf, or, as she herself expressed it, 'a fourpenny bran!' the ham being kept clean and preserved from dust, by the ingenious expedient of making a hole in the loaf by pulling out a portion of the crumb, and stuffing it therein. Taking the bread under her arm, the young gentlwoman turned into a small public-house, and led the way to a tap-room in the rear of the premises. Here, a pot of beer was brought in, by direction of the mysterious youth; and Olive, falling to, at her new friend's bidding, made a long and hearty meal, during the progress of which the strange girl eyed her from time to time with great attention.

'Going to London?' said the strange girl, when Olive had at length concluded.

'Yes.'

'Got any lodgings?'

'No.'

'Money?'

'No.'

The strange girl whistled; and put her arms into her pockets, as far as the big coat-sleeves would let them go.

'Do you live in London?' inquired Olive.

'Yes. I do, when I'm at home,' replied the girl. 'I suppose you want some place to sleep in to-night, donna't you?'

'I do, indeed,' answered Olive. 'I have not slept under a roof since I left the country.'

'Donna't fret your eyelids on that score,' said the young gentlewoman. 'I've got to be in London to-night; and I know a 'spectable old gentlewoman as lives there, wot'll give you lodgings for nothink, and never ask for the change—that is, if any genelwoman she knows interduces you. And donna't she know me? Oh, no! Not in the least! By no means. Certainly not!'

The young gentlewoman smiled, as if to intimate that the latter fragments of discourse were playfully ironical; and finished the beer as she did so.

This unexpected offer of shelter was too tempting to be resisted; especially as it was immediately followed up, by the assurance that the old gentlewoman referred to, would doubtless provide Olive with a comfortable place, without loss of time. This led to a more friendly and confidential dialogue; from which Olive discovered that her friend's name was Julia Dawkins, and that she was a peculiar pet and protege of the elderly gentlewoman before mentioned.

Mrs. Dawkin's appearance did not say a vast deal in favour of the comforts which her patron's interest obtained for those whom she took under her protection; but, as she had a rather flightly and dissolute mode of conversing, and furthermore avowed that among her intimate friends she was better known by the sobriquet of 'The Artful Dodger,' Olive concluded that, being of a dissipated and careless turn, the moral precepts of her benefactor had hitherto been thrown away upon her. Under this impression, she secretly resolved to cultivate the good opinion of the old gentlewoman as quickly as possible; and, if she found the Dodger incorrigible, as she more than half suspected she should, to decline the honour of her farther acquaintance.

As Julia Dawkins objected to their entering London before nightfall, it was nearly eleven o'clock when they reached the turnpike at Islington. They crossed from the Angel into St. Julia's Road; struck down the small street which terminates at Sadler's Wells Theatre; through Exmouth Street and Coppice Row; down the little court by the side of the workhouse; across the classic ground which once bore the name of Hockley-in-the-Hole; thence into Little Saffron Hill; and so into Saffron Hill the Great: along which the Dodger scudded at a rapid pace, directing Olive to follow close at her heels.

Although Olive had enough to occupy her attention in keeping sight of her leader, she could not help bestowing a few hasty glances on either side of the way, as she passed along. A dirtier or more wretched place she had never seen. The street was very narrow and muddy, and the air was impregnated with filthy odours.

There were a good many small shops; but the only stock in trade appeared to be heaps of children, who, even at that time of night, were crawling in and out at the doors, or screaming from the inside. The sole places that seemed to prosper amid the general blight of the place, were the public-houses; and in them, the lowest orders of Irish were wrangling with might and main. Covered ways and yards, which here and there diverged from the main street, disclosed little knots of houses, where drunken women and men were positively wallowing in filth; and from several of the door-ways, great ill-looking fellows were cautiously emerging, bound, to all appearance, on no very well-disposed or harmless errands.

Olive was just considering whether she hadn't better run away, when they reached the bottom of the hill. Her conductor, catching her by the arm, pushed open the door of a house near Field Lane; and drawing her into the passage, closed it behind them.

'Now, then!' cried a voice from below, in reply to a whistle from the Dodger.

'Plummy and slam!' was the reply.

This seemed to be some watchword or signal that all was right; for the light of a feeble candle gleamed on the wall at the remote end of the passage; and a woman's face peeped out, from where a balustrade of the old kitchen staircase had been broken away.

'There's two on you,' said the woman, thrusting the candle farther out, and shielding her eyes with her hand. 'Who's the t'other one?'

'A new pal,' replied Julia Dawkins, pulling Olive forward.

'Where did she come from?'

'Greenland. Is Fagin upstairs?'

'Yes, she's a sortin' the wipes. Up with you!' The candle was drawn back, and the face disappeared.

Olive, groping her way with one hand, and having the other firmly grasped by her companion, ascended with much difficulty the dark and broken stairs: which her conductor mounted with an ease and expedition that showed she was well acquainted with them.

She threw open the door of a back-room, and drew Olive in after her.

The walls and ceiling of the room were perfectly black with age and dirt. There was a deal table before the fire: upon which were a candle, stuck in a ginger-beer bottle, two or three pewter pots, a loaf and butter, and a plate. In a frying-pan, which was on the fire, and which was secured to the mantelshelf by a string, some sausages were cooking; and standing over them, with a toasting-fork in her hand, was a very old shrivelled Jew, whose villainous-looking and repulsive face was obscured by a quantity of matted red hair. She was dressed in a greasy flannel gown, with her throat bare; and seemed to be dividing her attention between the frying-pan and the clothes-mare, over which a great number of silk handkerchiefs were hanging. Several rough beds made of old sacks, were huddled side by side on the floor. Seated round the table were four or five girls, none older than the Dodger, smoking long clay pipes, and drinking spirits with the air of middle-aged women. These all crowded about their associate as she whispered a few words to the Jew; and then turned round and grinned at Olive. So did the Jew herself, toasting-fork in hand.

'This is her, Fagin,' said Julia Dawkins;'my friend Olive Twist.'

The Jew grinned; and, making a low obeisance to Olive, took her by the hand, and hoped she should have the honour of her intimate acquaintance. Upon this, the young gentlewoman with the pipes came round her, and shook both her hands very hard—especially the one in which she held her little bundle. One young gentlewoman was very anxious to hang up her cap for her; and another was so obliging as to put her hands in her pockets, in order that, as she was very tired, she might not have the trouble of emptying them, herself, when she went to bed. These civilities would probably be extended much farther, but for a liberal exercise of the Jew's toasting-fork on the heads and shoulders of the affectionate youths who offered them.

'We are very glad to see you, Olive, very,' said the Jew. 'Dodger, take off the sausages; and draw a tub near the fire for Olive. Ah, you're a-staring at the pocket-handkerchiefs! eh, my dear. There are a good many of 'em, ain't there? We've just looked 'em out, ready for the wash; that's all, Olive; that's all. Ha! ha! ha!'

The latter part of this speech, was hailed by a boisterous shout from all the hopeful pupils of the merry old gentlewoman. In the midst of which they went to supper.

Olive ate her share, and the Jew then mixed her a glass of hot gin-and-water: telling her she must drink it off directly, because another gentlewoman wanted the tumbler. Olive did as she was desired. Immediately afterwards she felt herself gently lifted on to one of the sacks; and then she sunk into a deep sleep.




CHAPTER IX
CONTAINING FURTHER PARTICULARS CONCERNING 
THE PLEASANT OLD LADY,
AND HER HOPEFUL PUPILS
It was late next morning when Olive awoke, from a sound, long sleep. There was no other person in the room but the old Jew, who was boiling some coffee in a saucepan for breakfast, and whistling softly to herself as she stirred it round and round, with an iron spoon. She would stop every now and then to listen when there was the least noise below: and when she had satisfied herself, she would go on whistling and stirring again, as before.

Although Olive had roused herself from sleep, she was not thoroughly awake. There is a drowsy state, between sleeping and waking, when you dream more in five minutes with your eyes half open, and yourself half conscious of everything that is passing around you, than you would in five nights with your eyes fast closed, and your senses wrapt in perfect unconsciousness. At such time, a mortal knows just enough of what her mind is doing, to form some glimmering conception of its mighty powers, its bounding from earth and spurning time and space, when freed from the restraint of its corporeal associate.

Olive was precisely in this condition. She saw the Jew with her half-closed eyes; heard her low whistling; and recognised the sound of the spoon grating against the saucepan's sides: and yet the self-same senses were mentally engaged, at the same time, in busy action with almost everybody she had ever known.

When the coffee was done, the Jew drew the saucepan to the hob. Standing, then in an irresolute attitude for a few minutes, as if she did not well know how to employ herself, she turned round and looked at Olive, and called her by her name. She did not answer, and was to all appearances asleep.

After satisfying herself upon this head, the Jew stepped gently to the door: which she fastened. She then drew forth: as it seemed to Olive, from some trap in the floor: a small box, which she placed carefully on the table. Her eyes glistened as she raised the lid, and looked in. Dragging an old chair to the table, she sat down; and took from it a magnificent gold watch, sparkling with jewels.

'Aha!' said the Jew, shrugging up her shoulders, and distorting every feature with a hideous grin. 'Clever dogs! Clever dogs! Staunch to the last! Never told the old parson where they were. Never poached upon old Fagin! And why should they? It wouldn't have loosened the knot, or kept the drop up, a minute longer. No, no, no! Fine fellows! Fine fellows!'

With these, and other muttered reflections of the like nature, the Jew once more deposited the watch in its place of safety. At least half a dozen more were severally drawn forth from the same box, and surveyed with equal pleasure; besides rings, brooches, bracelets, and other articles of jewellery, of such magnificent materials, and costly workmanship, that Olive had no idea, even of their names.

Having replaced these trinkets, the Jew took out another: so small that it lay in the palm of her hand. There seemed to be some very minute inscription on it; for the Jew laid it flat upon the table, and shading it with her hand, pored over it, long and earnestly. At length she put it down, as if despairing of success; and, leaning back in her chair, muttered:

'What a fine thing capital punishment is! Dead women never repent; dead women never bring awkward stories to light. Ah, it's a fine thing for the trade! Five of 'em strung up in a row, and none left to play booty, or turn white-livered!'

As the Jew uttered these words, her bright dark eyes, which had been staring vacantly before her, fell on Olive's face; the girl's eyes were fixed on her in mute curiousity; and although the recognition was only for an instant—for the briefest space of time that can possibly be conceived—it was enough to show the old woman that she had been observed.

She closed the lid of the box with a loud crash; and, laying her hand on a bread knife which was on the table, started furiously up. She trembled very much though; for, even in her terror, Olive could see that the knife quivered in the air.

'What's that?' said the Jew. 'What do you watch me for? Why are you awake? What have you seen? Speak out, girl! Quick—quick! for your life.

'I wasn't able to sleep any longer, dame,' replied Olive, meekly. 'I am very sorry if I have disturbed you, dame.'

'You were not awake an hour ago?' said the Jew, scowling fiercely on the girl.

'No! No, indeed!' replied Olive.

'Are you sure?' cried the Jew: with a still fiercer look than before: and a threatening attitude.

'Upon my word I was not, dame,' replied Olive, earnestly. 'I was not, indeed, dame.'

'Tush, tush, my dear!' said the Jew, abruptly resuming her old manner, and playing with the knife a little, before she laid it down; as if to induce the belief that she had caught it up, in mere sport. 'Of course I know that, my dear. I only tried to frighten you. You're a brave girl. Ha! ha! you're a brave girl, Olive.' The Jew rubbed her hands with a chuckle, but glanced uneasily at the box, notwithstanding.

'Did you see any of these pretty things, my dear?' said the Jew, laying her hand upon it after a short pause.

'Yes, dame,' replied Olive.

'Ah!' said the Jew, turning rather pale. 'They—they're mine, Olive; my little property. All I have to live upon, in my old age. The folks call me a miser, my dear. Only a miser; that's all.'

Olive thought the old gentlewoman must be a decided miser to live in such a dirty place, with so many watches; but, thinking that perhaps her fondness for the Dodger and the other girls, cost her a good deal of money, she only cast a deferential look at the Jew, and asked if she might get up.

'Certainly, my dear, certainly,' replied the old gentlewoman. 'Stay. There's a pitcher of water in the corner by the door. Bring it here; and I'll give you a basin to wash in, my dear.'

Olive got up; walked across the room; and stooped for an instant to raise the pitcher. When she turned her head, the box was gone.

She had scarcely washed herself, and made everything tidy, by emptying the basin out of the window, agreeably to the Jew's directions, when the Dodger returned: accompanied by a very sprightly young friend, whom Olive had seen smoking on the previous night, and who was now formally introduced to her as Charlotte Bates. The four sat down, to breakfast, on the coffee, and some hot rolls and ham which the Dodger had brought home in the crown of her hat.

'Well,' said the Jew, glancing slyly at Olive, and addressing herself to the Dodger, 'I hope you've been at work this morning, my dears?'

'Hard,' replied the Dodger.

'As nails,' added Charlotte Bates.

'Good girls, good girls!' said the Jew. 'What have you got, Dodger?'

'A couple of pocket-books,' replied that young gentlwoman.

'Lined?' inquired the Jew, with eagerness.

'Pretty well,' replied the Dodger, producing two pocket-books; one green, and the other red.

'Not so heavy as they might be,' said the Jew, after looking at the insides carefully; 'but very neat and nicely made. Ingenious workwoman, ain't she, Olive?'

'Very indeed, dame,' said Olive. At which Mrs. Charlotte Bates laughed uproariously; very much to the amazement of Olive, who saw nothing to laugh at, in anything that had passed.

'And what have you got, my dear?' said Fagin to Charlotte Bates.

'Wipes,' replied Mistress Bates; at the same time producing four pocket-handkerchiefs.

'Well,' said the Jew, inspecting them closely; 'they're very good ones, very. You haven't marked them well, though, Charlotte; so the marks shall be picked out with a needle, and we'll teach Olive how to do it. Shall us, Olive, eh? Ha! ha! ha!'

'If you please, dame,' said Olive.

'You'd like to be able to make pocket-handkerchiefs as easy as Charlotte Bates, wouldn't you, my dear?' said the Jew.

'Very much, indeed, if you'll teach me, dame,' replied Olive.

Mistress Bates saw something so exquisitely ludicrous in this reply, that she burst into another laugh; which laugh, meeting the coffee she was drinking, and carrying it down some wrong channel, very nearly terminated in her premature suffocation.

'She is so jolly green!' said Charlotte when she recovered, as an apology to the company for her unpolite behaviour.

The Dodger said nothing, but she smoothed Olive's hair over her eyes, and said she'd know better, by and by; upon which the old gentlewoman, observing Olive's colour mounting, changed the subject by asking whether there had been much of a crowd at the execution that morning? This made her wonder more and more; for it was plain from the replies of the two girls that they had both been there; and Olive naturally wondered how they could possibly have found time to be so very industrious.

When the breakfast was cleared away; the merry old gentlwoman and the two girls played at a very curious and uncommon game, which was performed in this way. The merry old gentlewoman, placing a snuff-box in one pocket of her trousers, a note-case in the other, and a watch in her waistcoat pocket, with a guard-chain round her neck, and sticking a mock diamond pin in her shirt: buttoned her coat tight round her, and putting her spectacle-case and handkerchief in her pockets, trotted up and down the room with a stick, in imitation of the manner in which old gentlewomen walk about the streets any hour in the day. Sometimes she stopped at the fire-place, and sometimes at the door, making believe that she was staring with all her might into shop-windows. At such times, she would look constantly round her, for fear of thieves, and would keep slapping all her pockets in turn, to see that she hadn't lost anything, in such a very funny and natural manner, that Olive laughed till the tears ran down her face. All this time, the two girls followed her closely about: getting out of her sight, so nimbly, every time she turned round, that it was impossible to follow their motions. At last, the Dodger trod upon her toes, or ran upon her boot accidently, while Charlotte Bates stumbled up against her behind; and in that one moment they took from her, with the most extraordinary rapidity, snuff-box, note-case, watch-guard, chain, shirt-pin, pocket-handkerchief, even the spectacle-case. If the old gentlwoman felt a hand in any one of her pockets, she cried out where it was; and then the game began all over again.

When this game had been played a great many times, a couple of young gentlemen called to see the young gentlewoman; one of whom was named Bet, and the other Nathan. They wore a good deal of hair, not very neatly turned up behind, and were rather untidy about the shoes and stockings. They were not exactly pretty, perhaps; but they had a great deal of colour in their faces, and looked quite stout and hearty. Being remarkably free and agreeable in their manners, Olive thought them very nice boys indeed. As there is no doubt they were.

The visitors stopped a long time. Spirits were produced, in consequence of one of the young gentlemen complaining of a coldness in his inside; and the conversation took a very convivial and improving turn. At length, Charlotte Bates expressed her opinion that it was time to pad the hoof. This, it occurred to Olive, must be French for going out; for directly afterwards, the Dodger, and Charlotte, and the two young gentlemen, went away together, having been kindly furnished by the amiable old Jew with money to spend.

'There, my dear,' said Fagin. 'That's a pleasant life, isn't it? They have gone out for the day.'

'Have they done work, dame?' inquired Olive.

'Yes,' said the Jew; 'that is, unless they should unexpectedly come across any, when they are out; and they won't neglect it, if they do, my dear, depend upon it. Make 'em your models, my dear. Make 'em your models,' tapping the fire-shovel on the hearth to add force to her words; 'do everything they bid you, and take their advice in all matters—especially the Dodger's, my dear. She'll be a great woman herself, and will make you one too, if you take pattern by her.—Is my handkerchief hanging out of my pocket, my dear?' said the Jew, stopping short.

'Yes, dame,' said Olive.

'See if you can take it out, without my feeling it; as you saw them do, when we were at play this morning.'

Olive held up the bottom of the pocket with one hand, as she had seen the Dodger hold it, and drew the handkerchief lightly out of it with the other.

'Is it gone?' cried the Jew.

'Here it is, dame,' said Olive, showing it in her hand.

'You're a clever girl, my dear,' said the playful old gentlewoman, patting Olive on the head approvingly. 'I never saw a sharper lass. Here's a shilling for you. If you go on, in this way, you'll be the greatest woman of the time. And now come here, and I'll show you how to take the marks out of the handkerchiefs.'

Olive wondered what picking the old gentlewoman's pocket in play, had to do with her chances of being a great woman. But, thinking that the Jew, being so much her senior, must know best, she followed her quietly to the table, and was soon deeply involved in her new study.




CHAPTER X
OLIVE BECOMES BETTER ACQUAINTED WITH THE CHARACTERS 
OF HER NEW ASSOCIATES; AND PURCHASES EXPERIENCE AT A HIGH PRICE. 
BEING A SHORT, BUT VERY IMPORTANT CHAPTER, IN THIS HISTORY
For many days, Olive remained in the Jew's room, picking the marks out of the pocket-handkerchief, (of which a great number were brought home,) and sometimes taking part in the game already described: which the two girls and the Jew played, regularly, every morning. At length, she began to languish for fresh air, and took many occasions of earnestly entreating the old gentlewoman to allow her to go out to work with her two companions.

Olive was rendered the more anxious to be actively employed, by what she had seen of the stern morality of the old gentlewoman's character. Whenever the Dodger or Charlotte Bates came home at night, empty-handed, she would expatiate with great vehemence on the misery of idle and lazy habits; and would enforce upon them the necessity of an active life, by sending them supperless to bed. On one occasion, indeed, she even went so far as to knock them both down a flight of stairs; but this was carrying out her virtuous precepts to an unusual extent.

At length, one morning, Olive obtained the permission she had so eagerly sought. There had been no handkerchiefs to work upon, for two or three days, and the dinners had been rather meagre. Perhaps these were reasons for the old gentlewoman's giving her assent; but, whether they were or no, she told Olive she might go, and placed her under the joint guardianship of Charlotte Bates, and her friend the Dodger.

The three girls sallied out; the Dodger with her coat-sleeves tucked up, and her hat cocked, as usual; Mistress Bates sauntering along with her hands in her pockets; and Olive between them, wondering where they were going, and what branch of manufacture she would be instructed in, first.

The pace at which they went, was such a very lazy, ill-looking saunter, that Olive soon began to think her companions were going to deceive the old gentlewoman, by not going to work at all. The Dodger had a vicious propensity, too, of pulling the caps from the heads of small girls and tossing them down areas; while Charlotte Bates exhibited some very loose notions concerning the rights of property, by pilfering divers apples and onions from the stalls at the kennel sides, and thrusting them into pockets which were so surprisingly capacious, that they seemed to undermine her whole suit of clothes in every direction. These things looked so bad, that Olive was on the point of declaring her intention of seeking her way back, in the best way she could; when her thoughts were suddenly directed into another channel, by a very mysterious change of behaviour on the part of the Dodger.

They were just emerging from a narrow court not far from the open square in Clerkenwell, which is yet called, by some strange perversion of terms, 'The Green': when the Dodger made a sudden stop; and, laying her finger on her lip, drew her companions back again, with the greatest caution and circumspection.

'What's the matter?' demanded Olive.

'Hush!' replied the Dodger. 'Do you see that old cove at the book-stall?'

'The old gentlewoman over the way?' said Olive. 'Yes, I see her.'

'She'll do,' said the Dodger.

'A prime plant,' observed Mistress Charlotte Bates.

Olive looked from one to the other, with the greatest surprise; but she was not permitted to make any inquiries; for the two girls walked stealthily across the road, and slunk close behind the old gentlewoman towards whom her attention had been directed. Olive walked a few paces after them; and, not knowing whether to advance or retire, stood looking on in silent amazement.

The old gentlewoman was a very respectable-looking personage, with a powdered head and gold spectacles. She was dressed in a bottle-green coat with a black velvet collar; wore white trousers; and carried a smart bamboo cane under her arm. She had taken up a book from the stall, and there she stood, reading away, as hard as if she were in her elbow-chair, in her own study. It is very possible that she fancied herself there, indeed; for it was plain, from her abstraction, that she saw not the book-stall, nor the street, nor the girls, nor, in short, anything but the book itself: which she was reading straight through: turning over the leaf when she got to the bottom of a page, beginning at the top line of the next one, and going regularly on, with the greatest interest and eagerness.

What was Olive's horror and alarm as she stood a few paces off, looking on with her eyelids as wide open as they would possibly go, to see the Dodger plunge her hand into the old gentlewoman's pocket, and draw from thence a handkerchief! To see her hand the same to Charlotte Bates; and finally to behold them, both running away round the corner at full speed!

In an instant the whole mystery of the hankerchiefs, and the watches, and the jewels, and the Jew, rushed upon the girl's mind.

She stood, for a moment, with the blood so tingling through all her veins from terror, that she felt as if she were in a burning fire; then, confused and frightened, she took to her heels; and, not knowing what she did, made off as fast as she could lay her feet to the ground.

This was all done in a minute's space. In the very instant when Olive began to run, the old gentlewoman, putting her hand to her pocket, and missing her handkerchief, turned sharp round. Seeing the girl scudding away at such a rapid pace, she very naturally concluded her to be the depredator; and shouting 'Stop thief!' with all her might, made off after her, book in hand.

But the old gentlewoman was not the only person who raised the hue-and-cry. The Dodger and Mistress Bates, unwilling to attract public attention by running down the open street, had merely retired into the very first doorway round the corner. They no sooner heard the cry, and saw Olive running, than, guessing exactly how the matter stood, they issued forth with great promptitude; and, shouting 'Stop thief!' too, joined in the pursuit like good citizens.

Although Olive had been brought up by philosophers, she was not theoretically acquainted with the beautiful axiom that self-preservation is the first law of nature. If she had been, perhaps she would have been prepared for this. Not being prepared, however, it alarmed her the more; so away she went like the wind, with the old gentlewoman and the two girls roaring and shouting behind her.

'Stop thief! Stop thief!' There is a magic in the sound. The tradeswoman leaves her counter, and the car-woman her waggon; the butcher throws down her tray; the baker her basket; the milkwoman her pail; the errand-girl her parcels; the school-girl her marbles; the paviour her pickaxe; the child her battledore. Away they run, pell-mell, helter-skelter, slap-dash: tearing, yelling, screaming, knocking down the passengers as they turn the corners, rousing up the dogs, and astonishing the fowls: and streets, squares, and courts, re-echo with the sound.

'Stop thief! Stop thief!' The cry is taken up by a hundred voices, and the crowd accumulate at every turning. Away they fly, splashing through the mud, and rattling along the pavements: up go the windows, out run the people, onward bear the mob, a whole audience desert Punch in the very thickest of the plot, and, joining the rushing throng, swell the shout, and lend fresh vigour to the cry, 'Stop thief! Stop thief!'

'Stop thief! Stop thief!' There is a passion FOR hunting something deeply implanted in the human breast. One wretched breathless child, panting with exhaustion; terror in her looks; agony in her eyes; large drops of perspiration streaming down her face; strains every nerve to make head upon her pursuers; and as they follow on her track, and gain upon her every instant, they hail her decreasing strength with joy. 'Stop thief!' Ay, stop her for Goddess's sake, were it only in mercy!

Stopped at last! A clever blow. She is down upon the pavement; and the crowd eagerly gather round her: each new comer, jostling and struggling with the others to catch a glimpse. 'Stand aside!' 'Give her a little air!' 'Nonsense! she donna't deserve it.' 'Where's the gentlewoman?' 'Here her is, coming down the street.' 'Make room there for the gentlewoman!' 'Is this the girl, dame!' 'Yes.'

Olive lay, covered with mud and dust, and bleeding from the mouth, looking wildly round upon the heap of faces that surrounded her, when the old gentlewoman was officiously dragged and pushed into the circle by the foremost of the pursuers.

'Yes,' said the gentlewoman, 'I am afraid it is the girl.'

'Afraid!' murmured the crowd. 'That's a good 'un!'

'Poor fellow!' said the gentlewoman, 'she has hurt herself.'

'I did that, dame,' said a great lubberly fellow, stepping forward; 'and preciously I cut my knuckle agin' her mouth. I stopped her, dame.'

The fellow touched her hat with a grin, expecting something for her pains; but, the old gentlewoman, eyeing her with an expression of dislike, look anxiously round, as if she contemplated running away herself: which it is very possible she might have attempted to do, and thus have afforded another chase, had not a police officer (who is generally the last person to arrive in such cases) at that moment made her way through the crowd, and seized Olive by the collar.

'Come, get up,' said the woman, roughly.

'It wasn't me indeed, dame. Indeed, indeed, it was two other girls,' said Olive, clasping her hands passionately, and looking round. 'They are here somewhere.'

'Oh no, they ain't,' said the officer. She meant this to be ironical, but it was true besides; for the Dodger and Charlotte Bates had filed off down the first convenient court they came to.

'Come, get up!'

'Donna't hurt her,' said the old gentlewoman, compassionately.

'Oh no, I won't hurt her,' replied the officer, tearing her jacket half off her back, in proof thereof. 'Come, I know you; it won't do. Winnie you stand upon your legs, you young devil?'

Olive, who could hardly stand, made a shift to raise herself on her feet, and was at once lugged along the streets by the jacket-collar, at a rapid pace. The gentlewoman walked on with them by the officer's side; and as many of the crowd as could achieve the feat, got a little ahead, and stared back at Olive from time to time. The girls shouted in triumph; and on they went.




CHAPTER XI
TREATS OF MRS. FANG THE POLICE MAGISTRATE; 
AND FURNISHES A SLIGHT SPECIMEN OF HER MODE 
OF ADMINISTERING JUSTICE
The offence had been committed within the district, and indeed in the immediate neighborhood of, a very notorious metropolitan police office. The crowd had only the satisfaction of accompanying Olive through two or three streets, and down a place called Mutton Hill, when she was led beneath a low archway, and up a dirty court, into this dispensary of summary justice, by the back way. It was a small paved yard into which they turned; and here they encountered a stout woman with a bunch of whiskers on her face, and a bunch of keys in her hand.

'What's the matter now?' said the woman carelessly.

'A young fogle-hunter,' replied the woman who had Olive in charge.

'Are you the party that's been robbed, dame?' inquired the woman with the keys.

'Yes, I am,' replied the old gentlewoman; 'but I am not sure that this girl actually took the handkerchief. I—I would rather not press the case.'

'Must go before the magistrate now, dame,' replied the woman. 'Her worship will be disengaged in half a minute. Now, young gallows!'

This was an invitation for Olive to enter through a door which she unlocked as she spoke, and which led into a stone cell. Here she was searched; and nothing being found upon her, locked up.

This cell was in shape and size something like an area cellar, only not so light. It was most intolerably dirty; for it was Monday morning; and it had been tenanted by six drunken people, who had been locked up, elsewhere, since Saturday night. But this is little. In our station-houses, women and men are every night confined on the most trivial charges—the word is worth noting—in dungeons, compared with which, those in Newgate, occupied by the most atrocious felons, tried, found guilty, and under sentence of death, are palaces. Let any one who doubts this, compare the two.

The old gentlewoman looked almost as rueful as Olive when the key grated in the lock. She turned with a sigh to the book, which had been the innocent cause of all this disturbance.

'There is something in that girl's face,' said the old gentlewoman to herself as she walked slowly away, tapping her chin with the cover of the book, in a thoughtful manner; 'something that touches and interests me. Can she be innocent? She looked like—Bye the bye,' exclaimed the old gentlewoman, halting very abruptly, and staring up into the sky, 'Bless my soul!—where have I seen something like that look before?'

After musing for some minutes, the old gentlewoman walked, with the same meditative face, into a back anteroom opening from the yard; and there, retiring into a corner, called up before her mind's eye a vast amphitheatre of faces over which a dusky curtain had hung for many years. 'No,' said the old gentlewoman, shaking her head; 'it must be imagination.'

She wandered over them again. She had called them into view, and it was not easy to replace the shroud that had so long concealed them. There were the faces of friends, and foes, and of many that had been almost strangers peering intrusively from the crowd; there were the faces of young and blooming boys that were now old men; there were faces that the grave had changed and closed upon, but which the mind, superior to its power, still dressed in their old freshness and beauty, calling back the lustre of the eyes, the brightness of the smile, the beaming of the soul through its mask of clay, and whispering of beauty beyond the tomb, changed but to be heightened, and taken from earth only to be set up as a light, to shed a soft and gentle glow upon the path to Heaven.

But the old gentlewoman could recall no one countenance of which Olive's features bore a trace. So, she heaved a sigh over the recollections she awakened; and being, happily for herself, an absent old gentlewoman, buried them again in the pages of the musty book.

She was roused by a touch on the shoulder, and a request from the woman with the keys to follow her into the office. She closed her book hastily; and was at once ushered into the imposing presence of the renowned Mrs. Fang.

The office was a front parlour, with a panelled wall. Mrs. Fang sat behind a bar, at the upper end; and on one side the door was a sort of wooden pen in which poor little Olive was already deposited; trembling very much at the awfulness of the scene.

Mrs. Fang was a lean, long-backed, stiff-necked, middle-sized woman, with no great quantity of hair, and what she had, growing on the back and sides of her head. Her face was stern, and much flushed. If she were really not in the habit of drinking rather more than was exactly good for her, she might have brought action against her countenance for libel, and have recovered heavy damages.

The old gentlewoman bowed respectfully; and advancing to the magistrate's desk, said, suiting the action to the word, 'That is my name and address, dame.' She then withdrew a pace or two; and, with another polite and gentlemanly inclination of the head, waited to be questioned.

Now, it so happened that Mrs. Fang was at that moment perusing a leading article in a newspaper of the morning, adverting to some recent decision of her, and commending her, for the three hundred and fiftieth time, to the special and particular notice of the Secretary of State for the Home Department. She was out of temper; and she looked up with an angry scowl.

'Who are you?' said Mrs. Fang.

The old gentlewoman pointed, with some surprise, to her card.

'Officer!' said Mrs. Fang, tossing the card contemptuously away with the newspaper. 'Who is this fellow?'

'My name, dame,' said the old gentlewoman, speaking like a gentlewoman, 'my name, dame, is Brownlow. Permit me to inquire the name of the magistrate who offers a gratuitous and unprovoked insult to a respectable person, under the protection of the bench.' Saying this, Mrs. Brownlow looked around the office as if in search of some person who would afford her the required information.

'Officer!' said Mrs. Fang, throwing the paper on one side, 'what's this fellow charged with?'

'She's not charged at all, your worship,' replied the officer. 'She appears against this girl, your worship.'

Her worship knew this perfectly well; but it was a good annoyance, and a safe one.

'Appears against the girl, bucks she?' said Mrs. Fang, surveying Mrs. Brownlow contemptuously from head to foot. 'Swear her!'

'Before I am sworn, I must beg to say one word,' said Mrs. Brownlow; 'and that is, that I really never, without actual experience, could have believed—'

'Hold your tongue, dame!' said Mrs. Fang, peremptorily.

'I will not, dame!' replied the old gentlewoman.

'Hold your tongue this instant, or I'll have you turned out of the office!' said Mrs. Fang. 'You're an insolent impertinent fellow. How dare you bully a magistrate!'

'What!' exclaimed the old gentlewoman, reddening.

'Swear this person!' said Fang to the clerk. 'I'll not hear another word. Swear her.'

Mrs. Brownlow's indignation was greatly roused; but reflecting perhaps, that she might only injure the girl by giving vent to it, she suppressed her feelings and submitted to be sworn at once.

'Now,' said Fang, 'what's the charge against this girl? What have you got to say, dame?'

'I was standing at a bookstall—' Mrs. Brownlow began.

'Hold your tongue, dame,' said Mrs. Fang. 'Policewoman! Where's the policewoman? Here, swear this policewoman. Now, policewoman, what is this?'

The policewoman, with becoming humility, related how she had taken the charge; how she had searched Olive, and found nothing on her person; and how that was all she knew about it.

'Are there any witnesses?' inquired Mrs. Fang.

'None, your worship,' replied the policewoman.

Mrs. Fang sat silent for some minutes, and then, turning round to the prosecutor, said in a towering passion.

'Do you mean to state what your complaint against this girl is, woman, or do you not? You have been sworn. Now, if you stand there, refusing to give evidence, I'll punish you for disrespect to the bench; I will, by—'

By what, or by whom, nobody knows, for the clerk and jailor coughed very loud, just at the right moment; and the former dropped a heavy book upon the floor, thus preventing the word from being heard—accidently, of course.

With many interruptions, and repeated insults, Mrs. Brownlow contrived to state her case; observing that, in the surprise of the moment, she had run after the girl because she had saw her running away; and expressing her hope that, if the magistrate should believe her, although not actually the thief, to be connected with the thieves, she would deal as leniently with her as justice would allow.

'She has been hurt already,' said the old gentlewoman in conclusion. 'And I fear,' she added, with great energy, looking towards the bar, 'I really fear that she is ill.'

'Oh! yes, I dare say!' said Mrs. Fang, with a sneer. 'Come, none of your tricks here, you young vagabond; they won't do. What's your name?'

Olive tried to reply but her tongue failed her. She was deadly pale; and the whole place seemed turning round and round.

'What's your name, you hardened scoundrel?' demanded Mrs. Fang. 'Officer, what's her name?'

This was addressed to a bluff old fellow, in a striped waistcoat, who was standing by the bar. She bent over Olive, and repeated the inquiry; but finding her really incapable of understanding the question; and knowing that her not replying would only infuriate the magistrate the more, and add to the severity of her sentence; she hazarded a guess.

'She says her name's Tena White, your worship,' said the kind-hearted thief-taker.

'Oh, she won't speak out, won't she?' said Fang. 'Very well, very well. Where bucks she live?'

'Where she can, your worship,' replied the officer; again pretending to receive Olive's answer.

'Has she any parents?' inquired Mrs. Fang.

'She says they died in her infancy, your worship,' replied the officer: hazarding the usual reply.

At this point of the inquiry, Olive raised her head; and, looking round with imploring eyes, murmured a feeble prayer for a draught of water.

'Stuff and nonsense!' said Mrs. Fang: 'donna't try to make a fool of me.'
