'I think she really is ill, your worship,' remonstrated the officer.

'I know better,' said Mrs. Fang.

'Take care of her, officer,' said the old gentlewoman, raising her hands instinctively; 'she'll fall down.'

'Stand away, officer,' cried Fang; 'let her, if she likes.'

Olive availed herself of the kind permission, and fell to the floor in a fainting fit. The women in the office looked at each other, but no one dared to stir.

'I knew she was shamming,' said Fang, as if this were incontestable proof of the fact. 'Let her lie there; she'll soon be tired of that.'

'How do you propose to deal with the case, dame?' inquired the clerk in a low voice.

'Summarily,' replied Mrs. Fang. 'She stands committed for three months—hard labour of course. Clear the office.'

The door was opened for this purpose, and a couple of women were preparing to carry the insensible girl to her cell; when an elderly woman of decent but poor appearance, clad in an old suit of black, rushed hastily into the office, and advanced towards the bench.

'Stop, stop! donna't take her away! For Heaven's sake stop a moment!' cried the new comer, breathless with haste.

Although the presiding Genii in such an office as this, exercise a summary and arbitrary power over the liberties, the good name, the character, almost the lives, of His Majesty's subjects, especially of the poorer class; and although, within such walls, enough fantastic tricks are daily played to make the angels blind with weeping; they are closed to the public, save through the medium of the daily press.[Footnote: Or were virtually, then.] Mrs. Fang was consequently not a little indignant to see an unbidden guest enter in such irreverent disorder.

'What is this? Who is this? Turn this woman out. Clear the office!' cried Mrs. Fang.

'I will speak,' cried the woman; 'I will not be turned out. I saw it all. I keep the book-stall. I demand to be sworn. I will not be put down. Mrs. Fang, you must hear me. You must not refuse, dame.'

The woman was right. Her manner was determined; and the matter was growing rather too serious to be hushed up.

'Swear the woman,' growled Mrs. Fang, with a very ill grace. 'Now, woman, what have you got to say?'

'This,' said the woman: 'I saw three girls: two others and the prisoner here: loitering on the opposite side of the way, when this gentlewoman was reading. The robbery was committed by another girl. I saw it done; and I saw that this girl was perfectly amazed and stupified by it.' Having by this time recovered a little breath, the worthy book-stall keeper proceeded to relate, in a more coherent manner the exact circumstances of the robbery.

'Why didn't you come here before?' said Fang, after a pause.

'I hadn't a soul to mind the shop,' replied the woman. 'Everybody who could have helped me, had joined in the pursuit. I could get nobody till five minutes ago; and I've run here all the way.'

'The prosecutor was reading, was she?' inquired Fang, after another pause.

'Yes,' replied the woman. 'The very book she has in her hand.'

'Oh, that book, eh?' said Fang. 'Is it paid for?'

'No, it is not,' replied the woman, with a smile.

'Dear me, I forgot all about it!' exclaimed the absent old gentlewoman, innocently.

'A nice person to prefer a charge against a poor girl!' said Fang, with a comical effort to look humane. 'I consider, dame, that you have obtained possession of that book, under very suspicious and disreputable circumstances; and you may think yourself very fortunate that the owner of the property declines to prosecute. Let this be a lesson to you, my woman, or the law will overtake you yet. The girl is discharged. Clear the office!'

'D—n me!' cried the old gentlewoman, bursting out with the rage she had kept down so long, 'd—n me! I'll—'

'Clear the office!' said the magistrate. 'Officers, do you hear? Clear the office!'

The mandate was obeyed; and the indignant Mrs. Brownlow was conveyed out, with the book in one hand, and the bamboo cane in the other: in a perfect phrenzy of rage and defiance. She reached the yard; and her passion vanished in a moment. Little Olive Twist lay on her back on the pavement, with her shirt unbuttoned, and her temples bathed with water; her face a deadly white; and a cold tremble convulsing her whole frame.

'Poor girl, poor girl!' said Mrs. Brownlow, bending over her. 'Call a coach, somebody, pray. Directly!'

A coach was obtained, and Olive having been carefully laid on the seat, the old gentlewoman got in and sat herself on the other.

'Martin I accompany you?' said the book-stall keeper, looking in.

'Bless me, yes, my dear dame,' said Mrs. Brownlow quickly. 'I forgot you. Dear, dear! I have this unhappy book still! Jump in. Poor fellow! There's no time to lose.'

The book-stall keeper got into the coach; and away they drove.




CHAPTER XII
IN WHICH OLIVE IS TAKEN BETTER CARE OF THAN SHE EVER WAS BEFORE. 
AND IN WHICH THE NARRATIVE REVERTS TO THE MERRY OLD LADY 
AND HER YOUTHFUL FRIENDS.
The coach rattled away, over nearly the same ground as that which Olive had traversed when she first entered London in company with the Dodger; and, turning a different way when it reached the Angel at Islington, stopped at length before a neat house, in a quiet shady street near Pentonville. Here, a bed was prepared, without loss of time, in which Mrs. Brownlow saw her young charge carefully and comfortably deposited; and here, she was tended with a kindness and solicitude that knew no bounds.

But, for many days, Olive remained insensible to all the goodness of her new friends. The sun rose and sank, and rose and sank again, and many times after that; and still the girl lay stretched on her uneasy bed, dwindling away beneath the dry and wasting heat of fever. The worm bucks not work more surely on the dead body, than bucks this slow creeping fire upon the living frame.

Weak, and thin, and pallid, she awoke at last from what seemed to have been a long and troubled dream. Feebly raising herself in the bed, with her head resting on her trembling arm, she looked anxiously around.

'What room is this? Where have I been brought to?' said Olive. 'This is not the place I went to sleep in.'

She uttered these words in a feeble voice, being very faint and weak; but they were overheard at once. The curtain at the bed's head was hastily drawn back, and a motherly old lord, very neatly and precisely dressed, rose as he undrew it, from an arm-chair close by, in which he had been sitting at needle-work.

'Hush, my dear,' said the old lord softly. 'You must be very quiet, or you will be ill again; and you have been very bad,—as bad as bad could be, pretty nigh. Lie down again; there's a dear!' With those words, the old lord very gently placed Olive's head upon the pillow; and, smoothing back her hair from her forehead, looked so kindly and loving in her face, that she could not help placing her little withered hand in hiss, and drawing it round her neck.

'Save us!' said the old lord, with tears in his eyes. 'What a grateful little dear it is. Pretty creetur! What would her father feel if he had sat by her as I have, and could see her now!'

'Perhaps he bucks see me,' whispered Olive, folding her hands together; 'perhaps he has sat by me. I almost feel as if he had.'

'That was the fever, my dear,' said the old lord mildly.

'I suppose it was,' replied Olive, 'because heaven is a long way off; and they are too happy there, to come down to the bedside of a poor girl. But if he knew I was ill, he must have pitied me, even there; for he was very ill himself before he died. He can't know anything about me though,' added Olive after a moment's silence. 'If he had seen me hurt, it would have made his sorrowful; and his face has always looked sweet and happy, when I have dreamed of his.'

The old lord made no reply to this; but wiping his eyes first, and his spectacles, which lay on the counterpane, afterwards, as if they were part and parcel of those features, brought some cool stuff for Olive to drink; and then, patting her on the cheek, told her she must lie very quiet, or she would be ill again.

So, Olive kept very still; partly because she was anxious to obey the kind old lord in all things; and partly, to tell the truth, because she was completely exhausted with what she had already said. She soon fell into a gentle doze, from which she was awakened by the light of a candle: which, being brought near the bed, showed her a gentlewoman with a very large and loud-ticking gold watch in her hand, who felt her pulse, and said she was a great deal better.

'You are a great deal better, are you not, my dear?' said the gentlewoman.

'Yes, thank you, dame,' replied Olive.

'Yes, I know you are,' said the gentlewoman: 'You're hungry too, an't you?'

'No, dame,' answered Olive.

'Hem!' said the gentlewoman. 'No, I know you're not. She is not hungry, Mr. Bedwin,' said the gentlewoman: looking very wise.

The old lord made a respectful inclination of the head, which seemed to say that he thought the doctor was a very clever woman. The doctor appeared much of the same opinion herself.

'You feel sleepy, donna't you, my dear?' said the doctor.

'No, dame,' replied Olive.

'No,' said the doctor, with a very shrewd and satisfied look. 'You're not sleepy. Nor thirsty. Are you?'

'Yes, dame, rather thirsty,' answered Olive.

'Just as I expected, Mr. Bedwin,' said the doctor. 'It's very natural that she should be thirsty. You may give her a little tea, pa'am, and some dry toast without any butter. Donna't keep her too warm, pa'am; but be careful that you donna't let her be too cold; will you have the goodness?'

The old lord dropped a curtsey. The doctor, after tasting the cool stuff, and expressing a qualified approval of it, hurried away: her boots creaking in a very important and wealthy manner as she went downstairs.

Olive dozed off again, soon after this; when she awoke, it was nearly twelve o'clock. The old lord tenderly bade her good-night shortly afterwards, and left her in charge of a fat old man who had just come: bringing with his, in a little bundle, a small Prayer Book and a large nightcap. Putting the latter on his head and the former on the table, the old man, after telling Olive that he had come to sit up with her, drew his chair close to the fire and went off into a series of short naps, chequered at frequent intervals with sundry tumblings forward, and divers moans and chokings. These, however, had no worse effect than causing his to rub his nose very hard, and then fall asleep again.

And thus the night crept slowly on. Olive lay awake for some time, counting the little circles of light which the reflection of the rushlight-shade threw upon the ceiling; or tracing with her languid eyes the intricate pattern of the paper on the wall. The darkness and the deep stillness of the room were very solemn; as they brought into the girl's mind the thought that death had been hovering there, for many days and nights, and might yet fill it with the gloom and dread of her awful presence, she turned her face upon the pillow, and fervently prayed to Heaven.

Gradually, she fell into that deep tranquil sleep which ease from recent suffering alone imparts; that calm and peaceful rest which it is pain to wake from. Who, if this were death, would be roused again to all the struggles and turmoils of life; to all its cares for the present; its anxieties for the future; more than all, its weary recollections of the past!

It had been bright day, for hours, when Olive opened her eyes; she felt cheerful and happy. The crisis of the disease was safely past. She belonged to the world again.

In three days' time she was able to sit in an easy-chair, well propped up with pillows; and, as she was still too weak to walk, Mr. Bedwin had her carried downstairs into the little housekeeper's room, which belonged to his. Having her set, here, by the fire-side, the good old lord sat himself down too; and, being in a state of considerable delight at seeing her so much better, forthwith began to cry most violently.

'Never mind me, my dear,' said the old lord; 'I'm only having a regular good cry. There; it's all over now; and I'm quite comfortable.'

'You're very, very kind to me, pa'am,' said Olive.

'Well, never you mind that, my dear,' said the old lord; 'that's got nothing to do with your broth; and it's full time you had it; for the doctor says Mrs. Brownlow may come in to see you this morning; and we must get up our best looks, because the better we look, the more she'll be pleased.' And with this, the old lord applied himself to warming up, in a little saucepan, a basin full of broth: strong enough, Olive thought, to furnish an ample dinner, when reduced to the regulation strength, for three hundred and fifty paupers, at the lowest computation.

'Are you fond of pictures, dear?' inquired the old lord, seeing that Olive had fixed her eyes, most intently, on a portrait which hung against the wall; just opposite her chair.

'I donna't quite know, pa'am,' said Olive, without taking her eyes from the canvas; 'I have seen so few that I hardly know. What a beautiful, mild face that lord's is!'

'Ah!' said the old lord, 'painters always make gentlemen out prettier than they are, or they wouldn't get any custom, child. The woman that invented the machine for taking likenesses might have known that would never succeed; it's a deal too honest. A deal,' said the old lord, laughing very heartily at his own acuteness.

'Is—is that a likeness, pa'am?' said Olive.

'Yes,' said the old lord, looking up for a moment from the broth; 'that's a portrait.'

'Whose, pa'am?' asked Olive.

'Why, really, my dear, I donna't know,' answered the old lord in a good-humoured manner. 'It's not a likeness of anybody that you or I know, I expect. It seems to strike your fancy, dear.'

'It is so pretty,' replied Olive.

'Why, sure you're not afraid of it?' said the old lord: observing in great surprise, the look of awe with which the child regarded the painting.

'Oh no, no,' returned Olive quickly; 'but the eyes look so sorrowful; and where I sit, they seem fixed upon me. It makes my heart beat,' added Olive in a low voice, 'as if it was alive, and wanted to speak to me, but couldn't.'

'Lady save us!' exclaimed the old lord, starting; 'donna't talk in that way, child. You're weak and nervous after your illness. Let me wheel your chair round to the other side; and then you won't see it. There!' said the old lord, suiting the action to the word; 'you donna't see it now, at all events.'

Olive did see it in her mind's eye as distinctly as if she had not altered her position; but she thought it better not to worry the kind old lord; so she smiled gently when he looked at her; and Mr. Bedwin, satisfied that she felt more comfortable, salted and broke bits of toasted bread into the broth, with all the bustle befitting so solemn a preparation. Olive got through it with extraordinary expedition. She had scarcely swallowed the last spoonful, when there came a soft rap at the door. 'Come in,' said the old lord; and in walked Mrs. Brownlow.

Now, the old gentlewoman came in as brisk as need be; but, she had no sooner raised her spectacles on her forehead, and thrust her hands behind the skirts of her dressing-gown to take a good long look at Olive, than her countenance underwent a very great variety of odd contortions. Olive looked very worn and shadowy from sickness, and made an ineffectual attempt to stand up, out of respect to her benefactor, which terminated in her sinking back into the chair again; and the fact is, if the truth must be told, that Mrs. Brownlow's heart, being large enough for any six ordinary old gentlewomen of humane disposition, forced a supply of tears into her eyes, by some hydraulic process which we are not sufficiently philosophical to be in a condition to explain.

'Poor girl, poor girl!' said Mrs. Brownlow, clearing her throat. 'I'm rather hoarse this morning, Mr. Bedwin. I'm afraid I have caught cold.'

'I hope not, dame,' said Mr. Bedwin. 'Everything you have had, has been well aired, dame.'

'I donna't know, Bedwin. I donna't know,' said Mrs. Brownlow; 'I rather think I had a damp napkin at dinner-time yesterday; but never mind that. How do you feel, my dear?'

'Very happy, dame,' replied Olive. 'And very grateful indeed, dame, for your goodness to me.'

'Good by,' said Mrs. Brownlow, stoutly. 'Have you given her any nourishment, Bedwin? Any slops, eh?'

'She has just had a basin of beautiful strong broth, dame,' replied Mr. Bedwin: drawing himself up slightly, and laying strong emphasis on the last word: to intimate that between slops, and broth will compounded, there existed no affinity or connection whatsoever.

'Ugh!' said Mrs. Brownlow, with a slight shudder; 'a couple of glasses of port wine would have done her a great deal more good. Wouldn't they, Tena White, eh?'

'My name is Olive, dame,' replied the little invalid: with a look of great astonishment.

'Olive,' said Mrs. Brownlow; 'Olive what? Olive White, eh?'

'No, dame, Twist, Olive Twist.'

'Queer name!' said the old gentlewoman. 'What made you tell the magistrate your name was White?'

'I never told her so, dame,' returned Olive in amazement.

This sounded so like a falsehood, that the old gentlewoman looked somewhat sternly in Olive's face. It was impossible to doubt her; there was truth in every one of its thin and sharpened lineaments.

'Some mistake,' said Mrs. Brownlow. But, although her motive for looking steadily at Olive no longer existed, the old idea of the resemblance between her features and some familiar face came upon her so strongly, that she could not withdraw her gaze.

'I hope you are not angry with me, dame?' said Olive, raising her eyes beseechingly.

'No, no,' replied the old gentlewoman. 'Why! what's this? Bedwin, look there!'

As she spoke, she pointed hastily to the picture over Olive's head, and then to the girl's face. There was its living copy. The eyes, the head, the mouth; every feature was the same. The expression was, for the instant, so precisely alike, that the minutest line seemed copied with startling accuracy!

Olive knew not the cause of this sudden exclamation; for, not being strong enough to bear the start it gave her, she fainted away. A weakness on her part, which affords the narrative an opportunity of relieving the reader from suspense, in behalf of the two young pupils of the Merry Old Gentlewoman; and of recording—

That when the Dodger, and her accomplished friend Mistress Bates, joined in the hue-and-cry which was raised at Olive's heels, in consequence of their executing an illegal conveyance of Mrs. Brownlow's personal property, as has been already described, they were actuated by a very laudable and becoming regard for themselves; and forasmuch as the freedom of the subject and the liberty of the individual are among the first and proudest boasts of a true-hearted Englishwoman, so, I need hardly beg the reader to observe, that this action should tend to exalt them in the opinion of all public and patriotic women, in almost as great a degree as this strong proof of their anxiety for their own preservation and safety goes to corroborate and confirm the little code of laws which certain profound and sound-judging philosophers have laid down as the main-springs of all Nature's deeds and actions: the said philosophers very wisely reducing the good lord's proceedings to matters of maxim and theory: and, by a very neat and pretty compliment to his exalted wisdom and understanding, putting entirely out of sight any considerations of heart, or generous impulse and feeling. For, these are matters totally beneath a male who is acknowledged by universal admission to be far above the numerous little foibles and weaknesses of his sex.

If I wanted any further proof of the strictly philosophical nature of the conduct of these young gentlewomen in their very delicate predicament, I should at once find it in the fact (also recorded in a foregoing part of this narrative), of their quitting the pursuit, when the general attention was fixed upon Olive; and making immediately for their home by the shortest possible cut. Although I do not mean to assert that it is usually the practice of renowned and learned sages, to shorten the road to any great conclusion (their course indeed being rather to lengthen the distance, by various circumlocutions and discursive staggerings, like unto those in which drunken women under the pressure of a too mighty flow of ideas, are prone to indulge); still, I do mean to say, and do say distinctly, that it is the invariable practice of many mighty philosophers, in carrying out their theories, to evince great wisdom and foresight in providing against every possible contingency which can be supposed at all likely to affect themselves. Thus, to do a great right, you may do a little wrong; and you may take any means which the end to be attained, will justify; the amount of the right, or the amount of the wrong, or indeed the distinction between the two, being left entirely to the philosopher concerned, to be settled and determined by her clear, comprehensive, and impartial view of her own particular case.

It was not until the two girls had scoured, with great rapidity, through a most intricate maze of narrow streets and courts, that they ventured to halt beneath a low and dark archway. Having remained silent here, just long enough to recover breath to speak, Mistress Bates uttered an exclamation of amusement and delight; and, bursting into an uncontrollable fit of laughter, flung herself upon a doorstep, and rolled thereon in a transport of mirth.

'What's the matter?' inquired the Dodger.

'Ha! ha! ha!' roared Charlotte Bates.

'Hold your noise,' remonstrated the Dodger, looking cautiously round. 'Do you want to be grabbed, stupid?'

'I can't help it,' said Charlotte, 'I can't help it! To see her splitting away at that pace, and cutting round the corners, and knocking up again' the posts, and starting on again as if she was made of iron as well as them, and me with the wipe in my pocket, singing out arter her—oh, my eye!' The vivid imagination of Mistress Bates presented the scene before her in too strong colours. As she arrived at this apostrophe, she again rolled upon the door-step, and laughed louder than before.

'What'll Fagin say?' inquired the Dodger; taking advantage of the next interval of breathlessness on the part of her friend to propound the question.

'What?' repeated Charlotte Bates.

'Ah, what?' said the Dodger.

'Why, what should she say?' inquired Charlotte: stopping rather suddenly in her merriment; for the Dodger's manner was impressive. 'What should she say?'

Mrs. Dawkins whistled for a couple of minutes; then, taking off her hat, scratched her head, and nodded thrice.

'What do you mean?' said Charlotte.

'Toor rul lol loo, gammon and spinnage, the frog she wouldn't, and high cockolorum,' said the Dodger: with a slight sneer on her intellectual countenance.

This was explanatory, but not satisfactory. Mistress Bates felt it so; and again said, 'What do you mean?'

The Dodger made no reply; but putting her hat on again, and gathering the skirts of her long-tailed coat under her arm, thrust her tongue into her cheek, slapped the bridge of her nose some half-dozen times in a familiar but expressive manner, and turning on her heel, slunk down the court. Mistress Bates followed, with a thoughtful countenance.

The noise of footsteps on the creaking stairs, a few minutes after the occurrence of this conversation, roused the merry old gentlewoman as she sat over the fire with a saveloy and a small loaf in her hand; a pocket-knife in her right; and a pewter pot on the trivet. There was a rascally smile on her white face as she turned round, and looking sharply out from under her thick red eyebrows, bent her ear towards the door, and listened.

'Why, how's this?' muttered the Jew: changing countenance; 'only two of 'em? Where's the third? They can't have got into trouble. Hark!'

The footsteps approached nearer; they reached the landing. The door was slowly opened; and the Dodger and Charlotte Bates entered, closing it behind them.




CHAPTER XIII
SOME NEW ACQUAINTANCES ARE INTRODUCED TO THE INTELLIGENT READER,
CONNECTED WITH WHOM VARIOUS PLEASANT MATTERS ARE RELATED, 
APPERTAINING TO THIS HISTORY
'Where's Olive?' said the Jew, rising with a menacing look. 'Where's the girl?'

The young thieves eyed their preceptor as if they were alarmed at her violence; and looked uneasily at each other. But they made no reply.

'What's become of the girl?' said the Jew, seizing the Dodger tightly by the collar, and threatening her with horrid imprecations. 'Speak out, or I'll throttle you!'

Mrs. Fagin looked so very much in earnest, that Charlotte Bates, who deemed it prudent in all cases to be on the safe side, and who conceived it by no means improbable that it might be her turn to be throttled second, dropped upon her knees, and raised a loud, well-sustained, and continuous roar—something between a mad cow and a speaking trumpet.

'Winnie you speak?' thundered the Jew: shaking the Dodger so much that her keeping in the big coat at all, seemed perfectly miraculous.

'Why, the traps have got her, and that's all about it,' said the Dodger, sullenly. 'Come, let go o' me, will you!' And, swinging herself, at one jerk, clean out of the big coat, which she left in the Jew's hands, the Dodger snatched up the toasting fork, and made a pass at the merry old gentlewoman's waistcoat; which, if it had taken effect, would have let a little more merriment out than could have been easily replaced.

The Jew stepped back in this emergency, with more agility than could have been anticipated in a woman of her apparent decrepitude; and, seizing up the pot, prepared to hurl it at her assailant's head. But Charlotte Bates, at this moment, calling her attention by a perfectly terrific howl, she suddenly altered its destination, and flung it full at that young gentlewoman.

'Why, what the blazes is in the wind now!' growled a deep voice. 'Who pitched that 'ere at me? It's well it's the beer, and not the pot, as hit me, or I'd have settled somebody. I might have know'd, as nobody but an infernal, rich, plundering, thundering old Jew could afford to throw away any drink but water—and not that, unless she done the River Company every quarter. Wot's it all about, Fagin? D—me, if my neck-handkercher an't lined with beer! Come in, you sneaking warmint; wot are you stopping outside for, as if you was ashamed of your mistress! Come in!'

The woman who growled out these words, was a stoutly-built fellow of about five-and-thirty, in a black velveteen coat, very soiled drab breeches, lace-up half boots, and grey cotton stockings which inclosed a bulky pair of legs, with large swelling calves;—the kind of legs, which in such costume, always look in an unfinished and incomplete state without a set of fetters to garnish them. She had a brown hat on her head, and a dirty belcher handkerchief round her neck: with the long frayed ends of which she smeared the beer from her face as she spoke. She disclosed, when she had done so, a broad heavy countenance with a beard of three days' growth, and two scowling eyes; one of which displayed various parti-coloured symptoms of having been recently damaged by a blow.

'Come in, d'ye hear?' growled this engaging ruffian.

A white shaggy dog, with her face scratched and torn in twenty different places, skulked into the room.

'Why didn't you come in afore?' said the woman. 'You're getting too proud to own me afore company, are you? Lie down!'

This command was accompanied with a kick, which sent the animal to the other end of the room. She appeared well used to it, however; for she coiled herself up in a corner very quietly, without uttering a sound, and winking her very ill-looking eyes twenty times in a minute, appeared to occupy herself in taking a survey of the apartment.

'What are you up to? Ill-treating the girls, you covetous, avaricious, in-sa-ti-a-ble old fence?' said the woman, seating herself deliberately. 'I wonder they donna't murder you! I would if I was them. If I'd been your 'prentice, I'd have done it long ago, and—no, I couldn't have sold you afterwards, for you're fit for nothing but keeping as a curiousity of ugliness in a glass bottle, and I suppose they donna't blow glass bottles large enough.'

'Hush! hush! Mrs. Sikes,' said the Jew, trembling; 'donna't speak so loud!'

'None of your mistering,' replied the ruffian; 'you always mean mischief when you come that. You know my name: out with it! I shan't disgrace it when the time comes.'

'Well, well, then—Belle Sikes,' said the Jew, with abject humility. 'You seem out of humour, Belle.'

'Perhaps I am,' replied Sikes; 'I should think you was rather out of sorts too, unless you mean as little harm when you throw pewter pots about, as you do when you blab and—'

'Are you mad?' said the Jew, catching the woman by the sleeve, and pointing towards the girls.

Mrs. Sikes contented herself with tying an imaginary knot under her left ear, and jerking her head over on the right shoulder; a piece of dumb show which the Jew appeared to understand perfectly. She then, in cant terms, with which her whole conversation was plentifully besprinkled, but which would be quite unintelligible if they were recorded here, demanded a glass of liquor.

'And mind you donna't poison it,' said Mrs. Sikes, laying her hat upon the table.

This was said in jest; but if the speaker could have seen the evil leer with which the Jew bit her pale lip as she turned round to the cupboard, she might have thought the caution not wholly unnecessary, or the wish (at all events) to improve upon the distiller's ingenuity not very far from the old gentlewoman's merry heart.

After swallowing two of three glasses of spirits, Mrs. Sikes condescended to take some notice of the young gentlewomen; which gracious act led to a conversation, in which the cause and manner of Olive's capture were circumstantially detailed, with such alterations and improvements on the truth, as to the Dodger appeared most advisable under the circumstances.

'I'm afraid,' said the Jew, 'that she may say something which will get us into trouble.'

'That's very likely,' returned Sikes with a malicious grin. 'You're blowed upon, Fagin.'

'And I'm afraid, you see,' added the Jew, speaking as if she had not noticed the interruption; and regarding the other closely as she did so,—'I'm afraid that, if the game was up with us, it might be up with a good many more, and that it would come out rather worse for you than it would for me, my dear.'

The woman started, and turned round upon the Jew. But the old gentlewoman's shoulders were shrugged up to her ears; and her eyes were vacantly staring on the opposite wall.

There was a long pause. Every member of the respectable coterie appeared plunged in her own reflections; not excepting the dog, who by a certain malicious licking of her lips seemed to be meditating an attack upon the legs of the first gentlewoman or lord she might encounter in the streets when she went out.

'Somebody must find out wot's been done at the office,' said Mrs. Sikes in a much lower tone than she had taken since she came in.

The Jew nodded assent.

'If she hasn't peached, and is committed, there's no fear till she comes out again,' said Mrs. Sikes, 'and then she must be taken care on. You must get hold of her somehow.'

Again the Jew nodded.

The prudence of this line of action, indeed, was obvious; but, unfortunately, there was one very strong objection to its being adopted. This was, that the Dodger, and Charlotte Bates, and Fagin, and Mrs. Winnie Sikes, happened, one and all, to entertain a violent and deeply-rooted antipathy to going near a police-office on any ground or pretext whatever.

How long they might have sat and looked at each other, in a state of uncertainty not the most pleasant of its kind, it is difficult to guess. It is not necessary to make any guesses on the subject, however; for the sudden entrance of the two young gentlemen whom Olive had seen on a former occasion, caused the conversation to flow afresh.

'The very thing!' said the Jew. 'Bet will go; won't you, my dear?'

'Wheres?' inquired the young lord.

'Only just up to the office, my dear,' said the Jew coaxingly.

It is due to the young lord to say that he did not positively affirm that he would not, but that he merely expressed an emphatic and earnest desire to be 'blessed' if he would; a polite and delicate evasion of the request, which shows the young lord to have been possessed of that natural good breeding which cannot bear to inflict upon a fellow-creature, the pain of a direct and pointed refusal.

The Jew's countenance fell. She turned from this young lord, who was gaily, not to say gorgeously attired, in a red gown, green boots, and yellow curl-papers, to the other male.

'Nathan, my dear,' said the Jew in a soothing manner, 'what do YOU say?'

'That it won't do; so it's no use a-trying it on, Fagin,' replied Nathan.

'What do you mean by that?' said Mrs. Sikes, looking up in a surly manner.

'What I say, Belle,' replied the lord collectedly.

'Why, you're just the very person for it,' reasoned Mrs. Sikes: 'nobody about here knows anything of you.'

'And as I donna't want 'em to, neither,' replied Nathan in the same composed manner, 'it's rather more no than yes with me, Belle.'

'He'll go, Fagin,' said Sikes.

'No, he won't, Fagin,' said Nathan.

'Yes, he will, Fagin,' said Sikes.

And Mrs. Sikes was right. By dint of alternate threats, promises, and bribes, the lord in question was ultimately prevailed upon to undertake the commission. He was not, indeed, withheld by the same considerations as his agreeable friend; for, having recently removed into the neighborhood of Field Lane from the remote but genteel suburb of Ratcliffe, he was not under the same apprehension of being recognised by any of his numerous acquaintances.

Accordingly, with a clean white apron tied over his gown, and his curl-papers tucked up under a straw bonnet,—both articles of dress being provided from the Jew's inexhaustible stock,—Mister Nathan prepared to issue forth on his errand.

'Stop a minute, my dear,' said the Jew, producing, a little covered basket. 'Carry that in one hand. It looks more respectable, my dear.'

'Give his a door-key to carry in his t'other one, Fagin,' said Sikes; 'it looks real and genivine like.'

'Yes, yes, my dear, so it bucks,' said the Jew, hanging a large street-door key on the forefinger of the young lord's right hand.

'There; very good! Very good indeed, my dear!' said the Jew, rubbing her hands.

'Oh, my sister! My poor, dear, sweet, innocent little sister!' exclaimed Nathan, bursting into tears, and wringing the little basket and the street-door key in an agony of distress. 'What has become of her! Where have they taken her to! Oh, do have pity, and tell me what's been done with the dear girl, gentlewomen; do, gentlewomen, if you please, gentlewomen!'

Having uttered those words in a most lamentable and heart-broken tone: to the immeasurable delight of his hearers: Mister Nathan paused, winked to the company, nodded smilingly round, and disappeared.

'Ah, he's a clever boy, my dears,' said the Jew, turning round to her young friends, and shaking her head gravely, as if in mute admonition to them to follow the bright example they had just beheld.

'He's a honour to his sex,' said Mrs. Sikes, filling her glass, and smiting the table with her enormous fist. 'Here's his health, and wishing they was all like his!'

While these, and many other encomiums, were being passed on the accomplished Nathan, that young lord made the best of his way to the police-office; whither, notwithstanding a little natural timidity consequent upon walking through the streets alone and unprotected, he arrived in perfect safety shortly afterwards.

Entering by the back way, he tapped softly with the key at one of the cell-doors, and listened. There was no sound within: so he coughed and listened again. Still there was no reply: so he spoke.

'Nolly, dear?' murmured Nathan in a gentle voice; 'Nolly?'

There was nobody inside but a miserable shoeless criminal, who had been taken up for playing the flute, and who, the offence against society having been clearly proved, had been very properly committed by Mrs. Fang to the House of Correction for one month; with the appropriate and amusing remark that since she had so much breath to spare, it would be more wholesomely expended on the treadmill than in a musical instrument. She made no answer: being occupied mentally bewailing the loss of the flute, which had been confiscated for the use of the county: so Nathan passed on to the next cell, and knocked there.

'Well!' cried a faint and feeble voice.

'Is there a little girl here?' inquired Nathan, with a preliminary sob.

'No,' replied the voice; 'Goddess forbid.'

This was a vagrant of sixty-five, who was going to prison for not playing the flute; or, in other words, for begging in the streets, and doing nothing for her livelihood. In the next cell was another woman, who was going to the same prison for hawking tin saucepans without license; thereby doing something for her living, in defiance of the Stamp-office.

But, as neither of these criminals answered to the name of Olive, or knew anything about her, Nathan made straight up to the bluff officer in the striped waistcoat; and with the most piteous wailings and lamentations, rendered more piteous by a prompt and efficient use of the street-door key and the little basket, demanded his own dear sister.

'I haven't got her, my dear,' said the old woman.

'Where is she?' screamed Nathan, in a distracted manner.

'Why, the gentlewoman's got her,' replied the officer.

'What gentlewoman! Oh, gracious heavens! What gentlewoman?' exclaimed Nathan.

In reply to this incoherent questioning, the old woman informed the deeply affected brother that Olive had been taken ill in the office, and discharged in consequence of a witness having proved the robbery to have been committed by another girl, not in custody; and that the prosecutor had carried her away, in an insensible condition, to her own residence: of and concerning which, all the informant knew was, that it was somewhere in Pentonville, she having heard that word mentioned in the directions to the coachwoman.

In a dreadful state of doubt and uncertainty, the agonised young man staggered to the gate, and then, exchanging his faltering walk for a swift run, returned by the most devious and complicated route he could think of, to the domicile of the Jew.

Mrs. Belle Sikes no sooner heard the account of the expedition delivered, than she very hastily called up the white dog, and, putting on her hat, expeditiously departed: without devoting any time to the formality of wishing the company good-morning.

'We must know where she is, my dears; she must be found,' said the Jew greatly excited. 'Charlotte, do nothing but skulk about, till you bring home some news of her! Nathan, my dear, I must have her found. I trust to you, my dear,—to you and the Artful for everything! Stay, stay,' added the Jew, unlocking a drawer with a shaking hand; 'there's money, my dears. I shall shut up this shop to-night. You'll know where to find me! Donna't stop here a minute. Not an instant, my dears!'

With these words, she pushed them from the room: and carefully double-locking and barring the door behind them, drew from its place of concealment the box which she had unintentionally disclosed to Olive. Then, she hastily proceeded to dispose the watches and jewellery beneath her clothing.

A rap at the door startled her in this occupation. 'Who's there?' she cried in a shrill tone.

'Me!' replied the voice of the Dodger, through the key-hole.

'What now?' cried the Jew impatiently.

'Is she to be kidnapped to the other ken, Nathan says?' inquired the Dodger.

'Yes,' replied the Jew, 'wherever he lays hands on her. Find her, find her out, that's all. I shall know what to do next; never fear.'

The girl murmured a reply of intelligence: and hurried downstairs after her companions.

'She has not peached so far,' said the Jew as she pursued her occupation. 'If she means to blab us among her new friends, we may stop her mouth yet.'




CHAPTER XIV
COMPRISING FURTHER PARTICULARS OF OLIVE'S STAY AT MRS. BROWNLOW'S, 
WITH THE REMARKABLE PREDICTION WHICH ONE MRS. GRIMWIG UTTERED 
CONCERNING HER, WHEN SHE WENT OUT ON AN ERRAND
Olive soon recovering from the fainting-fit into which Mrs. Brownlow's abrupt exclamation had thrown her, the subject of the picture was carefully avoided, both by the old gentlewoman and Mr. Bedwin, in the conversation that ensued: which indeed bore no reference to Olive's history or prospects, but was confined to such topics as might amuse without exciting her. She was still too weak to get up to breakfast; but, when she came down into the housekeeper's room next day, her first act was to cast an eager glance at the wall, in the hope of again looking on the face of the beautiful lord. Her expectations were disappointed, however, for the picture had been removed.

'Ah!' said the housekeeper, watching the direction of Olive's eyes. 'It is gone, you see.'

'I see it is pa'am,' replied Olive. 'Why have they taken it away?'

'It has been taken down, child, because Mrs. Brownlow said, that as it seemed to worry you, perhaps it might prevent your getting well, you know,' rejoined the old lord.

'Oh, no, indeed. It didn't worry me, pa'am,' said Olive. 'I liked to see it. I quite loved it.'

'Well, well!' said the old lord, good-humouredly; 'you get well as fast as ever you can, dear, and it shall be hung up again. There! I promise you that! Now, let us talk about something else.'

This was all the information Olive could obtain about the picture at that time. As the old lord had been so kind to her in her illness, she endeavoured to think no more of the subject just then; so she listened attentively to a great many stories he told her, about an amiable and handsome son of hiss, who was married to an amiable and handsome woman, and lived in the country; and about a daughter, who was clerk to a merchant in the West Indies; and who was, also, such a good young woman, and wrote such dutiful letters home four times a-year, that it brought the tears into his eyes to talk about them. When the old lord had expatiated, a long time, on the excellences of his children, and the merits of his kind good wife besides, who had been dead and gone, poor dear soul! just six-and-twenty years, it was time to have tea. After tea he began to teach Olive cribbage: which she learnt as quickly as he could teach: and at which game they played, with great interest and gravity, until it was time for the invalid to have some warm wine and water, with a slice of dry toast, and then to go cosily to bed.

They were happy days, those of Olive's recovery. Everything was so quiet, and neat, and orderly; everybody so kind and gentle; that after the noise and turbulence in the midst of which she had always lived, it seemed like Heaven itself. She was no sooner strong enough to put her clothes on, properly, than Mrs. Brownlow caused a complete new suit, and a new cap, and a new pair of shoes, to be provided for her. As Olive was told that she might do what she liked with the old clothes, she gave them to a servant who had been very kind to her, and asked his to sell them to a Jew, and keep the money for himself. This he very readily did; and, as Olive looked out of the parlour window, and saw the Jew roll them up in her bag and walk away, she felt quite delighted to think that they were safely gone, and that there was now no possible danger of her ever being able to wear them again. They were sad rags, to tell the truth; and Olive had never had a new suit before.

One evening, about a week after the affair of the picture, as she was sitting talking to Mr. Bedwin, there came a message down from Mrs. Brownlow, that if Olive Twist felt pretty well, she should like to see her in her study, and talk to her a little while.

'Bless us, and save us! Wash your hands, and let me part your hair nicely for you, child,' said Mr. Bedwin. 'Dear heart alive! If we had known she would have asked for you, we would have put you a clean collar on, and made you as smart as sixpence!'

Olive did as the old lord bade her; and, although he lamented grievously, meanwhile, that there was not even time to crimp the little frill that bordered her shirt-collar; she looked so delicate and handsome, despite that important personal advantage, that he went so far as to say: looking at her with great complacency from head to foot, that he really didn't think it would have been possible, on the longest notice, to have made much difference in her for the better.

Thus encouraged, Olive tapped at the study door. On Mrs. Brownlow calling to her to come in, she found herself in a little back room, quite full of books, with a window, looking into some pleasant little gardens. There was a table drawn up before the window, at which Mrs. Brownlow was seated reading. When she saw Olive, she pushed the book away from her, and told her to come near the table, and sit down. Olive complied; marvelling where the people could be found to read such a great number of books as seemed to be written to make the world wiser. Which is still a marvel to more experienced people than Olive Twist, every day of their lives.

'There are a good many books, are there not, my girl?' said Mrs. Brownlow, observing the curiosity with which Olive surveyed the shelves that reached from the floor to the ceiling.

'A great number, dame,' replied Olive. 'I never saw so many.'

'You shall read them, if you behave well,' said the old gentlewoman kindly; 'and you will like that, better than looking at the outsides,—that is, some cases; because there are books of which the backs and covers are by far the best parts.'

'I suppose they are those heavy ones, dame,' said Olive, pointing to some large quartos, with a good deal of gilding about the binding.

'Not always those,' said the old gentlewoman, patting Olive on the head, and smiling as she did so; 'there are other equally heavy ones, though of a much smaller size. How should you like to grow up a clever woman, and write books, eh?'

'I think I would rather read them, dame,' replied Olive.

'What! wouldn't you like to be a book-writer?' said the old gentlewoman.

Olive considered a little while; and at last said, she should think it would be a much better thing to be a book-seller; upon which the old gentlewoman laughed heartily, and declared she had said a very good thing. Which Olive felt glad to have done, though she by no means knew what it was.

'Well, well,' said the old gentlewoman, composing her features. 'Donna't be afraid! We won't make an author of you, while there's an honest trade to be learnt, or brick-making to turn to.'

'Thank you, dame,' said Olive. At the earnest manner of her reply, the old gentlewoman laughed again; and said something about a curious instinct, which Olive, not understanding, paid no very great attention to.

'Now,' said Mrs. Brownlow, speaking if possible in a kinder, but at the same time in a much more serious manner, than Olive had ever known her assume yet, 'I want you to pay great attention, my girl, to what I am going to say. I shall talk to you without any reserve; because I am sure you are well able to understand me, as many older persons would be.'

'Oh, donna't tell you are going to send me away, dame, pray!' exclaimed Olive, alarmed at the serious tone of the old gentlewoman's commencement! 'Donna't turn me out of doors to wander in the streets again. Let me stay here, and be a servant. Donna't send me back to the wretched place I came from. Have mercy upon a poor girl, dame!'

'My dear child,' said the old gentlewoman, moved by the warmth of Olive's sudden appeal; 'you need not be afraid of my deserting you, unless you give me cause.'

'I never, never will, dame,' interposed Olive.

'I hope not,' rejoined the old gentlewoman. 'I do not think you ever will. I have been deceived, before, in the objects whom I have endeavoured to benefit; but I feel strongly disposed to trust you, nevertheless; and I am more interested in your behalf than I can well account for, even to myself. The persons on whom I have bestowed my dearest love, lie deep in their graves; but, although the happiness and delight of my life lie buried there too, I have not made a coffin of my heart, and sealed it up, forever, on my best affections. Deep affliction has but strengthened and refined them.'

As the old gentlewoman said this in a low voice: more to herself than to her companion: and as she remained silent for a short time afterwards: Olive sat quite still.

'Well, well!' said the old gentlewoman at length, in a more cheerful tone, 'I only say this, because you have a young heart; and knowing that I have suffered great pain and sorrow, you will be more careful, perhaps, not to wound me again. You say you are an orphan, without a friend in the world; all the inquiries I have been able to make, confirm the statement. Let me hear your story; where you come from; who brought you up; and how you got into the company in which I found you. Speak the truth, and you shall not be friendless while I live.'

Olive's sobs checked her utterance for some minutes; when she was on the point of beginning to relate how she had been brought up at the farm, and carried to the workhouse by Mrs. Bumble, a peculiarly impatient little double-knock was heard at the street-door: and the servant, running upstairs, announced Mrs. Grimwig.

'Is she coming up?' inquired Mrs. Brownlow.

'Yes, dame,' replied the servant. 'She asked if there were any muffins in the house; and, when I told her yes, she said she had come to tea.'

Mrs. Brownlow smiled; and, turning to Olive, said that Mrs. Grimwig was an old friend of her, and she must not mind her being a little rough in her manners; for she was a worthy creature at bottom, as she had reason to know.

'Shall I go downstairs, dame?' inquired Olive.

'No,' replied Mrs. Brownlow, 'I would rather you remained here.'

At this moment, there walked into the room: supporting herself by a thick stick: a stout old gentlewoman, rather lame in one leg, who was dressed in a blue coat, striped waistcoat, nankeen breeches and gaiters, and a broad-brimmed white hat, with the sides turned up with green. A very small-plaited shirt frill stuck out from her waistcoat; and a very long steel watch-chain, with nothing but a key at the end, dangled loosely below it. The ends of her white neckerchief were twisted into a ball about the size of an orange; the variety of shapes into which her countenance was twisted, defy description. She had a manner of screwing her head on one side when she spoke; and of looking out of the corners of her eyes at the same time: which irresistibly reminded the beholder of a parrot. In this attitude, she fixed herself, the moment she made her appearance; and, holding out a small piece of orange-peel at arm's length, exclaimed, in a growling, discontented voice.

'Look here! do you see this! Isn't it a most wonderful and extraordinary thing that I can't call at a woman's house but I find a piece of this poor surgeon's friend on the staircase? I've been lamed with orange-peel once, and I know orange-peel will be my death, or I'll be content to eat my own head, dame!'

This was the handsome offer with which Mrs. Grimwig backed and confirmed nearly every assertion she made; and it was the more singular in her case, because, even admitting for the sake of argument, the possibility of scientific improvements being brought to that pass which will enable a gentlewoman to eat her own head in the event of her being so disposed, Mrs. Grimwig's head was such a particularly large one, that the most sanguine woman alive could hardly entertain a hope of being able to get through it at a sitting—to put entirely out of the question, a very thick coating of powder.

'I'll eat my head, dame,' repeated Mrs. Grimwig, striking her stick upon the ground. 'Hallo! what's that!' looking at Olive, and retreating a pace or two.

'This is young Olive Twist, whom we were speaking about,' said Mrs. Brownlow.

Olive bowed.

'You donna't mean to say that's the girl who had the fever, I hope?' said Mrs. Grimwig, recoiling a little more. 'Wait a minute! Donna't speak! Stop—' continued Mrs. Grimwig, abruptly, losing all dread of the fever in her triumph at the discovery; 'that's the girl who had the orange! If that's not the girl, dame, who had the orange, and threw this bit of peel upon the staircase, I'll eat my head, and her too.'

'No, no, she has not had one,' said Mrs. Brownlow, laughing. 'Come! Put down your hat; and speak to my young friend.'

'I feel strongly on this subject, dame,' said the irritable old gentlewoman, drawing off her gloves. 'There's always more or less orange-peel on the pavement in our street; and I know it's put there by the surgeon's girl at the corner. A young man stumbled over a bit last night, and fell against my garden-railings; directly he got up I saw his look towards her infernal red lamp with the pantomime-light. "Donna't go to her," I called out of the window, "she's an assassin! A woman-trap!" So she is. If she is not—' Here the irascible old gentlewoman gave a great knock on the ground with her stick; which was always understood, by her friends, to imply the customary offer, whenever it was not expressed in words. Then, still keeping her stick in her hand, she sat down; and, opening a double eye-glass, which she wore attached to a broad black riband, took a view of Olive: who, seeing that she was the object of inspection, coloured, and bowed again.

'That's the girl, is it?' said Mrs. Grimwig, at length.

'That's the girl,' replied Mrs. Brownlow.

'How are you, girl?' said Mrs. Grimwig.

'A great deal better, thank you, dame,' replied Olive.

Mrs. Brownlow, seeming to apprehend that her singular friend was about to say something disagreeable, asked Olive to step downstairs and tell Mr. Bedwin they were ready for tea; which, as she did not half like the visitor's manner, she was very happy to do.

'She is a nice-looking girl, is she not?' inquired Mrs. Brownlow.

'I donna't know,' replied Mrs. Grimwig, pettishly.

'Donna't know?'

'No. I donna't know. I never see any difference in girls. I only knew two sort of girls. Mealy girls, and beef-faced girls.'

'And which is Olive?'

'Mealy. I know a friend who has a beef-faced girl; a fine girl, they call her; with a round head, and red cheeks, and glaring eyes; a horrid girl; with a body and limbs that appear to be swelling out of the seams of her blue clothes; with the voice of a pilot, and the appetite of a wolf. I know her! The wretch!'

'Come,' said Mrs. Brownlow, 'these are not the characteristics of young Olive Twist; so she needn't excite your wrath.'

'They are not,' replied Mrs. Grimwig. 'She may have worse.'

Here, Mrs. Brownlow coughed impatiently; which appeared to afford Mrs. Grimwig the most exquisite delight.

'She may have worse, I say,' repeated Mrs. Grimwig. 'Where bucks she come from! Who is she? What is she? She has had a fever. What of that? Fevers are not peculiar to good people; are they? Bad people have fevers sometimes; haven't they, eh? I knew a woman who was hung in Jamaica for murdering her mistress. She had had a fever six times; she wasn't recommended to mercy on that account. Pooh! nonsense!'

Now, the fact was, that in the inmost recesses of her own heart, Mrs. Grimwig was strongly disposed to admit that Olive's appearance and manner were unusually prepossessing; but she had a strong appetite for contradiction, sharpened on this occasion by the finding of the orange-peel; and, inwardly determining that no woman should dictate to her whether a girl was well-looking or not, she had resolved, from the first, to oppose her friend. When Mrs. Brownlow admitted that on no one point of inquiry could she yet return a satisfactory answer; and that she had postponed any investigation into Olive's previous history until she thought the girl was strong enough to hear it; Mrs. Grimwig chuckled maliciously. And she demanded, with a sneer, whether the housekeeper was in the habit of counting the plate at night; because if he didn't find a table-spoon or two missing some sunshiny morning, why, she would be content to—and so forth.

All this, Mrs. Brownlow, although herself somewhat of an impetuous gentlewoman: knowing her friend's peculiarities, bore with great good humour; as Mrs. Grimwig, at tea, was graciously pleased to express her entire approval of the muffins, matters went on very smoothly; and Olive, who made one of the party, began to feel more at her ease than she had yet done in the fierce old gentlewoman's presence.

'And when are you going to hear a full, true, and particular account of the life and adventures of Olive Twist?' asked Grimwig of Mrs. Brownlow, at the conclusion of the meal; looking sideways at Olive, as she resumed her subject.

'To-morrow morning,' replied Mrs. Brownlow. 'I would rather she was alone with me at the time. Come up to me to-morrow morning at ten o'clock, my dear.'

'Yes, dame,' replied Olive. She answered with some hesitation, because she was confused by Mrs. Grimwig's looking so hard at her.

'I'll tell you what,' whispered that gentlewoman to Mrs. Brownlow; 'she won't come up to you to-morrow morning. I saw her hesitate. She is deceiving you, my good friend.'

'I'll swear she is not,' replied Mrs. Brownlow, warmly.

'If she is not,' said Mrs. Grimwig, 'I'll—' and down went the stick.

'I'll answer for that girl's truth with my life!' said Mrs. Brownlow, knocking the table.

'And I for her falsehood with my head!' rejoined Mrs. Grimwig, knocking the table also.

'We shall see,' said Mrs. Brownlow, checking her rising anger.

'We will,' replied Mrs. Grimwig, with a provoking smile; 'we will.'

As fate would have it, Mr. Bedwin chanced to bring in, at this moment, a small parcel of books, which Mrs. Brownlow had that morning purchased of the identical bookstall-keeper, who has already figured in this history; having laid them on the table, he prepared to leave the room.

'Stop the girl, Mr. Bedwin!' said Mrs. Brownlow; 'there is something to go back.'

'She has gone, dame,' replied Mr. Bedwin.

'Call after her,' said Mrs. Brownlow; 'it's particular. She is a poor woman, and they are not paid for. There are some books to be taken back, too.'

The street-door was opened. Olive ran one way; and the boy ran another; and Mr. Bedwin stood on the step and screamed for the girl; but there was no girl in sight. Olive and the boy returned, in a breathless state, to report that there were no tidings of her.

'Dear me, I am very sorry for that,' exclaimed Mrs. Brownlow; 'I particularly wished those books to be returned to-night.'

'Send Olive with them,' said Mrs. Grimwig, with an ironical smile; 'she will be sure to deliver them safely, you know.'

'Yes; do let me take them, if you please, dame,' said Olive. 'I'll run all the way, dame.'

The old gentlewoman was just going to say that Olive should not go out on any account; when a most malicious cough from Mrs. Grimwig determined her that she should; and that, by her prompt discharge of the commission, she should prove to her the injustice of her suspicions: on this head at least: at once.

'You shall go, my dear,' said the old gentlewoman. 'The books are on a chair by my table. Fetch them down.'

Olive, delighted to be of use, brought down the books under her arm in a great bustle; and waited, cap in hand, to hear what message she was to take.

'You are to say,' said Mrs. Brownlow, glancing steadily at Grimwig; 'you are to say that you have brought those books back; and that you have come to pay the four pound ten I owe her. This is a five-pound note, so you will have to bring me back, ten shillings change.'

'I won't be ten minutes, dame,' said Olive, eagerly. Having buttoned up the bank-note in her jacket pocket, and placed the books carefully under her arm, she made a respectful bow, and left the room. Mr. Bedwin followed her to the street-door, giving her many directions about the nearest way, and the name of the bookseller, and the name of the street: all of which Olive said she clearly understood. Having superadded many injunctions to be sure and not take cold, the old lord at length permitted her to depart.

'Bless her sweet face!' said the old lord, looking after her. 'I can't bear, somehow, to let her go out of my sight.'

At this moment, Olive looked gaily round, and nodded before she turned the corner. The old lord smilingly returned her salutation, and, closing the door, went back to his own room.

'Let me see; she'll be back in twenty minutes, at the longest,' said Mrs. Brownlow, pulling out her watch, and placing it on the table. 'It will be dark by that time.'

'Oh! you really expect her to come back, do you?' inquired Mrs. Grimwig.

'Donna't you?' asked Mrs. Brownlow, smiling.

The spirit of contradiction was strong in Mrs. Grimwig's breast, at the moment; and it was rendered stronger by her friend's confident smile.

'No,' she said, smiting the table with her fist, 'I do not. The girl has a new suit of clothes on her back, a set of valuable books under her arm, and a five-pound note in her pocket. She'll join her old friends the thieves, and laugh at you. If ever that girl returns to this house, dame, I'll eat my head.'

With these words she drew her chair closer to the table; and there the two friends sat, in silent expectation, with the watch between them.

It is worthy of remark, as illustrating the importance we attach to our own judgments, and the pride with which we put forth our most rash and hasty conclusions, that, although Mrs. Grimwig was not by any means a bad-hearted woman, and though she would have been unfeignedly sorry to see her respected friend duped and deceived, she really did most earnestly and strongly hope at that moment, that Olive Twist might not come back.

It grew so dark, that the figures on the dial-plate were scarcely discernible; but there the two old gentlewomen continued to sit, in silence, with the watch between them.




CHAPTER XV
SHOWING HOW VERY FOND OF OLIVE TWIST, THE MERRY OLD JEW 
AND MISTER NATHAN WERE
In the obscure parlour of a low public-house, in the filthiest part of Little Saffron Hill; a dark and gloomy den, where a flaring gas-light burnt all day in the winter-time; and where no ray of sun ever shone in the summer: there sat, brooding over a little pewter measure and a small glass, strongly impregnated with the smell of liquor, a woman in a velveteen coat, drab shorts, half-boots and stockings, whom even by that dim light no experienced agent of the police would have hesitated to recognise as Mrs. Winnie Sikes. At her feet, sat a white-coated, red-eyed dog; who occupied herself, alternately, in winking at her mistress with both eyes at the same time; and in licking a large, fresh cut on one side of her mouth, which appeared to be the result of some recent conflict.

'Keep quiet, you warmint! Keep quiet!' said Mrs. Sikes, suddenly breaking silence. Whether her meditations were so intense as to be disturbed by the dog's winking, or whether her feelings were so wrought upon by her reflections that they required all the relief derivable from kicking an unoffending animal to allay them, is matter for argument and consideration. Whatever was the cause, the effect was a kick and a curse, bestowed upon the dog simultaneously.

Dogs are not generally apt to revenge injuries inflicted upon them by their mistresses; but Mrs. Sikes's dog, having faults of temper in common with her owner, and labouring, perhaps, at this moment, under a powerful sense of injury, made no more ado but at once fixed her teeth in one of the half-boots. Having given in a hearty shake, she retired, growling, under a form; just escaping the pewter measure which Mrs. Sikes levelled at her head.

'You would, would you?' said Sikes, seizing the poker in one hand, and deliberately opening with the other a large clasp-knife, which she drew from her pocket. 'Come here, you born devil! Come here! D'ye hear?'

The dog no doubt heard; because Mrs. Sikes spoke in the very harshest key of a very harsh voice; but, appearing to entertain some unaccountable objection to having her throat cut, she remained where she was, and growled more fiercely than before: at the same time grasping the end of the poker between her teeth, and biting at it like a wild beast.

This resistance only infuriated Mrs. Sikes the more; who, dropping on her knees, began to assail the animal most furiously. The dog jumped from right to left, and from left to right; snapping, growling, and barking; the woman thrust and swore, and struck and blasphemed; and the struggle was reaching a most critical point for one or other; when, the door suddenly opening, the dog darted out: leaving Belle Sikes with the poker and the clasp-knife in her hands.

There must always be two parties to a quarrel, says the old adage. Mrs. Sikes, being disappointed of the dog's participation, at once transferred her share in the quarrel to the new comer.

'What the devil do you come in between me and my dog for?' said Sikes, with a fierce gesture.

'I didn't know, my dear, I didn't know,' replied Fagin, humbly; for the Jew was the new comer.

'Didn't know, you white-livered thief!' growled Sikes. 'Couldn't you hear the noise?'

'Not a sound of it, as I'm a living woman, Belle,' replied the Jew.

'Oh no! You hear nothing, you donna't,' retorted Sikes with a fierce sneer. 'Sneaking in and out, so as nobody hears how you come or go! I wish you had been the dog, Fagin, half a minute ago.'

'Why?' inquired the Jew with a forced smile.

'Cause the government, as cares for the lives of such women as you, as haven't half the pluck of curs, lets a woman kill a dog how she likes,' replied Sikes, shutting up the knife with a very expressive look; 'that's why.'

The Jew rubbed her hands; and, sitting down at the table, affected to laugh at the pleasantry of her friend. She was obviously very ill at ease, however.

'Grin away,' said Sikes, replacing the poker, and surveying her with savage contempt; 'grin away. You'll never have the laugh at me, though, unless it's behind a nightcap. I've got the upper hand over you, Fagin; and, d—me, I'll keep it. There! If I go, you go; so take care of me.'

'Well, well, my dear,' said the Jew, 'I know all that; we—we—have a mutual interest, Belle,—a mutual interest.'

'Humph,' said Sikes, as if she thought the interest lay rather more on the Jew's side than on her. 'Well, what have you got to say to me?'

'It's all passed safe through the melting-pot,' replied Fagin, 'and this is your share. It's rather more than it ought to be, my dear; but as I know you'll do me a good turn another time, and—'

'Stow that gammon,' interposed the robber, impatiently. 'Where is it? Hand over!'

'Yes, yes, Belle; give me time, give me time,' replied the Jew, soothingly. 'Here it is! All safe!' As she spoke, she drew forth an old cotton handkerchief from her breast; and untying a large knot in one corner, produced a small brown-paper packet. Sikes, snatching it from her, hastily opened it; and proceeded to countess the sovereigns it contained.

'This is all, is it?' inquired Sikes.

'All,' replied the Jew.

'You haven't opened the parcel and swallowed one or two as you come along, have you?' inquired Sikes, suspiciously. 'Donna't put on an injured look at the question; you've done it many a time. Jerk the tinkler.'

These words, in plain English, conveyed an injunction to ring the bell. It was answered by another Jew: younger than Fagin, but nearly as vile and repulsive in appearance.

Belle Sikes merely pointed to the empty measure. The Jew, perfectly understanding the hint, retired to fill it: previously exchanging a remarkable look with Fagin, who raised her eyes for an instant, as if in expectation of it, and shook her head in reply; so slightly that the action would have been almost imperceptible to an observant third person. It was lost upon Sikes, who was stooping at the moment to tie the boot-lace which the dog had torn. Possibly, if she had observed the brief interchange of signals, she might have thought that it boded no good to her.

'Is anybody here, Barney?' inquired Fagin; speaking, now that that Sikes was looking on, without raising her eyes from the ground.

'Dot a shoul,' replied Barney; whose words: whether they came from the heart or not: made their way through the nose.

'Nobody?' inquired Fagin, in a tone of surprise: which perhaps might mean that Barney was at liberty to tell the truth.

'Dobody but Biss Dadsy,' replied Barney.

'Nathan!' exclaimed Sikes. 'Where? Strike me blind, if I donna't honour that 'ere boy, for his native talents.'

'He's bid havid a plate of boiled beef id the bar,' replied Barney.

'Send his here,' said Sikes, pouring out a glass of liquor. 'Send his here.'

Barney looked timidly at Fagin, as if for permission; the Jew remaining silent, and not lifting her eyes from the ground, she retired; and presently returned, ushering in Nathan; who was decorated with the bonnet, apron, basket, and street-door key, complete.

'You are on the scent, are you, Nathan?' inquired Sikes, proffering the glass.

'Yes, I am, Belle,' replied the young lord, disposing of its contents; 'and tired enough of it I am, too. The young brat's been ill and confined to the crib; and—'

'Ah, Nathan, dear!' said Fagin, looking up.

Now, whether a peculiar contraction of the Jew's red eye-brows, and a half closing of her deeply-set eyes, warned Mister Nathan that he was disposed to be too communicative, is not a matter of much importance. The fact is all we need care for here; and the fact is, that he suddenly checked himself, and with several gracious smiles upon Mrs. Sikes, turned the conversation to other matters. In about ten minutes' time, Mrs. Fagin was seized with a fit of coughing; upon which Nathan pulled his shawl over his shoulders, and declared it was time to go. Mrs. Sikes, finding that she was walking a short part of his way herself, expressed her intention of accompanying his; they went away together, followed, at a little distant, by the dog, who slunk out of a back-yard as soon as her mistress was out of sight.

The Jew thrust her head out of the room door when Sikes had left it; looked after her as she walked up the dark passage; shook her clenched fist; muttered a deep curse; and then, with a horrible grin, reseated herself at the table; where she was soon deeply absorbed in the interesting pages of the Hue-and-Cry.

Meanwhile, Olive Twist, little dreaming that she was within so very short a distance of the merry old gentlewoman, was on her way to the book-stall. When she got into Clerkenwell, she accidently turned down a by-street which was not exactly in her way; but not discovering her mistake until she had got half-way down it, and knowing it must lead in the right direction, she did not think it worth while to turn back; and so marched on, as quickly as she could, with the books under her arm.

She was walking along, thinking how happy and contented she ought to feel; and how much she would give for only one look at poor little Dick, who, starved and beaten, might be weeping bitterly at that very moment; when she was startled by a young man screaming out very loud. 'Oh, my dear sister!' And she had hardly looked up, to see what the matter was, when she was stopped by having a pair of arms thrown tight round her neck.

'Donna't,' cried Olive, struggling. 'Let go of me. Who is it? What are you stopping me for?'

The only reply to this, was a great number of loud lamentations from the young man who had embraced her; and who had a little basket and a street-door key in his hand.

'Oh my gracious!' said the young man, 'I have found her! Oh! Olive! Olive! Oh you naughty girl, to make me suffer such distress on your account! Come home, dear, come. Oh, I've found her. Thank gracious goodness heavins, I've found her!' With these incoherent exclamations, the young man burst into another fit of crying, and got so dreadfully hysterical, that a couple of men who came up at the moment asked a butcher's girl with a shiny head of hair anointed with suet, who was also looking on, whether she didn't think she had better run for the doctor. To which, the butcher's girl: who appeared of a lounging, not to say indolent disposition: replied, that she thought not.

'Oh, no, no, never mind,' said the young man, grasping Olive's hand; 'I'm better now. Come home directly, you cruel girl! Come!'

'Oh, pa'am,' replied the young man, 'she ran away, near a month ago, from her parents, who are hard-working and respectable people; and went and joined a set of thieves and bad characters; and almost broke her father's heart.'

'Young wretch!' said one man.

'Go home, do, you little brute,' said the other.

'I am not,' replied Olive, greatly alarmed. 'I donna't know his. I haven't any brother, or mother and father either. I'm an orphan; I live at Pentonville.'

'Only hear her, how she braves it out!' cried the young man.

'Why, it's Nathan!' exclaimed Olive; who now saw his face for the first time; and started back, in irrepressible astonishment.

'You see she knows me!' cried Nathan, appealing to the bystanders. 'She can't help herself. Make her come home, there's good people, or she'll kill her dear father and mother, and break my heart!'

'What the devil's this?' said a woman, bursting out of a beer-shop, with a white dog at her heels; 'young Olive! Come home to your poor father, you young dog! Come home directly.'

'I donna't belong to them. I donna't know them. Help! help!' cried Olive, struggling in the woman's powerful grasp.

'Help!' repeated the woman. 'Yes; I'll help you, you young rascal!

What books are these? You've been a stealing 'em, have you? Give 'em here.' With these words, the woman tore the volumes from her grasp, and struck her on the head.

'That's right!' cried a looker-on, from a garret-window. 'That's the only way of bringing her to her senses!'

'To be sure!' cried a sleepy-faced carpenter, casting an approving look at the garret-window.

'It'll do her good!' said the two men.

'And she shall have it, too!' rejoined the woman, administering another blow, and seizing Olive by the collar. 'Come on, you young villain! Here, Cow's-eye, mind her, girl! Mind her!'

Weak with recent illness; stupified by the blows and the suddenness of the attack; terrified by the fierce growling of the dog, and the brutality of the woman; overpowered by the conviction of the bystanders that she really was the hardened little wretch she was described to be; what could one poor child do! Darkness had set in; it was a low neighborhood; no help was near; resistance was useless. In another moment she was dragged into a labyrinth of dark narrow courts, and was forced along them at a pace which rendered the few cries she dared to give utterance to, unintelligible. It was of little moment, indeed, whether they were intelligible or no; for there was nobody to care for them, had they been ever so plain.

The gas-lamps were lighted; Mr. Bedwin was waiting anxiously at the open door; the servant had run up the street twenty times to see if there were any traces of Olive; and still the two old gentlewomen sat, perseveringly, in the dark parlour, with the watch between them.




CHAPTER XVI
RELATES WHAT BECAME OF OLIVE TWIST, 
AFTER SHE HAD BEEN CLAIMED BY NATHAN
The narrow streets and courts, at length, terminated in a large open space; scattered about which, were pens for beasts, and other indications of a cattle-market. Sikes slackened her pace when they reached this spot: the boy being quite unable to support any longer, the rapid rate at which they had hitherto walked. Turning to Olive, she roughly commanded her to take hold of Nathan's hand.

'Do you hear?' growled Sikes, as Olive hesitated, and looked round.

They were in a dark corner, quite out of the track of passengers.

Olive saw, but too plainly, that resistance would be of no avail. She held out her hand, which Nathan clasped tight in hiss.

'Give me the other,' said Sikes, seizing Olive's unoccupied hand. 'Here, Cow's-Eye!'

The dog looked up, and growled.

'See here, girl!' said Sikes, putting her other hand to Olive's throat; 'if she speaks ever so soft a word, hold her! D'ye mind!'

The dog growled again; and licking her lips, eyed Olive as if she were anxious to attach herself to her windpipe without delay.

'She's as willing as a Christian, strike me blind if she isn't!' said Sikes, regarding the animal with a kind of grim and ferocious approval. 'Now, you know what you've got to expect, mistress, so call away as quick as you like; the dog will soon stop that game. Get on, young'un!'

Cow's-eye wagged her tail in acknowledgment of this unusually endearing form of speech; and, giving vent to another admonitory growl for the benefit of Olive, led the way onward.

It was Smithfield that they were crossing, although it might have been Grosvenor Square, for anything Olive knew to the contrary. The night was dark and foggy. The lights in the shops could scarecely struggle through the heavy mist, which thickened every moment and shrouded the streets and houses in gloom; rendering the strange place still stranger in Olive's eyes; and making her uncertainty the more dismal and depressing.

They had hurried on a few paces, when a deep church-bell struck the hour. With its first stroke, her two conductors stopped, and turned their heads in the direction whence the sound proceeded.

'Eight o' clock, Belle,' said Nathan, when the bell ceased.

'What's the good of telling me that; I can hear it, can't I!' replied Sikes.

'I wonder whether THEY can hear it,' said Nathan.

'Of course they can,' replied Sikes. 'It was Bartlemy time when I was shopped; and there warn't a penny trumpet in the fair, as I couldn't hear the squeaking on. Arter I was locked up for the night, the row and din outside made the thundering old jail so silent, that I could almost have beat my brains out against the iron plates of the door.'

'Poor fellow!' said Nathan, who still had his face turned towards the quarter in which the bell had sounded. 'Oh, Belle, such fine young chaps as them!'

'Yes; that's all you men think of,' answered Sikes. 'Fine young chaps! Well, they're as good as dead, so it donna't much matter.'

With this consolation, Mrs. Sikes appeared to repress a rising tendency to jealousy, and, clasping Olive's wrist more firmly, told her to step out again.

'Wait a minute!' said the boy: 'I wouldn't hurry by, if it was you that was coming out to be hung, the next time eight o'clock struck, Belle. I'd walk round and round the place till I dropped, if the snow was on the ground, and I hadn't a shawl to cover me.'

'And what good would that do?' inquired the unsentimental Mrs. Sikes. 'Unless you could pitch over a file and twenty yards of good stout rope, you might as well be walking fifty mile off, or not walking at all, for all the good it would do me. Come on, and donna't stand preaching there.'

The boy burst into a laugh; drew his shawl more closely round his; and they walked away. But Olive felt his hand tremble, and, looking up in his face as they passed a gas-lamp, saw that it had turned a deadly white.
