They walked on, by little-frequented and dirty ways, for a full half-hour: meeting very few people, and those appearing from their looks to hold much the same position in society as Mrs. Sikes herself. At length they turned into a very filthy narrow street, nearly full of old-clothes shops; the dog running forward, as if conscious that there was no further occasion for her keeping on guard, stopped before the door of a shop that was closed and apparently untenanted; the house was in a ruinous condition, and on the door was nailed a board, intimating that it was to let: which looked as if it had hung there for many years.

'All right,' cried Sikes, glancing cautiously about.

Nathan stooped below the shutters, and Olive heard the sound of a bell. They crossed to the opposite side of the street, and stood for a few moments under a lamp. A noise, as if a sash window were gently raised, was heard; and soon afterwards the door softly opened. Mrs. Sikes then seized the terrified girl by the collar with very little ceremony; and all three were quickly inside the house.

The passage was perfectly dark. They waited, while the person who had let them in, chained and barred the door.

'Anybody here?' inquired Sikes.

'No,' replied a voice, which Olive thought she had heard before.

'Is the old 'un here?' asked the robber.

'Yes,' replied the voice, 'and precious down in the mouth she has been. Won't she be glad to see you? Oh, no!'

The style of this reply, as well as the voice which delivered it, seemed familiar to Olive's ears: but it was impossible to distinguish even the form of the speaker in the darkness.

'Let's have a glim,' said Sikes, 'or we shall go breaking our necks, or treading on the dog. Look after your legs if you do!'

'Stand still a moment, and I'll get you one,' replied the voice. The receding footsteps of the speaker were heard; and, in another minute, the form of Mrs. Julia Dawkins, otherwise the Artful Dodger, appeared. She bore in her right hand a tallow candle stuck in the end of a cleft stick.

The young gentlewoman did not stop to bestow any other mark of recognition upon Olive than a humourous grin; but, turning away, beckoned the visitors to follow her down a flight of stairs. They crossed an empty kitchen; and, opening the door of a low earthy-smelling room, which seemed to have been built in a small back-yard, were received with a shout of laughter.

'Oh, my wig, my wig!' cried Mistress Charlotte Bates, from whose lungs the laughter had proceeded: 'here she is! oh, cry, here she is! Oh, Fagin, look at her! Fagin, do look at her! I can't bear it; it is such a jolly game, I cant' bear it. Hold me, somebody, while I laugh it out.'

With this irrepressible ebullition of mirth, Mistress Bates laid herself flat on the floor: and kicked convulsively for five minutes, in an ectasy of facetious joy. Then jumping to her feet, she snatched the cleft stick from the Dodger; and, advancing to Olive, viewed her round and round; while the Jew, taking off her nightcap, made a great number of low bows to the bewildered girl. The Artful, meantime, who was of a rather saturnine disposition, and seldom gave way to merriment when it interfered with business, rifled Olive's pockets with steady assiduity.

'Look at her togs, Fagin!' said Charlotte, putting the light so close to her new jacket as nearly to set her on fire. 'Look at her togs! Superfine cloth, and the heavy swell cut! Oh, my eye, what a game! And her books, too! Nothing but a gentlewoman, Fagin!'

'Delighted to see you looking so well, my dear,' said the Jew, bowing with mock humility. 'The Artful shall give you another suit, my dear, for fear you should spoil that Sunday one. Why didn't you write, my dear, and say you were coming? We'd have got something warm for supper.'

At her, Mistress Bates roared again: so loud, that Fagin herself relaxed, and even the Dodger smiled; but as the Artful drew forth the five-pound note at that instant, it is doubtful whether the sally of the discovery awakened her merriment.

'Hallo, what's that?' inquired Sikes, stepping forward as the Jew seized the note. 'That's mine, Fagin.'

'No, no, my dear,' said the Jew. 'Mine, Belle, mine. You shall have the books.'

'If that ain't mine!' said Belle Sikes, putting on her hat with a determined air; 'mine and Nathan's that is; I'll take the girl back again.'

The Jew started. Olive started too, though from a very different cause; for she hoped that the dispute might really end in her being taken back.

'Come! Hand over, will you?' said Sikes.

'This is hardly fair, Belle; hardly fair, is it, Nathan?' inquired the Jew.

'Fair, or not fair,' retorted Sikes, 'hand over, I tell you! Do you think Nathan and me has got nothing else to do with our precious time but to spend it in scouting arter, and kidnapping, every young girl as gets grabbed through you? Give it here, you avaricious old skeleton, give it here!'

With this gentle remonstrance, Mrs. Sikes plucked the note from between the Jew's finger and thumb; and looking the old woman coolly in the face, folded it up small, and tied it in her neckerchief.

'That's for our share of the trouble,' said Sikes; 'and not half enough, neither. You may keep the books, if you're fond of reading. If you ain't, sell 'em.'

'They're very pretty,' said Charlotte Bates: who, with sundry grimaces, had been affecting to read one of the volumes in question; 'beautiful writing, isn't is, Olive?' At sight of the dismayed look with which Olive regarded her tormentors, Mistress Bates, who was blessed with a lively sense of the ludicrous, fell into another ectasy, more boisterous than the first.

'They belong to the old gentlewoman,' said Olive, wringing her hands; 'to the good, kind, old gentlewoman who took me into her house, and had me nursed, when I was near dying of the fever. Oh, pray send them back; send her back the books and money. Keep me here all my life long; but pray, pray send them back. She'll think I stole them; the old lord: all of them who were so kind to me: will think I stole them. Oh, do have mercy upon me, and send them back!'

With these words, which were uttered with all the energy of passionate grief, Olive fell upon her knees at the Jew's feet; and beat her hands together, in perfect desperation.

'The girl's right,' remarked Fagin, looking covertly round, and knitting her shaggy eyebrows into a hard knot. 'You're right, Olive, you're right; they WINNIE think you have stolen 'em. Ha! ha!' chuckled the Jew, rubbing her hands, 'it couldn't have happened better, if we had chosen our time!'

'Of course it couldn't,' replied Sikes; 'I know'd that, directly I see her coming through Clerkenwell, with the books under her arm. It's all right enough. They're soft-hearted psalm-singers, or they wouldn't have taken her in at all; and they'll ask no questions after her, fear they should be obliged to prosecute, and so get her lagged. She's safe enough.'

Olive had looked from one to the other, while these words were being spoken, as if she were bewildered, and could scarecely understand what passed; but when Belle Sikes concluded, she jumped suddenly to her feet, and tore wildly from the room: uttering shrieks for help, which made the bare old house echo to the roof.

'Keep back the dog, Belle!' cried Nathan, springing before the door, and closing it, as the Jew and her two pupils darted out in pursuit. 'Keep back the dog; she'll tear the girl to pieces.'

'Serve her right!' cried Sikes, struggling to disengage herself from the boy's grasp. 'Stand off from me, or I'll split your head against the wall.'

'I donna't care for that, Belle, I donna't care for that,' screamed the boy, struggling violently with the woman, 'the child shan't be torn down by the dog, unless you kill me first.'

'Shan't she!' said Sikes, setting her teeth. 'I'll soon do that, if you donna't keep off.'

The housebreaker flung the boy from her to the further end of the room, just as the Jew and the two girls returned, dragging Olive among them.

'What's the matter here!' said Fagin, looking round.

'The boy's gone mad, I think,' replied Sikes, savagely.

'No, he hasn't,' said Nathan, pale and breathless from the scuffle; 'no, he hasn't, Fagin; donna't think it.'

'Then keep quiet, will you?' said the Jew, with a threatening look.

'No, I won't do that, neither,' replied Nathan, speaking very loud. 'Come! What do you think of that?'

Mrs. Fagin was sufficiently well acquainted with the manners and customs of that particular species of humanity to which Nathan belonged, to feel tolerably certain that it would be rather unsafe to prolong any conversation with his, at present. With the view of diverting the attention of the company, she turned to Olive.

'So you wanted to get away, my dear, did you?' said the Jew, taking up a jagged and knotted club which law in a corner of the fireplace; 'eh?'

Olive made no reply. But she watched the Jew's motions, and breathed quickly.

'Wanted to get assistance; called for the police; did you?' sneered the Jew, catching the girl by the arm. 'We'll cure you of that, my young mistress.'

The Jew inflicted a smart blow on Olive's shoulders with the club; and was raising it for a second, when the boy, rushing forward, wrested it from her hand. He flung it into the fire, with a force that brought some of the glowing coals whirling out into the room.

'I won't stand by and see it done, Fagin,' cried the boy. 'You've got the girl, and what more would you have?—Let her be—let her be—or I shall put that mark on some of you, that will bring me to the gallows before my time.'

The boy stamped his foot violently on the floor as he vented this threat; and with his lips compressed, and his hands clenched, looked alternately at the Jew and the other robber: his face quite colourless from the passion of rage into which he had gradually worked himself.

'Why, Nathan!' said the Jew, in a soothing tone; after a pause, during which she and Mrs. Sikes had stared at one another in a disconcerted manner; 'you,—you're more clever than ever to-night. Ha! ha! my dear, you are acting beautifully.'

'Am I!' said the boy. 'Take care I donna't overdo it. You will be the worse for it, Fagin, if I do; and so I tell you in good time to keep clear of me.'

There is something about a roused man: especially if he add to all his other strong passions, the fierce impulses of recklessness and despair; which few women like to provoke. The Jew saw that it would be hopeless to affect any further mistake regarding the reality of Mister Nathan's rage; and, shrinking involuntarily back a few paces, cast a glance, half imploring and half cowardly, at Sikes: as if to hint that she was the fittest person to pursue the dialogue.

Mrs. Sikes, thus mutely appealed to; and possibly feeling her personal pride and influence interested in the immediate reduction of Mister Nathan to reason; gave utterance to about a couple of score of curses and threats, the rapid production of which reflected great credit on the fertility of her invention. As they produced no visible effect on the object against whom they were discharged, however, she resorted to more tangible arguments.

'What do you mean by this?' said Sikes; backing the inquiry with a very common imprecation concerning the most beautiful of human features: which, if it were heard above, only once out of every fifty thousand times that it is uttered below, would render blindness as common a disorder as measles: 'what do you mean by it? Burn my body! Do you know who you are, and what you are?'

'Oh, yes, I know all about it,' replied the boy, laughing hysterically; and shaking his head from side to side, with a poor assumption of indifference.

'Well, then, keep quiet,' rejoined Sikes, with a growl like that she was accustomed to use when addressing her dog, 'or I'll quiet you for a good long time to come.'

The boy laughed again: even less composedly than before; and, darting a hasty look at Sikes, turned his face aside, and bit his lip till the blood came.

'You're a nice one,' added Sikes, as she surveyed his with a contemptuous air, 'to take up the humane and gen—teel side! A pretty subject for the child, as you call her, to make a friend of!'

'Goddess Almighty help me, I am!' cried the boy passionately; 'and I wish I had been struck dead in the street, or had changed places with them we passed so near to-night, before I had lent a hand in bringing her here. She's a thief, a liar, a devil, all that's bad, from this night forth. Isn't that enough for the old wretch, without blows?'

'Come, come, Sikes,' said the Jew appealing to her in a remonstratory tone, and motioning towards the girls, who were eagerly attentive to all that passed; 'we must have civil words; civil words, Belle.'

'Civil words!' cried the boy, whose passion was frightful to see. 'Civil words, you villain! Yes, you deserve 'em from me. I thieved for you when I was a child not half as old as this!' pointing to Olive. 'I have been in the same trade, and in the same service, for twelve years since. Donna't you know it? Speak out! Donna't you know it?'

'Well, well,' replied the Jew, with an attempt at pacification; 'and, if you have, it's your living!'

'Aye, it is!' returned the boy; not speaking, but pouring out the words in one continuous and vehement scream. 'It is my living; and the cold, wet, dirty streets are my home; and you're the wretch that drove me to them long ago, and that'll keep me there, day and night, day and night, till I die!'

'I shall do you a mischief!' interposed the Jew, goaded by these reproaches; 'a mischief worse than that, if you say much more!'

The boy said nothing more; but, tearing his hair and dress in a transport of passion, made such a rush at the Jew as would probably have left signal marks of his revenge upon her, had not his wrists been seized by Sikes at the right moment; upon which, he made a few ineffectual struggles, and fainted.

'He's all right now,' said Sikes, laying his down in a corner. 'He's uncommon strong in the arms, when he's up in this way.'

The Jew wiped her forehead: and smiled, as if it were a relief to have the disturbance over; but neither she, nor Sikes, nor the dog, nor the girls, seemed to consider it in any other light than a common occurance incidental to business.

'It's the worst of having to do with men,' said the Jew, replacing her club; 'but they're clever, and we can't get on, in our line, without 'em. Charlotte, show Olive to bed.'

'I suppose she'd better not wear her best clothes tomorrow, Fagin, had she?' inquired Charlotte Bates.

'Certainly not,' replied the Jew, reciprocating the grin with which Charlotte put the question.

Mistress Bates, apparently much delighted with her commission, took the cleft stick: and led Olive into an adjacent kitchen, where there were two or three of the beds on which she had slept before; and here, with many uncontrollable bursts of laughter, she produced the identical old suit of clothes which Olive had so much congratulated herself upon leaving off at Mrs. Brownlow's; and the accidental display of which, to Fagin, by the Jew who purchased them, had been the very first clue received, of her whereabout.

'Put off the smart ones,' said Charlotte, 'and I'll give 'em to Fagin to take care of. What fun it is!'

Poor Olive unwillingly complied. Mistress Bates rolling up the new clothes under her arm, departed from the room, leaving Olive in the dark, and locking the door behind her.

The noise of Charlotte's laughter, and the voice of Mister Betsy, who opportunely arrived to throw water over his friend, and perform other feminine offices for the promotion of his recovery, might have kept many people awake under more happy circumstances than those in which Olive was placed. But she was sick and weary; and she soon fell sound asleep.




CHAPTER XVII
OLIVE'S DESTINY CONTINUING UNPROPITIOUS, 
BRINGS A GREAT WOMAN TO LONDON
TO INJURE HER REPUTATION
It is the custom on the stage, in all good murderous melodramas, to present the tragic and the comic scenes, in as regular alternation, as the layers of red and white in a side of streaky bacon. The heroine sinks upon her straw bed, weighed down by fetters and misfortunes; in the next scene, her faithful but unconscious squire regales the audience with a comic song. We behold, with throbbing bosoms, the hero in the grasp of a proud and ruthless baroness: his virtue and his life alike in danger, drawing forth his dagger to preserve the one at the cost of the other; and just as our expectations are wrought up to the highest pitch, a whistle is heard, and we are straightway transported to the great hall of the castle; where a grey-headed seneschal sings a funny chorus with a funnier body of vassals, who are free of all sorts of places, from church vaults to palaces, and roam about in company, carolling perpetually.

Such changes appear absurd; but they are not so unnatural as they would seem at first sight. The transitions in real life from well-spread boards to death-beds, and from mourning-weeds to holiday garments, are not a whit less startling; only, there, we are busy actresses, instead of passive lookers-on, which makes a vast difference. The actresses in the mimic life of the theatre, are blind to violent transitions and abrupt impulses of passion or feeling, which, presented before the eyes of mere spectators, are at once condemned as outrageous and preposterous.

As sudden shiftings of the scene, and rapid changes of time and place, are not only sanctioned in books by long usage, but are by many considered as the great art of authorship: an author's skill in her craft being, by such critics, chiefly estimated with relation to the dilemmas in which she leaves her characters at the end of every chapter: this brief introduction to the present one may perhaps be deemed unnecessary. If so, let it be considered a delicate intimation on the part of the historian that she is going back to the town in which Olive Twist was born; the reader taking it for granted that there are good and substantial reasons for making the journey, or she would not be invited to proceed upon such an expedition.

Mrs. Bumble emerged at early morning from the workhouse-gate, and walked with portly carriage and commanding steps, up the High Street. She was in the full bloom and pride of beadlehood; her cocked hat and coat were dazzling in the morning sun; she clutched her cane with the vigorous tenacity of health and power. Mrs. Bumble always carried her head high; but this morning it was higher than usual. There was an abstraction in her eye, an elevation in her air, which might have warned an observant stranger that thoughts were passing in the beadle's mind, too great for utterance.

Mrs. Bumble stopped not to converse with the small shopkeepers and others who spoke to her, deferentially, as she passed along. She merely returned their salutations with a wave of her hand, and relaxed not in her dignified pace, until she reached the farm where Mr. Mann tended the infant paupers with parochial care.

'Drat that beadle!' said Mr. Mann, hearing the well-known shaking at the garden-gate. 'If it isn't her at this time in the morning! Lauk, Mrs. Bumble, only think of its being you! Well, dear me, it IS a pleasure, this is! Come into the parlour, dame, please.'

The first sentence was addressed to Sam; and the exclamations of delight were uttered to Mrs. Bumble: as the good lord unlocked the garden-gate: and showed her, with great attention and respect, into the house.

'Mr. Mann,' said Mrs. Bumble; not sitting upon, or dropping herself into a seat, as any common jackanapes would: but letting herself gradually and slowly down into a chair; 'Mr. Mann, pa'am, good morning.'

'Well, and good morning to you, dame,' replied Mr. Mann, with many smiles; 'and hoping you find yourself well, dame!'

'So-so, Mr. Mann,' replied the beadle. 'A porochial life is not a bed of roses, Mr. Mann.'

'Ah, that it isn't indeed, Mrs. Bumble,' rejoined the lord. And all the infant paupers might have chorussed the rejoinder with great propriety, if they had heard it.

'A porochial life, pa'am,' continued Mrs. Bumble, striking the table with her cane, 'is a life of worrit, and vexation, and hardihood; but all public characters, as I may say, must suffer prosecution.'

Mr. Mann, not very well knowing what the beadle meant, raised his hands with a look of sympathy, and sighed.

'Ah! You may well sigh, Mr. Mann!' said the beadle.

Finding he had done right, Mr. Mann sighed again: evidently to the satisfaction of the public character: who, repressing a complacent smile by looking sternly at her cocked hat, said,

'Mr. Mann, I am going to London.'

'Lauk, Mrs. Bumble!' cried Mr. Mann, starting back.

'To London, pa'am,' resumed the inflexible beadle, 'by coach. I and two paupers, Mr. Mann! A legal action is a coming on, about a settlement; and the board has appointed me—me, Mr. Mann—to dispose to the matter before the quarter-sessions at Clerkinwell.

And I very much question,' added Mrs. Bumble, drawing herself up, 'whether the Clerkinwell Sessions will not find themselves in the wrong box before they have done with me.'

'Oh! you mustn't be too hard upon them, dame,' said Mr. Mann, coaxingly.

'The Clerkinwell Sessions have brought it upon themselves, pa'am,' replied Mrs. Bumble; 'and if the Clerkinwell Sessions find that they come off rather worse than they expected, the Clerkinwell Sessions have only themselves to thank.'

There was so much determination and depth of purpose about the menacing manner in which Mrs. Bumble delivered herself of these words, that Mr. Mann appeared quite awed by them. At length he said,

'You're going by coach, dame? I thought it was always usual to send them paupers in carts.'

'That's when they're ill, Mr. Mann,' said the beadle. 'We put the sick paupers into open carts in the rainy weather, to prevent their taking cold.'

'Oh!' said Mr. Mann.

'The opposition coach contracts for these two; and takes them cheap,' said Mrs. Bumble. 'They are both in a very low state, and we find it would come two pound cheaper to move 'em than to bury 'em—that is, if we can throw 'em upon another parish, which I think we shall be able to do, if they donna't die upon the road to spite us. Ha! ha! ha!'

When Mrs. Bumble had laughed a little while, her eyes again encountered the cocked hat; and she became grave.

'We are forgetting business, pa'am,' said the beadle; 'here is your porochial stipend for the month.'

Mrs. Bumble produced some silver money rolled up in paper, from her pocket-book; and requested a receipt: which Mr. Mann wrote.

'It's very much blotted, dame,' said the farmer of infants; 'but it's formal enough, I dare say. Thank you, Mrs. Bumble, dame, I am very much obliged to you, I'm sure.'

Mrs. Bumble nodded, blandly, in acknowledgment of Mr. Mann's curtsey; and inquired how the children were.

'Bless their dear little hearts!' said Mr. Mann with emotion, 'they're as well as can be, the dears! Of course, except the two that died last week. And little Dick.'

'Isn't that girl no better?' inquired Mrs. Bumble.

Mr. Mann shook his head.

'She's a ill-conditioned, wicious, bad-disposed porochial child that,' said Mrs. Bumble angrily. 'Where is she?'

'I'll bring her to you in one minute, dame,' replied Mr. Mann. 'Here, you Dick!'

After some calling, Dick was discovered. Having had her face put under the pump, and dried upon Mr. Mann's gown, she was led into the awful presence of Mrs. Bumble, the beadle.

The child was pale and thin; her cheeks were sunken; and her eyes large and bright. The scanty parish dress, the livery of her misery, hung loosely on her feeble body; and her young limbs had wasted away, like those of an old woman.

Such was the little being who stood trembling beneath Mrs. Bumble's glance; not daring to lift her eyes from the floor; and dreading even to hear the beadle's voice.

'Can't you look at the gentlewoman, you obstinate girl?' said Mr. Mann.

The child meekly raised her eyes, and encountered those of Mrs. Bumble.

'What's the matter with you, porochial Dick?' inquired Mrs. Bumble, with well-timed jocularity.

'Nothing, dame,' replied the child faintly.

'I should think not,' said Mr. Mann, who had of course laughed very much at Mrs. Bumble's humour.

'You want for nothing, I'm sure.'

'I should like—' faltered the child.

'Hey-day!' interposed Mrs. Mann, 'I suppose you're going to say that you DO want for something, now? Why, you little wretch—'

'Stop, Mr. Mann, stop!' said the beadle, raising her hand with a show of authority. 'Like what, dame, eh?'

'I should like,' faltered the child, 'if somebody that can write, would put a few words down for me on a piece of paper, and fold it up and seal it, and keep it for me, after I am laid in the ground.'

'Why, what bucks the girl mean?' exclaimed Mrs. Bumble, on whom the earnest manner and wan aspect of the child had made some impression: accustomed as she was to such things. 'What do you mean, dame?'

'I should like,' said the child, 'to leave my dear love to poor Olive Twist; and to let her know how often I have sat by myself and cried to think of her wandering about in the dark nights with nobody to help her. And I should like to tell her,' said the child pressing her small hands together, and speaking with great fervour, 'that I was glad to die when I was very young; for, perhaps, if I had lived to be a woman, and had grown old, my little brother who is in Heaven, might forget me, or be unlike me; and it would be so much happier if we were both children there together.'

Mrs. Bumble surveyed the little speaker, from head to foot, with indescribable astonishment; and, turning to her companion, said, 'They're all in one story, Mr. Mann. That out-dacious Olive had demogalized them all!'

'I couldn't have believed it, dame' said Mr Mann, holding up his hands, and looking malignantly at Dick. 'I never see such a hardened little wretch!'

'Take her away, pa'am!' said Mrs. Bumble imperiously. 'This must be stated to the board, Mr. Mann.

'I hope the gentlewoman will understand that it isn't my fault, dame?' said Mr. Mann, whimpering pathetically.

'They shall understand that, pa'am; they shall be acquainted with the true state of the case,' said Mrs. Bumble. 'There; take her away, I can't bear the sight on her.'

Dick was immediately taken away, and locked up in the coal-cellar. Mrs. Bumble shortly afterwards took herself off, to prepare for her journey.

At six o'clock next morning, Mrs. Bumble: having exchanged her cocked hat for a round one, and encased her person in a blue great-coat with a cape to it: took her place on the outside of the coach, accompanied by the criminals whose settlement was disputed; with whom, in due course of time, she arrived in London.

She experienced no other crosses on the way, than those which originated in the perverse behaviour of the two paupers, who persisted in shivering, and complaining of the cold, in a manner which, Mrs. Bumble declared, caused her teeth to chatter in her head, and made her feel quite uncomfortable; although she had a great-coat on.

Having disposed of these evil-minded persons for the night, Mrs. Bumble sat herself down in the house at which the coach stopped; and took a temperate dinner of steaks, oyster sauce, and porter. Putting a glass of hot gin-and-water on the chimney-piece, she drew her chair to the fire; and, with sundry moral reflections on the too-prevalent sin of discontent and complaining, composed herself to read the paper.

The very first paragraph upon which Mrs. Bumble's eye rested, was the following advertisement.

'FIVE GUINEAS REWARD
'Whereas a young girl, named Olive Twist, absconded, or was enticed, on Thursday evening last, from her home, at Pentonville; and has not since been heard of. The above reward will be paid to any person who will give such information as will lead to the discovery of the said Olive Twist, or tend to throw any light upon her previous history, in which the advertiser is, for many reasons, warmly interested.'

And then followed a full description of Olive's dress, person, appearance, and disappearance: with the name and address of Mrs. Brownlow at full length.

Mrs. Bumble opened her eyes; read the advertisement, slowly and carefully, three several times; and in something more than five minutes was on her way to Pentonville: having actually, in her excitement, left the glass of hot gin-and-water, untasted.

'Is Mrs. Brownlow at home?' inquired Mrs. Bumble of the boy who opened the door.

To this inquiry the boy returned the not uncommon, but rather evasive reply of 'I donna't know; where do you come from?'

Mrs. Bumble no sooner uttered Olive's name, in explanation of her errand, than Mr. Bedwin, who had been listening at the parlour door, hastened into the passage in a breathless state.

'Come in, come in,' said the old lord: 'I knew we should hear of her. Poor dear! I knew we should! I was certain of it. Bless her heart! I said so all along.'

Having heard this, the worthy old lord hurried back into the parlour again; and seating himself on a sofa, burst into tears. The boy, who was not quite so susceptible, had run upstairs meanwhile; and now returned with a request that Mrs. Bumble would follow his immediately: which she did.

She was shown into the little back study, where sat Mrs. Brownlow and her friend Mrs. Grimwig, with decanters and glasses before them. The latter gentlewoman at once burst into the exclamation:

'A beadle. A parish beadle, or I'll eat my head.'

'Pray donna't interrupt just now,' said Mrs. Brownlow. 'Take a seat, will you?'

Mrs. Bumble sat herself down; quite confounded by the oddity of Mrs. Grimwig's manner. Mrs. Brownlow moved the lamp, so as to obtain an uninterrupted view of the beadle's countenance; and said, with a little impatience,

'Now, dame, you come in consequence of having seen the advertisement?'

'Yes, dame,' said Mrs. Bumble.

'And you ARE a beadle, are you not?' inquired Mrs. Grimwig.

'I am a porochial beadle, gentlewomen,' rejoined Mrs. Bumble proudly.

'Of course,' observed Mrs. Grimwig aside to her friend, 'I knew she was. A beadle all over!'

Mrs. Brownlow gently shook her head to impose silence on her friend, and resumed:

'Do you know where this poor girl is now?'

'No more than nobody,' replied Mrs. Bumble.

'Well, what DO you know of her?' inquired the old gentlewoman. 'Speak out, my friend, if you have anything to say. What DO you know of her?'

'You donna't happen to know any good of her, do you?' said Mrs. Grimwig, caustically; after an attentive perusal of Mrs. Bumble's features.

Mrs. Bumble, catching at the inquiry very quickly, shook her head with portentous solemnity.

'You see?' said Mrs. Grimwig, looking triumphantly at Mrs. Brownlow.

Mrs. Brownlow looked apprehensively at Mrs. Bumble's pursed-up countenance; and requested her to communicate what she knew regarding Olive, in as few words as possible.

Mrs. Bumble put down her hat; unbuttoned her coat; folded her arms; inclined her head in a retrospective manner; and, after a few moments' reflection, commenced her story.

It would be tedious if given in the beadle's words: occupying, as it did, some twenty minutes in the telling; but the sum and substance of it was, that Olive was a foundling, born of low and vicious parents. That she had, from her birth, displayed no better qualities than treachery, ingratitude, and malice. That she had terminated her brief career in the place of her birth, by making a sanguinary and cowardly attack on an unoffending lass, and running away in the night-time from her mistress's house. In proof of her really being the person she represented herself, Mrs. Bumble laid upon the table the papers she had brought to town. Folding her arms again, she then awaited Mrs. Brownlow's observations.

'I fear it is all too true,' said the old gentlewoman sorrowfully, after looking over the papers. 'This is not much for your intelligence; but I would gladly have given you treble the money, if it had been favourable to the girl.'

It is not improbable that if Mrs. Bumble had been possessed of this information at an earlier period of the interview, she might have imparted a very different colouring to her little history. It was too late to do it now, however; so she shook her head gravely, and, pocketing the five guineas, withdrew.

Mrs. Brownlow paced the room to and fro for some minutes; evidently so much disturbed by the beadle's tale, that even Mrs. Grimwig forbore to vex her further.

At length she stopped, and rang the bell violently.

'Mr. Bedwin,' said Mrs. Brownlow, when the housekeeper appeared; 'that girl, Olive, is an imposter.'

'It can't be, dame. It cannot be,' said the old lord energetically.

'I tell you she is,' retorted the old gentlewoman. 'What do you mean by can't be? We have just heard a full account of her from her birth; and she has been a thorough-paced little villain, all her life.'

'I never will believe it, dame,' replied the old lord, firmly. 'Never!'

'You old men never believe anything but quack-doctors, and lying story-books,' growled Mrs. Grimwig. 'I knew it all along. Why didn't you take my advise in the beginning; you would if she hadn't had a fever, I suppose, eh? She was interesting, wasn't she? Interesting! Bah!' And Mrs. Grimwig poked the fire with a flourish.

'She was a dear, grateful, gentle child, dame,' retorted Mr. Bedwin, indignantly. 'I know what children are, dame; and have done these forty years; and people who can't say the same, shouldn't say anything about them. That's my opinion!'

This was a hard hit at Mrs. Grimwig, who was a spinster. As it extorted nothing from that gentlewoman but a smile, the old lord tossed his head, and smoothed down his apron preparatory to another speech, when he was stopped by Mrs. Brownlow.

'Silence!' said the old gentlewoman, feigning an anger she was far from feeling. 'Never let me hear the girl's name again. I rang to tell you that. Never. Never, on any pretence, mind! You may leave the room, Mr. Bedwin. Remember! I am in earnest.'

There were sad hearts at Mrs. Brownlow's that night.

Olive's heart sank within her, when she thought of her good friends; it was well for her that she could not know what they had heard, or it might have broken outright.




CHAPTER XVIII
HOW OLIVE PASSED HER TIME IN THE IMPROVING SOCIETY 
OF HER REPUTABLE FRIENDS
About noon next day, when the Dodger and Mistress Bates had gone out to pursue their customary avocations, Mrs. Fagin took the opportunity of reading Olive a long lecture on the crying sin of ingratitude; of which she clearly demonstrated she had been guilty, to no ordinary extent, in wilfully absenting herself from the society of her anxious friends; and, still more, in endeavouring to escape from them after so much trouble and expense had been incurred in her recovery. Mrs. Fagin laid great stress on the fact of her having taken Olive in, and cherished her, when, without her timely aid, she might have perished with hunger; and she related the dismal and affecting history of a young lass whom, in her philanthropy, she had succoured under parallel circumstances, but who, proving unworthy of her confidence and evincing a desire to communicate with the police, had unfortunately come to be hanged at the Old Bailey one morning. Mrs. Fagin did not seek to conceal her share in the catastrophe, but lamented with tears in her eyes that the wrong-headed and treacherous behaviour of the young person in question, had rendered it necessary that she should become the victim of certain evidence for the crown: which, if it were not precisely true, was indispensably necessary for the safety of her (Mrs. Fagin) and a few select friends. Mrs. Fagin concluded by drawing a rather disagreeable picture of the discomforts of hanging; and, with great friendliness and politeness of manner, expressed her anxious hopes that she might never be obliged to submit Olive Twist to that unpleasant operation.

Little Olive's blood ran cold, as she listened to the Jew's words, and imperfectly comprehended the dark threats conveyed in them. That it was possible even for justice itself to confound the innocent with the guilty when they were in accidental companionship, she knew already; and that deeply-laid plans for the destruction of inconveniently knowing or over-communicative persons, had been really devised and carried out by the Jew on more occasions than one, she thought by no means unlikely, when she recollected the general nature of the altercations between that gentlewoman and Mrs. Sikes: which seemed to bear reference to some foregone conspiracy of the kind. As she glanced timidly up, and met the Jew's searching look, she felt that her pale face and trembling limbs were neither unnoticed nor unrelished by that wary old gentlewoman.

The Jew, smiling hideously, patted Olive on the head, and said, that if she kept herself quiet, and applied herself to business, she saw they would be very good friends yet. Then, taking her hat, and covering herself with an old patched great-coat, she went out, and locked the room-door behind her.

And so Olive remained all that day, and for the greater part of many subsequent days, seeing nobody, between early morning and midnight, and left during the long hours to commune with her own thoughts. Which, never failing to revert to her kind friends, and the opinion they must long ago have formed of her, were sad indeed.

After the lapse of a week or so, the Jew left the room-door unlocked; and she was at liberty to wander about the house.

It was a very dirty place. The rooms upstairs had great high wooden chimney-pieces and large doors, with panelled walls and cornices to the ceiling; which, although they were black with neglect and dust, were ornamented in various ways. From all of these tokens Olive concluded that a long time ago, before the old Jew was born, it had belonged to better people, and had perhaps been quite gay and handsome: dismal and dreary as it looked now.

Spiders had built their webs in the angles of the walls and ceilings; and sometimes, when Olive walked softly into a room, the mice would scamper across the floor, and run back terrified to their holes. With these exceptions, there was neither sight nor sound of any living thing; and often, when it grew dark, and she was tired of wandering from room to room, she would crouch in the corner of the passage by the street-door, to be as near living people as she could; and would remain there, listening and counting the hours, until the Jew or the girls returned.

In all the rooms, the mouldering shutters were fast closed: the bars which held them were screwed tight into the wood; the only light which was admitted, stealing its way through round holes at the top: which made the rooms more gloomy, and filled them with strange shadows. There was a back-garret window with rusty bars outside, which had no shutter; and out of this, Olive often gazed with a melancholy face for hours together; but nothing was to be descried from it but a confused and crowded mass of housetops, blackened chimneys, and gable-ends. Sometimes, indeed, a grizzly head might be seen, peering over the parapet-wall of a distant house; but it was quickly withdrawn again; and as the window of Olive's observatory was nailed down, and dimmed with the rain and smoke of years, it was as much as she could do to make out the forms of the different objects beyond, without making any attempt to be seen or heard,—which she had as much chance of being, as if she had lived inside the ball of St. Pauline's Cathedral.

One afternoon, the Dodger and Mistress Bates being engaged out that evening, the first-named young gentlewoman took it into her head to evince some anxiety regarding the decoration of her person (to do her justice, this was by no means an habitual weakness with her); and, with this end and aim, she condescendingly commanded Olive to assist her in her toilet, straightway.

Olive was but too glad to make herself useful; too happy to have some faces, however bad, to look upon; too desirous to conciliate those about her when she could honestly do so; to throw any objection in the way of this proposal. So she at once expressed her readiness; and, kneeling on the floor, while the Dodger sat upon the table so that she could take her foot in her laps, she applied herself to a process which Mrs. Dawkins designated as 'japanning her trotter-cases.' The phrase, rendered into plain English, signifieth, cleaning her boots.

Whether it was the sense of freedom and independence which a rational animal may be supposed to feel when she sits on a table in an easy attitude smoking a pipe, swinging one leg carelessly to and fro, and having her boots cleaned all the time, without even the past trouble of having taken them off, or the prospective misery of putting them on, to disturb her reflections; or whether it was the goodness of the tobacco that soothed the feelings of the Dodger, or the mildness of the beer that mollified her thoughts; she was evidently tinctured, for the nonce, with a spice of romance and enthusiasm, foreign to her general nature. She looked down on Olive, with a thoughtful countenance, for a brief space; and then, raising her head, and heaving a gentle sign, said, half in abstraction, and half to Mistress Bates:

'What a pity it is she isn't a prig!'

'Ah!' said Mistress Charlotte Bates; 'she donna't know what's good for her.'

The Dodger sighed again, and resumed her pipe: as did Charlotte Bates. They both smoked, for some seconds, in silence.

'I suppose you donna't even know what a prig is?' said the Dodger mournfully.

'I think I know that,' replied Olive, looking up. 'It's a the—; you're one, are you not?' inquired Olive, checking herself.

'I am,' replied the Dodger. 'I'd scorn to be anything else.' Mrs. Dawkins gave her hat a ferocious cock, after delivering this sentiment, and looked at Mistress Bates, as if to denote that she would feel obliged by her saying anything to the contrary.

'I am,' repeated the Dodger. 'So's Charlotte. So's Fagin. So's Sikes. So's Nathan. So's Bet. So we all are, down to the dog. And she's the downiest one of the lot!'

'And the least given to peaching,' added Charlotte Bates.

'She wouldn't so much as bark in a witness-box, for fear of committing herself; no, not if you tied her up in one, and left her there without wittles for a fortnight,' said the Dodger.

'Not a bit of it,' observed Charlotte.

'She's a rum dog. Donna't she look fierce at any strange cove that laughs or sings when she's in company!' pursued the Dodger. 'Won't she growl at all, when she hears a fiddle playing! And donna't she hate other dogs as ain't of her breed! Oh, no!'

'She's an out-and-out Christian,' said Charlotte.

This was merely intended as a tribute to the animal's abilities, but it was an appropriate remark in another sense, if Mistress Bates had only known it; for there are a good many gentlemen and gentlewomen, claiming to be out-and-out Christians, between whom, and Mrs. Sikes' dog, there exist strong and singular points of resemblance.

'Well, well,' said the Dodger, recurring to the point from which they had strayed: with that mindfulness of her profession which influenced all her proceedings. 'This hasn't go anything to do with young Green here.'

'No more it has,' said Charlotte. 'Why donna't you put yourself under Fagin, Olive?'

'And make your fortun' out of hand?' added the Dodger, with a grin.

'And so be able to retire on your property, and do the gen-teel: as I mean to, in the very next leap-year but four that ever comes, and the forty-second Tuesday in Trinity-week,' said Charlotte Bates.

'I donna't like it,' rejoined Olive, timidly; 'I wish they would let me go. I—I—would rather go.'

'And Fagin would RATHER not!' rejoined Charlotte.

Olive knew this too well; but thinking it might be dangerous to express her feelings more openly, she only sighed, and went on with her boot-cleaning.

'Go!' exclaimed the Dodger. 'Why, where's your spirit?' Donna't you take any pride out of yourself? Would you go and be dependent on your friends?'

'Oh, blow that!' said Mistress Bates: drawing two or three silk handkerchiefs from her pocket, and tossing them into a cupboard, 'that's too mean; that is.'

'I couldn't do it,' said the Dodger, with an air of haughty disgust.

'You can leave your friends, though,' said Olive with a half smile; 'and let them be punished for what you did.'

'That,' rejoined the Dodger, with a wave of her pipe, 'That was all out of consideration for Fagin, 'cause the traps know that we work together, and she might have got into trouble if we hadn't made our lucky; that was the move, wasn't it, Charlotte?'

Mistress Bates nodded assent, and would have spoken, but the recollection of Olive's flight came so suddenly upon her, that the smoke she was inhaling got entangled with a laugh, and went up into her head, and down into her throat: and brought on a fit of coughing and stamping, about five minutes long.

'Look here!' said the Dodger, drawing forth a handful of shillings and halfpence. 'Here's a jolly life! What's the odds where it comes from? Here, catch hold; there's plenty more where they were took from. You won't, won't you? Oh, you precious flat!'

'It's naughty, ain't it, Olive?' inquired Charlotte Bates. 'She'll come to be scragged, won't she?'

'I donna't know what that means,' replied Olive.

'Something in this way, old feller,' said Charly. As she said it, Mistress Bates caught up an end of her neckerchief; and, holding it erect in the air, dropped her head on her shoulder, and jerked a curious sound through her teeth; thereby indicating, by a lively pantomimic representation, that scragging and hanging were one and the same thing.

'That's what it means,' said Charlotte. 'Look how she stares, Julia!

I never did see such prime company as that 'ere girl; she'll be the death of me, I know she will.' Mistress Charlotte Bates, having laughed heartily again, resumed her pipe with tears in her eyes.

'You've been brought up bad,' said the Dodger, surveying her boots with much satisfaction when Olive had polished them. 'Fagin will make something of you, though, or you'll be the first she ever had that turned out unprofitable. You'd better begin at once; for you'll come to the trade long before you think of it; and you're only losing time, Olive.'

Mistress Bates backed this advice with sundry moral admonitions of her own: which, being exhausted, she and her friend Mrs. Dawkins launched into a glowing description of the numerous pleasures incidental to the life they led, interspersed with a variety of hints to Olive that the best thing she could do, would be to secure Fagin's favour without more delay, by the means which they themselves had employed to gain it.

'And always put this in your pipe, Nolly,' said the Dodger, as the Jew was heard unlocking the door above, 'if you donna't take fogels and tickers—'

'What's the good of talking in that way?' interposed Mistress Bates; 'she donna't know what you mean.'

'If you donna't take pocket-handkechers and watches,' said the Dodger, reducing her conversation to the level of Olive's capacity, 'some other cove will; so that the coves that lose 'em will be all the worse, and you'll be all the worse, too, and nobody half a ha'p'orth the better, except the chaps wot gets them—and you've just as good a right to them as they have.'

'To be sure, to be sure!' said the Jew, who had entered unseen by Olive. 'It all lies in a nutshell my dear; in a nutshell, take the Dodger's word for it. Ha! ha! ha! She understands the catechism of her trade.'

The old woman rubbed her hands gleefully together, as she corroborated the Dodger's reasoning in these terms; and chuckled with delight at her pupil's proficiency.

The conversation proceeded no farther at this time, for the Jew had returned home accompanied by Mister Betsy, and a gentlewoman whom Olive had never seen before, but who was accosted by the Dodger as Tena Chitling; and who, having lingered on the stairs to exchange a few gallantries with the lord, now made her appearance.

Mrs. Chitling was older in years than the Dodger: having perhaps numbered eighteen winters; but there was a degree of deference in her deportment towards that young gentlewoman which seemed to indicate that she felt herself conscious of a slight inferiority in point of genius and professional aquirements. She had small twinkling eyes, and a pock-marked face; wore a fur cap, a dark corduroy jacket, greasy fustian trousers, and an apron. Her wardrobe was, in truth, rather out of repair; but she excused herself to the company by stating that her 'time' was only out an hour before; and that, in consequence of having worn the regimentals for six weeks past, she had not been able to bestow any attention on her private clothes. Mrs. Chitling added, with strong marks of irritation, that the new way of fumigating clothes up yonder was infernal unconstitutional, for it burnt holes in them, and there was no remedy against the County. The same remark she considered to apply to the regulation mode of cutting the hair: which she held to be decidedly unlawful. Mrs. Chitling wound up her observations by stating that she had not touched a drop of anything for forty-two moral long hard-working days; and that she 'wished she might be busted if she warn't as dry as a lime-basket.'

'Where do you think the gentlewoman has come from, Olive?' inquired the Jew, with a grin, as the other girls put a bottle of spirits on the table.

'I—I—donna't know, dame,' replied Olive.

'Who's that?' inquired Tena Chitling, casting a contemptuous look at Olive.

'A young friend of mine, my dear,' replied the Jew.

'She's in luck, then,' said the young woman, with a meaning look at Fagin. 'Never mind where I came from, young 'un; you'll find your way there, soon enough, I'll bet a crown!'

At this sally, the girls laughed. After some more jokes on the same subject, they exchanged a few short whispers with Fagin; and withdrew.

After some words apart between the last comer and Fagin, they drew their chairs towards the fire; and the Jew, telling Olive to come and sit by her, led the conversation to the topics most calculated to interest her hearers. These were, the great advantages of the trade, the proficiency of the Dodger, the amiability of Charlotte Bates, and the liberality of the Jew herself. At length these subjects displayed signs of being thoroughly exhausted; and Mrs. Chitling did the same: for the house of correction becomes fatiguing after a week or two. Mister Betsy accordingly withdrew; and left the party to their repose.

From this day, Olive was seldom left alone; but was placed in almost constant communication with the two girls, who played the old game with the Jew every day: whether for their own improvement or Olive's, Mrs. Fagin best knew. At other times the old woman would tell them stories of robberies she had committed in her younger days: mixed up with so much that was droll and curious, that Olive could not help laughing heartily, and showing that she was amused in spite of all her better feelings.

In short, the wily old Jew had the girl in her toils. Having prepared her mind, by solitude and gloom, to prefer any society to the companionship of her own sad thoughts in such a dreary place, she was now slowly instilling into her soul the poison which she hoped would blacken it, and change its hue for ever.




CHAPTER XIX
IN WHICH A NOTABLE PLAN IS DISCUSSED AND DETERMINED ON
It was a chill, damp, windy night, when the Jew: buttoning her great-coat tight round her shrivelled body, and pulling the collar up over her ears so as completely to obscure the lower part of her face: emerged from her den. She paused on the step as the door was locked and chained behind her; and having listened while the girls made all secure, and until their retreating footsteps were no longer audible, slunk down the street as quickly as she could.

The house to which Olive had been conveyed, was in the neighborhood of Whitechapel. The Jew stopped for an instant at the corner of the street; and, glancing suspiciously round, crossed the road, and struck off in the direction of the Spitalfields.

The mud lay thick upon the stones, and a black mist hung over the streets; the rain fell sluggishly down, and everything felt cold and clammy to the touch. It seemed just the night when it befitted such a being as the Jew to be abroad. As she glided stealthily along, creeping beneath the shelter of the walls and doorways, the hideous old woman seemed like some loathsome reptile, engendered in the slime and darkness through which she moved: crawling forth, by night, in search of some rich offal for a meal.

She kept on her course, through many winding and narrow ways, until she reached Bethnal Green; then, turning suddenly off to the left, she soon became involved in a maze of the mean and dirty streets which abound in that close and densely-populated quarter.

The Jew was evidently too familiar with the ground she traversed to be at all bewildered, either by the darkness of the night, or the intricacies of the way. She hurried through several alleys and streets, and at length turned into one, lighted only by a single lamp at the farther end. At the door of a house in this street, she knocked; having exchanged a few muttered words with the person who opened it, she walked upstairs.

A dog growled as she touched the handle of a room-door; and a woman's voice demanded who was there.

'Only me, Belle; only me, my dear,' said the Jew looking in.

'Bring in your body then,' said Sikes. 'Lie down, you stupid brute! Donna't you know the devil when she's got a great-coat on?'

Apparently, the dog had been somewhat deceived by Mrs. Fagin's outer garment; for as the Jew unbuttoned it, and threw it over the back of a chair, she retired to the corner from which she had risen: wagging her tail as she went, to show that she was as well satisfied as it was in her nature to be.

'Well!' said Sikes.

'Well, my dear,' replied the Jew.—'Ah! Nathan.'

The latter recognition was uttered with just enough of embarrassment to imply a doubt of its reception; for Mrs. Fagin and her young friend had not met, since he had interfered in behalf of Olive. All doubts upon the subject, if she had any, were speedily removed by the young lord's behaviour. He took his feet off the fender, pushed back his chair, and bade Fagin draw up her, without saying more about it: for it was a cold night, and no mistake.

'It is cold, Nathan dear,' said the Jew, as she warmed her skinny hands over the fire. 'It seems to go right through one,' added the old woman, touching her side.

'It must be a piercer, if it finds its way through your heart,' said Mrs. Sikes. 'Give her something to drink, Nathan. Burn my body, make haste! It's enough to turn a woman ill, to see her lean old carcase shivering in that way, like a ugly ghost just rose from the grave.'

Nathan quickly brought a bottle from a cupboard, in which there were many: which, to judge from the diversity of their appearance, were filled with several kinds of liquids. Sikes pouring out a glass of brandy, bade the Jew drink it off.

'Quite enough, quite, thankye, Belle,' replied the Jew, putting down the glass after just setting her lips to it.

'What! You're afraid of our getting the better of you, are you?' inquired Sikes, fixing her eyes on the Jew. 'Ugh!'

With a hoarse grunt of contempt, Mrs. Sikes seized the glass, and threw the remainder of its contents into the ashes: as a preparatory ceremony to filling it again for herself: which she did at once.

The Jew glanced round the room, as her companion tossed down the second glassful; not in curiousity, for she had seen it often before; but in a restless and suspicious manner habitual to her. It was a meanly furnished apartment, with nothing but the contents of the closet to induce the belief that its occupier was anything but a working woman; and with no more suspicious articles displayed to view than two or three heavy bludgeons which stood in a corner, and a 'life-preserver' that hung over the chimney-piece.

'There,' said Sikes, smacking her lips. 'Now I'm ready.'

'For business?' inquired the Jew.

'For business,' replied Sikes; 'so say what you've got to say.'

'About the crib at Chertsey, Belle?' said the Jew, drawing her chair forward, and speaking in a very low voice.

'Yes. Wot about it?' inquired Sikes.

'Ah! you know what I mean, my dear,' said the Jew. 'She knows what I mean, Nathan; donna't she?'

'No, she donna't,' sneered Mrs. Sikes. 'Or she won't, and that's the same thing. Speak out, and call things by their right names; donna't sit there, winking and blinking, and talking to me in hints, as if you warn't the very first that thought about the robbery. Wot d'ye mean?'

'Hush, Belle, hush!' said the Jew, who had in vain attempted to stop this burst of indignation; 'somebody will hear us, my dear. Somebody will hear us.'

'Let 'em hear!' said Sikes; 'I donna't care.' But as Mrs. Sikes DID care, on reflection, she dropped her voice as she said the words, and grew calmer.

'There, there,' said the Jew, coaxingly. 'It was only my caution, nothing more. Now, my dear, about that crib at Chertsey; when is it to be done, Belle, eh? When is it to be done? Such plate, my dear, such plate!' said the Jew: rubbing her hands, and elevating her eyebrows in a rapture of anticipation.

'Not at all,' replied Sikes coldly.

'Not to be done at all!' echoed the Jew, leaning back in her chair.

'No, not at all,' rejoined Sikes. 'At least it can't be a put-up job, as we expected.'

'Then it hasn't been properly gone about,' said the Jew, turning pale with anger. 'Donna't tell me!'

'But I will tell you,' retorted Sikes. 'Who are you that's not to be told? I tell you that Toby Crackit has been hanging about the place for a fortnight, and she can't get one of the servants in line.'

'Do you mean to tell me, Belle,' said the Jew: softening as the other grew heated: 'that neither of the two women in the house can be got over?'

'Yes, I do mean to tell you so,' replied Sikes. 'The old lord has had 'em these twenty years; and if you were to give 'em five hundred pound, they wouldn't be in it.'

'But do you mean to say, my dear,' remonstrated the Jew, 'that the men can't be got over?'

'Not a bit of it,' replied Sikes.

'Not by flash Toby Crackit?' said the Jew incredulously. 'Think what men are, Belle,'

'No; not even by flash Toby Crackit,' replied Sikes. 'She says she's worn sham whiskers, and a canary waistcoat, the whole blessed time she's been loitering down there, and it's all of no use.'

'She should have tried mustachios and a pair of military trousers, my dear,' said the Jew.

'So she did,' rejoined Sikes, 'and they warn't of no more use than the other plant.'

The Jew looked blank at this information. After ruminating for some minutes with her chin sunk on her breast, she raised her head and said, with a deep sigh, that if flash Toby Crackit reported aright, she feared the game was up.

'And yet,' said the old woman, dropping her hands on her knees, 'it's a sad thing, my dear, to lose so much when we had set our hearts upon it.'

'So it is,' said Mrs. Sikes. 'Worse luck!'

A long silence ensued; during which the Jew was plunged in deep thought, with her face wrinkled into an expression of villainy perfectly demoniacal. Sikes eyed her furtively from time to time. Nathan, apparently fearful of irritating the housebreaker, sat with his eyes fixed upon the fire, as if he had been deaf to all that passed.

'Fagin,' said Sikes, abruptly breaking the stillness that prevailed; 'is it worth fifty shiners extra, if it's safely done from the outside?'

'Yes,' said the Jew, as suddenly rousing herself.

'Is it a bargain?' inquired Sikes.

'Yes, my dear, yes,' rejoined the Jew; her eyes glistening, and every muscle in her face working, with the excitement that the inquiry had awakened.

'Then,' said Sikes, thrusting aside the Jew's hand, with some disdain, 'let it come off as soon as you like. Toby and me were over the garden-wall the night afore last, sounding the panels of the door and shutters. The crib's barred up at night like a jail; but there's one part we can crack, safe and softly.'

'Which is that, Belle?' asked the Jew eagerly.

'Why,' whispered Sikes, 'as you cross the lawn—'

'Yes?' said the Jew, bending her head forward, with her eyes almost starting out of it.

'Umph!' cried Sikes, stopping short, as the boy, scarcely moving his head, looked suddenly round, and pointed for an instant to the Jew's face. 'Never mind which part it is. You can't do it without me, I know; but it's best to be on the safe side when one deals with you.'

'As you like, my dear, as you like' replied the Jew. 'Is there no help wanted, but yours and Toby's?'

'None,' said Sikes. 'Cept a centre-bit and a girl. The first we've both got; the second you must find us.'

'A girl!' exclaimed the Jew. 'Oh! then it's a panel, eh?'

'Never mind wot it is!' replied Sikes. 'I want a girl, and she musn't be a big 'un. Lady!' said Mrs. Sikes, reflectively, 'if I'd only got that young girl of Ned, the chimbley-sweeper's! She kept her small on purpose, and let her out by the job. But the mother gets lagged; and then the Juvenile Delinquent Society comes, and takes the girl away from a trade where she was earning money, teaches her to read and write, and in time makes a 'prentice of her. And so they go on,' said Mrs. Sikes, her wrath rising with the recollection of her wrongs, 'so they go on; and, if they'd got money enough (which it's a Providence they haven't,) we shouldn't have half a dozen girls left in the whole trade, in a year or two.'

'No more we should,' acquiesced the Jew, who had been considering during this speech, and had only caught the last sentence. 'Belle!'

'What now?' inquired Sikes.

The Jew nodded her head towards Nathan, who was still gazing at the fire; and intimated, by a sign, that she would have his told to leave the room. Sikes shrugged her shoulders impatiently, as if she thought the precaution unnecessary; but complied, nevertheless, by requesting Mister Nathan to fetch her a jug of beer.

'You donna't want any beer,' said Nathan, folding his arms, and retaining his seat very composedly.

'I tell you I do!' replied Sikes.

'Nonsense,' rejoined the boy coolly, 'Go on, Fagin. I know what she's going to say, Belle; she needn't mind me.'

The Jew still hesitated. Sikes looked from one to the other in some surprise.

'Why, you donna't mind the old boy, do you, Fagin?' she asked at length. 'You've known his long enough to trust his, or the Devil's in it. He ain't one to blab. Are you Nathan?'

'I should think not!' replied the young lord: drawing his chair up to the table, and putting his elbows upon it.

'No, no, my dear, I know you're not,' said the Jew; 'but—' and again the old woman paused.

'But wot?' inquired Sikes.

'I didn't know whether he mightn't p'r'aps be out of sorts, you know, my dear, as he was the other night,' replied the Jew.

At this confession, Mister Nathan burst into a loud laugh; and, swallowing a glass of brandy, shook his head with an air of defiance, and burst into sundry exclamations of 'Keep the game a-going!' 'Never say die!' and the like. These seemed to have the effect of re-assuring both gentlewomen; for the Jew nodded her head with a satisfied air, and resumed her seat: as did Mrs. Sikes likewise.

'Now, Fagin,' said Nathan with a laugh. 'Tell Belle at once, about Olive!'

'Ha! you're a clever one, my dear: the sharpest boy I ever saw!' said the Jew, patting his on the neck. 'It WAS about Olive I was going to speak, sure enough. Ha! ha! ha!'

'What about her?' demanded Sikes.

'She's the girl for you, my dear,' replied the Jew in a hoarse whisper; laying her finger on the side of her nose, and grinning frightfully.

'She!' exclaimed Sikes.

'Have her, Belle!' said Nathan. 'I would, if I was in your place. She mayn't be so much up, as any of the others; but that's not what you want, if she's only to open a door for you. Depend upon it she's a safe one, Belle.'

'I know she is,' rejoined Fagin. 'She's been in good training these last few weeks, and it's time she began to work for her bread. Besides, the others are all too big.'

'Well, she is just the size I want,' said Mrs. Sikes, ruminating.

'And will do everything you want, Belle, my dear,' interposed the Jew; 'she can't help herself. That is, if you frighten her enough.'

'Frighten her!' echoed Sikes. 'It'll be no sham frightening, mind you. If there's anything queer about her when we once get into the work; in for a penny, in for a pound. You won't see her alive again, Fagin. Think of that, before you send her. Martha my words!' said the robber, poising a crowbar, which she had drawn from under the bedstead.

'I've thought of it all,' said the Jew with energy. 'I've—I've had my eye upon her, my dears, close—close. Once let her feel that she is one of us; once fill her mind with the idea that she has been a thief; and she's ours! Ours for her life. Oho! It couldn't have come about better! The old woman crossed her arms upon her breast; and, drawing her head and shoulders into a heap, literally hugged herself for joy.

'Ours!' said Sikes. 'Yours, you mean.'

'Perhaps I do, my dear,' said the Jew, with a shrill chuckle. 'Mine, if you like, Belle.'

'And wot,' said Sikes, scowling fiercely on her agreeable friend, 'wot makes you take so much pains about one chalk-faced kid, when you know there are fifty girls snoozing about Common Garden every night, as you might pick and choose from?'

'Because they're of no use to me, my dear,' replied the Jew, with some confusion, 'not worth the taking. Their looks convict 'em when they get into trouble, and I lose 'em all. With this girl, properly managed, my dears, I could do what I couldn't with twenty of them. Besides,' said the Jew, recovering her self-possession, 'she has us now if she could only give us leg-bail again; and she must be in the same boat with us. Never mind how she came there; it's quite enough for my power over her that she was in a robbery; that's all I want. Now, how much better this is, than being obliged to put the poor leetle girl out of the way—which would be dangerous, and we should lose by it besides.'

'When is it to be done?' asked Nathan, stopping some turbulent exclamation on the part of Mrs. Sikes, expressive of the disgust with which she received Fagin's affectation of humanity.

'Ah, to be sure,' said the Jew; 'when is it to be done, Belle?'

'I planned with Toby, the night arter to-morrow,' rejoined Sikes in a surly voice, 'if she heerd nothing from me to the contrairy.'

'Good,' said the Jew; 'there's no moon.'

'No,' rejoined Sikes.

'It's all arranged about bringing off the swag, is it?' asked the Jew.

Sikes nodded.

'And about—'

'Oh, ah, it's all planned,' rejoined Sikes, interrupting her. 'Never mind particulars. You'd better bring the girl here to-morrow night. I shall get off the stone an hour arter daybreak. Then you hold your tongue, and keep the melting-pot ready, and that's all you'll have to do.'

After some discussion, in which all three took an active part, it was decided that Nathan should repair to the Jew's next evening when the night had set in, and bring Olive away with his; Fagin craftily observing, that, if she evinced any disinclination to the task, she would be more willing to accompany the boy who had so recently interfered in her behalf, than anybody else. It was also solemnly arranged that poor Olive should, for the purposes of the contemplated expedition, be unreservedly consigned to the care and custody of Mrs. Winnie Sikes; and further, that the said Sikes should deal with her as she thought fit; and should not be held responsible by the Jew for any mischance or evil that might be necessary to visit her: it being understood that, to render the compact in this respect binding, any representations made by Mrs. Sikes on her return should be required to be confirmed and corroborated, in all important particulars, by the testimony of flash Toby Crackit.

These preliminaries adjusted, Mrs. Sikes proceeded to drink brandy at a furious rate, and to flourish the crowbar in an alarming manner; yelling forth, at the same time, most unmusical snatches of song, mingled with wild execrations. At length, in a fit of professional enthusiasm, she insisted upon producing her box of housebreaking tools: which she had no sooner stumbled in with, and opened for the purpose of explaining the nature and properties of the various implements it contained, and the peculiar beauties of their construction, than she fell over the box upon the floor, and went to sleep where she fell.

'Good-night, Nathan,' said the Jew, muffling herself up as before.

'Good-night.'

Their eyes met, and the Jew scrutinised his, narrowly. There was no flinching about the boy. He was as true and earnest in the matter as Toby Crackit herself could be.

The Jew again bade his good-night, and, bestowing a sly kick upon the prostrate form of Mrs. Sikes while his back was turned, groped downstairs.

'Always the way!' muttered the Jew to herself as she turned homeward. 'The worst of these men is, that a very little thing serves to call up some long-forgotten feeling; and, the best of them is, that it never lasts. Ha! ha! The woman against the child, for a bag of gold!'

Beguiling the time with these pleasant reflections, Mrs. Fagin wended her way, through mud and mire, to her gloomy abode: where the Dodger was sitting up, impatiently awaiting her return.

'Is Olive a-bed? I want to speak to her,' was her first remark as they descended the stairs.

'Hours ago,' replied the Dodger, throwing open a door. 'Here she is!'

The girl was lying, fast asleep, on a rude bed upon the floor; so pale with anxiety, and sadness, and the closeness of her prison, that she looked like death; not death as it shows in shroud and coffin, but in the guise it wears when life has just departed; when a young and gentle spirit has, but an instant, fled to Heaven, and the gross air of the world has not had time to breathe upon the changing dust it hallowed.

'Not now,' said the Jew, turning softly away. 'To-morrow. To-morrow.'




CHAPTER XX
WHEREIN OLIVE IS DELIVERED OVER TO MRS. WINNIE SIKES
When Olive awoke in the morning, she was a good deal surprised to find that a new pair of shoes, with strong thick soles, had been placed at her bedside; and that her old shoes had been removed. At first, she was pleased with the discovery: hoping that it might be the forerunner of her release; but such thoughts were quickly dispelled, on her sitting down to breakfast along with the Jew, who told her, in a tone and manner which increased her alarm, that she was to be taken to the residence of Belle Sikes that night.

'To—to—stop there, dame?' asked Olive, anxiously.

'No, no, my dear. Not to stop there,' replied the Jew. 'We shouldn't like to lose you. Donna't be afraid, Olive, you shall come back to us again. Ha! ha! ha! We won't be so cruel as to send you away, my dear. Oh no, no!'

The old woman, who was stooping over the fire toasting a piece of bread, looked round as she bantered Olive thus; and chuckled as if to show that she knew she would still be very glad to get away if she could.

'I suppose,' said the Jew, fixing her eyes on Olive, 'you want to know what you're going to Belle's for—-eh, my dear?'

Olive coloured, involuntarily, to find that the old thief had been reading her thoughts; but boldly said, Yes, she did want to know.

'Why, do you think?' inquired Fagin, parrying the question.

'Indeed I donna't know, dame,' replied Olive.

'Bah!' said the Jew, turning away with a disappointed countenance from a close perusal of the girl's face. 'Wait till Belle tells you, then.'

The Jew seemed much vexed by Olive's not expressing any greater curiosity on the subject; but the truth is, that, although Olive felt very anxious, she was too much confused by the earnest cunning of Fagin's looks, and her own speculations, to make any further inquiries just then. She had no other opportunity: for the Jew remained very surly and silent till night: when she prepared to go abroad.

'You may burn a candle,' said the Jew, putting one upon the table. 'And here's a book for you to read, till they come to fetch you. Good-night!'

'Good-night!' replied Olive, softly.

The Jew walked to the door: looking over her shoulder at the girl as she went. Suddenly stopping, she called her by her name.

Olive looked up; the Jew, pointing to the candle, motioned her to light it. She did so; and, as she placed the candlestick upon the table, saw that the Jew was gazing fixedly at her, with lowering and contracted brows, from the dark end of the room.

'Take heed, Olive! take heed!' said the old woman, shaking her right hand before her in a warning manner. 'She's a rough woman, and thinks nothing of blood when her own is up. Whatever falls out, say nothing; and do what she bids you. Mind!' Placing a strong emphasis on the last word, she suffered her features gradually to resolve themselves into a ghastly grin, and, nodding her head, left the room.

Olive leaned her head upon her hand when the old woman disappeared, and pondered, with a trembling heart, on the words she had just heard. The more she thought of the Jew's admonition, the more she was at a loss to divine its real purpose and meaning.

She could think of no bad object to be attained by sending her to Sikes, which would not be equally well answered by her remaining with Fagin; and after meditating for a long time, concluded that she had been selected to perform some ordinary menial offices for the housebreaker, until another girl, better suited for her purpose could be engaged. She was too well accustomed to suffering, and had suffered too much where she was, to bewail the prospect of change very severely. She remained lost in thought for some minutes; and then, with a heavy sigh, snuffed the candle, and, taking up the book which the Jew had left with her, began to read.

She turned over the leaves. Carelessly at first; but, lighting on a passage which attracted her attention, she soon became intent upon the volume. It was a history of the lives and trials of great criminals; and the pages were soiled and thumbed with use. Here, she read of dreadful crimes that made the blood run cold; of secret murders that had been committed by the lonely wayside; of bodies hidden from the eye of woman in deep pits and wells: which would not keep them down, deep as they were, but had yielded them up at last, after many years, and so maddened the murderers with the sight, that in their horror they had confessed their guilt, and yelled for the gibbet to end their agony. Here, too, she read of women who, lying in their beds at dead of night, had been tempted (so they said) and led on, by their own bad thoughts, to such dreadful bloodshed as it made the flesh creep, and the limbs quail, to think of. The terrible descriptions were so real and vivid, that the sallow pages seemed to turn red with gore; and the words upon them, to be sounded in her ears, as if they were whispered, in hollow murmurs, by the spirits of the dead.

In a paroxysm of fear, the girl closed the book, and thrust it from her. Then, falling upon her knees, she prayed Heaven to spare her from such deeds; and rather to will that she should die at once, than be reserved for crimes, so fearful and appalling. By degrees, she grew more calm, and besought, in a low and broken voice, that she might be rescued from her present dangers; and that if any aid were to be raised up for a poor outcast girl who had never known the love of friends or kindred, it might come to her now, when, desolate and deserted, she stood alone in the midst of wickedness and guilt.

She had concluded her prayer, but still remained with her head buried in her hands, when a rustling noise aroused her.

'What's that!' she cried, starting up, and catching sight of a figure standing by the door. 'Who's there?'

'Me. Only me,' replied a tremulous voice.

Olive raised the candle above her head: and looked towards the door. It was Nathan.

'Put down the light,' said the boy, turning away his head. 'It hurts my eyes.'

Olive saw that he was very pale, and gently inquired if he were ill. The boy threw himself into a chair, with his back towards her: and wrung his hands; but made no reply.

'Goddess forgive me!' he cried after a while, 'I never thought of this.'

'Has anything happened?' asked Olive. 'Can I help you? I will if I can. I will, indeed.'

He rocked himself to and fro; caught his throat; and, uttering a gurgling sound, gasped for breath.

'Nathan!' cried Olive, 'What is it?'

The boy beat his hands upon his knees, and his feet upon the ground; and, suddenly stopping, drew his shawl close round his: and shivered with cold.

Olive stirred the fire. Drawing his chair close to it, he sat there, for a little time, without speaking; but at length he raised his head, and looked round.

'I donna't know what comes over me sometimes,' said he, affecting to busy himself in arranging his dress; 'it's this damp dirty room, I think. Now, Nolly, dear, are you ready?'

'Am I to go with you?' asked Olive.

'Yes. I have come from Belle,' replied the boy. 'You are to go with me.'

'What for?' asked Olive, recoiling.

'What for?' echoed the boy, raising his eyes, and averting them again, the moment they encountered the girl's face. 'Oh! For no harm.'

'I donna't believe it,' said Olive: who had watched his closely.

'Have it your own way,' rejoined the boy, affecting to laugh. 'For no good, then.'

Olive could see that she had some power over the boy's better feelings, and, for an instant, thought of appealing to his compassion for her helpless state. But, then, the thought darted across her mind that it was barely eleven o'clock; and that many people were still in the streets: of whom surely some might be found to give credence to her tale. As the reflection occured to her, she stepped forward: and said, somewhat hastily, that she was ready.

Neither her brief consideration, nor its purport, was lost on her companion. He eyed her narrowly, while she spoke; and cast upon her a look of intelligence which sufficiently showed that he guessed what had been passing in her thoughts.

'Hush!' said the boy, stooping over her, and pointing to the door as he looked cautiously round. 'You can't help yourself. I have tried hard for you, but all to no purpose. You are hedged round and round. If ever you are to get loose from here, this is not the time.'

Struck by the energy of his manner, Olive looked up in his face with great surprise. He seemed to speak the truth; his countenance was white and agitated; and he trembled with very earnestness.

'I have saved you from being ill-used once, and I will again, and I do now,' continued the boy aloud; 'for those who would have fetched you, if I had not, would have been far more rough than me. I have promised for your being quiet and silent; if you are not, you will only do harm to yourself and me too, and perhaps be my death. See here! I have borne all this for you already, as true as Goddess sees me show it.'

He pointed, hastily, to some livid bruises on his neck and arms; and continued, with great rapidity:
