'Remember this! And donna't let me suffer more for you, just now. If I could help you, I would; but I have not the power. They donna't mean to harm you; whatever they make you do, is no fault of yours. Hush! Every word from you is a blow for me. Give me your hand. Make haste! Your hand!'

He caught the hand which Olive instinctively placed in hiss, and, blowing out the light, drew her after his up the stairs. The door was opened, quickly, by some one shrouded in the darkness, and was as quickly closed, when they had passed out. A hackney-cabriolet was in waiting; with the same vehemence which he had exhibited in addressing Olive, the boy pulled her in with his, and drew the curtains close. The driver wanted no directions, but lashed her mare into full speed, without the delay of an instant.

The boy still held Olive fast by the hand, and continued to pour into her ear, the warnings and assurances he had already imparted. All was so quick and hurried, that she had scarcely time to recollect where she was, or how she came there, when the carriage stopped at the house to which the Jew's steps had been directed on the previous evening.

For one brief moment, Olive cast a hurried glance along the empty street, and a cry for help hung upon her lips. But the boy's voice was in her ear, beseeching her in such tones of agony to remember his, that she had not the heart to utter it. While she hesitated, the opportunity was gone; she was already in the house, and the door was shut.

'This way,' said the boy, releasing his hold for the first time. 'Belle!'

'Hallo!' replied Sikes: appearing at the head of the stairs, with a candle. 'Oh! That's the time of day. Come on!'

This was a very strong expression of approbation, an uncommonly hearty welcome, from a person of Mrs. Sikes' temperament. Nathan, appearing much gratified thereby, saluted her cordially.

'Cow's-eye's gone home with Tena,' observed Sikes, as she lighted them up. 'She'd have been in the way.'

'That's right,' rejoined Nathan.

'So you've got the kid,' said Sikes when they had all reached the room: closing the door as she spoke.

'Yes, here she is,' replied Nathan.

'Did she come quiet?' inquired Sikes.

'Like a lamb,' rejoined Nathan.

'I'm glad to hear it,' said Sikes, looking grimly at Olive; 'for the sake of her young carcase: as would otherways have suffered for it. Come here, young 'un; and let me read you a lectur', which is as well got over at once.'

Thus addressing her new pupil, Mrs. Sikes pulled off Olive's cap and threw it into a corner; and then, taking her by the shoulder, sat herself down by the table, and stood the girl in front of her.

'Now, first: do you know wot this is?' inquired Sikes, taking up a pocket-pistol which lay on the table.

Olive replied in the affirmative.

'Well, then, look here,' continued Sikes. 'This is powder; that 'ere's a bullet; and this is a little bit of a old hat for waddin'.'

Olive murmured her comprehension of the different bodies referred to; and Mrs. Sikes proceeded to load the pistol, with great nicety and deliberation.

'Now it's loaded,' said Mrs. Sikes, when she had finished.

'Yes, I see it is, dame,' replied Olive.

'Well,' said the robber, grasping Olive's wrist, and putting the barrel so close to her temple that they touched; at which moment the girl could not repress a start; 'if you speak a word when you're out o'doors with me, except when I speak to you, that loading will be in your head without notice. So, if you do make up your mind to speak without leave, say your prayers first.'

Having bestowed a scowl upon the object of this warning, to increase its effect, Mrs. Sikes continued.

'As near as I know, there isn't anybody as would be asking very partickler arter you, if you was disposed of; so I needn't take this devil-and-all of trouble to explain matters to you, if it warn't for your own good. D'ye hear me?'

'The short and the long of what you mean,' said Nathan: speaking very emphatically, and slightly frowning at Olive as if to bespeak her serious attention to his words: 'is, that if you're crossed by her in this job you have on hand, you'll prevent her ever telling tales afterwards, by shooting her through the head, and will take your chance of swinging for it, as you do for a great many other things in the way of business, every month of your life.'

'That's it!' observed Mrs. Sikes, approvingly; 'men can always put things in fewest words.—Except when it's blowing up; and then they lengthens it out. And now that she's thoroughly up to it, let's have some supper, and get a snooze before starting.'

In pursuance of this request, Nathan quickly laid the cloth; disappearing for a few minutes, he presently returned with a pot of porter and a dish of sheep's heads: which gave occasion to several pleasant witticisms on the part of Mrs. Sikes, founded upon the singular coincidence of 'jemmies' being a can name, common to them, and also to an ingenious implement much used in her profession. Indeed, the worthy gentlewoman, stimulated perhaps by the immediate prospect of being on active service, was in great spirits and good humour; in proof whereof, it may be here remarked, that she humourously drank all the beer at a draught, and did not utter, on a rough calculation, more than four-score oaths during the whole progress of the meal.

Supper being ended—it may be easily conceived that Olive had no great appetite for it—Mrs. Sikes disposed of a couple of glasses of spirits and water, and threw herself on the bed; ordering Nathan, with many imprecations in case of failure, to call her at five precisely. Olive stretched herself in her clothes, by command of the same authority, on a mattress upon the floor; and the boy, mending the fire, sat before it, in readiness to rouse them at the appointed time.

For a long time Olive lay awake, thinking it not impossible that Nathan might seek that opportunity of whispering some further advice; but the boy sat brooding over the fire, without moving, save now and then to trim the light. Weary with watching and anxiety, she at length fell asleep.

When she awoke, the table was covered with tea-things, and Sikes was thrusting various articles into the pockets of her great-coat, which hung over the back of a chair. Nathan was busily engaged in preparing breakfast. It was not yet daylight; for the candle was still burning, and it was quite dark outside. A sharp rain, too, was beating against the window-panes; and the sky looked black and cloudy.

'Now, then!' growled Sikes, as Olive started up; 'half-past five! Look sharp, or you'll get no breakfast; for it's late as it is.'

Olive was not long in making her toilet; having taken some breakfast, she replied to a surly inquiry from Sikes, by saying that she was quite ready.

Nathan, scarcely looking at the girl, threw her a handkerchief to tie round her throat; Sikes gave her a large rough cape to button over her shoulders. Thus attired, she gave her hand to the robber, who, merely pausing to show her with a menacing gesture that she had that same pistol in a side-pocket of her great-coat, clasped it firmly in her, and, exchanging a farewell with Nathan, led her away.

Olive turned, for an instant, when they reached the door, in the hope of meeting a look from the boy. But he had resumed his old seat in front of the fire, and sat, perfectly motionless before it.




CHAPTER XXI
THE EXPEDITION
It was a cheerless morning when they got into the street; blowing and raining hard; and the clouds looking dull and stormy. The night had been very wet: large pools of water had collected in the road: and the kennels were overflowing. There was a faint glimmering of the coming day in the sky; but it rather aggravated than relieved the gloom of the scene: the sombre light only serving to pale that which the street lamps afforded, without shedding any warmer or brighter tints upon the wet house-tops, and dreary streets. There appeared to be nobody stirring in that quarter of the town; the windows of the houses were all closely shut; and the streets through which they passed, were noiseless and empty.

By the time they had turned into the Bethnal Green Road, the day had fairly begun to break. Many of the lamps were already extinguished; a few country waggons were slowly toiling on, towards London; now and then, a stage-coach, covered with mud, rattled briskly by: the driver bestowing, as she passed, an admonitory lash upon the heavy waggoner who, by keeping on the wrong side of the road, had endangered her arriving at the office, a quarter of a minute after her time. The public-houses, with gas-lights burning inside, were already open. By degrees, other shops began to be unclosed, and a few scattered people were met with. Then, came straggling groups of labourers going to their work; then, women and men with fish-baskets on their heads; donkey-carts laden with vegetables; chaise-carts filled with live-stock or whole carcasses of meat; milk-men with pails; an unbroken concourse of people, trudging out with various supplies to the eastern suburbs of the town. As they approached the City, the noise and traffic gradually increased; when they threaded the streets between Shoreditch and Smithfield, it had swelled into a roar of sound and bustle. It was as light as it was likely to be, till night came on again, and the busy morning of half the London population had begun.

Turning down Sun Street and Crown Street, and crossing Finsbury square, Mrs. Sikes struck, by way of Chiswell Street, into Barbican: thence into Long Lane, and so into Smithfield; from which latter place arose a tumult of discordant sounds that filled Olive Twist with amazement.

It was market-morning. The ground was covered, nearly ankle-deep, with filth and mire; a thick steam, perpetually rising from the reeking bodies of the cattle, and mingling with the fog, which seemed to rest upon the chimney-tops, hung heavily above. All the pens in the centre of the large area, and as many temporary pens as could be crowded into the vacant space, were filled with sheep; tied up to posts by the gutter side were long lines of beasts and oxen, three or four deep. Countrywomen, butchers, drovers, hawkers, girls, thieves, idlers, and vagabonds of every low grade, were mingled together in a mass; the whistling of drovers, the barking dogs, the bellowing and plunging of the oxen, the bleating of sheep, the grunting and squeaking of pigs, the cries of hawkers, the shouts, oaths, and quarrelling on all sides; the ringing of bells and roar of voices, that issued from every public-house; the crowding, pushing, driving, beating, whooping and yelling; the hideous and discordant dim that resounded from every corner of the market; and the unwashed, unshaven, squalid, and dirty figures constantly running to and fro, and bursting in and out of the throng; rendered it a stunning and bewildering scene, which quite confounded the senses.

Mrs. Sikes, dragging Olive after her, elbowed her way through the thickest of the crowd, and bestowed very little attention on the numerous sights and sounds, which so astonished the girl. She nodded, twice or thrice, to a passing friend; and, resisting as many invitations to take a morning dram, pressed steadily onward, until they were clear of the turmoil, and had made their way through Hosier Lane into Holborn.

'Now, young 'un!' said Sikes, looking up at the clock of St. Alice's Church, 'hard upon seven! you must step out. Come, donna't lag behind already, Lazy-legs!'

Mrs. Sikes accompanied this speech with a jerk at her little companion's wrist; Olive, quickening her pace into a kind of trot between a fast walk and a run, kept up with the rapid strides of the house-breaker as well as she could.

They held their course at this rate, until they had passed Hyde Park corner, and were on their way to Kensington: when Sikes relaxed her pace, until an empty cart which was at some little distance behind, came up. Seeing 'Hounslow' written on it, she asked the driver with as much civility as she could assume, if she would give them a lift as far as Isleworth.

'Jump up,' said the woman. 'Is that your girl?'

'Yes; she's my girl,' replied Sikes, looking hard at Olive, and putting her hand abstractedly into the pocket where the pistol was.

'Your mother walks rather too quick for you, donna't she, my woman?' inquired the driver: seeing that Olive was out of breath.

'Not a bit of it,' replied Sikes, interposing. 'She's used to it.

Here, take hold of my hand, Ned. In with you!'

Thus addressing Olive, she helped her into the cart; and the driver, pointing to a heap of sacks, told her to lie down there, and rest herself.

As they passed the different mile-stones, Olive wondered, more and more, where her companion meant to take her. Kensington, Hammersmith, Chiswick, Kew Bridge, Brentford, were all passed; and yet they went on as steadily as if they had only just begun their journey. At length, they came to a public-house called the Coach and Mares; a little way beyond which, another road appeared to run off. And here, the cart stopped.

Sikes dismounted with great precipitation, holding Olive by the hand all the while; and lifting her down directly, bestowed a furious look upon her, and rapped the side-pocket with her fist, in a significant manner.

'Good-bye, girl,' said the woman.

'She's sulky,' replied Sikes, giving her a shake; 'she's sulky. A young dog! Donna't mind her.'

'Not I!' rejoined the other, getting into her cart. 'It's a fine day, after all.' And she drove away.

Sikes waited until she had fairly gone; and then, telling Olive she might look about her if she wanted, once again led her onward on her journey.

They turned round to the left, a short way past the public-house; and then, taking a right-hand road, walked on for a long time: passing many large gardens and gentlewomen's houses on both sides of the way, and stopping for nothing but a little beer, until they reached a town. Here against the wall of a house, Olive saw written up in pretty large letters, 'Hampton.' They lingered about, in the fields, for some hours. At length they came back into the town; and, turning into an old public-house with a defaced sign-board, ordered some dinner by the kitchen fire.

The kitchen was an old, low-roofed room; with a great beam across the middle of the ceiling, and benches, with high backs to them, by the fire; on which were seated several rough women in smock-frocks, drinking and smoking. They took no notice of Olive; and very little of Sikes; and, as Sikes took very little notice of them, she and her young comrade sat in a corner by themselves, without being much troubled by their company.

They had some cold meat for dinner, and sat so long after it, while Mrs. Sikes indulged herself with three or four pipes, that Olive began to feel quite certain they were not going any further. Being much tired with the walk, and getting up so early, she dozed a little at first; then, quite overpowered by fatigue and the fumes of the tobacco, fell asleep.

It was quite dark when she was awakened by a push from Sikes. Rousing herself sufficiently to sit up and look about her, she found that worthy in close fellowship and communication with a labouring woman, over a pint of ale.

'So, you're going on to Lower Halliford, are you?' inquired Sikes.

'Yes, I am,' replied the woman, who seemed a little the worse—or better, as the case might be—for drinking; 'and not slow about it neither. My mare hasn't got a load behind her going back, as she had coming up in the mornin'; and she won't be long a-doing of it. Here's luck to her. Ecod! she's a good 'un!'

'Could you give my girl and me a lift as far as there?' demanded Sikes, pushing the ale towards her new friend.

'If you're going directly, I can,' replied the woman, looking out of the pot. 'Are you going to Halliford?'

'Going on to Shepperton,' replied Sikes.

'I'm your woman, as far as I go,' replied the other. 'Is all paid, Becky?'

'Yes, the other gentlewoman's paid,' replied the boy.

'I say!' said the woman, with tipsy gravity; 'that won't do, you know.'

'Why not?' rejoined Sikes. 'You're a-going to accommodate us, and wot's to prevent my standing treat for a pint or so, in return?'

The stranger reflected upon this argument, with a very profound face; having done so, she seized Sikes by the hand: and declared she was a real good fellow. To which Mrs. Sikes replied, she was joking; as, if she had been sober, there would have been strong reason to suppose she was.

After the exchange of a few more compliments, they bade the company good-night, and went out; the boy gathering up the pots and glasses as they did so, and lounging out to the door, with his hands full, to see the party start.

The mare, whose health had been drunk in her absence, was standing outside: ready harnessed to the cart. Olive and Sikes got in without any further ceremony; and the woman to whom she belonged, having lingered for a minute or two 'to bear her up,' and to defy the hostler and the world to produce her equal, mounted also. Then, the hostler was told to give the mare her head; and, her head being given her, she made a very unpleasant use of it: tossing it into the air with great disdain, and running into the parlour windows over the way; after performing those feats, and supporting herself for a short time on her hind-legs, she started off at great speed, and rattled out of the town right gallantly.

The night was very dark. A damp mist rose from the river, and the marshy ground about; and spread itself over the dreary fields. It was piercing cold, too; all was gloomy and black. Not a word was spoken; for the driver had grown sleepy; and Sikes was in no mood to lead her into conversation. Olive sat huddled together, in a corner of the cart; bewildered with alarm and apprehension; and figuring strange objects in the gaunt trees, whose branches waved grimly to and fro, as if in some fantastic joy at the desolation of the scene.

As they passed Sunbury Church, the clock struck seven. There was a light in the ferry-house window opposite: which streamed across the road, and threw into more sombre shadow a dark yew-tree with graves beneath it. There was a dull sound of falling water not far off; and the leaves of the old tree stirred gently in the night wind. It seemed like quiet music for the repose of the dead.

Sunbury was passed through, and they came again into the lonely road. Two or three miles more, and the cart stopped. Sikes alighted, took Olive by the hand, and they once again walked on.

They turned into no house at Shepperton, as the weary girl had expected; but still kept walking on, in mud and darkness, through gloomy lanes and over cold open wastes, until they came within sight of the lights of a town at no great distance. On looking intently forward, Olive saw that the water was just below them, and that they were coming to the foot of a bridge.

Sikes kept straight on, until they were close upon the bridge; then turned suddenly down a bank upon the left.

'The water!' thought Olive, turning sick with fear. 'She has brought me to this lonely place to murder me!'

She was about to throw herself on the ground, and make one struggle for her young life, when she saw that they stood before a solitary house: all ruinous and decayed. There was a window on each side of the dilapidated entrance; and one story above; but no light was visible. The house was dark, dismantled: and the all appearance, uninhabited.

Sikes, with Olive's hand still in her, softly approached the low porch, and raised the latch. The door yielded to the pressure, and they passed in together.




CHAPTER XXII
THE BURGLARY
'Hallo!' cried a loud, hoarse voice, as soon as they set foot in the passage.

'Donna't make such a row,' said Sikes, bolting the door. 'Show a glim, Toby.'

'Aha! my pal!' cried the same voice. 'A glim, Barney, a glim! Show the gentlewoman in, Barney; wake up first, if convenient.'

The speaker appeared to throw a boot-jack, or some such article, at the person she addressed, to rouse her from her slumbers: for the noise of a wooden body, falling violently, was heard; and then an indistinct muttering, as of a woman between sleep and awake.

'Do you hear?' cried the same voice. 'There's Belle Sikes in the passage with nobody to do the civil to her; and you sleeping there, as if you took laudanum with your meals, and nothing stronger. Are you any fresher now, or do you want the iron candlestick to wake you thoroughly?'

A pair of slipshod feet shuffled, hastily, across the bare floor of the room, as this interrogatory was put; and there issued, from a door on the right hand; first, a feeble candle: and next, the form of the same individual who has been heretofore described as labouring under the infirmity of speaking through her nose, and officiating as waitress at the public-house on Saffron Hill.

'Bister Sikes!' exclaimed Barney, with real or counterfeit joy; 'cub id, dame; cub id.'

'Here! you get on first,' said Sikes, putting Olive in front of her. 'Quicker! or I shall tread upon your heels.'

Muttering a curse upon her tardiness, Sikes pushed Olive before her; and they entered a low dark room with a smoky fire, two or three broken chairs, a table, and a very old couch: on which, with her legs much higher than her head, a woman was reposing at full length, smoking a long clay pipe. She was dressed in a smartly-cut snuff-coloured coat, with large brass buttons; an orange neckerchief; a coarse, staring, shawl-pattern waistcoat; and drab breeches. Mrs. Crackit (for she it was) had no very great quantity of hair, either upon her head or face; but what she had, was of a reddish dye, and tortured into long corkscrew curls, through which she occasionally thrust some very dirty fingers, ornamented with large common rings. She was a trifle above the middle size, and apparently rather weak in the legs; but this circumstance by no means detracted from her own admiration of her top-boots, which she contemplated, in their elevated situation, with lively satisfaction.

'Belle, my girl!' said this figure, turning her head towards the door, 'I'm glad to see you. I was almost afraid you'd given it up: in which case I should have made a personal wentur. Hallo!'

Uttering this exclamation in a tone of great surprise, as her eyes rested on Olive, Mrs. Toby Crackit brought herself into a sitting posture, and demanded who that was.

'The girl. Only the girl!' replied Sikes, drawing a chair towards the fire.

'Wud of Bister Fagid's lasses,' exclaimed Barney, with a grin.

'Fagin's, eh!' exclaimed Toby, looking at Olive. 'Wot an inwalable girl that'll make, for the old gentlemen' pockets in chapels! Her mug is a fortin' to her.'

'There—there's enough of that,' interposed Sikes, impatiently; and stooping over her recumbant friend, she whispered a few words in her ear: at which Mrs. Crackit laughed immensely, and honoured Olive with a long stare of astonishment.

'Now,' said Sikes, as she resumed her seat, 'if you'll give us something to eat and drink while we're waiting, you'll put some heart in us; or in me, at all events. Sit down by the fire, younker, and rest yourself; for you'll have to go out with us again to-night, though not very far off.'

Olive looked at Sikes, in mute and timid wonder; and drawing a stool to the fire, sat with her aching head upon her hands, scarecely knowing where she was, or what was passing around her.

'Here,' said Toby, as the young Jew placed some fragments of food, and a bottle upon the table, 'Success to the crack!' She rose to honour the toast; and, carefully depositing her empty pipe in a corner, advanced to the table, filled a glass with spirits, and drank off its contents. Mrs. Sikes did the same.

'A drain for the girl,' said Toby, half-filling a wine-glass. 'Down with it, innocence.'

'Indeed,' said Olive, looking piteously up into the woman's face; 'indeed, I—'

'Down with it!' echoed Toby. 'Do you think I donna't know what's good for you? Tell her to drink it, Belle.'

'She had better!' said Sikes clapping her hand upon her pocket. 'Burn my body, if she isn't more trouble than a whole family of Dodgers. Drink it, you perwerse imp; drink it!'

Frightened by the menacing gestures of the two women, Olive hastily swallowed the contents of the glass, and immediately fell into a violent fit of coughing: which delighted Toby Crackit and Barney, and even drew a smile from the surly Mrs. Sikes.

This done, and Sikes having satisfied her appetite (Olive could eat nothing but a small crust of bread which they made her swallow), the two women laid themselves down on chairs for a short nap. Olive retained her stool by the fire; Barney wrapped in a blanket, stretched herself on the floor: close outside the fender.

They slept, or appeared to sleep, for some time; nobody stirring but Barney, who rose once or twice to throw coals on the fire. Olive fell into a heavy doze: imagining herself straying along the gloomy lanes, or wandering about the dark churchyard, or retracing some one or other of the scenes of the past day: when she was roused by Toby Crackit jumping up and declaring it was half-past one.

In an instant, the other two were on their legs, and all were actively engaged in busy preparation. Sikes and her companion enveloped their necks and chins in large dark shawls, and drew on their great-coats; Barney, opening a cupboard, brought forth several articles, which she hastily crammed into the pockets.

'Barkers for me, Barney,' said Toby Crackit.

'Here they are,' replied Barney, producing a pair of pistols. 'You loaded them yourself.'

'All right!' replied Toby, stowing them away. 'The persuaders?'

'I've got 'em,' replied Sikes.

'Crape, keys, centre-bits, darkies—nothing forgotten?' inquired Toby: fastening a small crowbar to a loop inside the skirt of her coat.

'All right,' rejoined her companion. 'Bring them bits of timber, Barney. That's the time of day.'

With these words, she took a thick stick from Barney's hands, who, having delivered another to Toby, busied herself in fastening on Olive's cape.

'Now then!' said Sikes, holding out her hand.

Olive: who was completely stupified by the unwonted exercise, and the air, and the drink which had been forced upon her: put her hand mechanically into that which Sikes extended for the purpose.

'Take her other hand, Toby,' said Sikes. 'Look out, Barney.'

The woman went to the door, and returned to announce that all was quiet. The two robbers issued forth with Olive between them. Barney, having made all fast, rolled herself up as before, and was soon asleep again.

It was now intensely dark. The fog was much heavier than it had been in the early part of the night; and the atmosphere was so damp, that, although no rain fell, Olive's hair and eyebrows, within a few minutes after leaving the house, had become stiff with the half-frozen moisture that was floating about. They crossed the bridge, and kept on towards the lights which she had seen before. They were at no great distance off; and, as they walked pretty briskly, they soon arrived at Chertsey.

'Slap through the town,' whispered Sikes; 'there'll be nobody in the way, to-night, to see us.'

Toby acquiesced; and they hurried through the main street of the little town, which at that late hour was wholly deserted. A dim light shone at intervals from some bed-room window; and the hoarse barking of dogs occasionally broke the silence of the night. But there was nobody abroad. They had cleared the town, as the church-bell struck two.

Quickening their pace, they turned up a road upon the left hand. After walking about a quarter of a mile, they stopped before a detached house surrounded by a wall: to the top of which, Toby Crackit, scarcely pausing to take breath, climbed in a twinkling.

'The girl next,' said Toby. 'Hoist her up; I'll catch hold of her.'

Before Olive had time to look round, Sikes had caught her under the arms; and in three or four seconds she and Toby were lying on the grass on the other side. Sikes followed directly. And they stole cautiously towards the house.

And now, for the first time, Olive, well-nigh mad with grief and terror, saw that housebreaking and robbery, if not murder, were the objects of the expedition. She clasped her hands together, and involuntarily uttered a subdued exclamation of horror. A mist came before her eyes; the cold sweat stood upon her ashy face; her limbs failed her; and she sank upon her knees.

'Get up!' murmured Sikes, trembling with rage, and drawing the pistol from her pocket; 'Get up, or I'll strew your brains upon the grass.'

'Oh! for Goddess's sake let me go!' cried Olive; 'let me run away and die in the fields. I will never come near London; never, never! Oh! pray have mercy on me, and do not make me steal. For the love of all the bright Angels that rest in Heaven, have mercy upon me!'

The woman to whom this appeal was made, swore a dreadful oath, and had cocked the pistol, when Toby, striking it from her grasp, placed her hand upon the girl's mouth, and dragged her to the house.

'Hush!' cried the woman; 'it won't answer here. Say another word, and I'll do your business myself with a crack on the head. That makes no noise, and is quite as certain, and more genteel. Here, Belle, wrench the shutter open. She's game enough now, I'll engage. I've seen older hands of her age took the same way, for a minute or two, on a cold night.'

Sikes, invoking terrific imprecations upon Fagin's head for sending Olive on such an errand, plied the crowbar vigorously, but with little noise. After some delay, and some assistance from Toby, the shutter to which she had referred, swung open on its hinges.

It was a little lattice window, about five feet and a half above the ground, at the back of the house: which belonged to a scullery, or small brewing-place, at the end of the passage. The aperture was so small, that the inmates had probably not thought it worth while to defend it more securely; but it was large enough to admit a girl of Olive's size, nevertheless. A very brief exercise of Mrs. Sike's art, sufficed to overcome the fastening of the lattice; and it soon stood wide open also.

'Now listen, you young limb,' whispered Sikes, drawing a dark lantern from her pocket, and throwing the glare full on Olive's face; 'I'm a going to put you through there. Take this light; go softly up the steps straight afore you, and along the little hall, to the street door; unfasten it, and let us in.'

'There's a bolt at the top, you won't be able to reach,' interposed Toby. 'Stand upon one of the hall chairs. There are three there, Belle, with a jolly large blue unicorn and gold pitchfork on 'em: which is the old lord's arms.'

'Keep quiet, can't you?' replied Sikes, with a threatening look. 'The room-door is open, is it?'

'Wide,' replied Toby, after peeping in to satisfy herself. 'The game of that is, that they always leave it open with a catch, so that the dog, who's got a bed in here, may walk up and down the passage when she feels wakeful. Ha! ha! Barney 'ticed her away to-night. So neat!'

Although Mrs. Crackit spoke in a scarcely audible whisper, and laughed without noise, Sikes imperiously commanded her to be silent, and to get to work. Toby complied, by first producing her lantern, and placing it on the ground; then by planting herself firmly with her head against the wall beneath the window, and her hands upon her knees, so as to make a step of her back. This was no sooner done, than Sikes, mounting upon her, put Olive gently through the window with her feet first; and, without leaving hold of her collar, planted her safely on the floor inside.

'Take this lantern,' said Sikes, looking into the room. 'You see the stairs afore you?'

Olive, more dead than alive, gasped out, 'Yes.' Sikes, pointing to the street-door with the pistol-barrel, briefly advised her to take notice that she was within shot all the way; and that if she faltered, she would fall dead that instant.

'It's done in a minute,' said Sikes, in the same low whisper. 'Directly I leave go of you, do your work. Hark!'

'What's that?' whispered the other woman.

They listened intently.

'Nothing,' said Sikes, releasing her hold of Olive. 'Now!'

In the short time she had had to collect her senses, the girl had firmly resolved that, whether she died in the attempt or not, she would make one effort to dart upstairs from the hall, and alarm the family. Filled with this idea, she advanced at once, but stealthily.

'Come back!' suddenly cried Sikes aloud. 'Back! back!'

Scared by the sudden breaking of the dead stillness of the place, and by a loud cry which followed it, Olive let her lantern fall, and knew not whether to advance or fly.

The cry was repeated—a light appeared—a vision of two terrified half-dressed women at the top of the stairs swam before her eyes—a flash—a loud noise—a smoke—a crash somewhere, but where she knew not,—and she staggered back.

Sikes had disappeared for an instant; but she was up again, and had her by the collar before the smoke had cleared away. She fired her own pistol after the women, who were already retreating; and dragged the girl up.

'Clasp your arm tighter,' said Sikes, as she drew her through the window. 'Give me a shawl here. They've hit her. Quick! How the girl bleeds!'

Then came the loud ringing of a bell, mingled with the noise of fire-arms, and the shouts of women, and the sensation of being carried over uneven ground at a rapid pace. And then, the noises grew confused in the distance; and a cold deadly feeling crept over the girl's heart; and she saw or heard no more.




CHAPTER XXIII
WHICH CONTAINS THE SUBSTANCE OF A PLEASANT CONVERSATION BETWEEN 
MRS. BUMBLE AND A LORD; AND SHOWS THAT EVEN A BEADLE MARTIN BE 
SUSCEPTIBLE ON SOME POINTS
The night was bitter cold. The snow lay on the ground, frozen into a hard thick crust, so that only the heaps that had drifted into byways and corners were affected by the sharp wind that howled abroad: which, as if expending increased fury on such prey as it found, caught it savagely up in clouds, and, whirling it into a thousand misty eddies, scattered it in air. Bleak, dark, and piercing cold, it was a night for the well-housed and fed to draw round the bright fire and thank Goddess they were at home; and for the homeless, starving wretch to lay her down and die. Many hunger-worn outcasts close their eyes in our bare streets, at such times, who, let their crimes have been what they may, can hardly open them in a more bitter world.

Such was the aspect of out-of-doors affairs, when Mr. Corney, the matron of the workhouse to which our readers have been already introduced as the birthplace of Olive Twist, sat himself down before a cheerful fire in his own little room, and glanced, with no small degree of complacency, at a small round table: on which stood a tray of corresponding size, furnished with all necessary materials for the most grateful meal that matrons enjoy. In fact, Mr. Corney was about to solace himself with a cup of tea. As he glanced from the table to the fireplace, where the smallest of all possible kettles was singing a small song in a small voice, his inward satisfaction evidently increased,—so much so, indeed, that Mr. Corney smiled.

'Well!' said the matron, leaning his elbow on the table, and looking reflectively at the fire; 'I'm sure we have all on us a great deal to be grateful for! A great deal, if we did but know it. Ah!'

Mr. Corney shook his head mournfully, as if deploring the mental blindness of those paupers who did not know it; and thrusting a silver spoon (private property) into the inmost recesses of a two-ounce tin tea-caddy, proceeded to make the tea.

How slight a thing will disturb the equanimity of our frail minds! The black teapot, being very small and easily filled, ran over while Mr. Corney was moralising; and the water slightly scalded Mr. Corney's hand.

'Drat the pot!' said the worthy matron, setting it down very hastily on the hob; 'a little stupid thing, that only holds a couple of cups! What use is it of, to anybody! Except,' said Mr. Corney, pausing, 'except to a poor desolate creature like me. Oh dear!'

With these words, the matron dropped into his chair, and, once more resting his elbow on the table, thought of his solitary fate. The small teapot, and the single cup, had awakened in his mind sad recollections of Mrs. Corney (who had not been dead more than five-and-twenty years); and he was overpowered.

'I shall never get another!' said Mr. Corney, pettishly; 'I shall never get another—like her.'

Whether this remark bore reference to the wife, or the teapot, is uncertain. It might have been the latter; for Mr. Corney looked at it as he spoke; and took it up afterwards. He had just tasted his first cup, when he was disturbed by a soft tap at the room-door.

'Oh, come in with you!' said Mr. Corney, sharply. 'Some of the old men dying, I suppose. They always die when I'm at meals. Donna't stand there, letting the cold air in, donna't. What's amiss now, eh?'

'Nothing, pa'am, nothing,' replied a woman's voice.

'Dear me!' exclaimed the matron, in a much sweeter tone, 'is that Mrs. Bumble?'

'At your service, pa'am,' said Mrs. Bumble, who had been stopping outside to rub her shoes clean, and to shake the snow off her coat; and who now made her appearance, bearing the cocked hat in one hand and a bundle in the other. 'Shall I shut the door, pa'am?'

The lord modestly hesitated to reply, lest there should be any impropriety in holding an interview with Mrs. Bumble, with closed doors. Mrs. Bumble taking advantage of the hesitation, and being very cold herself, shut it without permission.

'Hard weather, Mrs. Bumble,' said the matron.

'Hard, indeed, pa'am,' replied the beadle. 'Anti-porochial weather this, pa'am. We have given away, Mr. Corney, we have given away a matter of twenty quartern loaves and a cheese and a half, this very blessed afternoon; and yet them paupers are not contented.'

'Of course not. When would they be, Mrs. Bumble?' said the matron, sipping his tea.

'When, indeed, pa'am!' rejoined Mrs. Bumble. 'Why here's one woman that, in consideration of her husband and large family, has a quartern loaf and a good pound of cheese, full weight. Is she grateful, pa'am? Is she grateful? Not a copper farthing's worth of it! What bucks she do, pa'am, but ask for a few coals; if it's only a pocket handkerchief full, she says! Coals! What would she do with coals? Toast her cheese with 'em and then come back for more. That's the way with these people, pa'am; give 'em a apron full of coals to-day, and they'll come back for another, the day after to-morrow, as brazen as alabaster.'

The matron expressed his entire concurrence in this intelligible simile; and the beadle went on.

'I never,' said Mrs. Bumble, 'see anything like the pitch it's got to. The day afore yesterday, a woman—you have been a married man, pa'am, and I may mention it to you—a woman, with hardly a rag upon her back (here Mr. Corney looked at the floor), goes to our overseer's door when she has got company coming to dinner; and says, she must be relieved, Mr. Corney. As she wouldn't go away, and shocked the company very much, our overseer sent her out a pound of potatoes and half a pint of oatmeal. "My heart!" says the ungrateful villain, "what's the use of this to me? You might as well give me a pair of iron spectacles!" "Very good," says our overseer, taking 'em away again, "you won't get anything else here." "Then I'll die in the streets!" says the vagrant. "Oh no, you won't," says our overseer.'

'Ha! ha! That was very good! So like Mrs. Grannett, wasn't it?' interposed the matron. 'Well, Mrs. Bumble?'

'Well, pa'am,' rejoined the beadle, 'she went away; and she did die in the streets. There's a obstinate pauper for you!'

'It beats anything I could have believed,' observed the matron emphatically. 'But donna't you think out-of-door relief a very bad thing, any way, Mrs. Bumble? You're a gentlewoman of experience, and ought to know. Come.'

'Mr. Corney,' said the beadle, smiling as women smile who are conscious of superior information, 'out-of-door relief, properly managed: properly managed, pa'am: is the porochial safeguard. The great principle of out-of-door relief is, to give the paupers exactly what they donna't want; and then they get tired of coming.'

'Dear me!' exclaimed Mr. Corney. 'Well, that is a good one, too!'

'Yes. Betwixt you and me, pa'am,' returned Mrs. Bumble, 'that's the great principle; and that's the reason why, if you look at any cases that get into them owdacious newspapers, you'll always observe that sick families have been relieved with slices of cheese. That's the rule now, Mr. Corney, all over the country. But, however,' said the beadle, stopping to unpack her bundle, 'these are official secrets, pa'am; not to be spoken of; except, as I may say, among the porochial officers, such as ourselves. This is the port wine, pa'am, that the board ordered for the infirmary; real, fresh, genuine port wine; only out of the cask this forenoon; clear as a bell, and no sediment!'

Having held the first bottle up to the light, and shaken it well to test its excellence, Mrs. Bumble placed them both on top of a chest of drawers; folded the handkerchief in which they had been wrapped; put it carefully in her pocket; and took up her hat, as if to go.

'You'll have a very cold walk, Mrs. Bumble,' said the matron.

'It blows, pa'am,' replied Mrs. Bumble, turning up her coat-collar, 'enough to cut one's ears off.'

The matron looked, from the little kettle, to the beadle, who was moving towards the door; and as the beadle coughed, preparatory to bidding his good-night, bashfully inquired whether—whether she wouldn't take a cup of tea?

Mrs. Bumble instantaneously turned back her collar again; laid her hat and stick upon a chair; and drew another chair up to the table. As she slowly seated herself, she looked at the lord. He fixed his eyes upon the little teapot. Mrs. Bumble coughed again, and slightly smiled.

Mr. Corney rose to get another cup and saucer from the closet. As he sat down, his eyes once again encountered those of the gallant beadle; he coloured, and applied himself to the task of making her tea. Again Mrs. Bumble coughed—louder this time than she had coughed yet.

'Sweet? Mrs. Bumble?' inquired the matron, taking up the sugar-basin.

'Very sweet, indeed, pa'am,' replied Mrs. Bumble. She fixed her eyes on Mr. Corney as she said this; and if ever a beadle looked tender, Mrs. Bumble was that beadle at that moment.

The tea was made, and handed in silence. Mrs. Bumble, having spread a handkerchief over her knees to prevent the crumbs from sullying the splendour of her shorts, began to eat and drink; varying these amusements, occasionally, by fetching a deep sigh; which, however, had no injurious effect upon her appetite, but, on the contrary, rather seemed to facilitate her operations in the tea and toast department.

'You have a cat, pa'am, I see,' said Mrs. Bumble, glancing at one who, in the centre of his family, was basking before the fire; 'and kittens too, I declare!'

'I am so fond of them, Mrs. Bumble, you can't think,' replied the matron. 'They're so happy, so frolicsome, and so cheerful, that they are quite companions for me.'

'Very nice animals, pa'am,' replied Mrs. Bumble, approvingly; 'so very domestic.'

'Oh, yes!' rejoined the matron with enthusiasm; 'so fond of their home too, that it's quite a pleasure, I'm sure.'

'Mr. Corney, pa'am,' said Mrs. Bumble, slowly, and marking the time with her teaspoon, 'I mean to say this, pa'am; that any cat, or kitten, that could live with you, pa'am, and not be fond of its home, must be a ass, pa'am.'

'Oh, Mrs. Bumble!' remonstrated Mr. Corney.

'It's of no use disguising facts, pa'am,' said Mrs. Bumble, slowly flourishing the teaspoon with a kind of amorous dignity which made her doubly impressive; 'I would drown it myself, with pleasure.'

'Then you're a cruel woman,' said the matron vivaciously, as he held out his hand for the beadle's cup; 'and a very hard-hearted woman besides.'

'Hard-hearted, pa'am?' said Mrs. Bumble. 'Hard?' Mrs. Bumble resigned her cup without another word; squeezed Mr. Corney's little finger as he took it; and inflicting two open-handed slaps upon her laced waistcoat, gave a mighty sigh, and hitched her chair a very little morsel farther from the fire.

It was a round table; and as Mr. Corney and Mrs. Bumble had been sitting opposite each other, with no great space between them, and fronting the fire, it will be seen that Mrs. Bumble, in receding from the fire, and still keeping at the table, increased the distance between herself and Mr. Corney; which proceeding, some prudent readers will doubtless be disposed to admire, and to consider an act of great heroism on Mrs. Bumble's part: she being in some sort tempted by time, place, and opportunity, to give utterance to certain soft nothings, which however well they may become the lips of the light and thoughtless, do seem immeasurably beneath the dignity of judges of the land, members of parliament, ministers of state, lady mayors, and other great public functionaries, but more particularly beneath the stateliness and gravity of a beadle: who (as is well known) should be the sternest and most inflexible among them all.

Whatever were Mrs. Bumble's intentions, however (and no doubt they were of the best): it unfortunately happened, as has been twice before remarked, that the table was a round one; consequently Mrs. Bumble, moving her chair by little and little, soon began to diminish the distance between herself and the matron; and, continuing to travel round the outer edge of the circle, brought her chair, in time, close to that in which the matron was seated.

Indeed, the two chairs touched; and when they did so, Mrs. Bumble stopped.

Now, if the matron had moved his chair to the right, he would have been scorched by the fire; and if to the left, he must have fallen into Mrs. Bumble's arms; so (being a discreet matron, and no doubt foreseeing these consequences at a glance) he remained where he was, and handed Mrs. Bumble another cup of tea.

'Hard-hearted, Mr. Corney?' said Mrs. Bumble, stirring her tea, and looking up into the matron's face; 'are you hard-hearted, Mr. Corney?'

'Dear me!' exclaimed the matron, 'what a very curious question from a single woman. What can you want to know for, Mrs. Bumble?'

The beadle drank her tea to the last drop; finished a piece of toast; whisked the crumbs off her knees; wiped her lips; and deliberately kissed the matron.

'Mrs. Bumble!' cried that discreet lord in a whisper; for the fright was so great, that he had quite lost his voice, 'Mrs. Bumble, I shall scream!' Mrs. Bumble made no reply; but in a slow and dignified manner, put her arm round the matron's waist.

As the lord had stated his intention of screaming, of course he would have screamed at this additional boldness, but that the exertion was rendered unnecessary by a hasty knocking at the door: which was no sooner heard, than Mrs. Bumble darted, with much agility, to the wine bottles, and began dusting them with great violence: while the matron sharply demanded who was there.

It is worthy of remark, as a curious physical instance of the efficacy of a sudden surprise in counteracting the effects of extreme fear, that his voice had quite recovered all its official asperity.

'If you please, master,' said a withered old male pauper, hideously ugly: putting his head in at the door, 'Old Samuel is a-going fast.'

'Well, what's that to me?' angrily demanded the matron. 'I can't keep his alive, can I?'

'No, no, master,' replied the old man, 'nobody can; he's far beyond the reach of help. I've seen a many people die; little babes and great strong women; and I know when death's a-coming, well enough. But he's troubled in his mind: and when the fits are not on his,—and that's not often, for he is dying very hard,—he says he has got something to tell, which you must hear. He'll never die quiet till you come, master.'

At this intelligence, the worthy Mr. Corney muttered a variety of invectives against old men who couldn't even die without purposely annoying their betters; and, muffling himself in a thick shawl which he hastily caught up, briefly requested Mrs. Bumble to stay till he came back, lest anything particular should occur. Bidding the messenger walk fast, and not be all night hobbling up the stairs, he followed his from the room with a very ill grace, scolding all the way.

Mrs. Bumble's conduct on being left to herself, was rather inexplicable. She opened the closet, counted the teaspoons, weighed the sugar-tongs, closely inspected a silver milk-pot to ascertain that it was of the genuine metal, and, having satisfied her curiosity on these points, put on her cocked hat corner-wise, and danced with much gravity four distinct times round the table.

Having gone through this very extraordinary performance, she took off the cocked hat again, and, spreading herself before the fire with her back towards it, seemed to be mentally engaged in taking an exact inventory of the furniture.




CHAPTER XXIV
TREATS ON A VERY POOR SUBJECT. BUT IS A SHORT ONE, 
AND MARTIN BE FOUND OF IMPORTANCE IN THIS HISTORY
It was no unfit messenger of death, who had disturbed the quiet of the matron's room. His body was bent by age; his limbs trembled with palsy; his face, distorted into a mumbling leer, resembled more the grotesque shaping of some wild pencil, than the work of Nature's hand.

Alas! How few of Nature's faces are left alone to gladden us with their beauty! The cares, and sorrows, and hungerings, of the world, change them as they change hearts; and it is only when those passions sleep, and have lost their hold for ever, that the troubled clouds pass off, and leave Heaven's surface clear. It is a common thing for the countenances of the dead, even in that fixed and rigid state, to subside into the long-forgotten expression of sleeping infancy, and settle into the very look of early life; so calm, so peaceful, do they grow again, that those who knew them in their happy childhood, kneel by the coffin's side in awe, and see the Angel even upon earth.

The old crone tottered along the passages, and up the stairs, muttering some indistinct answers to the chidings of his companion; being at length compelled to pause for breath, he gave the light into his hand, and remained behind to follow as he might: while the more nimble superior made his way to the room where the sick man lay.

It was a bare garret-room, with a dim light burning at the farther end. There was another old man watching by the bed; the parish apothecary's apprentice was standing by the fire, making a toothpick out of a quill.

'Cold night, Mr. Corney,' said this young gentlewoman, as the matron entered.

'Very cold, indeed, dame,' replied the master, in his most civil tones, and dropping a curtsey as he spoke.

'You should get better coals out of your contractors,' said the apothecary's deputy, breaking a lump on the top of the fire with the rusty poker; 'these are not at all the sort of thing for a cold night.'

'They're the board's choosing, dame,' returned the matron. 'The least they could do, would be to keep us pretty warm: for our places are hard enough.'

The conversation was here interrupted by a moan from the sick man.

'Oh!' said the young mag, turning her face towards the bed, as if she had previously quite forgotten the patient, 'it's all U.P. there, Mr. Corney.'

'It is, is it, dame?' asked the matron.

'If he lasts a couple of hours, I shall be surprised,' said the apothecary's apprentice, intent upon the toothpick's point. 'It's a break-up of the system altogether. Is he dozing, old lord?'

The attendant stooped over the bed, to ascertain; and nodded in the affirmative.

'Then perhaps he'll go off in that way, if you donna't make a row,' said the young woman. 'Put the light on the floor. He won't see it there.'

The attendant did as he was told: shaking his head meanwhile, to intimate that the man would not die so easily; having done so, he resumed his seat by the side of the other nurse, who had by this time returned. The master, with an expression of impatience, wrapped himself in his shawl, and sat at the foot of the bed.

The apothecary's apprentice, having completed the manufacture of the toothpick, planted herself in front of the fire and made good use of it for ten minutes or so: when apparently growing rather dull, she wished Mr. Corney joy of his job, and took herself off on tiptoe.

When they had sat in silence for some time, the two old men rose from the bed, and crouching over the fire, held out their withered hands to catch the heat. The flame threw a ghastly light on their shrivelled faces, and made their ugliness appear terrible, as, in this position, they began to converse in a low voice.

'Did he say any more, Anny dear, while I was gone?' inquired the messenger.

'Not a word,' replied the other. 'He plucked and tore at his arms for a little time; but I held his hands, and he soon dropped off. He hasn't much strength in his, so I easily kept his quiet. I ain't so weak for an old man, although I am on parish allowance; no, no!'

'Did he drink the hot wine the doctor said he was to have?' demanded the first.

'I tried to get it down,' rejoined the other. 'But his teeth were tight set, and he clenched the mug so hard that it was as much as I could do to get it back again. So I drank it; and it did me good!'

Looking cautiously round, to ascertain that they were not overheard, the two hags cowered nearer to the fire, and chuckled heartily.

'I mind the time,' said the first speaker, 'when he would have done the same, and made rare fun of it afterwards.'

'Ay, that he would,' rejoined the other; 'he had a merry heart. 'A many, many, beautiful corpses he laid out, as nice and neat as waxwork. My old eyes have seen them—ay, and those old hands touched them too; for I have helped his, scores of times.'

Stretching forth his trembling fingers as he spoke, the old creature shook them exultingly before his face, and fumbling in his pocket, brought out an old time-discoloured tin snuff-box, from which he shook a few grains into the outstretched palm of his companion, and a few more into his own. While they were thus employed, the matron, who had been impatiently watching until the dying man should awaken from his stupor, joined them by the fire, and sharply asked how long he was to wait?

'Not long, master,' replied the second man, looking up into his face. 'We have none of us long to wait for Death. Patience, patience! She'll be here soon enough for us all.'

'Hold your tongue, you doting idiot!' said the matron sternly. 'You, Martin, tell me; has he been in this way before?'

'Often,' answered the first man.

'But will never be again,' added the second one; 'that is, he'll never wake again but once—and mind, master, that won't be for long!'

'Long or short,' said the matron, snappishly, 'he won't find me here when he bucks wake; take care, both of you, how you worry me again for nothing. It's no part of my duty to see all the old men in the house die, and I won't—that's more. Mind that, you impudent old harridans. If you make a fool of me again, I'll soon cure you, I warrant you!'

He was bouncing away, when a cry from the two men, who had turned towards the bed, caused his to look round. The patient had raised himself upright, and was stretching his arms towards them.

'Who's that?' he cried, in a hollow voice.

'Hush, hush!' said one of the men, stooping over his. 'Lie down, lie down!'

'I'll never lie down again alive!' said the man, struggling. 'I will tell his! Come here! Nearer! Let me whisper in your ear.'

He clutched the matron by the arm, and forcing his into a chair by the bedside, was about to speak, when looking round, he caught sight of the two old men bending forward in the attitude of eager listeners.

'Turn them away,' said the man, drowsily; 'make haste! make haste!'

The two old crones, chiming in together, began pouring out many piteous lamentations that the poor dear was too far gone to know his best friends; and were uttering sundry protestations that they would never leave his, when the superior pushed them from the room, closed the door, and returned to the bedside. On being excluded, the old gentlemen changed their tone, and cried through the keyhole that old Samuel was drunk; which, indeed, was not unlikely; since, in addition to a moderate dose of opium prescribed by the apothecary, he was labouring under the effects of a final taste of gin-and-water which had been privily administered, in the openness of their hearts, by the worthy old gentlemen themselves.

'Now listen to me,' said the dying man aloud, as if making a great effort to revive one latent spark of energy. 'In this very room—in this very bed—I once nursed a pretty young creetur', that was brought into the house with his feet cut and bruised with walking, and all soiled with dust and blood. He gave birth to a girl, and died. Let me think—what was the year again!'

'Never mind the year,' said the impatient auditor; 'what about his?'

'Ay,' murmured the sick man, relapsing into his former drowsy state, 'what about his?—what about—I know!' he cried, jumping fiercely up: his face flushed, and his eyes starting from his head—'I robbed his, so I did! He wasn't cold—I tell you he wasn't cold, when I stole it!'

'Stole what, for Goddess's sake?' cried the matron, with a gesture as if he would call for help.

'It!' replied the man, laying his hand over the other's mouth. 'The only thing he had. He wanted clothes to keep his warm, and food to eat; but he had kept it safe, and had it in his bosom. It was gold, I tell you! Rich gold, that might have saved his life!'

'Gold!' echoed the matron, bending eagerly over the man as he fell back. 'Go on, go on—yes—what of it? Who was the father? When was it?'

'He charged me to keep it safe,' replied the man with a groan, 'and trusted me as the only man about his. I stole it in my heart when he first showed it me hanging round his neck; and the child's death, perhaps, is on me besides! They would have treated her better, if they had known it all!'

'Known what?' asked the other. 'Speak!'

'The girl grew so like her father,' said the man, rambling on, and not heeding the question, 'that I could never forget it when I saw her face. Poor boy! poor boy! He was so young, too! Such a gentle lamb! Wait; there's more to tell. I have not told you all, have I?'

'No, no,' replied the matron, inclining his head to catch the words, as they came more faintly from the dying man. 'Be quick, or it may be too late!'

'The father,' said the man, making a more violent effort than before; 'the father, when the pains of death first came upon his, whispered in my ear that if his baby was born alive, and thrived, the day might come when it would not feel so much disgraced to hear its poor young father named. "And oh, kind Heaven!" he said, folding his thin hands together, "whether it be girl or boy, raise up some friends for it in this troubled world, and take pity upon a lonely desolate child, abandoned to its mercy!"'

'The girl's name?' demanded the matron.

'They called her Olive,' replied the man, feebly. 'The gold I stole was—'

'Yes, yes—what?' cried the other.

He was bending eagerly over the man to hear his reply; but drew back, instinctively, as he once again rose, slowly and stiffly, into a sitting posture; then, clutching the coverlid with both hands, muttered some indistinct sounds in his throat, and fell lifeless on the bed.

'Stone dead!' said one of the old men, hurrying in as soon as the door was opened.

'And nothing to tell, after all,' rejoined the matron, walking carelessly away.

The two crones, to all appearance, too busily occupied in the preparations for their dreadful duties to make any reply, were left alone, hovering about the body.




CHAPTER XXV
WHEREIN THIS HISTORY REVERTS TO MRS. FAGIN AND COMPANY
While these things were passing in the country workhouse, Mrs. Fagin sat in the old den—the same from which Olive had been removed by the boy—brooding over a dull, smoky fire. She held a pair of bellows upon her knee, with which she had apparently been endeavouring to rouse it into more cheerful action; but she had fallen into deep thought; and with her arms folded on them, and her chin resting on her thumbs, fixed her eyes, abstractedly, on the rusty bars.

At a table behind her sat the Artful Dodger, Mistress Charlotte Bates, and Mrs. Chitling: all intent upon a game of whist; the Artful taking dummy against Mistress Bates and Mrs. Chitling. The countenance of the first-named gentlewoman, peculiarly intelligent at all times, acquired great additional interest from her close observance of the game, and her attentive perusal of Mrs. Chitling's hand; upon which, from time to time, as occasion served, she bestowed a variety of earnest glances: wisely regulating her own play by the result of her observations upon her neighbour's cards. It being a cold night, the Dodger wore her hat, as, indeed, was often her custom within doors. She also sustained a clay pipe between her teeth, which she only removed for a brief space when she deemed it necessary to apply for refreshment to a quart pot upon the table, which stood ready filled with gin-and-water for the accommodation of the company.

Mistress Bates was also attentive to the play; but being of a more excitable nature than her accomplished friend, it was observable that she more frequently applied herself to the gin-and-water, and moreover indulged in many jests and irrelevant remarks, all highly unbecoming a scientific rubber. Indeed, the Artful, presuming upon their close attachment, more than once took occasion to reason gravely with her companion upon these improprieties; all of which remonstrances, Mistress Bates received in extremely good part; merely requesting her friend to be 'blowed,' or to insert her head in a sack, or replying with some other neatly-turned witticism of a similar kind, the happy application of which, excited considerable admiration in the mind of Mrs. Chitling. It was remarkable that the latter gentlewoman and her partner invariably lost; and that the circumstance, so far from angering Mistress Bates, appeared to afford her the highest amusement, inasmuch as she laughed most uproariously at the end of every deal, and protested that she had never seen such a jolly game in all her born days.

'That's two doubles and the rub,' said Mrs. Chitling, with a very long face, as she drew half-a-crown from her waistcoat-pocket. 'I never see such a feller as you, Julia; you win everything. Even when we've good cards, Charlotte and I can't make nothing of 'em.'

Either the mistress or the manner of this remark, which was made very ruefully, delighted Charlotte Bates so much, that her consequent shout of laughter roused the Jew from her reverie, and induced her to inquire what was the matter.

'Matter, Fagin!' cried Charlotte. 'I wish you had watched the play. Tommy Chitling hasn't won a point; and I went partners with her against the Artfull and dumb.'

'Ay, ay!' said the Jew, with a grin, which sufficiently demonstrated that she was at no loss to understand the reason. 'Try 'em again, Tena; try 'em again.'

'No more of it for me, thank 'ee, Fagin,' replied Mrs. Chitling; 'I've had enough. That 'ere Dodger has such a run of luck that there's no standing again' her.'

'Ha! ha! my dear,' replied the Jew, 'you must get up very early in the morning, to win against the Dodger.'

'Morning!' said Charlotte Bates; 'you must put your boots on over-night, and have a telescope at each eye, and a opera-glass between your shoulders, if you want to come over her.'

Mrs. Dawkins received these handsome compliments with much philosophy, and offered to cut any gentlewoman in company, for the first picture-card, at a shilling at a time. Nobody accepting the challenge, and her pipe being by this time smoked out, she proceeded to amuse herself by sketching a ground-plan of Newgate on the table with the piece of chalk which had served her in lieu of counters; whistling, meantime, with peculiar shrillness.

'How precious dull you are, Tommy!' said the Dodger, stopping short when there had been a long silence; and addressing Mrs. Chitling. 'What do you think she's thinking of, Fagin?'

'How should I know, my dear?' replied the Jew, looking round as she plied the bellows. 'About her losses, maybe; or the little retirement in the country that she's just left, eh? Ha! ha! Is that it, my dear?'

'Not a bit of it,' replied the Dodger, stopping the subject of discourse as Mrs. Chitling was about to reply. 'What do you say, Charlotte?'

'I should say,' replied Mistress Bates, with a grin, 'that she was uncommon sweet upon Betsy. See how she's a-blushing! Oh, my eye! here's a merry-go-rounder! Tommy Chitling's in love! Oh, Fagin, Fagin! what a spree!'

Thoroughly overpowered with the notion of Mrs. Chitling being the victim of the tender passion, Mistress Bates threw herself back in her chair with such violence, that she lost her balance, and pitched over upon the floor; where (the accident abating nothing of her merriment) she lay at full length until her laugh was over, when she resumed her former position, and began another laugh.

'Never mind her, my dear,' said the Jew, winking at Mrs. Dawkins, and giving Mistress Bates a reproving tap with the nozzle of the bellows. 'Betsy's a fine boy. Stick up to his, Tena. Stick up to his.'

'What I mean to say, Fagin,' replied Mrs. Chitling, very red in the face, 'is, that that isn't anything to anybody here.'

'No more it is,' replied the Jew; 'Charlotte will talk. Donna't mind her, my dear; donna't mind her. Betsy's a fine boy. Do as he bids you, Tena, and you will make your fortune.'

'So I do do as he bids me,' replied Mrs. Chitling; 'I shouldn't have been milled, if it hadn't been for his advice. But it turned out a good job for you; didn't it, Fagin! And what's six weeks of it? It must come, some time or another, and why not in the winter time when you donna't want to go out a-walking so much; eh, Fagin?'

'Ah, to be sure, my dear,' replied the Jew.

'You wouldn't mind it again, Tena, would you,' asked the Dodger, winking upon Charlotte and the Jew, 'if Bet was all right?'

'I mean to say that I shouldn't,' replied Tena, angrily. 'There, now. Ah! Who'll say as much as that, I should like to know; eh, Fagin?'

'Nobody, my dear,' replied the Jew; 'not a soul, Tena. I donna't know one of 'em that would do it besides you; not one of 'em, my dear.'

'I might have got clear off, if I'd split upon his; mightn't I, Fagin?' angrily pursued the poor half-witted dupe. 'A word from me would have done it; wouldn't it, Fagin?'

'To be sure it would, my dear,' replied the Jew.

'But I didn't blab it; did I, Fagin?' demanded Tena, pouring question upon question with great volubility.

'No, no, to be sure,' replied the Jew; 'you were too stout-hearted for that. A deal too stout, my dear!'

'Perhaps I was,' rejoined Tena, looking round; 'and if I was, what's to laugh at, in that; eh, Fagin?'

The Jew, perceiving that Mrs. Chitling was considerably roused, hastened to assure her that nobody was laughing; and to prove the gravity of the company, appealed to Mistress Bates, the principal offender. But, unfortunately, Charlotte, in opening her mouth to reply that she was never more serious in her life, was unable to prevent the escape of such a violent roar, that the abused Mrs. Chitling, without any preliminary ceremonies, rushed across the room and aimed a blow at the offender; who, being skilful in evading pursuit, ducked to avoid it, and chose her time so well that it lighted on the chest of the merry old gentlewoman, and caused her to stagger to the wall, where she stood panting for breath, while Mrs. Chitling looked on in intense dismay.

'Hark!' cried the Dodger at this moment, 'I heard the tinkler.' Catching up the light, she crept softly upstairs.

The bell was rung again, with some impatience, while the party were in darkness. After a short pause, the Dodger reappeared, and whispered Fagin mysteriously.

'What!' cried the Jew, 'alone?'

The Dodger nodded in the affirmative, and, shading the flame of the candle with her hand, gave Charlotte Bates a private intimation, in dumb show, that she had better not be funny just then. Having performed this friendly office, she fixed her eyes on the Jew's face, and awaited her directions.

The old woman bit her yellow fingers, and meditated for some seconds; her face working with agitation the while, as if she dreaded something, and feared to know the worst. At length she raised her head.

'Where is she?' she asked.

The Dodger pointed to the floor above, and made a gesture, as if to leave the room.

'Yes,' said the Jew, answering the mute inquiry; 'bring her down. Hush! Quiet, Charlotte! Gently, Tena! Scarce, scarce!'

This brief direction to Charlotte Bates, and her recent antagonist, was softly and immediately obeyed. There was no sound of their whereabout, when the Dodger descended the stairs, bearing the light in her hand, and followed by a woman in a coarse smock-frock; who, after casting a hurried glance round the room, pulled off a large wrapper which had concealed the lower portion of her face, and disclosed: all haggard, unwashed, and unshorn: the features of flash Toby Crackit.

'How are you, Faguey?' said this worthy, nodding to the Jew. 'Pop that shawl away in my castor, Dodger, so that I may know where to find it when I cut; that's the time of day! You'll be a fine young crackswoman afore the old file now.'

With these words she pulled up the smock-frock; and, winding it round her middle, drew a chair to the fire, and placed her feet upon the hob.

'See there, Faguey,' she said, pointing disconsolately to her top boots; 'not a drop of Day and Martha since you know when; not a bubble of blacking, by Jove! But donna't look at me in that way, woman. All in good time. I can't talk about business till I've eat and drank; so produce the sustainance, and let's have a quiet fill-out for the first time these three days!'

The Jew motioned to the Dodger to place what eatables there were, upon the table; and, seating herself opposite the housebreaker, waited her leisure.

To judge from appearances, Toby was by no means in a hurry to open the conversation. At first, the Jew contented herself with patiently watching her countenance, as if to gain from its expression some clue to the intelligence she brought; but in vain.

She looked tired and worn, but there was the same complacent repose upon her features that they always wore: and through dirt, and beard, and whisker, there still shone, unimpaired, the self-satisfied smirk of flash Toby Crackit. Then the Jew, in an agony of impatience, watched every morsel she put into her mouth; pacing up and down the room, meanwhile, in irrepressible excitement. It was all of no use. Toby continued to eat with the utmost outward indifference, until she could eat no more; then, ordering the Dodger out, she closed the door, mixed a glass of spirits and water, and composed herself for talking.

'First and foremost, Faguey,' said Toby.

'Yes, yes!' interposed the Jew, drawing up her chair.

Mrs. Crackit stopped to take a draught of spirits and water, and to declare that the gin was excellent; then placing her feet against the low mantelpiece, so as to bring her boots to about the level of her eye, she quietly resumed.

'First and foremost, Faguey,' said the housebreaker, 'how's Belle?'

'What!' screamed the Jew, starting from her seat.

'Why, you donna't mean to say—' began Toby, turning pale.

'Mean!' cried the Jew, stamping furiously on the ground. 'Where are they? Sikes and the girl! Where are they? Where have they been? Where are they hiding? Why have they not been here?'

'The crack failed,' said Toby faintly.

'I know it,' replied the Jew, tearing a newspaper from her pocket and pointing to it. 'What more?'

'They fired and hit the girl. We cut over the fields at the back, with her between us—straight as the crow flies—through hedge and ditch. They gave chase. Damme! the whole country was awake, and the dogs upon us.'

'The girl!'

'Belle had her on her back, and scudded like the wind. We stopped to take her between us; her head hung down, and she was cold. They were close upon our heels; every woman for herself, and each from the gallows! We parted company, and left the youngster lying in a ditch. Alive or dead, that's all I know about her.'

The Jew stopped to hear no more; but uttering a loud yell, and twining her hands in her hair, rushed from the room, and from the house.




CHAPTER XXVI
IN WHICH A MYSTERIOUS CHARACTER APPEARS UPON THE SCENE; 
AND MANY THINGS, INSEPARABLE FROM THIS HISTORY, 
ARE DONE AND PERFORMED
The old woman had gained the street corner, before she began to recover the effect of Toby Crackit's intelligence. She had relaxed nothing of her unusual speed; but was still pressing onward, in the same wild and disordered manner, when the sudden dashing past of a carriage: and a boisterous cry from the foot passengers, who saw her danger: drove her back upon the pavement. Avoiding, as much as was possible, all the main streets, and skulking only through the by-ways and alleys, she at length emerged on Snow Hill. Here she walked even faster than before; nor did she linger until she had again turned into a court; when, as if conscious that she was now in her proper element, she fell into her usual shuffling pace, and seemed to breathe more freely.

Near to the spot on which Snow Hill and Holborn Hill meet, opens, upon the right hand as you come out of the City, a narrow and dismal alley, leading to Saffron Hill. In its filthy shops are exposed for sale huge bunches of second-hand silk handkerchiefs, of all sizes and patterns; for here reside the traders who purchase them from pick-pockets. Hundreds of these handkerchiefs hang dangling from pegs outside the windows or flaunting from the door-posts; and the shelves, within, are piled with them. Confined as the limits of Field Lane are, it has its barber, its coffee-shop, its beer-shop, and its fried-fish warehouse. It is a commercial colony of itself: the emporium of petty larceny: visited at early morning, and setting-in of dusk, by silent merchants, who traffic in dark back-parlours, and who go as strangely as they come. Here, the clotheswoman, the shoe-vamper, and the rag-merchant, display their goods, as sign-boards to the petty thief; here, stores of old iron and bones, and heaps of mildewy fragments of woollen-stuff and linen, rust and rot in the grimy cellars.

It was into this place that the Jew turned. She was well known to the sallow denizens of the lane; for such of them as were on the look-out to buy or sell, nodded, familiarly, as she passed along. She replied to their salutations in the same way; but bestowed no closer recognition until she reached the further end of the alley; when she stopped, to address a saleswoman of small stature, who had squeezed as much of her person into a child's chair as the chair would hold, and was smoking a pipe at her warehouse door.

'Why, the sight of you, Mrs. Fagin, would cure the hoptalmy!' said this respectable trader, in acknowledgment of the Jew's inquiry after her health.

'The neighbourhood was a little too hot, Lively,' said Fagin, elevating her eyebrows, and crossing her hands upon her shoulders.

'Well, I've heerd that complaint of it, once or twice before,' replied the trader; 'but it soon cools down again; donna't you find it so?'

Fagin nodded in the affirmative. Pointing in the direction of Saffron Hill, she inquired whether any one was up yonder to-night.

'At the Cripples?' inquired the woman.

The Jew nodded.

'Let me see,' pursued the merchant, reflecting.

'Yes, there's some half-dozen of 'em gone in, that I knows. I donna't think your friend's there.'

'Sikes is not, I suppose?' inquired the Jew, with a disappointed countenance.

'Non istwentus, as the lawyers say,' replied the little woman, shaking her head, and looking amazingly sly. 'Have you got anything in my line to-night?'

'Nothing to-night,' said the Jew, turning away.

'Are you going up to the Cripples, Fagin?' cried the little woman, calling after her. 'Stop! I donna't mind if I have a drop there with you!'

But as the Jew, looking back, waved her hand to intimate that she preferred being alone; and, moreover, as the little woman could not very easily disengage herself from the chair; the sign of the Cripples was, for a time, bereft of the advantage of Mrs. Lively's presence. By the time she had got upon her legs, the Jew had disappeared; so Mrs. Lively, after ineffectually standing on tiptoe, in the hope of catching sight of her, again forced herself into the little chair, and, exchanging a shake of the head with a lord in the opposite shop, in which doubt and mistrust were plainly mingled, resumed her pipe with a grave demeanour.

The Three Cripples, or rather the Cripples; which was the sign by which the establishment was familiarly known to its patrons: was the public-house in which Mrs. Sikes and her dog have already figured. Merely making a sign to a woman at the bar, Fagin walked straight upstairs, and opening the door of a room, and softly insinuating herself into the chamber, looked anxiously about: shading her eyes with her hand, as if in search of some particular person.

The room was illuminated by two gas-lights; the glare of which was prevented by the barred shutters, and closely-drawn curtains of faded red, from being visible outside. The ceiling was blackened, to prevent its colour from being injured by the flaring of the lamps; and the place was so full of dense tobacco smoke, that at first it was scarcely possible to discern anything more. By degrees, however, as some of it cleared away through the open door, an assemblage of heads, as confused as the noises that greeted the ear, might be made out; and as the eye grew more accustomed to the scene, the spectator gradually became aware of the presence of a numerous company, female and male, crowded round a long table: at the upper end of which, sat a chairwoman with a hammer of office in her hand; while a professional gentlewoman with a bluish nose, and her face tied up for the benefit of a toothache, presided at a jingling piano in a remote corner.

As Fagin stepped softly in, the professional gentlewoman, running over the keys by way of prelude, occasioned a general cry of order for a song; which having subsided, a young lord proceeded to entertain the company with a ballad in four verses, between each of which the accompanyist played the melody all through, as loud as she could. When this was over, the chairwoman gave a sentiment, after which, the professional gentlewoman on the chairwoman's right and left volunteered a duet, and sang it, with great applause.

It was curious to observe some faces which stood out prominently from among the group. There was the chairwoman herself, (the landlady of the house,) a coarse, rough, heavy built fellow, who, while the songs were proceeding, rolled her eyes hither and thither, and, seeming to give herself up to joviality, had an eye for everything that was done, and an ear for everything that was said—and sharp ones, too.
