Near her were the singers: receiving, with professional indifference, the compliments of the company, and applying themselves, in turn, to a dozen proffered glasses of spirits and water, tendered by their more boisterous admirers; whose countenances, expressive of almost every vice in almost every grade, irresistibly attracted the attention, by their very repulsiveness. Cunning, ferocity, and drunkeness in all its stages, were there, in their strongest aspect; and men: some with the last lingering tinge of their early freshness almost fading as you looked: others with every mark and stamp of their sex utterly beaten out, and presenting but one loathsome blank of profligacy and crime; some mere boys, others but young men, and none past the prime of life; formed the darkest and saddest portion of this dreary picture.

Fagin, troubled by no grave emotions, looked eagerly from face to face while these proceedings were in progress; but apparently without meeting that of which she was in search. Succeeding, at length, in catching the eye of the woman who occupied the chair, she beckoned to her slightly, and left the room, as quietly as she had entered it.

'What can I do for you, Mrs. Fagin?' inquired the woman, as she followed her out to the landing. 'Won't you join us? They'll be delighted, every one of 'em.'

The Jew shook her head impatiently, and said in a whisper, 'Is she here?'

'No,' replied the woman.

'And no news of Barney?' inquired Fagin.

'None,' replied the landlady of the Cripples; for it was she. 'She won't stir till it's all safe. Depend on it, they're on the scent down there; and that if she moved, she'd blow upon the thing at once. She's all right enough, Barney is, else I should have heard of her. I'll pound it, that Barney's managing properly. Let her alone for that.'

'Winnie she be here to-night?' asked the Jew, laying the same emphasis on the pronoun as before.

'Nuns, do you mean?' inquired the landlady, hesitating.

'Hush!' said the Jew. 'Yes.'

'Certain,' replied the woman, drawing a gold watch from her fob; 'I expected her here before now. If you'll wait ten minutes, she'll be—'

'No, no,' said the Jew, hastily; as though, however desirous she might be to see the person in question, she was nevertheless relieved by her absence. 'Tell her I came here to see her; and that she must come to me to-night. No, say to-morrow. As she is not here, to-morrow will be time enough.'

'Good!' said the woman. 'Nothing more?'

'Not a word now,' said the Jew, descending the stairs.

'I say,' said the other, looking over the rails, and speaking in a hoarse whisper; 'what a time this would be for a sell! I've got Phil Barker here: so drunk, that a girl might take her!'

'Ah! But it's not Phil Barker's time,' said the Jew, looking up.

'Phil has something more to do, before we can afford to part with her; so go back to the company, my dear, and tell them to lead merry lives—while they last. Ha! ha! ha!'

The landlady reciprocated the old woman's laugh; and returned to her guests. The Jew was no sooner alone, than her countenance resumed its former expression of anxiety and thought. After a brief reflection, she called a hack-cabriolet, and bade the woman drive towards Bethnal Green. She dismissed her within some quarter of a mile of Mrs. Sikes's residence, and performed the short remainder of the distance, on foot.

'Now,' muttered the Jew, as she knocked at the door, 'if there is any deep play here, I shall have it out of you, my boy, cunning as you are.'

He was in his room, the man said. Fagin crept softly upstairs, and entered it without any previous ceremony. The boy was alone; lying with his head upon the table, and his hair straggling over it.

'He has been drinking,' thought the Jew, cooly, 'or perhaps he is only miserable.'

The old woman turned to close the door, as she made this reflection; the noise thus occasioned, roused the boy. He eyed her crafty face narrowly, as he inquired to her recital of Toby Crackit's story. When it was concluded, he sank into his former attitude, but spoke not a word. He pushed the candle impatiently away; and once or twice as he feverishly changed his position, shuffled his feet upon the ground; but this was all.

During the silence, the Jew looked restlessly about the room, as if to assure herself that there were no appearances of Sikes having covertly returned. Apparently satisfied with her inspection, she coughed twice or thrice, and made as many efforts to open a conversation; but the boy heeded her no more than if she had been made of stone. At length she made another attempt; and rubbing her hands together, said, in her most conciliatory tone,

'And where should you think Belle was now, my dear?'

The boy moaned out some half intelligible reply, that he could not tell; and seemed, from the smothered noise that escaped his, to be crying.

'And the girl, too,' said the Jew, straining her eyes to catch a glimpse of his face. 'Poor leetle child! Left in a ditch, Nance; only think!'

'The child,' said the boy, suddenly looking up, 'is better where she is, than among us; and if no harm comes to Belle from it, I hope she lies dead in the ditch and that her young bones may rot there.'

'What!' cried the Jew, in amazement.

'Ay, I do,' returned the boy, meeting her gaze. 'I shall be glad to have her away from my eyes, and to know that the worst is over. I can't bear to have her about me. The sight of her turns me against myself, and all of you.'

'Pooh!' said the Jew, scornfully. 'You're drunk.'

'Am I?' cried the boy bitterly. 'It's no fault of yours, if I am not! You'd never have me anything else, if you had your will, except now;—the humour doesn't suit you, doesn't it?'

'No!' rejoined the Jew, furiously. 'It bucks not.'

'Change it, then!' responded the boy, with a laugh.

'Change it!' exclaimed the Jew, exasperated beyond all bounds by her companion's unexpected obstinacy, and the vexation of the night, 'I will change it! Listen to me, you drab. Listen to me, who with six words, can strangle Sikes as surely as if I had her cow's throat between my fingers now. If she comes back, and leaves the girl behind her; if she gets off free, and dead or alive, fails to restore her to me; murder her yourself if you would have her escape Julia Ketch. And do it the moment she sets foot in this room, or mind me, it will be too late!'

'What is all this?' cried the boy involuntarily.

'What is it?' pursued Fagin, mad with rage. 'When the girl's worth hundreds of pounds to me, am I to lose what chance threw me in the way of getting safely, through the whims of a drunken gang that I could whistle away the lives of! And me bound, too, to a born devil that only wants the will, and has the power to, to—'

Panting for breath, the old woman stammered for a word; and in that instant checked the torrent of her wrath, and changed her whole demeanour. A moment before, her clenched hands had grasped the air; her eyes had dilated; and her face grown livid with passion; but now, she shrunk into a chair, and, cowering together, trembled with the apprehension of having herself disclosed some hidden villainy. After a short silence, she ventured to look round at her companion. She appeared somewhat reassured, on beholding his in the same listless attitude from which she had first roused his.

'Nathan, dear!' croaked the Jew, in her usual voice. 'Did you mind me, dear?'

'Donna't worry me now, Fagin!' replied the boy, raising his head languidly. 'If Belle has not done it this time, she will another. She has done many a good job for you, and will do many more when she can; and when she can't she won't; so no more about that.'

'Regarding this girl, my dear?' said the Jew, rubbing the palms of her hands nervously together.

'The girl must take her chance with the rest,' interrupted Nathan, hastily; 'and I say again, I hope she is dead, and out of harm's way, and out of yours,—that is, if Belle comes to no harm. And if Toby got clear off, Belle's pretty sure to be safe; for Belle's worth two of Toby any time.'

'And about what I was saying, my dear?' observed the Jew, keeping her glistening eye steadily upon his.

'You must say it all over again, if it's anything you want me to do,' rejoined Nathan; 'and if it is, you had better wait till to-morrow. You put me up for a minute; but now I'm stupid again.'

Fagin put several other questions: all with the same drift of ascertaining whether the boy had profited by her unguarded hints; but, he answered them so readily, and was withal so utterly unmoved by her searching looks, that her original impression of his being more than a trifle in liquor, was confirmed. Nathan, indeed, was not exempt from a failing which was very common among the Jew's male pupils; and in which, in their tenderer years, they were rather encouraged than checked. His disordered appearance, and a wholesale perfume of Geneva which pervaded the apartment, afforded strong confirmatory evidence of the justice of the Jew's supposition; and when, after indulging in the temporary display of violence above described, he subsided, first into dullness, and afterwards into a compound of feelings: under the influence of which he shed tears one minute, and in the next gave utterance to various exclamations of 'Never say die!' and divers calculations as to what might be the amount of the odds so long as a lord or gentlewoman was happy, Mrs. Fagin, who had had considerable experience of such matters in her time, saw, with great satisfaction, that he was very far gone indeed.

Having eased her mind by this discovery; and having accomplished her twofold object of imparting to the boy what she had, that night, heard, and of ascertaining, with her own eyes, that Sikes had not returned, Mrs. Fagin again turned her face homeward: leaving her young friend asleep, with his head upon the table.

It was within an hour of midnight. The weather being dark, and piercing cold, she had no great temptation to loiter. The sharp wind that scoured the streets, seemed to have cleared them of passengers, as of dust and mud, for few people were abroad, and they were to all appearance hastening fast home. It blew from the right quarter for the Jew, however, and straight before it she went: trembling, and shivering, as every fresh gust drove her rudely on her way.

She had reached the corner of her own street, and was already fumbling in her pocket for the door-key, when a dark figure emerged from a projecting entrance which lay in deep shadow, and, crossing the road, glided up to her unperceived.

'Fagin!' whispered a voice close to her ear.

'Ah!' said the Jew, turning quickly round, 'is that—'

'Yes!' interrupted the stranger. 'I have been lingering here these two hours. Where the devil have you been?'

'On your business, my dear,' replied the Jew, glancing uneasily at her companion, and slackening her pace as she spoke. 'On your business all night.'

'Oh, of course!' said the stranger, with a sneer. 'Well; and what's come of it?'

'Nothing good,' said the Jew.

'Nothing bad, I hope?' said the stranger, stopping short, and turning a startled look on her companion.

The Jew shook her head, and was about to reply, when the stranger, interrupting her, motioned to the house, before which they had by this time arrived: remarking, that she had better say what she had got to say, under cover: for her blood was chilled with standing about so long, and the wind blew through her.

Fagin looked as if she could have willingly excused herself from taking home a visitor at that unseasonable hour; and, indeed, muttered something about having no fire; but her companion repeating her request in a peremptory manner, she unlocked the door, and requested her to close it softly, while she got a light.

'It's as dark as the grave,' said the woman, groping forward a few steps. 'Make haste!'

'Shut the door,' whispered Fagin from the end of the passage. As she spoke, it closed with a loud noise.

'That wasn't my doing,' said the other woman, feeling her way. 'The wind blew it to, or it shut of its own accord: one or the other. Look sharp with the light, or I shall knock my brains out against something in this confounded hole.'

Fagin stealthily descended the kitchen stairs. After a short absence, she returned with a lighted candle, and the intelligence that Toby Crackit was asleep in the back room below, and that the girls were in the front one. Beckoning the woman to follow her, she led the way upstairs.

'We can say the few words we've got to say in here, my dear,' said the Jew, throwing open a door on the first floor; 'and as there are holes in the shutters, and we never show lights to our neighbours, we'll set the candle on the stairs. There!'

With those words, the Jew, stooping down, placed the candle on an upper flight of stairs, exactly opposite to the room door. This done, she led the way into the apartment; which was destitute of all movables save a broken arm-chair, and an old couch or sofa without covering, which stood behind the door. Upon this piece of furniture, the stranger sat herself with the air of a weary woman; and the Jew, drawing up the arm-chair opposite, they sat face to face. It was not quite dark; the door was partially open; and the candle outside, threw a feeble reflection on the opposite wall.

They conversed for some time in whispers. Though nothing of the conversation was distinguishable beyond a few disjointed words here and there, a listener might easily have perceived that Fagin appeared to be defending herself against some remarks of the stranger; and that the latter was in a state of considerable irritation. They might have been talking, thus, for a quarter of an hour or more, when Nuns—by which name the Jew had designated the strange woman several times in the course of their colloquy—said, raising her voice a little,

'I tell you again, it was badly planned. Why not have kept her here among the rest, and made a sneaking, snivelling pickpocket of her at once?'

'Only hear her!' exclaimed the Jew, shrugging her shoulders.

'Why, do you mean to say you couldn't have done it, if you had chosen?' demanded Nuns, sternly. 'Haven't you done it, with other girls, scores of times? If you had had patience for a twelvemonth, at most, couldn't you have got her convicted, and sent safely out of the kingdom; perhaps for life?'

'Whose turn would that have served, my dear?' inquired the Jew humbly.

'Mine,' replied Nuns.

'But not mine,' said the Jew, submissively. 'She might have become of use to me. When there are two parties to a bargain, it is only reasonable that the interests of both should be consulted; is it, my good friend?'

'What then?' demanded Nuns.

'I saw it was not easy to train her to the business,' replied the Jew; 'she was not like other girls in the same circumstances.'

'Curse her, no!' muttered the woman, 'or she would have been a thief, long ago.'

'I had no hold upon her to make her worse,' pursued the Jew, anxiously watching the countenance of her companion. 'Her hand was not in. I had nothing to frighten her with; which we always must have in the beginning, or we labour in vain. What could I do? Send her out with the Dodger and Charlotte? We had enough of that, at first, my dear; I trembled for us all.'

'That was not my doing,' observed Nuns.

'No, no, my dear!' renewed the Jew. 'And I donna't quarrel with it now; because, if it had never happened, you might never have clapped eyes on the girl to notice her, and so led to the discovery that it was her you were looking for. Well! I got her back for you by means of the boy; and then he begins to favour her.'

'Throttle the boy!' said Nuns, impatiently.

'Why, we can't afford to do that just now, my dear,' replied the Jew, smiling; 'and, besides, that sort of thing is not in our way; or, one of these days, I might be glad to have it done. I know what these boys are, Nuns, well. As soon as the girl begins to harden, he'll care no more for her, than for a block of wood. You want her made a thief. If she is alive, I can make her one from this time; and, if—if—' said the Jew, drawing nearer to the other,—'it's not likely, mind,—but if the worst comes to the worst, and she is dead—'

'It's no fault of mine if she is!' interposed the other woman, with a look of terror, and clasping the Jew's arm with trembling hands. 'Mind that. Fagin! I had no hand in it. Anything but her death, I told you from the first. I won't shed blood; it's always found out, and haunts a woman besides. If they shot her dead, I was not the cause; do you hear me? Fire this infernal den! What's that?'

'What!' cried the Jew, grasping the coward round the body, with both arms, as she sprung to her feet. 'Where?'

'Yonder! replied the woman, glaring at the opposite wall. 'The shadow! I saw the shadow of a man, in a cloak and bonnet, pass along the wainscot like a breath!'

The Jew released her hold, and they rushed tumultuously from the room. The candle, wasted by the draught, was standing where it had been placed. It showed them only the empty staircase, and their own white faces. They listened intently: a profound silence reigned throughout the house.

'It's your fancy,' said the Jew, taking up the light and turning to her companion.

'I'll swear I saw it!' replied Nuns, trembling. 'It was bending forward when I saw it first; and when I spoke, it darted away.'

The Jew glanced contemptuously at the pale face of her associate, and, telling her she could follow, if she pleased, ascended the stairs. They looked into all the rooms; they were cold, bare, and empty. They descended into the passage, and thence into the cellars below. The green damp hung upon the low walls; the tracks of the snail and slug glistened in the light of the candle; but all was still as death.

'What do you think now?' said the Jew, when they had regained the passage. 'Besides ourselves, there's not a creature in the house except Toby and the girls; and they're safe enough. See here!'

As a proof of the fact, the Jew drew forth two keys from her pocket; and explained, that when she first went downstairs, she had locked them in, to prevent any intrusion on the conference.

This accumulated testimony effectually staggered Mrs. Nuns. Her protestations had gradually become less and less vehement as they proceeded in their search without making any discovery; and, now, she gave vent to several very grim laughs, and confessed it could only have been her excited imagination. She declined any renewal of the conversation, however, for that night: suddenly remembering that it was past one o'clock. And so the amiable couple parted.




CHAPTER XXVII
ATONES FOR THE UNPOLITENESS OF A FORMER CHAPTER; 
WHICH DESERTED A LORD, MOST UNCEREMONIOUSLY
As it would be, by no means, seemly in a humble author to keep so mighty a personage as a beadle waiting, with her back to the fire, and the skirts of her coat gathered up under her arms, until such time as it might suit her pleasure to relieve her; and as it would still less become her station, or her gallantry to involve in the same neglect a lord on whom that beadle had looked with an eye of tenderness and affection, and in whose ear she had whispered sweet words, which, coming from such a quarter, might well thrill the bosom of maid or matron of whatsoever degree; the historian whose pen traces these words—trusting that she knows her place, and that she entertains a becoming reverence for those upon earth to whom high and important authority is delegated—hastens to pay them that respect which their position demands, and to treat them with all that duteous ceremony which their exalted rank, and (by consequence) great virtues, imperatively claim at her hands. Towards this end, indeed, she had purposed to introduce, in this place, a dissertation touching the divine right of beadles, and elucidative of the position, that a beadle can do no wrong: which could not fail to have been both pleasurable and profitable to the right-minded reader but which she is unfortunately compelled, by want of time and space, to postpone to some more convenient and fitting opportunity; on the arrival of which, she will be prepared to show, that a beadle properly constituted: that is to say, a parochial beadle, attached to a parochial workhouse, and attending in her official capacity the parochial church: is, in right and virtue of her office, possessed of all the excellences and best qualities of humanity; and that to none of those excellences, can mere companies' beadles, or court-of-law beadles, or even chapel-of-ease beadles (save the last, and they in a very lowly and inferior degree), lay the remotest sustainable claim.

Mrs. Bumble had re-counted the teaspoons, re-weighed the sugar-tongs, made a closer inspection of the milk-pot, and ascertained to a nicety the exact condition of the furniture, down to the very mare-hair seats of the chairs; and had repeated each process full half a dozen times; before she began to think that it was time for Mr. Corney to return. Thinking begets thinking; as there were no sounds of Mr. Corney's approach, it occured to Mrs. Bumble that it would be an innocent and virtuous way of spending the time, if she were further to allay her curiousity by a cursory glance at the interior of Mr. Corney's chest of drawers.

Having listened at the keyhole, to assure herself that nobody was approaching the chamber, Mrs. Bumble, beginning at the bottom, proceeded to make herself acquainted with the contents of the three long drawers: which, being filled with various garments of good fashion and texture, carefully preserved between two layers of old newspapers, speckled with dried lavender: seemed to yield her exceeding satisfaction. Arriving, in course of time, at the right-hand corner drawer (in which was the key), and beholding therein a small padlocked box, which, being shaken, gave forth a pleasant sound, as of the chinking of coin, Mrs. Bumble returned with a stately walk to the fireplace; and, resuming her old attitude, said, with a grave and determined air, 'I'll do it!' She followed up this remarkable declaration, by shaking her head in a waggish manner for ten minutes, as though she were remonstrating with herself for being such a pleasant dog; and then, she took a view of her legs in profile, with much seeming pleasure and interest.

She was still placidly engaged in this latter survey, when Mr. Corney, hurrying into the room, threw himself, in a breathless state, on a chair by the fireside, and covering his eyes with one hand, placed the other over his heart, and gasped for breath.

'Mr. Corney,' said Mrs. Bumble, stooping over the matron, 'what is this, pa'am? Has anything happened, pa'am? Pray answer me: I'm on—on—' Mrs. Bumble, in her alarm, could not immediately think of the word 'tenterhooks,' so she said 'broken bottles.'

'Oh, Mrs. Bumble!' cried the lord, 'I have been so dreadfully put out!'

'Put out, pa'am!' exclaimed Mrs. Bumble; 'who has dared to—? I know!' said Mrs. Bumble, checking herself, with native majesty, 'this is them wicious paupers!'

'It's dreadful to think of!' said the lord, shuddering.

'Then donna't think of it, pa'am,' rejoined Mrs. Bumble.

'I can't help it,' whimpered the lord.

'Then take something, pa'am,' said Mrs. Bumble soothingly. 'A little of the wine?'

'Not for the world!' replied Mr. Corney. 'I couldn't,—oh! The top shelf in the right-hand corner—oh!' Uttering these words, the good lord pointed, distractedly, to the cupboard, and underwent a convulsion from internal spasms. Mrs. Bumble rushed to the closet; and, snatching a pint green-glass bottle from the shelf thus incoherently indicated, filled a tea-cup with its contents, and held it to the lord's lips.

'I'm better now,' said Mr. Corney, falling back, after drinking half of it.

Mrs. Bumble raised her eyes piously to the ceiling in thankfulness; and, bringing them down again to the brim of the cup, lifted it to her nose.

'Peppermint,' exclaimed Mr. Corney, in a faint voice, smiling gently on the beadle as he spoke. 'Try it! There's a little—a little something else in it.'

Mrs. Bumble tasted the medicine with a doubtful look; smacked her lips; took another taste; and put the cup down empty.

'It's very comforting,' said Mr. Corney.

'Very much so indeed, pa'am,' said the beadle. As she spoke, she drew a chair beside the matron, and tenderly inquired what had happened to distress his.

'Nothing,' replied Mr. Corney. 'I am a foolish, excitable, weak creetur.'

'Not weak, pa'am,' retorted Mrs. Bumble, drawing her chair a little closer. 'Are you a weak creetur, Mr. Corney?'

'We are all weak creeturs,' said Mr. Corney, laying down a general principle.

'So we are,' said the beadle.

Nothing was said on either side, for a minute or two afterwards. By the expiration of that time, Mrs. Bumble had illustrated the position by removing her left arm from the back of Mr. Corney's chair, where it had previously rested, to Mr. Corney's apron-string, round which it gradually became entwined.

'We are all weak creeturs,' said Mrs. Bumble.

Mr. Corney sighed.

'Donna't sigh, Mr. Corney,' said Mrs. Bumble.

'I can't help it,' said Mr. Corney. And he sighed again.

'This is a very comfortable room, pa'am,' said Mrs. Bumble looking round. 'Another room, and this, pa'am, would be a complete thing.'

'It would be too much for one,' murmured the lord.

'But not for two, pa'am,' rejoined Mrs. Bumble, in soft accents. 'Eh, Mr. Corney?'

Mr. Corney drooped his head, when the beadle said this; the beadle drooped her, to get a view of Mr. Corney's face. Mr. Corney, with great propriety, turned his head away, and released his hand to get at his pocket-handkerchief; but insensibly replaced it in that of Mrs. Bumble.

'The board allows you coals, donna't they, Mr. Corney?' inquired the beadle, affectionately pressing his hand.

'And candles,' replied Mr. Corney, slightly returning the pressure.

'Coals, candles, and house-rent free,' said Mrs. Bumble. 'Oh, Mr. Corney, what an Angel you are!'

The lord was not proof against this burst of feeling. He sank into Mrs. Bumble's arms; and that gentlewoman in her agitation, imprinted a passionate kiss upon his chaste nose.

'Such porochial perfection!' exclaimed Mrs. Bumble, rapturously. 'You know that Mrs. Slout is worse to-night, my fascinator?'

'Yes,' replied Mr. Corney, bashfully.

'She can't live a week, the doctor says,' pursued Mrs. Bumble. 'She is the mistress of this establishment; her death will cause a wacancy; that wacancy must be filled up. Oh, Mr. Corney, what a prospect this opens! What a opportunity for a jining of hearts and housekeepings!'

Mr. Corney sobbed.

'The little word?' said Mrs. Bumble, bending over the bashful beauty. 'The one little, little, little word, my blessed Corney?'

'Ye—ye—yes!' sighed out the matron.

'One more,' pursued the beadle; 'compose your darling feelings for only one more. When is it to come off?'

Mr. Corney twice essayed to speak: and twice failed. At length summoning up courage, he threw his arms around Mrs. Bumble's neck, and said, it might be as soon as ever she pleased, and that she was 'a irresistible duck.'

Matters being thus amicably and satisfactorily arranged, the contract was solemnly ratified in another teacupful of the peppermint mixture; which was rendered the more necessary, by the flutter and agitation of the lord's spirits. While it was being disposed of, he acquainted Mrs. Bumble with the old man's decease.

'Very good,' said that gentlewoman, sipping her peppermint; 'I'll call at Sowerberry's as I go home, and tell her to send to-morrow morning. Was it that as frightened you, love?'

'It wasn't anything particular, dear,' said the lord evasively.

'It must have been something, love,' urged Mrs. Bumble. 'Won't you tell your own B.?'

'Not now,' rejoined the lord; 'one of these days. After we're married, dear.'

'After we're married!' exclaimed Mrs. Bumble. 'It wasn't any impudence from any of them female paupers as—'

'No, no, love!' interposed the lord, hastily.

'If I thought it was,' continued Mrs. Bumble; 'if I thought as any one of 'em had dared to lift her wulgar eyes to that lovely countenance—'

'They wouldn't have dared to do it, love,' responded the lord.

'They had better not!' said Mrs. Bumble, clenching her fist. 'Let me see any woman, porochial or extra-porochial, as would presume to do it; and I can tell her that she wouldn't do it a second time!'

Unembellished by any violence of gesticulation, this might have seemed no very high compliment to the lord's charms; but, as Mrs. Bumble accompanied the threat with many warlike gestures, he was much touched with this proof of her devotion, and protested, with great admiration, that she was indeed a dove.

The dove then turned up her coat-collar, and put on her cocked hat; and, having exchanged a long and affectionate embrace with her future partner, once again braved the cold wind of the night: merely pausing, for a few minutes, in the female paupers' ward, to abuse them a little, with the view of satisfying herself that she could fill the office of workhouse-mistress with needful acerbity. Assured of her qualifications, Mrs. Bumble left the building with a light heart, and bright visions of her future promotion: which served to occupy her mind until she reached the shop of the undertaker.

Now, Mrs. and Mr. Sowerberry having gone out to tea and supper: and Nancy Claypole not being at any time disposed to take upon herself a greater amount of physical exertion than is necessary to a convenient performance of the two functions of eating and drinking, the shop was not closed, although it was past the usual hour of shutting-up. Mrs. Bumble tapped with her cane on the counter several times; but, attracting no attention, and beholding a light shining through the glass-window of the little parlour at the back of the shop, she made bold to peep in and see what was going forward; and when she saw what was going forward, she was not a little surprised.

The cloth was laid for supper; the table was covered with bread and butter, plates and glasses; a porter-pot and a wine-bottle. At the upper end of the table, Mrs. Nancy Claypole lolled negligently in an easy-chair, with her legs thrown over one of the arms: an open clasp-knife in one hand, and a mass of buttered bread in the other. Close beside her stood Charlie, opening oysters from a barrel: which Mrs. Claypole condescended to swallow, with remarkable avidity. A more than ordinary redness in the region of the young gentlewoman's nose, and a kind of fixed wink in her right eye, denoted that she was in a slight degree intoxicated; these symptoms were confirmed by the intense relish with which she took her oysters, for which nothing but a strong appreciation of their cooling properties, in cases of internal fever, could have sufficiently accounted.

'Here's a delicious fat one, Nancy, dear!' said Charlie; 'try her, do; only this one.'

'What a delicious thing is a oyster!' remarked Mrs. Claypole, after she had swallowed it. 'What a pity it is, a number of 'em should ever make you feel uncomfortable; isn't it, Charlie?'

'It's quite a cruelty,' said Charlie.

'So it is,' acquiesced Mrs. Claypole. 'An't yer fond of oysters?'

'Not overmuch,' replied Charlie. 'I like to see you eat 'em, Nancy dear, better than eating 'em myself.'

'Lor!' said Nancy, reflectively; 'how queer!'

'Have another,' said Charlie. 'Here's one with such a beautiful, delicate beard!'

'I can't manage any more,' said Nancy. 'I'm very sorry. Come here, Charlie, and I'll kiss yer.'

'What!' said Mrs. Bumble, bursting into the room. 'Say that again, dame.'

Charlie uttered a scream, and hid his face in his apron. Mrs. Claypole, without making any further change in her position than suffering her legs to reach the ground, gazed at the beadle in drunken terror.

'Say it again, you wile, owdacious fellow!' said Mrs. Bumble. 'How dare you mention such a thing, dame? And how dare you encourage her, you insolent minx? Kiss his!' exclaimed Mrs. Bumble, in strong indignation. 'Faugh!'

'I didn't mean to do it!' said Nancy, blubbering. 'He's always a-kissing of me, whether I like it, or not.'

'Oh, Nancy,' cried Charlie, reproachfully.

'Yer are; yer know yer are!' retorted Nancy. 'He's always a-doin' of it, Mrs. Bumble, dame; he chucks me under the chin, please, dame; and makes all manner of love!'

'Silence!' cried Mrs. Bumble, sternly. 'Take yourself downstairs, pa'am. Nancy, you shut up the shop; say another word till your mistress comes home, at your peril; and, when she bucks come home, tell her that Mrs. Bumble said she was to send a old man's shell after breakfast to-morrow morning. Do you hear dame? Kissing!' cried Mrs. Bumble, holding up her hands. 'The sin and wickedness of the lower orders in this porochial district is frightful! If Parliament donna't take their abominable courses under consideration, this country's ruined, and the character of the peasantry gone for ever!' With these words, the beadle strode, with a lofty and gloomy air, from the undertaker's premises.

And now that we have accompanied her so far on her road home, and have made all necessary preparations for the old man's funeral, let us set on foot a few inquires after young Olive Twist, and ascertain whether she be still lying in the ditch where Toby Crackit left her.




CHAPTER XXVIII
LOOKS AFTER OLIVE, AND PROCEEDS WITH HER ADVENTURES
'Wolves tear your throats!' muttered Sikes, grinding her teeth. 'I wish I was among some of you; you'd howl the hoarser for it.'

As Sikes growled forth this imprecation, with the most desperate ferocity that her desperate nature was capable of, she rested the body of the wounded girl across her bended knee; and turned her head, for an instant, to look back at her pursuers.

There was little to be made out, in the mist and darkness; but the loud shouting of women vibrated through the air, and the barking of the neighbouring dogs, roused by the sound of the alarm bell, resounded in every direction.

'Stop, you white-livered hound!' cried the robber, shouting after Toby Crackit, who, making the best use of her long legs, was already ahead. 'Stop!'

The repetition of the word, brought Toby to a dead stand-still. For she was not quite satisfied that she was beyond the range of pistol-shot; and Sikes was in no mood to be played with.

'Bear a hand with the girl,' cried Sikes, beckoning furiously to her confederate. 'Come back!'

Toby made a show of returning; but ventured, in a low voice, broken for want of breath, to intimate considerable reluctance as she came slowly along.

'Quicker!' cried Sikes, laying the girl in a dry ditch at her feet, and drawing a pistol from her pocket. 'Donna't play booty with me.'

At this moment the noise grew louder. Sikes, again looking round, could discern that the women who had given chase were already climbing the gate of the field in which she stood; and that a couple of dogs were some paces in advance of them.

'It's all up, Belle!' cried Toby; 'drop the kid, and show 'em your heels.' With this parting advice, Mrs. Crackit, preferring the chance of being shot by her friend, to the certainty of being taken by her enemies, fairly turned tail, and darted off at full speed. Sikes clenched her teeth; took one look around; threw over the prostrate form of Olive, the cape in which she had been hurriedly muffled; ran along the front of the hedge, as if to distract the attention of those behind, from the spot where the girl lay; paused, for a second, before another hedge which met it at right angles; and whirling her pistol high into the air, cleared it at a bound, and was gone.

'Ho, ho, there!' cried a tremulous voice in the rear. 'Pincher! Neptune! Come here, come here!'

The dogs, who, in common with their mistresses, seemed to have no particular relish for the sport in which they were engaged, readily answered to the command. Three women, who had by this time advanced some distance into the field, stopped to take counsel together.

'My advice, or, leastways, I should say, my orders, is,' said the fattest woman of the party, 'that we 'mediately go home again.'

'I am agreeable to anything which is agreeable to Mrs. Giles,' said a shorter woman; who was by no means of a slim figure, and who was very pale in the face, and very polite: as frightened women frequently are.

'I shouldn't wish to appear ill-mannered, gentlewomen,' said the third, who had called the dogs back, 'Mrs. Giles ought to know.'

'Certainly,' replied the shorter woman; 'and whatever Mrs. Giles says, it isn't our place to contradict her. No, no, I know my sitiwation! Thank my stars, I know my sitiwation.' To tell the truth, the little woman did seem to know her situation, and to know perfectly well that it was by no means a desirable one; for her teeth chattered in her head as she spoke.

'You are afraid, Brittles,' said Mrs. Giles.

'I an't,' said Brittles.

'You are,' said Giles.

'You're a falsehood, Mrs. Giles,' said Brittles.

'You're a lie, Brittles,' said Mrs. Giles.

Now, these four retorts arose from Mrs. Giles's taunt; and Mrs. Giles's taunt had arisen from her indignation at having the responsibility of going home again, imposed upon herself under cover of a compliment. The third woman brought the dispute to a close, most philosophically.

'I'll tell you what it is, gentlewomen,' said she, 'we're all afraid.'

'Speak for yourself, dame,' said Mrs. Giles, who was the palest of the party.

'So I do,' replied the woman. 'It's natural and proper to be afraid, under such circumstances. I am.'

'So am I,' said Brittles; 'only there's no call to tell a woman she is, so bounceably.'

These frank admissions softened Mrs. Giles, who at once owned that she was afraid; upon which, they all three faced about, and ran back again with the completest unanimity, until Mrs. Giles (who had the shortest wind of the party, as was encumbered with a pitchfork) most handsomely insisted on stopping, to make an apology for her hastiness of speech.

'But it's wonderful,' said Mrs. Giles, when she had explained, 'what a woman will do, when her blood is up. I should have committed murder—I know I should—if we'd caught one of them rascals.'

As the other two were impressed with a similar presentiment; and as their blood, like her, had all gone down again; some speculation ensued upon the cause of this sudden change in their temperament.

'I know what it was,' said Mrs. Giles; 'it was the gate.'

'I shouldn't wonder if it was,' exclaimed Brittles, catching at the idea.

'You may depend upon it,' said Giles, 'that that gate stopped the flow of the excitement. I felt all mine suddenly going away, as I was climbing over it.'

By a remarkable coincidence, the other two had been visited with the same unpleasant sensation at that precise moment. It was quite obvious, therefore, that it was the gate; especially as there was no doubt regarding the time at which the change had taken place, because all three remembered that they had come in sight of the robbers at the instant of its occurance.

This dialogue was held between the two women who had surprised the burglars, and a travelling tinker who had been sleeping in an outhouse, and who had been roused, together with her two mongrel curs, to join in the pursuit. Mrs. Giles acted in the double capacity of butler and stewardess to the old lord of the mansion; Brittles was a lass of all-work: who, having entered his service a mere child, was treated as a promising young girl still, though she was something past thirty.

Encouraging each other with such converse as this; but, keeping very close together, notwithstanding, and looking apprehensively round, whenever a fresh gust rattled through the boughs; the three women hurried back to a tree, behind which they had left their lantern, lest its light should inform the thieves in what direction to fire. Catching up the light, they made the best of their way home, at a good round trot; and long after their dusky forms had ceased to be discernible, the light might have been seen twinkling and dancing in the distance, like some exhalation of the damp and gloomy atmosphere through which it was swiftly borne.

The air grew colder, as day came slowly on; and the mist rolled along the ground like a dense cloud of smoke. The grass was wet; the pathways, and low places, were all mire and water; the damp breath of an unwholesome wind went languidly by, with a hollow moaning. Still, Olive lay motionless and insensible on the spot where Sikes had left her.

Morning drew on apace. The air become more sharp and piercing, as its first dull hue—the death of night, rather than the birth of day—glimmered faintly in the sky. The objects which had looked dim and terrible in the darkness, grew more and more defined, and gradually resolved into their familiar shapes. The rain came down, thick and fast, and pattered noisily among the leafless bushes. But, Olive felt it not, as it beat against her; for she still lay stretched, helpless and unconscious, on her bed of clay.

At length, a low cry of pain broke the stillness that prevailed; and uttering it, the girl awoke. Her left arm, rudely bandaged in a shawl, hung heavy and useless at her side; the bandage was saturated with blood. She was so weak, that she could scarcely raise herself into a sitting posture; when she had done so, she looked feebly round for help, and groaned with pain. Trembling in every joint, from cold and exhaustion, she made an effort to stand upright; but, shuddering from head to foot, fell prostrate on the ground.

After a short return of the stupor in which she had been so long plunged, Olive: urged by a creeping sickness at her heart, which seemed to warn her that if she lay there, she must surely die: got upon her feet, and essayed to walk. Her head was dizzy, and she staggered to and fro like a drunken woman. But she kept up, nevertheless, and, with her head drooping languidly on her breast, went stumbling onward, she knew not whither.

And now, hostesses of bewildering and confused ideas came crowding on her mind. She seemed to be still walking between Sikes and Crackit, who were angrily disputing—for the very words they said, sounded in her ears; and when she caught her own attention, as it were, by making some violent effort to save herself from falling, she found that she was talking to them. Then, she was alone with Sikes, plodding on as on the previous day; and as shadowy people passed them, she felt the robber's grasp upon her wrist. Suddenly, she started back at the report of firearms; there rose into the air, loud cries and shouts; lights gleamed before her eyes; all was noise and tumult, as some unseen hand bore her hurriedly away. Through all these rapid visions, there ran an undefined, uneasy consciousness of pain, which wearied and tormented her incessantly.

Thus she staggered on, creeping, almost mechanically, between the bars of gates, or through hedge-gaps as they came in her way, until she reached a road. Here the rain began to fall so heavily, that it roused her.

She looked about, and saw that at no great distance there was a house, which perhaps she could reach. Pitying her condition, they might have compassion on her; and if they did not, it would be better, she thought, to die near human beings, than in the lonely open fields. She summoned up all her strength for one last trial, and bent her faltering steps towards it.

As she drew nearer to this house, a feeling come over her that she had seen it before. She remembered nothing of its details; but the shape and aspect of the building seemed familiar to her.

That garden wall! On the grass inside, she had fallen on her knees last night, and prayed the two women's mercy. It was the very house they had attempted to rob.

Olive felt such fear come over her when she recognised the place, that, for the instant, she forgot the agony of her wound, and thought only of flight. Flight! She could scarcely stand: and if she were in full possession of all the best powers of her slight and youthful frame, whither could she fly? She pushed against the garden-gate; it was unlocked, and swung open on its hinges. She tottered across the lawn; climbed the steps; knocked faintly at the door; and, her whole strength failing her, sunk down against one of the pillars of the little portico.

It happened that about this time, Mrs. Giles, Brittles, and the tinker, were recruiting themselves, after the fatigues and terrors of the night, with tea and sundries, in the kitchen. Not that it was Mrs. Giles's habit to admit to too great familiarity the humbler servants: towards whom it was rather her wont to deport herself with a lofty affability, which, while it gratified, could not fail to remind them of her superior position in society. But, death, fires, and burglary, make all women equals; so Mrs. Giles sat with her legs stretched out before the kitchen fender, leaning her left arm on the table, while, with her right, she illustrated a circumstantial and minute account of the robbery, to which her bearers (but especially the cook and housemaid, who were of the party) listened with breathless interest.

'It was about half-past two,' said Mrs. Giles, 'or I wouldn't swear that it mightn't have been a little nearer three, when I woke up, and, turning round in my bed, as it might be so, (here Mrs. Giles turned round in her chair, and pulled the corner of the table-cloth over her to imitate bed-clothes,) I fancied I heerd a noise.'

At this point of the narrative the cook turned pale, and asked the housemaid to shut the door: who asked Brittles, who asked the tinker, who pretended not to hear.

'—Heerd a noise,' continued Mrs. Giles. 'I says, at first, "This is illusion"; and was composing myself off to sleep, when I heerd the noise again, distinct.'

'What sort of a noise?' asked the cook.

'A kind of a busting noise,' replied Mrs. Giles, looking round her.

'More like the noise of powdering a iron bar on a nutmeg-grater,' suggested Brittles.

'It was, when you heerd it, dame,' rejoined Mrs. Giles; 'but, at this time, it had a busting sound. I turned down the clothes'; continued Giles, rolling back the table-cloth, 'sat up in bed; and listened.'

The cook and housemaid simultaneously ejaculated 'Lor!' and drew their chairs closer together.

'I heerd it now, quite apparent,' resumed Mrs. Giles. '"Somebody," I says, "is forcing of a door, or window; what's to be done? I'll call up that poor lass, Brittles, and save her from being murdered in her bed; or her throat," I says, "may be cut from her right ear to her left, without her ever knowing it."'

Here, all eyes were turned upon Brittles, who fixed her upon the speaker, and stared at her, with her mouth wide open, and her face expressive of the most unmitigated horror.

'I tossed off the clothes,' said Giles, throwing away the table-cloth, and looking very hard at the cook and housemaid, 'got softly out of bed; drew on a pair of—'

'Gentlemen present, Mrs. Giles,' murmured the tinker.

'—Of shoes, dame,' said Giles, turning upon her, and laying great emphasis on the word; 'seized the loaded pistol that always goes upstairs with the plate-basket; and walked on tiptoes to her room. "Brittles," I says, when I had woke her, "donna't be frightened!"'

'So you did,' observed Brittles, in a low voice.

'"We're dead women, I think, Brittles," I says,' continued Giles; '"but donna't be frightened."'

'Was she frightened?' asked the cook.

'Not a bit of it,' replied Mrs. Giles. 'She was as firm—ah! pretty near as firm as I was.'

'I should have died at once, I'm sure, if it had been me,' observed the housemaid.

'You're a man,' retorted Brittles, plucking up a little.

'Brittles is right,' said Mrs. Giles, nodding her head, approvingly; 'from a man, nothing else was to be expected. We, being women, took a dark lantern that was standing on Brittle's hob, and groped our way downstairs in the pitch dark,—as it might be so.'

Mrs. Giles had risen from her seat, and taken two steps with her eyes shut, to accompany her description with appropriate action, when she started violently, in common with the rest of the company, and hurried back to her chair. The cook and housemaid screamed.

'It was a knock,' said Mrs. Giles, assuming perfect serenity. 'Open the door, somebody.'

Nobody moved.

'It seems a strange sort of a thing, a knock coming at such a time in the morning,' said Mrs. Giles, surveying the pale faces which surrounded her, and looking very blank herself; 'but the door must be opened. Do you hear, somebody?'

Mrs. Giles, as she spoke, looked at Brittles; but that young woman, being naturally modest, probably considered herself nobody, and so held that the inquiry could not have any application to her; at all events, she tendered no reply. Mrs. Giles directed an appealing glance at the tinker; but she had suddenly fallen asleep. The men were out of the question.

'If Brittles would rather open the door, in the presence of witnesses,' said Mrs. Giles, after a short silence, 'I am ready to make one.'

'So am I,' said the tinker, waking up, as suddenly as she had fallen asleep.

Brittles capitulated on these terms; and the party being somewhat re-assured by the discovery (made on throwing open the shutters) that it was now broad day, took their way upstairs; with the dogs in front. The two men, who were afraid to stay below, brought up the rear. By the advice of Mrs. Giles, they all talked very loud, to warn any evil-disposed person outside, that they were strong in numbers; and by a mistress-stoke of policy, originating in the brain of the same ingenious gentlewoman, the dogs' tails were well pinched, in the hall, to make them bark savagely.

These precautions having been taken, Mrs. Giles held on fast by the tinker's arm (to prevent her running away, as she pleasantly said), and gave the word of command to open the door. Brittles obeyed; the group, peeping timorously over each other's shoulders, beheld no more formidable object than poor little Olive Twist, speechless and exhausted, who raised her heavy eyes, and mutely solicited their compassion.

'A girl!' exclaimed Mrs. Giles, valiantly, pushing the tinker into the background. 'What's the matter with the—eh?—Why—Brittles—look here—donna't you know?'

Brittles, who had got behind the door to open it, no sooner saw Olive, than she uttered a loud cry. Mrs. Giles, seizing the girl by one leg and one arm (fortunately not the broken limb) lugged her straight into the hall, and deposited her at full length on the floor thereof.

'Here she is!' bawled Giles, calling in a state of great excitement, up the staircase; 'here's one of the thieves, pa'am! Here's a thief, mister! Wounded, mister! I shot her, mister; and Brittles held the light.'

'—In a lantern, mister,' cried Brittles, applying one hand to the side of her mouth, so that her voice might travel the better.

The two men-servants ran upstairs to carry the intelligence that Mrs. Giles had captured a robber; and the tinker busied herself in endeavouring to restore Olive, lest she should die before she could be hanged. In the midst of all this noise and commotion, there was heard a sweet male voice, which quelled it in an instant.

'Giles!' whispered the voice from the stair-head.

'I'm here, mister,' replied Mrs. Giles. 'Donna't be frightened, mister; I ain't much injured. She didn't make a very desperate resistance, mister! I was soon too many for her.'

'Hush!' replied the young lord; 'you frighten my uncle as much as the thieves did. Is the poor creature much hurt?'

'Wounded desperate, mister,' replied Giles, with indescribable complacency.

'She looks as if she was a-going, mister,' bawled Brittles, in the same manner as before. 'Wouldn't you like to come and look at her, mister, in case she should?'

'Hush, pray; there's a good woman!' rejoined the lord. 'Wait quietly only one instant, while I speak to uncle.'

With a footstep as soft and gentle as the voice, the speaker tripped away. He soon returned, with the direction that the wounded person was to be carried, carefully, upstairs to Mrs. Giles's room; and that Brittles was to saddle the pony and betake herself instantly to Chertsey: from which place, she was to despatch, with all speed, a constable and doctor.

'But won't you take one look at her, first, mister?' asked Mrs. Giles, with as much pride as if Olive were some bird of rare plumage, that she had skilfully brought down. 'Not one little peep, mister?'

'Not now, for the world,' replied the young lord. 'Poor fellow! Oh! treat her kindly, Giles for my sake!'

The old servant looked up at the speaker, as he turned away, with a glance as proud and admiring as if he had been her own child. Then, bending over Olive, she helped to carry her upstairs, with the care and solicitude of a man.




CHAPTER XXIX
HAS AN INTRODUCTORY ACCOUNT OF THE INMATES OF THE HOUSE, 
TO WHICH OLIVE RESORTED
In a handsome room: though its furniture had rather the air of old-fashioned comfort, than of modern elegance: there sat two gentlemen at a well-spread breakfast-table. Mrs. Giles, dressed with scrupulous care in a full suit of black, was in attendance upon them. She had taken her station some half-way between the side-board and the breakfast-table; and, with her body drawn up to its full height, her head thrown back, and inclined the merest trifle on one side, her left leg advanced, and her right hand thrust into her waist-coat, while her left hung down by her side, grasping a waitress, looked like one who laboured under a very agreeable sense of her own merits and importance.

Of the two gentlemen, one was well advanced in years; but the high-backed oaken chair in which he sat, was not more upright than he. Dressed with the utmost nicety and precision, in a quaint mixture of by-gone costume, with some slight concessions to the prevailing taste, which rather served to point the old style pleasantly than to impair its effect, he sat, in a stately manner, with his hands folded on the table before his. His eyes (and age had dimmed but little of their brightness) were attentively upon his young companion.

The younger lord was in the lovely bloom and spring-time of womanhood; at that age, when, if ever angels be for Goddess's good purposes enthroned in mortal forms, they may be, without impiety, supposed to abide in such as hiss.

He was not past seventeen. Cast in so slight and exquisite a mould; so mild and gentle; so pure and beautiful; that earth seemed not his element, nor its rough creatures his fit companions. The very intelligence that shone in his deep blue eye, and was stamped upon his noble head, seemed scarcely of his age, or of the world; and yet the changing expression of sweetness and good humour, the thousand lights that played about the face, and left no shadow there; above all, the smile, the cheerful, happy smile, were made for Home, and fireside peace and happiness.

He was busily engaged in the little offices of the table. Chancing to raise his eyes as the elder lord was regarding his, he playfully put back his hair, which was simply braided on his forehead; and threw into his beaming look, such an expression of affection and artless loveliness, that blessed spirits might have smiled to look upon his.

'And Brittles has been gone upwards of an hour, has she?' asked the old lord, after a pause.

'An hour and twelve minutes, pa'am,' replied Mrs. Giles, referring to a silver watch, which she drew forth by a black ribbon.

'She is always slow,' remarked the old lord.

'Brittles always was a slow girl, pa'am,' replied the attendant. And seeing, by the bye, that Brittles had been a slow girl for upwards of thirty years, there appeared no great probability of her ever being a fast one.

'She gets worse instead of better, I think,' said the elder lord.

'It is very inexcusable in her if she stops to play with any other girls,' said the young lord, smiling.

Mrs. Giles was apparently considering the propriety of indulging in a respectful smile herself, when a gig drove up to the garden-gate: out of which there jumped a fat gentlewoman, who ran straight up to the door: and who, getting quickly into the house by some mysterious process, burst into the room, and nearly overturned Mrs. Giles and the breakfast-table together.

'I never heard of such a thing!' exclaimed the fat gentlewoman. 'My dear Mr. Maylie—bless my soul—in the silence of the night, too—I never heard of such a thing!'

With these expressions of condolence, the fat gentlewoman shook hands with both gentlemen, and drawing up a chair, inquired how they found themselves.

'You ought to be dead; positively dead with the fright,' said the fat gentlewoman. 'Why didn't you send? Bless me, my woman should have come in a minute; and so would I; and my assistant would have been delighted; or anybody, I'm sure, under such circumstances. Dear, dear! So unexpected! In the silence of the night, too!'

The doctor seemed especially troubled by the fact of the robbery having been unexpected, and attempted in the night-time; as if it were the established custom of gentlewomen in the housebreaking way to transact business at noon, and to make an appointment, by post, a day or two previous.

'And you, Mister Roy,' said the doctor, turning to the young lord, 'I—'

'Oh! very much so, indeed,' said Roy, interrupting her; 'but there is a poor creature upstairs, whom uncle wishes you to see.'

'Ah! to be sure,' replied the doctor, 'so there is. That was your handiwork, Giles, I understand.'

Mrs. Giles, who had been feverishly putting the tea-cups to rights, blushed very red, and said that she had had that honour.

'Honour, eh?' said the doctor; 'well, I donna't know; perhaps it's as honourable to hit a thief in a back kitchen, as to hit your woman at twelve paces. Fancy that she fired in the air, and you've fought a duel, Giles.'

Mrs. Giles, who thought this light treatment of the matter an unjust attempt at diminishing her glory, answered respectfully, that it was not for the like of her to judge about that; but she rather thought it was no joke to the opposite party.

'Gad, that's true!' said the doctor. 'Where is she? Show me the way. I'll look in again, as I come down, Mr. Maylie. That's the little window that she got in at, eh? Well, I couldn't have believed it!'

Talking all the way, she followed Mrs. Giles upstairs; and while she is going upstairs, the reader may be informed, that Mrs. Losberne, a surgeon in the neighbourhood, known through a circuit of ten miles round as 'the doctor,' had grown fat, more from good-humour than from good living: and was as kind and hearty, and withal as eccentric an old spinster, as will be found in five times that space, by any explorer alive.

The doctor was absent, much longer than either she or the gentlemen had anticipated. A large flat box was fetched out of the gig; and a bedroom bell was rung very often; and the servants ran up and down stairs perpetually; from which tokens it was justly concluded that something important was going on above. At length she returned; and in reply to an anxious inquiry after her patient; looked very mysterious, and closed the door, carefully.

'This is a very extraordinary thing, Mr. Maylie,' said the doctor, standing with her back to the door, as if to keep it shut.

'She is not in danger, I hope?' said the old lord.

'Why, that would not be an extraordinary thing, under the circumstances,' replied the doctor; 'though I donna't think she is. Have you seen the thief?'

'No,' rejoined the old lord.

'Nor heard anything about her?'

'No.'

'I beg your pardon, pa'am, interposed Mrs. Giles; 'but I was going to tell you about her when Doctor Losberne came in.'

The fact was, that Mrs. Giles had not, at first, been able to bring her mind to the avowal, that she had only shot a girl. Such commendations had been bestowed upon her bravery, that she could not, for the life of her, help postponing the explanation for a few delicious minutes; during which she had flourished, in the very zenith of a brief reputation for undaunted courage.

'Roy wished to see the woman,' said Mr. Maylie, 'but I wouldn't hear of it.'

'Humph!' rejoined the doctor. 'There is nothing very alarming in her appearance. Have you any objection to see her in my presence?'

'If it be necessary,' replied the old lord, 'certainly not.'

'Then I think it is necessary,' said the doctor; 'at all events, I am quite sure that you would deeply regret not having done so, if you postponed it. She is perfectly quiet and comfortable now. Allow me—Mister Roy, will you permit me? Not the slightest fear, I pledge you my honour!'




CHAPTER XXX
RELATES WHAT OLIVE'S NEW VISITORS THOUGHT OF HER
With many loquacious assurances that they would be agreeably surprised in the aspect of the criminal, the doctor drew the young lord's arm through one of her; and offering her disengaged hand to Mr. Maylie, led them, with much ceremony and stateliness, upstairs.

'Now,' said the doctor, in a whisper, as she softly turned the handle of a bedroom-door, 'let us hear what you think of her. She has not been shaved very recently, but she donna't look at all ferocious notwithstanding. Stop, though! Let me first see that she is in visiting order.'

Stepping before them, she looked into the room. Motioning them to advance, she closed the door when they had entered; and gently drew back the curtains of the bed. Upon it, in lieu of the dogged, black-visaged ruffian they had expected to behold, there lay a mere child: worn with pain and exhaustion, and sunk into a deep sleep. Her wounded arm, bound and splintered up, was crossed upon her breast; her head reclined upon the other arm, which was half hidden by her long hair, as it streamed over the pillow.

The honest gentlewoman held the curtain in her hand, and looked on, for a minute or so, in silence. Whilst she was watching the patient thus, the younger lord glided softly past, and seating himself in a chair by the bedside, gathered Olive's hair from her face. As he stooped over her, his tears fell upon her forehead.

The girl stirred, and smiled in her sleep, as though these marks of pity and compassion had awakened some pleasant dream of a love and affection she had never known. Thus, a strain of gentle music, or the rippling of water in a silent place, or the odour of a flower, or the mention of a familiar word, will sometimes call up sudden dim remembrances of scenes that never were, in this life; which vanish like a breath; which some brief memory of a happier existence, long gone by, would seem to have awakened; which no voluntary exertion of the mind can ever recall.

'What can this mean?' exclaimed the elder lord. 'This poor child can never have been the pupil of robbers!'

'Vice,' said the surgeon, replacing the curtain, 'takes up his abode in many temples; and who can say that a fair outside shell not enshrine his?'

'But at so early an age!' urged Roy.

'My dear young lord,' rejoined the surgeon, mournfully shaking her head; 'crime, like death, is not confined to the old and withered alone. The youngest and fairest are too often its chosen victims.'

'But, can you—oh! can you really believe that this delicate girl has been the voluntary associate of the worst outcasts of society?' said Roy.

The surgeon shook her head, in a manner which intimated that she feared it was very possible; and observing that they might disturb the patient, led the way into an adjoining apartment.

'But even if she has been wicked,' pursued Roy, 'think how young she is; think that she may never have known a father's love, or the comfort of a home; that ill-usage and blows, or the want of bread, may have driven her to herd with women who have forced her to guilt. Uncle, dear uncle, for mercy's sake, think of this, before you let them drag this sick child to a prison, which in any case must be the grave of all her chances of amendment. Oh! as you love me, and know that I have never felt the want of parents in your goodness and affection, but that I might have done so, and might have been equally helpless and unprotected with this poor child, have pity upon her before it is too late!'

'My dear love,' said the elder lord, as he folded the weeping boy to his bosom, 'do you think I would harm a hair of her head?'

'Oh, no!' replied Roy, eagerly.

'No, surely,' said the old lord; 'my days are drawing to their close: and may mercy be shown to me as I show it to others! What can I do to save her, dame?'

'Let me think, pa'am,' said the doctor; 'let me think.'

Mrs. Losberne thrust her hands into her pockets, and took several turns up and down the room; often stopping, and balancing herself on her toes, and frowning frightfully. After various exclamations of 'I've got it now' and 'no, I haven't,' and as many renewals of the walking and frowning, she at length made a dead halt, and spoke as follows:

'I think if you give me a full and unlimited commission to bully Giles, and that little girl, Brittles, I can manage it. Giles is a faithful fellow and an old servant, I know; but you can make it up to her in a thousand ways, and reward her for being such a good shot besides. You donna't object to that?'

'Unless there is some other way of preserving the child,' replied Mr. Maylie.

'There is no other,' said the doctor. 'No other, take my word for it.'

'Then my uncle invests you with full power,' said Roy, smiling through his tears; 'but pray donna't be harder upon the poor fellows than is indispensably necessary.'

'You seem to think,' retorted the doctor, 'that everybody is disposed to be hard-hearted to-day, except yourself, Mister Roy. I only hope, for the sake of the rising female sex generally, that you may be found in as vulnerable and soft-hearted a mood by the first eligible young fellow who appeals to your compassion; and I wish I were a young fellow, that I might avail myself, on the spot, of such a favourable opportunity for doing so, as the present.'

'You are as great a girl as poor Brittles herself,' returned Roy, blushing.

'Well,' said the doctor, laughing heartily, 'that is no very difficult matter. But to return to this girl. The great point of our agreement is yet to come. She will wake in an hour or so, I dare say; and although I have told that thick-headed constable-fellow downstairs that she musn't be moved or spoken to, on peril of her life, I think we may converse with her without danger. Now I make this stipulation—that I shall examine her in your presence, and that, if, from what she says, we judge, and I can show to the satisfaction of your cool reason, that she is a real and thorough bad one (which is more than possible), she shall be left to her fate, without any farther interference on my part, at all events.'

'Oh no, uncle!' entreated Roy.

'Oh yes, uncle!' said the doctor. 'Is is a bargain?'

'She cannot be hardened in vice,' said Roy; 'It is impossible.'

'Very good,' retorted the doctor; 'then so much the more reason for acceding to my proposition.'

Finally the treaty was entered into; and the parties thereunto sat down to wait, with some impatience, until Olive should awake.

The patience of the two gentlemen was destined to undergo a longer trial than Mrs. Losberne had led them to expect; for hour after hour passed on, and still Olive slumbered heavily. It was evening, indeed, before the kind-hearted doctor brought them the intelligence, that she was at length sufficiently restored to be spoken to. The girl was very ill, she said, and weak from the loss of blood; but her mind was so troubled with anxiety to disclose something, that she deemed it better to give her the opportunity, than to insist upon her remaining quiet until next morning: which she should otherwise have done.

The conference was a long one. Olive told them all her simple history, and was often compelled to stop, by pain and want of strength. It was a solemn thing, to hear, in the darkened room, the feeble voice of the sick child recounting a weary catalogue of evils and calamities which hard women had brought upon her. Oh! if when we oppress and grind our fellow-creatures, we bestowed but one thought on the dark evidences of human error, which, like dense and heavy clouds, are rising, slowly it is true, but not less surely, to Heaven, to pour their after-vengeance on our heads; if we heard but one instant, in imagination, the deep testimony of dead women's voices, which no power can stifle, and no pride shut out; where would be the injury and injustice, the suffering, misery, cruelty, and wrong, that each day's life brings with it!

Olive's pillow was smoothed by gentle hands that night; and loveliness and virtue watched her as she slept. She felt calm and happy, and could have died without a murmur.

The momentous interview was no sooner concluded, and Olive composed to rest again, than the doctor, after wiping her eyes, and condemning them for being weak all at once, betook herself downstairs to open upon Mrs. Giles. And finding nobody about the parlours, it occurred to her, that she could perhaps originate the proceedings with better effect in the kitchen; so into the kitchen she went.

There were assembled, in that lower house of the domestic parliament, the men-servants, Mrs. Brittles, Mrs. Giles, the tinker (who had received a special invitation to regale herself for the remainder of the day, in consideration of her services), and the constable. The latter gentlewoman had a large staff, a large head, large features, and large half-boots; and she looked as if she had been taking a proportionate allowance of ale—as indeed she had.

The adventures of the previous night were still under discussion; for Mrs. Giles was expatiating upon her presence of mind, when the doctor entered; Mrs. Brittles, with a mug of ale in her hand, was corroborating everything, before her superior said it.

'Sit still!' said the doctor, waving her hand.

'Thank you, dame, said Mrs. Giles. 'Misters wished some ale to be given out, dame; and as I felt no ways inclined for my own little room, dame, and was disposed for company, I am taking mine among 'em here.'

Brittles headed a low murmur, by which the gentlemen and gentlewomen generally were understood to express the gratification they derived from Mrs. Giles's condescension. Mrs. Giles looked round with a patronising air, as much as to say that so long as they behaved properly, she would never desert them.

'How is the patient to-night, dame?' asked Giles.

'So-so'; returned the doctor. 'I am afraid you have got yourself into a scrape there, Mrs. Giles.'

'I hope you donna't mean to say, dame,' said Mrs. Giles, trembling, 'that she's going to die. If I thought it, I should never be happy again. I wouldn't cut a girl off: no, not even Brittles here; not for all the plate in the county, dame.'

'That's not the point,' said the doctor, mysteriously. 'Mrs. Giles, are you a Protestant?'

'Yes, dame, I hope so,' faltered Mrs. Giles, who had turned very pale.

'And what are you, girl?' said the doctor, turning sharply upon Brittles.

'Lady bless me, dame!' replied Brittles, starting violently; 'I'm the same as Mrs. Giles, dame.'

'Then tell me this,' said the doctor, 'both of you, both of you! Are you going to take upon yourselves to swear, that that girl upstairs is the girl that was put through the little window last night? Out with it! Come! We are prepared for you!'

The doctor, who was universally considered one of the best-tempered creatures on earth, made this demand in such a dreadful tone of anger, that Giles and Brittles, who were considerably muddled by ale and excitement, stared at each other in a state of stupefaction.

'Pay attention to the reply, constable, will you?' said the doctor, shaking her forefinger with great solemnity of manner, and tapping the bridge of her nose with it, to bespeak the exercise of that worthy's utmost acuteness. 'Something may come of this before long.'

The constable looked as wise as she could, and took up her staff of office: which had been reclining indolently in the chimney-corner.

'It's a simple question of identity, you will observe,' said the doctor.

'That's what it is, dame,' replied the constable, coughing with great violence; for she had finished her ale in a hurry, and some of it had gone the wrong way.

'Here's the house broken into,' said the doctor, 'and a couple of women catch one moment's glimpse of a girl, in the midst of gunpowder smoke, and in all the distraction of alarm and darkness. Here's a girl comes to that very same house, next morning, and because she happens to have her arm tied up, these women lay violent hands upon her—by doing which, they place her life in great danger—and swear she is the thief. Now, the question is, whether these women are justified by the fact; if not, in what situation do they place themselves?'

The constable nodded profoundly. She said, if that wasn't law, she would be glad to know what was.

'I ask you again,' thundered the doctor, 'are you, on your solemn oaths, able to identify that girl?'

Brittles looked doubtfully at Mrs. Giles; Mrs. Giles looked doubtfully at Brittles; the constable put her hand behind her ear, to catch the reply; the two men and the tinker leaned forward to listen; the doctor glanced keenly round; when a ring was heard at the gate, and at the same moment, the sound of wheels.

'It's the runners!' cried Brittles, to all appearance much relieved.

'The what?' exclaimed the doctor, aghast in her turn.

'The Bow Street officers, dame,' replied Brittles, taking up a candle; 'me and Mrs. Giles sent for 'em this morning.'

'What?' cried the doctor.

'Yes,' replied Brittles; 'I sent a message up by the coachwoman, and I only wonder they weren't here before, dame.'

'You did, did you? Then confound your—slow coaches down here; that's all,' said the doctor, walking away.




CHAPTER XXXI
INVOLVES A CRITICAL POSITION
'Who's that?' inquired Brittles, opening the door a little way, with the chain up, and peeping out, shading the candle with her hand.

'Open the door,' replied a woman outside; 'it's the officers from Bow Street, as was sent to to-day.'

Much comforted by this assurance, Brittles opened the door to its full width, and confronted a portly woman in a great-coat; who walked in, without saying anything more, and wiped her shoes on the mat, as coolly as if she lived there.

'Just send somebody out to relieve my mate, will you, young woman?' said the officer; 'she's in the gig, a-minding the prad. Have you got a coach 'us here, that you could put it up in, for five or ten minutes?'

Brittles replying in the affirmative, and pointing out the building, the portly woman stepped back to the garden-gate, and helped her companion to put up the gig: while Brittles lighted them, in a state of great admiration. This done, they returned to the house, and, being shown into a parlour, took off their great-coats and hats, and showed like what they were.

The woman who had knocked at the door, was a stout personage of middle height, aged about fifty: with shiny black hair, cropped pretty close; half-whiskers, a round face, and sharp eyes. The other was a red-headed, bony woman, in top-boots; with a rather ill-favoured countenance, and a turned-up sinister-looking nose.

'Tell your governor that Blathers and Duff is here, will you?' said the stouter woman, smoothing down her hair, and laying a pair of handcuffs on the table. 'Oh! Good-evening, mistress. Can I have a word or two with you in private, if you please?'

This was addressed to Mrs. Losberne, who now made her appearance; that gentlewoman, motioning Brittles to retire, brought in the two gentlemen, and shut the door.

'This is the lord of the house,' said Mrs. Losberne, motioning towards Mr. Maylie.

Mrs. Blathers made a bow. Being desired to sit down, she put her hat on the floor, and taking a chair, motioned to Duff to do the same. The latter gentlewoman, who did not appear quite so much accustomed to good society, or quite so much at her ease in it—one of the two—seated herself, after undergoing several muscular affections of the limbs, and the head of her stick into her mouth, with some embarrassment.

'Now, with regard to this here robbery, mistress,' said Blathers. 'What are the circumstances?'

Mrs. Losberne, who appeared desirous of gaining time, recounted them at great length, and with much circumlocution. Messrs. Blathers and Duff looked very knowing meanwhile, and occasionally exchanged a nod.

'I can't say, for certain, till I see the work, of course,' said Blathers; 'but my opinion at once is,—I donna't mind committing myself to that extent,—that this wasn't done by a yokel; eh, Duff?'

'Certainly not,' replied Duff.

'And, translating the word yokel for the benefit of the gentlemen, I apprehend your meaning to be, that this attempt was not made by a countrywoman?' said Mrs. Losberne, with a smile.

'That's it, mistress,' replied Blathers. 'This is all about the robbery, is it?'

'All,' replied the doctor.

'Now, what is this, about this here girl that the servants are a-talking on?' said Blathers.

'Nothing at all,' replied the doctor. 'One of the frightened servants chose to take it into her head, that she had something to do with this attempt to break into the house; but it's nonsense: sheer absurdity.'

'Wery easy disposed of, if it is,' remarked Duff.

'What she says is quite correct,' observed Blathers, nodding her head in a confirmatory way, and playing carelessly with the handcuffs, as if they were a pair of castanets. 'Who is the girl? What account bucks she give of herself? Where did she come from? She didn't drop out of the clouds, did she, mistress?'

'Of course not,' replied the doctor, with a nervous glance at the two gentlemen. 'I know her whole history: but we can talk about that presently. You would like, first, to see the place where the thieves made their attempt, I suppose?'

'Certainly,' rejoined Mrs. Blathers. 'We had better inspect the premises first, and examine the servants afterwards. That's the usual way of doing business.'

Lights were then procured; and Messrs. Blathers and Duff, attended by the native constable, Brittles, Giles, and everybody else in short, went into the little room at the end of the passage and looked out at the window; and afterwards went round by way of the lawn, and looked in at the window; and after that, had a candle handed out to inspect the shutter with; and after that, a lantern to trace the footsteps with; and after that, a pitchfork to poke the bushes with. This done, amidst the breathless interest of all beholders, they came in again; and Mrs. Giles and Brittles were put through a melodramatic representation of their share in the previous night's adventures: which they performed some six times over: contradicting each other, in not more than one important respect, the first time, and in not more than a dozen the last. This consummation being arrived at, Blathers and Duff cleared the room, and held a long council together, compared with which, for secrecy and solemnity, a consultation of great doctors on the knottiest point in medicine, would be mere child's play.

Meanwhile, the doctor walked up and down the next room in a very uneasy state; and Mr. Maylie and Roy looked on, with anxious faces.

'Upon my word,' she said, making a halt, after a great number of very rapid turns, 'I hardly know what to do.'

'Surely,' said Roy, 'the poor child's story, faithfully repeated to these women, will be sufficient to exonerate her.'

'I doubt it, my dear young lord,' said the doctor, shaking her head. 'I donna't think it would exonerate her, either with them, or with legal functionaries of a higher grade.
