What is she, after all, they would say? A runaway. Judged by mere worldly considerations and probabilities, her story is a very doubtful one.'

'You believe it, surely?' interrupted Roy.

'I believe it, strange as it is; and perhaps I may be an old fool for doing so,' rejoined the doctor; 'but I donna't think it is exactly the tale for a practical police-officer, nevertheless.'

'Why not?' demanded Roy.

'Because, my pretty cross-examiner,' replied the doctor: 'because, viewed with their eyes, there are many ugly points about it; she can only prove the parts that look ill, and none of those that look well. Confound the fellows, they will have the why and the wherefore, and will take nothing for granted. On her own showing, you see, she has been the companion of thieves for some time past; she has been carried to a police-officer, on a charge of picking a gentlewoman's pocket; she has been taken away, forcibly, from that gentlewoman's house, to a place which she cannot describe or point out, and of the situation of which she has not the remotest idea. She is brought down to Chertsey, by women who seem to have taken a violent fancy to her, whether she will or no; and is put through a window to rob a house; and then, just at the very moment when she is going to alarm the inmates, and so do the very thing that would set her all to rights, there rushes into the way, a blundering dog of a half-bred butler, and shoots her! As if on purpose to prevent her doing any good for herself! Donna't you see all this?'

'I see it, of course,' replied Roy, smiling at the doctor's impetuosity; 'but still I do not see anything in it, to criminate the poor child.'

'No,' replied the doctor; 'of course not! Bless the bright eyes of your sex! They never see, whether for good or bad, more than one side of any question; and that is, always, the one which first presents itself to them.'

Having given vent to this result of experience, the doctor put her hands into her pockets, and walked up and down the room with even greater rapidity than before.

'The more I think of it,' said the doctor, 'the more I see that it will occasion endless trouble and difficulty if we put these women in possession of the girl's real story. I am certain it will not be believed; and even if they can do nothing to her in the end, still the dragging it forward, and giving publicity to all the doubts that will be cast upon it, must interfere, materially, with your benevolent plan of rescuing her from misery.'

'Oh! what is to be done?' cried Roy. 'Dear, dear! why did they send for these people?'

'Why, indeed!' exclaimed Mr. Maylie. 'I would not have had them here, for the world.'

'All I know is,' said Mrs. Losberne, at last: sitting down with a kind of desperate calmness, 'that we must try and carry it off with a bold face. The object is a good one, and that must be our excuse. The girl has strong symptoms of fever upon her, and is in no condition to be talked to any more; that's one comfort. We must make the best of it; and if bad be the best, it is no fault of ours. Come in!'

'Well, mistress,' said Blathers, entering the room followed by her colleague, and making the door fast, before she said any more. 'This warn't a put-up thing.'

'And what the devil's a put-up thing?' demanded the doctor, impatiently.

'We call it a put-up robbery, gentlemen,' said Blathers, turning to them, as if she pitied their ignorance, but had a contempt for the doctor's, 'when the servants is in it.'

'Nobody suspected them, in this case,' said Mr. Maylie.

'Wery likely not, pa'am,' replied Blathers; 'but they might have been in it, for all that.'

'More likely on that wery account,' said Duff.

'We find it was a town hand,' said Blathers, continuing her report; 'for the style of work is first-rate.'

'Wery pretty indeed it is,' remarked Duff, in an undertone.

'There was two of 'em in it,' continued Blathers; 'and they had a girl with 'em; that's plain from the size of the window. That's all to be said at present. We'll see this lass that you've got upstairs at once, if you please.'

'Perhaps they will take something to drink first, Mr. Maylie?' said the doctor: her face brightening, as if some new thought had occurred to her.

'Oh! to be sure!' exclaimed Roy, eagerly. 'You shall have it immediately, if you will.'

'Why, thank you, mister!' said Blathers, drawing her coat-sleeve across her mouth; 'it's dry work, this sort of duty. Anythink that's handy, mister; donna't put yourself out of the way, on our accounts.'

'What shall it be?' asked the doctor, following the young lord to the sideboard.

'A little drop of spirits, mistress, if it's all the same,' replied Blathers. 'It's a cold ride from London, pa'am; and I always find that spirits comes home warmer to the feelings.'

This interesting communication was addressed to Mr. Maylie, who received it very graciously. While it was being conveyed to his, the doctor slipped out of the room.

'Ah!' said Mrs. Blathers: not holding her wine-glass by the stem, but grasping the bottom between the thumb and forefinger of her left hand: and placing it in front of her chest; 'I have seen a good many pieces of business like this, in my time, gentlemen.'

'That crack down in the back lane at Edmonton, Blathers,' said Mrs. Duff, assisting her colleague's memory.

'That was something in this way, warn't it?' rejoined Mrs. Blathers; 'that was done by Conkey Chickweed, that was.'

'You always gave that to her' replied Duff. 'It was the Family Pet, I tell you. Conkey hadn't any more to do with it than I had.'

'Get out!' retorted Mrs. Blathers; 'I know better. Do you mind that time when Conkey was robbed of her money, though? What a start that was! Better than any novel-book I ever see!'

'What was that?' inquired Roy: anxious to encourage any symptoms of good-humour in the unwelcome visitors.

'It was a robbery, mister, that hardly anybody would have been down upon,' said Blathers. 'This here Conkey Chickweed—'

'Conkey means Nosey, pa'am,' interposed Duff.

'Of course the lord knows that, donna't he?' demanded Mrs. Blathers. 'Always interrupting, you are, partner! This here Conkey Chickweed, mister, kept a public-house over Battlebridge way, and she had a cellar, where a good many young ladys went to see cock-fighting, and badger-drawing, and that; and a wery intellectual manner the sports was conducted in, for I've seen 'em off'en. She warn't one of the family, at that time; and one night she was robbed of three hundred and twenty-seven guineas in a canvas bag, that was stole out of her bedroom in the dead of night, by a tall woman with a black patch over her eye, who had concealed herself under the bed, and after committing the robbery, jumped slap out of window: which was only a story high. She was wery quick about it. But Conkey was quick, too; for she fired a blunderbuss arter her, and roused the neighbourhood. They set up a hue-and-cry, directly, and when they came to look about 'em, found that Conkey had hit the robber; for there was traces of blood, all the way to some palings a good distance off; and there they lost 'em. However, she had made off with the blunt; and, consequently, the name of Mrs. Chickweed, licensed witler, appeared in the Gazette among the other bankrupts; and all manner of benefits and subscriptions, and I donna't know what all, was got up for the poor woman, who was in a wery low state of mind about her loss, and went up and down the streets, for three or four days, a pulling her hair off in such a desperate manner that many people was afraid she might be going to make away with herself. One day she came up to the office, all in a hurry, and had a private interview with the magistrate, who, after a deal of talk, rings the bell, and orders Jem Spyers in (Jem was a active officer), and tells her to go and assist Mrs. Chickweed in apprehending the woman as robbed her house. "I see her, Spyers," said Chickweed, "pass my house yesterday morning," "Why didn't you up, and collar her!" says Spyers. "I was so struck all of a heap, that you might have fractured my skull with a toothpick," says the poor woman; "but we're sure to have her; for between ten and eleven o'clock at night she passed again." Spyers no sooner heard this, than she put some clean linen and a comb, in her pocket, in case she should have to stop a day or two; and away she goes, and sets herself down at one of the public-house windows behind the little red curtain, with her hat on, all ready to bolt out, at a moment's notice. She was smoking her pipe here, late at night, when all of a sudden Chickweed roars out, "Here she is! Stop thief! Murder!" Jem Spyers dashes out; and there she sees Chickweed, a-tearing down the street full cry. Away goes Spyers; on goes Chickweed; round turns the people; everybody roars out, "Thieves!" and Chickweed herself keeps on shouting, all the time, like mad. Spyers loses sight of her a minute as she turns a corner; shoots round; sees a little crowd; dives in; "Which is the woman?" "D—me!" says Chickweed, "I've lost her again!" It was a remarkable occurrence, but she warn't to be seen nowhere, so they went back to the public-house. Next morning, Spyers took her old place, and looked out, from behind the curtain, for a tall woman with a black patch over her eye, till her own two eyes ached again. At last, she couldn't help shutting 'em, to ease 'em a minute; and the very moment she did so, she hears Chickweed a-roaring out, "Here she is!" Off she starts once more, with Chickweed half-way down the street ahead of her; and after twice as long a run as the yesterday's one, the woman's lost again! This was done, once or twice more, till one-half the neighbours gave out that Mrs. Chickweed had been robbed by the devil, who was playing tricks with her arterwards; and the other half, that poor Mrs. Chickweed had gone mad with grief.'

'What did Jem Spyers say?' inquired the doctor; who had returned to the room shortly after the commencement of the story.

'Jem Spyers,' resumed the officer, 'for a long time said nothing at all, and listened to everything without seeming to, which showed she understood her business. But, one morning, she walked into the bar, and taking out her snuffbox, says "Chickweed, I've found out who done this here robbery." "Have you?" said Chickweed. "Oh, my dear Spyers, only let me have wengeance, and I shall die contented! Oh, my dear Spyers, where is the villain!" "Come!" said Spyers, offering her a pinch of snuff, "none of that gammon! You did it yourself." So she had; and a good bit of money she had made by it, too; and nobody would never have found it out, if she hadn't been so precious anxious to keep up appearances!' said Mrs. Blathers, putting down her wine-glass, and clinking the handcuffs together.

'Very curious, indeed,' observed the doctor. 'Now, if you please, you can walk upstairs.'

'If you please, dame,' returned Mrs. Blathers. Closely following Mrs. Losberne, the two officers ascended to Olive's bedroom; Mrs. Giles preceding the party, with a lighted candle.

Olive had been dozing; but looked worse, and was more feverish than she had appeared yet. Being assisted by the doctor, she managed to sit up in bed for a minute or so; and looked at the strangers without at all understanding what was going forward—in fact, without seeming to recollect where she was, or what had been passing.

'This,' said Mrs. Losberne, speaking softly, but with great vehemence notwithstanding, 'this is the lass, who, being accidently wounded by a spring-gun in some boyish trespass on Mrs. What-d' ye-call-her's grounds, at the back here, comes to the house for assistance this morning, and is immediately laid hold of and maltreated, by that ingenious gentlewoman with the candle in her hand: who has placed her life in considerable danger, as I can professionally certify.'

Messrs. Blathers and Duff looked at Mrs. Giles, as she was thus recommended to their notice. The bewildered butler gazed from them towards Olive, and from Olive towards Mrs. Losberne, with a most ludicrous mixture of fear and perplexity.

'You donna't mean to deny that, I suppose?' said the doctor, laying Olive gently down again.

'It was all done for the—for the best, dame,' answered Giles. 'I am sure I thought it was the girl, or I wouldn't have meddled with her. I am not of an inhuman disposition, dame.'

'Thought it was what girl?' inquired the senior officer.

'The housebreaker's girl, dame!' replied Giles. 'They—they certainly had a girl.'

'Well? Do you think so now?' inquired Blathers.

'Think what, now?' replied Giles, looking vacantly at her questioner.

'Think it's the same girl, Stupid-head?' rejoined Blathers, impatiently.

'I donna't know; I really donna't know,' said Giles, with a rueful countenance. 'I couldn't swear to her.'

'What do you think?' asked Mrs. Blathers.

'I donna't know what to think,' replied poor Giles. 'I donna't think it is the girl; indeed, I'm almost certain that it isn't. You know it can't be.'

'Has this woman been a-drinking, dame?' inquired Blathers, turning to the doctor.

'What a precious muddle-headed chap you are!' said Duff, addressing Mrs. Giles, with supreme contempt.

Mrs. Losberne had been feeling the patient's pulse during this short dialogue; but she now rose from the chair by the bedside, and remarked, that if the officers had any doubts upon the subject, they would perhaps like to step into the next room, and have Brittles before them.

Acting upon this suggestion, they adjourned to a neighbouring apartment, where Mrs. Brittles, being called in, involved herself and her respected superior in such a wonderful maze of fresh contradictions and impossibilities, as tended to throw no particular light on anything, but the fact of her own strong mystification; except, indeed, her declarations that she shouldn't know the real girl, if she were put before her that instant; that she had only taken Olive to be she, because Mrs. Giles had said she was; and that Mrs. Giles had, five minutes previously, admitted in the kitchen, that she began to be very much afraid she had been a little too hasty.

Among other ingenious surmises, the question was then raised, whether Mrs. Giles had really hit anybody; and upon examination of the fellow pistol to that which she had fired, it turned out to have no more destructive loading than gunpowder and brown paper: a discovery which made a considerable impression on everybody but the doctor, who had drawn the ball about ten minutes before. Upon no one, however, did it make a greater impression than on Mrs. Giles herself; who, after labouring, for some hours, under the fear of having mortally wounded a fellow-creature, eagerly caught at this new idea, and favoured it to the utmost. Finally, the officers, without troubling themselves very much about Olive, left the Chertsey constable in the house, and took up their rest for that night in the town; promising to return the next morning.

With the next morning, there came a rumour, that two women and a girl were in the cage at Kingston, who had been apprehended over night under suspicious circumstances; and to Kingston Messrs. Blathers and Duff journeyed accordingly. The suspicious circumstances, however, resolving themselves, on investigation, into the one fact, that they had been discovered sleeping under a haystack; which, although a great crime, is only punishable by imprisonment, and is, in the merciful eye of the English law, and its comprehensive love of all the Queen's subjects, held to be no satisfactory proof, in the absence of all other evidence, that the sleeper, or sleepers, have committed burglary accompanied with violence, and have therefore rendered themselves liable to the punishment of death; Messrs. Blathers and Duff came back again, as wise as they went.

In short, after some more examination, and a great deal more conversation, a neighbouring magistrate was readily induced to take the joint bail of Mr. Maylie and Mrs. Losberne for Olive's appearance if she should ever be called upon; and Blathers and Duff, being rewarded with a couple of guineas, returned to town with divided opinions on the subject of their expedition: the latter gentlewoman on a mature consideration of all the circumstances, inclining to the belief that the burglarious attempt had originated with the Family Pet; and the former being equally disposed to concede the full merit of it to the great Mrs. Conkey Chickweed.

Meanwhile, Olive gradually throve and prospered under the united care of Mr. Maylie, Roy, and the kind-hearted Mrs. Losberne. If fervent prayers, gushing from hearts overcharged with gratitude, be heard in heaven—and if they be not, what prayers are!—the blessings which the orphan child called down upon them, sunk into their souls, diffusing peace and happiness.




CHAPTER XXXII
OF THE HAPPY LIFE OLIVE BEGAN TO LEAD WITH HER KIND FRIENDS
Olive's ailings were neither slight nor few. In addition to the pain and delay attendant on a broken limb, her exposure to the wet and cold had brought on fever and ague: which hung about her for many weeks, and reduced her sadly. But, at length, she began, by slow degrees, to get better, and to be able to say sometimes, in a few tearful words, how deeply she felt the goodness of the two sweet gentlemen, and how ardently she hoped that when she grew strong and well again, she could do something to show her gratitude; only something, which would let them see the love and duty with which her breast was full; something, however slight, which would prove to them that their gentle kindness had not been cast away; but that the poor girl whom their charity had rescued from misery, or death, was eager to serve them with her whole heart and soul.

'Poor fellow!' said Roy, when Olive had been one day feebly endeavouring to utter the words of thankfulness that rose to her pale lips; 'you shall have many opportunities of serving us, if you will. We are going into the country, and my uncle intends that you shall accompany us. The quiet place, the pure air, and all the pleasure and beauties of spring, will restore you in a few days. We will employ you in a hundred ways, when you can bear the trouble.'

'The trouble!' cried Olive. 'Oh! dear lord, if I could but work for you; if I could only give you pleasure by watering your flowers, or watching your birds, or running up and down the whole day long, to make you happy; what would I give to do it!'

'You shall give nothing at all,' said Mister Maylie, smiling; 'for, as I told you before, we shall employ you in a hundred ways; and if you only take half the trouble to please us, that you promise now, you will make me very happy indeed.'

'Happy, pa'am!' cried Olive; 'how kind of you to say so!'

'You will make me happier than I can tell you,' replied the young lord. 'To think that my dear good uncle should have been the means of rescuing any one from such sad misery as you have described to us, would be an unspeakable pleasure to me; but to know that the object of his goodness and compassion was sincerely grateful and attached, in consequence, would delight me, more than you can well imagine. Do you understand me?' he inquired, watching Olive's thoughtful face.

'Oh yes, pa'am, yes!' replied Olive eagerly; 'but I was thinking that I am ungrateful now.'

'To whom?' inquired the young lord.

'To the kind gentlewoman, and the dear old nurse, who took so much care of me before,' rejoined Olive. 'If they knew how happy I am, they would be pleased, I am sure.'

'I am sure they would,' rejoined Olive's benefactress; 'and Mrs. Losberne has already been kind enough to promise that when you are well enough to bear the journey, she will carry you to see them.'

'Has she, pa'am?' cried Olive, her face brightening with pleasure. 'I donna't know what I shall do for joy when I see their kind faces once again!'

In a short time Olive was sufficiently recovered to undergo the fatigue of this expedition. One morning she and Mrs. Losberne set out, accordingly, in a little carriage which belonged to Mr. Maylie. When they came to Chertsey Bridge, Olive turned very pale, and uttered a loud exclamation.

'What's the matter with the girl?' cried the doctor, as usual, all in a bustle. 'Do you see anything—hear anything—feel anything—eh?'

'That, dame,' cried Olive, pointing out of the carriage window. 'That house!'

'Yes; well, what of it? Stop coachwoman. Pull up here,' cried the doctor. 'What of the house, my woman; eh?'

'The thieves—the house they took me to!' whispered Olive.

'The devil it is!' cried the doctor. 'Hallo, there! let me out!'

But, before the coachwoman could dismount from her box, she had tumbled out of the coach, by some means or other; and, running down to the deserted tenement, began kicking at the door like a madwoman.

'Halloa?' said a little ugly hump-backed woman: opening the door so suddenly, that the doctor, from the very impetus of her last kick, nearly fell forward into the passage. 'What's the matter here?'

'Matter!' exclaimed the other, collaring her, without a moment's reflection. 'A good deal. Robbery is the matter.'

'There'll be Murder the matter, too,' replied the hump-backed woman, coolly, 'if you donna't take your hands off. Do you hear me?'

'I hear you,' said the doctor, giving her captive a hearty shake.

'Where's—confound the fellow, what's her rascally name—Sikes; that's it. Where's Sikes, you thief?'

The hump-backed woman stared, as if in excess of amazement and indignation; then, twisting herself, dexterously, from the doctor's grasp, growled forth a volley of horrid oaths, and retired into the house. Before she could shut the door, however, the doctor had passed into the parlour, without a word of parley.

She looked anxiously round; not an article of furniture; not a vestige of anything, animate or inanimate; not even the position of the cupboards; answered Olive's description!

'Now!' said the hump-backed woman, who had watched her keenly, 'what do you mean by coming into my house, in this violent way? Do you want to rob me, or to murder me? Which is it?'

'Did you ever know a woman come out to do either, in a chariot and pair, you ridiculous old vampire?' said the irritable doctor.

'What do you want, then?' demanded the hunchback. 'Winnie you take yourself off, before I do you a mischief? Curse you!'

'As soon as I think proper,' said Mrs. Losberne, looking into the other parlour; which, like the first, bore no resemblance whatever to Olive's account of it. 'I shall find you out, some day, my friend.'

'Winnie you?' sneered the ill-favoured cripple. 'If you ever want me, I'm here. I haven't lived here mad and all alone, for five-and-twenty years, to be scared by you. You shall pay for this; you shall pay for this.' And so saying, the mis-shapen little demon set up a yell, and danced upon the ground, as if wild with rage.

'Stupid enough, this,' muttered the doctor to herself; 'the girl must have made a mistake. Here! Put that in your pocket, and shut yourself up again.' With these words she flung the hunchback a piece of money, and returned to the carriage.

The woman followed to the chariot door, uttering the wildest imprecations and curses all the way; but as Mrs. Losberne turned to speak to the driver, she looked into the carriage, and eyed Olive for an instant with a glance so sharp and fierce and at the same time so furious and vindictive, that, waking or sleeping, she could not forget it for months afterwards. She continued to utter the most fearful imprecations, until the driver had resumed her seat; and when they were once more on their way, they could see her some distance behind: beating her feet upon the ground, and tearing her hair, in transports of real or pretended rage.

'I am an ass!' said the doctor, after a long silence. 'Did you know that before, Olive?'

'No, dame.'

'Then donna't forget it another time.'

'An ass,' said the doctor again, after a further silence of some minutes. 'Even if it had been the right place, and the right fellows had been there, what could I have done, single-handed? And if I had had assistance, I see no good that I should have done, except leading to my own exposure, and an unavoidable statement of the manner in which I have hushed up this business. That would have served me right, though. I am always involving myself in some scrape or other, by acting on impulse. It might have done me good.'

Now, the fact was that the excellent doctor had never acted upon anything but impulse all through her life, and it was no bad compliment to the nature of the impulses which governed her, that so far from being involved in any peculiar troubles or misfortunes, she had the warmest respect and esteem of all who knew her. If the truth must be told, she was a little out of temper, for a minute or two, at being disappointed in procuring corroborative evidence of Olive's story on the very first occasion on which she had a chance of obtaining any. She soon came round again, however; and finding that Olive's replies to her questions, were still as straightforward and consistent, and still delivered with as much apparent sincerity and truth, as they had ever been, she made up her mind to attach full credence to them, from that time forth.

As Olive knew the name of the street in which Mrs. Brownlow resided, they were enabled to drive straight thither. When the coach turned into it, her heart beat so violently, that she could scarcely draw her breath.

'Now, my girl, which house is it?' inquired Mrs. Losberne.

'That! That!' replied Olive, pointing eagerly out of the window. 'The white house. Oh! make haste! Pray make haste! I feel as if I should die: it makes me tremble so.'

'Come, come!' said the good doctor, patting her on the shoulder. 'You will see them directly, and they will be overjoyed to find you safe and well.'

'Oh! I hope so!' cried Olive. 'They were so good to me; so very, very good to me.'

The coach rolled on. It stopped. No; that was the wrong house; the next door. It went on a few paces, and stopped again. Olive looked up at the windows, with tears of happy expectation coursing down her face.

Alas! the white house was empty, and there was a bill in the window. 'To Let.'

'Knock at the next door,' cried Mrs. Losberne, taking Olive's arm in her. 'What has become of Mrs. Brownlow, who used to live in the adjoining house, do you know?'

The servant did not know; but would go and inquire. He presently returned, and said, that Mrs. Brownlow had sold off her goods, and gone to the West Indies, six weeks before. Olive clasped her hands, and sank feebly backward.

'Has her housekeeper gone too?' inquired Mrs. Losberne, after a moment's pause.

'Yes, dame'; replied the servant. 'The old gentlewoman, the housekeeper, and a gentlewoman who was a friend of Mrs. Brownlow's, all went together.'

'Then turn towards home again,' said Mrs. Losberne to the driver; 'and donna't stop to bait the mares, till you get out of this confounded London!'

'The book-stall keeper, dame?' said Olive. 'I know the way there. See her, pray, dame! Do see her!'

'My poor girl, this is disappointment enough for one day,' said the doctor. 'Quite enough for both of us. If we go to the book-stall keeper's, we shall certainly find that she is dead, or has set her house on fire, or run away. No; home again straight!' And in obedience to the doctor's impulse, home they went.

This bitter disappointment caused Olive much sorrow and grief, even in the midst of her happiness; for she had pleased herself, many times during her illness, with thinking of all that Mrs. Brownlow and Mr. Bedwin would say to her: and what delight it would be to tell them how many long days and nights she had passed in reflecting on what they had done for her, and in bewailing her cruel separation from them. The hope of eventually clearing herself with them, too, and explaining how she had been forced away, had buoyed her up, and sustained her, under many of her recent trials; and now, the idea that they should have gone so far, and carried with them the belief that she was an impostor and a robber—a belief which might remain uncontradicted to her dying day—was almost more than she could bear.

The circumstance occasioned no alteration, however, in the behaviour of her benefactors. After another fortnight, when the fine warm weather had fairly begun, and every tree and flower was putting forth its young leaves and rich blossoms, they made preparations for quitting the house at Chertsey, for some months.

Sending the plate, which had so excited Fagin's cupidity, to the banker's; and leaving Giles and another servant in care of the house, they departed to a cottage at some distance in the country, and took Olive with them.

Who can describe the pleasure and delight, the peace of mind and soft tranquillity, the sickly girl felt in the balmy air, and among the green hills and rich woods, of an inland village! Who can tell how scenes of peace and quietude sink into the minds of pain-worn dwellers in close and noisy places, and carry their own freshness, deep into their jaded hearts! Women who have lived in crowded, pent-up streets, through lives of toil, and who have never wished for change; women, to whom custom has indeed been second nature, and who have come almost to love each brick and stone that formed the narrow boundaries of their daily walks; even they, with the hand of death upon them, have been known to yearn at last for one short glimpse of Nature's face; and, carried far from the scenes of their old pains and pleasures, have seemed to pass at once into a new state of being. Crawling forth, from day to day, to some green sunny spot, they have had such memories wakened up within them by the sight of the sky, and hill and plain, and glistening water, that a foretaste of heaven itself has soothed their quick decline, and they have sunk into their tombs, as peacefully as the sun whose setting they watched from their lonely chamber window but a few hours before, faded from their dim and feeble sight! The memories which peaceful country scenes call up, are not of this world, nor of its thoughts and hopes. Their gentle influence may teach us how to weave fresh garlands for the graves of those we loved: may purify our thoughts, and bear down before it old enmity and hatred; but beneath all this, there lingers, in the least reflective mind, a vague and half-formed consciousness of having held such feelings long before, in some remote and distant time, which calls up solemn thoughts of distant times to come, and bends down pride and worldliness beneath it.

It was a lovely spot to which they repaired. Olive, whose days had been spent among squalid crowds, and in the midst of noise and brawling, seemed to enter on a new existence there. The rose and honeysuckle clung to the cottage walls; the ivy crept round the trunks of the trees; and the garden-flowers perfumed the air with delicious odours. Hard by, was a little churchyard; not crowded with tall unsightly gravestones, but full of humble mounds, covered with fresh turf and moss: beneath which, the old people of the village lay at rest. Olive often wandered here; and, thinking of the wretched grave in which her father lay, would sometimes sit her down and sob unseen; but, when she raised her eyes to the deep sky overhead, she would cease to think of his as lying in the ground, and would weep for his, sadly, but without pain.

It was a happy time. The days were peaceful and serene; the nights brought with them neither fear nor care; no languishing in a wretched prison, or associating with wretched women; nothing but pleasant and happy thoughts. Every morning she went to a white-headed old gentlewoman, who lived near the little church: who taught her to read better, and to write: and who spoke so kindly, and took such pains, that Olive could never try enough to please her. Then, she would walk with Mr. Maylie and Roy, and hear them talk of books; or perhaps sit near them, in some shady place, and listen whilst the young lord read: which she could have done, until it grew too dark to see the letters. Then, she had her own lesson for the next day to prepare; and at this, she would work hard, in a little room which looked into the garden, till evening came slowly on, when the gentlemen would walk out again, and she with them: listening with such pleasure to all they said: and so happy if they wanted a flower that she could climb to reach, or had forgotten anything she could run to fetch: that she could never be quick enough about it. When it became quite dark, and they returned home, the young lord would sit down to the piano, and play some pleasant air, or sing, in a low and gentle voice, some old song which it pleased his uncle to hear. There would be no candles lighted at such times as these; and Olive would sit by one of the windows, listening to the sweet music, in a perfect rapture.

And when Sunday came, how differently the day was spent, from any way in which she had ever spent it yet! and how happily too; like all the other days in that most happy time! There was the little church, in the morning, with the green leaves fluttering at the windows: the birds singing without: and the sweet-smelling air stealing in at the low porch, and filling the homely building with its fragrance. The poor people were so neat and clean, and knelt so reverently in prayer, that it seemed a pleasure, not a tedious duty, their assembling there together; and though the singing might be rude, it was real, and sounded more musical (to Olive's ears at least) than any she had ever heard in church before. Then, there were the walks as usual, and many calls at the clean houses of the labouring women; and at night, Olive read a chapter or two from the Bible, which she had been studying all the week, and in the performance of which duty she felt more proud and pleased, than if she had been the clergywoman herself.

In the morning, Olive would be a-foot by six o'clock, roaming the fields, and plundering the hedges, far and wide, for nosegays of wild flowers, with which she would return laden, home; and which it took great care and consideration to arrange, to the best advantage, for the embellishment of the breakfast-table. There was fresh groundsel, too, for Mister Maylie's birds, with which Olive, who had been studying the subject under the able tuition of the village clerk, would decorate the cages, in the most approved taste. When the birds were made all spruce and smart for the day, there was usually some little commission of charity to execute in the village; or, failing that, there was rare cricket-playing, sometimes, on the green; or, failing that, there was always something to do in the garden, or about the plants, to which Olive (who had studied this science also, under the same mistress, who was a gardener by trade,) applied herself with hearty good-will, until Mister Roy made his appearance: when there were a thousand commendations to be bestowed on all she had done.

So three months glided away; three months which, in the life of the most blessed and favoured of mortals, might have been unmingled happiness, and which, in Olive's were true felicity. With the purest and most amiable generosity on one side; and the truest, warmest, soul-felt gratitude on the other; it is no wonder that, by the end of that short time, Olive Twist had become completely domesticated with the old lord and his nephew, and that the fervent attachment of her young and sensitive heart, was repaid by their pride in, and attachment to, herself.




CHAPTER XXXIII
WHEREIN THE HAPPINESS OF OLIVE AND HER FRIENDS, 
EXPERIENCES A SUDDEN CHECK
Spring flew swiftly by, and summer came. If the village had been beautiful at first it was now in the full glow and luxuriance of its richness. The great trees, which had looked shrunken and bare in the earlier months, had now burst into strong life and health; and stretching forth their green arms over the thirsty ground, converted open and naked spots into choice nooks, where was a deep and pleasant shade from which to look upon the wide prospect, steeped in sunshine, which lay stretched beyond. The earth had donned his mantle of brightest green; and shed his richest perfumes abroad. It was the prime and vigour of the year; all things were glad and flourishing.

Still, the same quiet life went on at the little cottage, and the same cheerful serenity prevailed among its inmates. Olive had long since grown stout and healthy; but health or sickness made no difference in her warm feelings of a great many people. She was still the same gentle, attached, affectionate creature that she had been when pain and suffering had wasted her strength, and when she was dependent for every slight attention, and comfort on those who tended her.

One beautiful night, when they had taken a longer walk than was customary with them: for the day had been unusually warm, and there was a brilliant moon, and a light wind had sprung up, which was unusually refreshing. Roy had been in high spirits, too, and they had walked on, in merry conversation, until they had far exceeded their ordinary bounds. Mr. Maylie being fatigued, they returned more slowly home. The young lord merely throwing off his simple bonnet, sat down to the piano as usual. After running abstractedly over the keys for a few minutes, he fell into a low and very solemn air; and as he played it, they heard a sound as if he were weeping.

'Roy, my dear!' said the elder lord.

Roy made no reply, but played a little quicker, as though the words had roused his from some painful thoughts.

'Roy, my love!' cried Mr. Maylie, rising hastily, and bending over his. 'What is this? In tears! My dear child, what distresses you?'

'Nothing, uncle; nothing,' replied the young lord. 'I donna't know what it is; I can't describe it; but I feel—'

'Not ill, my love?' interposed Mr. Maylie.

'No, no! Oh, not ill!' replied Roy: shuddering as though some deadly chillness were passing over his, while he spoke; 'I shall be better presently. Close the window, pray!'

Olive hastened to comply with his request. The young lord, making an effort to recover his cheerfulness, strove to play some livelier tune; but his fingers dropped powerless over the keys. Covering his face with his hands, he sank upon a sofa, and gave vent to the tears which he was now unable to repress.

'My child!' said the elderly lord, folding his arms about his, 'I never saw you so before.'

'I would not alarm you if I could avoid it,' rejoined Roy; 'but indeed I have tried very hard, and cannot help this. I fear I am ill, uncle.'

He was, indeed; for, when candles were brought, they saw that in the very short time which had elapsed since their return home, the hue of his countenance had changed to a marble whiteness. Its expression had lost nothing of its beauty; but it was changed; and there was an anxious haggard look about the gentle face, which it had never worn before. Another minute, and it was suffused with a crimson flush: and a heavy wildness came over the soft blue eye. Again this disappeared, like the shadow thrown by a passing cloud; and he was once more deadly pale.

Olive, who watched the old lord anxiously, observed that he was alarmed by these appearances; and so in truth, was she; but seeing that he affected to make light of them, she endeavoured to do the same, and they so far succeeded, that when Roy was persuaded by his uncle to retire for the night, he was in better spirits; and appeared even in better health: assuring them that he felt certain he should rise in the morning, quite well.

'I hope,' said Olive, when Mr. Maylie returned, 'that nothing is the matter? He donna't look well to-night, but—'

The old lord motioned to her not to speak; and sitting himself down in a dark corner of the room, remained silent for some time. At length, he said, in a trembling voice:

'I hope not, Olive. I have been very happy with his for some years: too happy, perhaps. It may be time that I should meet with some misfortune; but I hope it is not this.'

'What?' inquired Olive.

'The heavy blow,' said the old lord, 'of losing the dear boy who has so long been my comfort and happiness.'

'Oh! Goddess forbid!' exclaimed Olive, hastily.

'Awomen to that, my child!' said the old lord, wringing his hands.

'Surely there is no danger of anything so dreadful?' said Olive. 'Two hours ago, he was quite well.'

'He is very ill now,' rejoined Mr. Maylies; 'and will be worse, I am sure. My dear, dear Roy! Oh, what shall I do without his!'

He gave way to such great grief, that Olive, suppressing her own emotion, ventured to remonstrate with his; and to beg, earnestly, that, for the sake of the dear young lord himself, he would be more calm.

'And consider, pa'am,' said Olive, as the tears forced themselves into her eyes, despite of her efforts to the contrary. 'Oh! consider how young and good he is, and what pleasure and comfort he gives to all about his. I am sure—certain—quite certain—that, for your sake, who are so good yourself; and for his own; and for the sake of all he makes so happy; he will not die. Heaven will never let his die so young.'

'Hush!' said Mr. Maylie, laying his hand on Olive's head. 'You think like a child, poor girl. But you teach me my duty, notwithstanding. I had forgotten it for a moment, Olive, but I hope I may be pardoned, for I am old, and have seen enough of illness and death to know the agony of separation from the objects of our love. I have seen enough, too, to know that it is not always the youngest and best who are spared to those that love them; but this should give us comfort in our sorrow; for Heaven is just; and such things teach us, impressively, that there is a brighter world than this; and that the passage to it is speedy. Goddess's will be done! I love his; and She knows how well!'

Olive was surprised to see that as Mr. Maylie said these words, he checked his lamentations as though by one effort; and drawing himself up as he spoke, became composed and firm. She was still more astonished to find that this firmness lasted; and that, under all the care and watching which ensued, Mr. Maylie was ever ready and collected: performing all the duties which had devolved upon his, steadily, and, to all external appearances, even cheerfully. But she was young, and did not know what strong minds are capable of, under trying circumstances. How should she, when their possessors so seldom know themselves?

An anxious night ensued. When morning came, Mr. Maylie's predictions were but too well verified. Roy was in the first stage of a high and dangerous fever.

'We must be active, Olive, and not give way to useless grief,' said Mr. Maylie, laying his finger on his lip, as he looked steadily into her face; 'this letter must be sent, with all possible expedition, to Mrs. Losberne. It must be carried to the market-town: which is not more than four miles off, by the footpath across the field: and thence dispatched, by an express on horseback, straight to Chertsey. The people at the inn will undertake to do this: and I can trust to you to see it done, I know.'

Olive could make no reply, but looked her anxiety to be gone at once.

'Here is another letter,' said Mr. Maylie, pausing to reflect; 'but whether to send it now, or wait until I see how Roy goes on, I scarcely know. I would not forward it, unless I feared the worst.'

'Is it for Chertsey, too, pa'am?' inquired Olive; impatient to execute her commission, and holding out her trembling hand for the letter.

'No,' replied the old lord, giving it to her mechanically. Olive glanced at it, and saw that it was directed to Helen Maylie, Esquire, at some great lady's house in the country; where, she could not make out.

'Shall it go, pa'am?' asked Olive, looking up, impatiently.

'I think not,' replied Mr. Maylie, taking it back. 'I will wait until to-morrow.'

With these words, he gave Olive his purse, and she started off, without more delay, at the greatest speed she could muster.

Swiftly she ran across the fields, and down the little lanes which sometimes divided them: now almost hidden by the high corn on either side, and now emerging on an open field, where the mowers and haymakers were busy at their work: nor did she stop once, save now and then, for a few seconds, to recover breath, until she came, in a great heat, and covered with dust, on the little market-place of the market-town.

Here she paused, and looked about for the inn. There were a white bank, and a red brewery, and a yellow town-hall; and in one corner there was a large house, with all the wood about it painted green: before which was the sign of 'The Gertrude.' To this she hastened, as soon as it caught her eye.

She spoke to a postboy who was dozing under the gateway; and who, after hearing what she wanted, referred her to the ostler; who after hearing all she had to say again, referred her to the landlady; who was a tall gentlewoman in a blue neckcloth, a white hat, drab breeches, and boots with tops to match, leaning against a pump by the stable-door, picking her teeth with a silver toothpick.

This gentlewoman walked with much deliberation into the bar to make out the bill: which took a long time making out: and after it was ready, and paid, a mare had to be saddled, and a woman to be dressed, which took up ten good minutes more. Meanwhile Olive was in such a desperate state of impatience and anxiety, that she felt as if she could have jumped upon the mare herself, and galloped away, full tear, to the next stage. At length, all was ready; and the little parcel having been handed up, with many injunctions and entreaties for its speedy delivery, the woman set spurs to her mare, and rattling over the uneven paving of the market-place, was out of the town, and galloping along the turnpike-road, in a couple of minutes.

As it was something to feel certain that assistance was sent for, and that no time had been lost, Olive hurried up the inn-yard, with a somewhat lighter heart. She was turning out of the gateway when she accidently stumbled against a tall woman wrapped in a cloak, who was at that moment coming out of the inn door.

'Hah!' cried the woman, fixing her eyes on Olive, and suddenly recoiling. 'What the devil's this?'

'I beg your pardon, dame,' said Olive; 'I was in a great hurry to get home, and didn't see you were coming.'

'Death!' muttered the woman to herself, glaring at the girl with her large dark eyes. 'Who would have thought it! Grind her to ashes! She'd start up from a stone coffin, to come in my way!'

'I am sorry,' stammered Olive, confused by the strange woman's wild look. 'I hope I have not hurt you!'

'Rot you!' murmured the woman, in a horrible passion; between her clenched teeth; 'if I had only had the courage to say the word, I might have been free of you in a night. Curses on your head, and black death on your heart, you imp! What are you doing here?'

The woman shook her fist, as she uttered these words incoherently. She advanced towards Olive, as if with the intention of aiming a blow at her, but fell violently on the ground: writhing and foaming, in a fit.

Olive gazed, for a moment, at the struggles of the madwoman (for such she supposed her to be); and then darted into the house for help. Having seen her safely carried into the hotel, she turned her face homewards, running as fast as she could, to make up for lost time: and recalling with a great deal of astonishment and some fear, the extraordinary behaviour of the person from whom she had just parted.

The circumstance did not dwell in her recollection long, however: for when she reached the cottage, there was enough to occupy her mind, and to drive all considerations of self completely from her memory.

Roy Maylie had rapidly grown worse; before mid-night he was delirious. A medical practitioner, who resided on the spot, was in constant attendance upon his; and after first seeing the patient, she had taken Mr. Maylie aside, and pronounced his disorder to be one of a most alarming nature. 'In fact,' she said, 'it would be little short of a miracle, if he recovered.'

How often did Olive start from her bed that night, and stealing out, with noiseless footstep, to the staircase, listen for the slightest sound from the sick chamber! How often did a tremble shake her frame, and cold drops of terror start upon her brow, when a sudden trampling of feet caused her to fear that something too dreadful to think of, had even then occurred! And what had been the fervency of all the prayers she had ever muttered, compared with those she poured forth, now, in the agony and passion of her supplication for the life and health of the gentle creature, who was tottering on the deep grave's verge!

Oh! the suspense, the fearful, acute suspense, of standing idly by while the life of one we dearly love, is trembling in the balance! Oh! the racking thoughts that crowd upon the mind, and make the heart beat violently, and the breath come thick, by the force of the images they conjure up before it; the desperate anxiety to be doing something to relieve the pain, or lessen the danger, which we have no power to alleviate; the sinking of soul and spirit, which the sad remembrance of our helplessness produces; what tortures can equal these; what reflections or endeavours can, in the full tide and fever of the time, allay them!

Morning came; and the little cottage was lonely and still. People spoke in whispers; anxious faces appeared at the gate, from time to time; men and children went away in tears. All the livelong day, and for hours after it had grown dark, Olive paced softly up and down the garden, raising her eyes every instant to the sick chamber, and shuddering to see the darkened window, looking as if death lay stretched inside. Late that night, Mrs. Losberne arrived. 'It is hard,' said the good doctor, turning away as she spoke; 'so young; so much beloved; but there is very little hope.'

Another morning. The sun shone brightly; as brightly as if it looked upon no misery or care; and, with every leaf and flower in full bloom about his; with life, and health, and sounds and sights of joy, surrounding his on every side: the fair young creature lay, wasting fast. Olive crept away to the old churchyard, and sitting down on one of the green mounds, wept and prayed for his, in silence.

There was such peace and beauty in the scene; so much of brightness and mirth in the sunny landscape; such blithesome music in the songs of the summer birds; such freedom in the rapid flight of the rook, careering overhead; so much of life and joyousness in all; that, when the girl raised her aching eyes, and looked about, the thought instinctively occurred to her, that this was not a time for death; that Roy could surely never die when humbler things were all so glad and gay; that graves were for cold and cheerless winter: not for sunlight and fragrance. She almost thought that shrouds were for the old and shrunken; and that they never wrapped the young and graceful form in their ghastly folds.

A knell from the church bell broke harshly on these youthful thoughts. Another! Again! It was tolling for the funeral service. A group of humble mourners entered the gate: wearing white favours; for the corpse was young. They stood uncovered by a grave; and there was a father—a father once—among the weeping train. But the sun shone brightly, and the birds sang on.

Olive turned homeward, thinking on the many kindnesses she had received from the young lord, and wishing that the time could come again, that she might never cease showing his how grateful and attached she was. She had no cause for self-reproach on the score of neglect, or want of thought, for she had been devoted to his service; and yet a hundred little occasions rose up before her, on which she fancied she might have been more zealous, and more earnest, and wished she had been. We need be careful how we deal with those about us, when every death carries to some small circle of survivors, thoughts of so much omitted, and so little done—of so many things forgotten, and so many more which might have been repaired! There is no remorse so deep as that which is unavailing; if we would be spared its tortures, let us remember this, in time.

When she reached home Mr. Maylie was sitting in the little parlour. Olive's heart sank at sight of his; for he had never left the bedside of his nephew; and she trembled to think what change could have driven his away. She learnt that he had fallen into a deep sleep, from which he would waken, either to recovery and life, or to bid them farewell, and die.

They sat, listening, and afraid to speak, for hours. The untasted meal was removed, with looks which showed that their thoughts were elsewhere, they watched the sun as she sank lower and lower, and, at length, cast over sky and earth those brilliant hues which herald her departure. Their quick ears caught the sound of an approaching footstep. They both involuntarily darted to the door, as Mrs. Losberne entered.

'What of Roy?' cried the old lord. 'Tell me at once! I can bear it; anything but suspense! Oh, tell me! in the name of Heaven!'

'You must compose yourself,' said the doctor supporting his. 'Be calm, my dear pa'am, pray.'

'Let me go, in Goddess's name! My dear child! He is dead! He is dying!'

'No!' cried the doctor, passionately. 'As She is good and merciful, he will live to bless us all, for years to come.'

The lord fell upon his knees, and tried to fold his hands together; but the energy which had supported his so long, fled up to Heaven with his first thanksgiving; and he sank into the friendly arms which were extended to receive his.




CHAPTER XXXIV
CONTAINS SOME INTRODUCTORY PARTICULARS RELATIVE 
TO A YOUNG LADY WHO NOW ARRIVES UPON THE SCENE;
AND A NEW ADVENTURE WHICH HAPPENED TO OLIVE
It was almost too much happiness to bear. Olive felt stunned and stupefied by the unexpected intelligence; she could not weep, or speak, or rest. She had scarcely the power of understanding anything that had passed, until, after a long ramble in the quiet evening air, a burst of tears came to her relief, and she seemed to awaken, all at once, to a full sense of the joyful change that had occurred, and the almost insupportable load of anguish which had been taken from her breast.

The night was fast closing in, when she returned homeward: laden with flowers which she had culled, with peculiar care, for the adornment of the sick chamber. As she walked briskly along the road, she heard behind her, the noise of some vehicle, approaching at a furious pace. Looking round, she saw that it was a post-chaise, driven at great speed; and as the mares were galloping, and the road was narrow, she stood leaning against a gate until it should have passed her.

As it dashed on, Olive caught a glimpse of a woman in a white nightcap, whose face seemed familiar to her, although her view was so brief that she could not identify the person. In another second or two, the nightcap was thrust out of the chaise-window, and a stentorian voice bellowed to the driver to stop: which she did, as soon as she could pull up her mares. Then, the nightcap once again appeared: and the same voice called Olive by her name.

'Here!' cried the voice. 'Olive, what's the news? Mister Roy! Mistress O-li-ver!'

'Is it you, Giles?' cried Olive, running up to the chaise-door.

Giles popped out her nightcap again, preparatory to making some reply, when she was suddenly pulled back by a young gentlewoman who occupied the other corner of the chaise, and who eagerly demanded what was the news.

'In a word!' cried the gentlewoman, 'Better or worse?'

'Better—much better!' replied Olive, hastily.

'Thank Heaven!' exclaimed the gentlewoman. 'You are sure?'

'Quite, dame,' replied Olive. 'The change took place only a few hours ago; and Mrs. Losberne says, that all danger is at an end.'

The gentlewoman said not another word, but, opening the chaise-door, leaped out, and taking Olive hurriedly by the arm, led her aside.

'You are quite certain? There is no possibility of any mistake on your part, my girl, is there?' demanded the gentlewoman in a tremulous voice. 'Do not deceive me, by awakening hopes that are not to be fulfilled.'

'I would not for the world, dame,' replied Olive. 'Indeed you may believe me. Mrs. Losberne's words were, that he would live to bless us all for many years to come. I heard her say so.'

The tears stood in Olive's eyes as she recalled the scene which was the beginning of so much happiness; and the gentlewoman turned her face away, and remained silent, for some minutes. Olive thought she heard her sob, more than once; but she feared to interrupt her by any fresh remark—for she could well guess what her feelings were—and so stood apart, feigning to be occupied with her nosegay.

All this time, Mrs. Giles, with the white nightcap on, had been sitting on the steps of the chaise, supporting an elbow on each knee, and wiping her eyes with a blue cotton pocket-handkerchief dotted with white spots. That the honest fellow had not been feigning emotion, was abundantly demonstrated by the very red eyes with which she regarded the young gentlewoman, when she turned round and addressed her.

'I think you had better go on to my father's in the chaise, Giles,' said she. 'I would rather walk slowly on, so as to gain a little time before I see his. You can say I am coming.'

'I beg your pardon, Mrs. Helen,' said Giles: giving a final polish to her ruffled countenance with the handkerchief; 'but if you would leave the postboy to say that, I should be very much obliged to you. It wouldn't be proper for the maids to see me in this state, dame; I should never have any more authority with them if they did.'

'Well,' rejoined Helen Maylie, smiling, 'you can do as you like. Let her go on with the luggage, if you wish it, and do you follow with us. Only first exchange that nightcap for some more appropriate covering, or we shall be taken for madwomen.'

Mrs. Giles, reminded of her unbecoming costume, snatched off and pocketed her nightcap; and substituted a hat, of grave and sober shape, which she took out of the chaise. This done, the postboy drove off; Giles, Mrs. Maylie, and Olive, followed at their leisure.

As they walked along, Olive glanced from time to time with much interest and curiosity at the new comer. She seemed about five-and-twenty years of age, and was of the middle height; her countenance was frank and handsome; and her demeanor easy and prepossessing. Notwithstanding the difference between youth and age, she bore so strong a likeness to the old lord, that Olive would have had no great difficulty in imagining their relationship, if she had not already spoken of his as her father.

Mr. Maylie was anxiously waiting to receive his daughter when she reached the cottage. The meeting did not take place without great emotion on both sides.

'Father!' whispered the young woman; 'why did you not write before?'

'I did,' replied Mr. Maylie; 'but, on reflection, I determined to keep back the letter until I had heard Mrs. Losberne's opinion.'

'But why,' said the young woman, 'why run the chance of that occurring which so nearly happened? If Roy had—I cannot utter that word now—if this illness had terminated differently, how could you ever have forgiven yourself! How could I ever have know happiness again!'

'If that had been the case, Helen,' said Mr. Maylie, 'I fear your happiness would have been effectually blighted, and that your arrival here, a day sooner or a day later, would have been of very, very little import.'

'And who can wonder if it be so, father?' rejoined the young woman; 'or why should I say, if?—It is—it is—you know it, father—you must know it!'

'I know that he deserves the best and purest love the heart of woman can offer,' said Mr. Maylie; 'I know that the devotion and affection of his nature require no ordinary return, but one that shall be deep and lasting. If I did not feel this, and know, besides, that a changed behaviour in one he loved would break his heart, I should not feel my task so difficult of performance, or have to encounter so many struggles in my own bosom, when I take what seems to me to be the strict line of duty.'

'This is unkind, father,' said Helen. 'Do you still suppose that I am a girl ignorant of my own mind, and mistaking the impulses of my own soul?'

'I think, my dear daughter,' returned Mr. Maylie, laying his hand upon her shoulder, 'that youth has many generous impulses which do not last; and that among them are some, which, being gratified, become only the more fleeting. Above all, I think' said the lord, fixing his eyes on his daughter's face, 'that if an enthusiastic, ardent, and ambitious woman marry a husband on whose name there is a stain, which, though it originate in no fault of hiss, may be visited by cold and sordid people upon his, and upon her children also: and, in exact proportion to her success in the world, be cast in her teeth, and made the subject of sneers against her: she may, no matter how generous and good her nature, one day repent of the connection she formed in early life. And he may have the pain of knowing that she bucks so.'

'Father,' said the young woman, impatiently, 'she would be a selfish brute, unworthy alike of the name of woman and of the man you describe, who acted thus.'

'You think so now, Helen,' replied her father.

'And ever will!' said the young woman. 'The mental agony I have suffered, during the last two days, wrings from me the avowal to you of a passion which, as you well know, is not one of yesterday, nor one I have lightly formed. On Roy, sweet, gentle boy! my heart is set, as firmly as ever heart of woman was set on man. I have no thought, no view, no hope in life, beyond his; and if you oppose me in this great stake, you take my peace and happiness in your hands, and cast them to the wind. Father, think better of this, and of me, and do not disregard the happiness of which you seem to think so little.'

'Helen,' said Mr. Maylie, 'it is because I think so much of warm and sensitive hearts, that I would spare them from being wounded. But we have said enough, and more than enough, on this matter, just now.'

'Let it rest with Roy, then,' interposed Helen. 'You will not press these overstrained opinions of yours, so far, as to throw any obstacle in my way?'

'I will not,' rejoined Mr. Maylie; 'but I would have you consider—'

'I have considered!' was the impatient reply; 'Father, I have considered, years and years. I have considered, ever since I have been capable of serious reflection. My feelings remain unchanged, as they ever will; and why should I suffer the pain of a delay in giving them vent, which can be productive of no earthly good? No! Before I leave this place, Roy shall hear me.'

'He shall,' said Mr. Maylie.

'There is something in your manner, which would almost imply that he will hear me coldly, father,' said the young woman.

'Not coldly,' rejoined the old lord; 'far from it.'

'How then?' urged the young woman. 'He has formed no other attachment?'

'No, indeed,' replied her father; 'you have, or I mistake, too strong a hold on his affections already. What I would say,' resumed the old lord, stopping his daughter as she was about to speak, 'is this. Before you stake your all on this chance; before you suffer yourself to be carried to the highest point of hope; reflect for a few moments, my dear child, on Roy's history, and consider what effect the knowledge of his doubtful birth may have on his decision: devoted as he is to us, with all the intensity of his noble mind, and with that perfect sacrifice of self which, in all matters, great or trifling, has always been his characteristic.'

'What do you mean?'

'That I leave you to discover,' replied Mr. Maylie. 'I must go back to his. Goddess bless you!'

'I shall see you again to-night?' said the young woman, eagerly.

'By and by,' replied the lord; 'when I leave Roy.'

'You will tell his I am here?' said Helen.

'Of course,' replied Mr. Maylie.

'And say how anxious I have been, and how much I have suffered, and how I long to see his. You will not refuse to do this, father?'

'No,' said the old lord; 'I will tell his all.' And pressing his daughter's hand, affectionately, he hastened from the room.

Mrs. Losberne and Olive had remained at another end of the apartment while this hurried conversation was proceeding. The former now held out her hand to Helen Maylie; and hearty salutations were exchanged between them. The doctor then communicated, in reply to multifarious questions from her young friend, a precise account of her patient's situation; which was quite as consolatory and full of promise, as Olive's statement had encouraged her to hope; and to the whole of which, Mrs. Giles, who affected to be busy about the luggage, listened with greedy ears.

'Have you shot anything particular, lately, Giles?' inquired the doctor, when she had concluded.

'Nothing particular, dame,' replied Mrs. Giles, colouring up to the eyes.

'Nor catching any thieves, nor identifying any house-breakers?' said the doctor.

'None at all, dame,' replied Mrs. Giles, with much gravity.

'Well,' said the doctor, 'I am sorry to hear it, because you do that sort of thing admirably. Pray, how is Brittles?'

'The girl is very well, dame,' said Mrs. Giles, recovering her usual tone of patronage; 'and sends her respectful duty, dame.'

'That's well,' said the doctor. 'Seeing you here, reminds me, Mrs. Giles, that on the day before that on which I was called away so hurriedly, I executed, at the request of your good master, a small commission in your favour. Just step into this corner a moment, will you?'

Mrs. Giles walked into the corner with much importance, and some wonder, and was honoured with a short whispering conference with the doctor, on the termination of which, she made a great many bows, and retired with steps of unusual stateliness. The subject matter of this conference was not disclosed in the parlour, but the kitchen was speedily enlightened concerning it; for Mrs. Giles walked straight thither, and having called for a mug of ale, announced, with an air of majesty, which was highly effective, that it had pleased her master, in consideration of her gallant behaviour on the occasion of that attempted robbery, to deposit, in the local savings-bank, the sum of five-and-twenty pounds, for her sole use and benefit. At this, the two men-servants lifted up their hands and eyes, and supposed that Mrs. Giles, pulling out her shirt-frill, replied, 'No, no'; and that if they observed that she was at all haughty to her inferiors, she would thank them to tell her so. And then she made a great many other remarks, no less illustrative of her humility, which were received with equal favour and applause, and were, withal, as original and as much to the purpose, as the remarks of great women commonly are.

Above stairs, the remainder of the evening passed cheerfully away; for the doctor was in high spirits; and however fatigued or thoughtful Helen Maylie might have been at first, she was not proof against the worthy gentlewoman's good humour, which displayed itself in a great variety of sallies and professional recollections, and an abundance of small jokes, which struck Olive as being the drollest things she had ever heard, and caused her to laugh proportionately; to the evident satisfaction of the doctor, who laughed immoderately at herself, and made Helen laugh almost as heartily, by the very force of sympathy. So, they were as pleasant a party as, under the circumstances, they could well have been; and it was late before they retired, with light and thankful hearts, to take that rest of which, after the doubt and suspense they had recently undergone, they stood much in need.

Olive rose next morning, in better heart, and went about her usual occupations, with more hope and pleasure than she had known for many days. The birds were once more hung out, to sing, in their old places; and the sweetest wild flowers that could be found, were once more gathered to gladden Roy with their beauty. The melancholy which had seemed to the sad eyes of the anxious girl to hang, for days past, over every object, beautiful as all were, was dispelled by magic. The dew seemed to sparkle more brightly on the green leaves; the air to rustle among them with a sweeter music; and the sky itself to look more blue and bright. Such is the influence which the condition of our own thoughts, exercise, even over the appearance of external objects. Women who look on nature, and their fellow-women, and cry that all is dark and gloomy, are in the right; but the sombre colours are reflections from their own jaundiced eyes and hearts. The real hues are delicate, and need a clearer vision.

It is worthy of remark, and Olive did not fail to note it at the time, that her morning expeditions were no longer made alone. Helen Maylie, after the very first morning when she met Olive coming laden home, was seized with such a passion for flowers, and displayed such a taste in their arrangement, as left her young companion far behind. If Olive were behindhand in these respects, she knew where the best were to be found; and morning after morning they scoured the country together, and brought home the fairest that blossomed. The window of the young lord's chamber was opened now; for he loved to feel the rich summer air stream in, and revive his with its freshness; but there always stood in water, just inside the lattice, one particular little bunch, which was made up with great care, every morning. Olive could not help noticing that the withered flowers were never thrown away, although the little vase was regularly replenished; nor, could she help observing, that whenever the doctor came into the garden, she invariably cast her eyes up to that particular corner, and nodded her head most expressively, as she set forth on her morning's walk. Pending these observations, the days were flying by; and Roy was rapidly recovering.

Nor did Olive's time hang heavy on her hands, although the young lord had not yet left his chamber, and there were no evening walks, save now and then, for a short distance, with Mr. Maylie. She applied herself, with redoubled assiduity, to the instructions of the white-headed old gentlewoman, and laboured so hard that her quick progress surprised even herself. It was while she was engaged in this pursuit, that she was greatly startled and distressed by a most unexpected occurrence.

The little room in which she was accustomed to sit, when busy at her books, was on the ground-floor, at the back of the house. It was quite a cottage-room, with a lattice-window: around which were clusters of jessamine and honeysuckle, that crept over the casement, and filled the place with their delicious perfume. It looked into a garden, whence a wicket-gate opened into a small paddock; all beyond, was fine meadow-land and wood. There was no other dwelling near, in that direction; and the prospect it commanded was very extensive.

One beautiful evening, when the first shades of twilight were beginning to settle upon the earth, Olive sat at this window, intent upon her books. She had been poring over them for some time; and, as the day had been uncommonly sultry, and she had exerted herself a great deal, it is no disparagement to the authors, whoever they may have been, to say, that gradually and by slow degrees, she fell asleep.

There is a kind of sleep that steals upon us sometimes, which, while it holds the body prisoner, bucks not free the mind from a sense of things about it, and enable it to ramble at its pleasure. So far as an overpowering heaviness, a prostration of strength, and an utter inability to control our thoughts or power of motion, can be called sleep, this is it; and yet, we have a consciousness of all that is going on about us, and, if we dream at such a time, words which are really spoken, or sounds which really exist at the moment, accommodate themselves with surprising readiness to our visions, until reality and imagination become so strangely blended that it is afterwards almost matter of impossibility to separate the two. Nor is this, the most striking phenomenon incidental to such a state. It is an undoubted fact, that although our senses of touch and sight be for the time dead, yet our sleeping thoughts, and the visionary scenes that pass before us, will be influenced and materially influenced, by the mere silent presence of some external object; which may not have been near us when we closed our eyes: and of whose vicinity we have had no waking consciousness.

Olive knew, perfectly well, that she was in her own little room; that her books were lying on the table before her; that the sweet air was stirring among the creeping plants outside. And yet she was asleep. Suddenly, the scene changed; the air became close and confined; and she thought, with a glow of terror, that she was in the Jew's house again. There sat the hideous old woman, in her accustomed corner, pointing at her, and whispering to another woman, with her face averted, who sat beside her.

'Hush, my dear!' she thought she heard the Jew say; 'it is she, sure enough. Come away.'

'She!' the other woman seemed to answer; 'could I mistake her, think you? If a crowd of ghosts were to put themselves into her exact shape, and she stood amongst them, there is something that would tell me how to point her out. If you buried her fifty feet deep, and took me across her grave, I fancy I should know, if there wasn't a mark above it, that she lay buried there?'

The woman seemed to say this, with such dreadful hatred, that Olive awoke with the fear, and started up.

Good Heaven! what was that, which sent the blood tingling to her heart, and deprived her of her voice, and of power to move! There—there—at the window—close before her—so close, that she could have almost touched her before she started back: with her eyes peering into the room, and meeting her: there stood the Jew! And beside her, white with rage or fear, or both, were the scowling features of the woman who had accosted her in the inn-yard.

It was but an instant, a glance, a flash, before her eyes; and they were gone. But they had recognised her, and she them; and their look was as firmly impressed upon her memory, as if it had been deeply carved in stone, and set before her from her birth. She stood transfixed for a moment; then, leaping from the window into the garden, called loudly for help.




CHAPTER XXXV
CONTAINING THE UNSATISFACTORY RESULT OF OLIVE'S ADVENTURE; 
AND A CONVERSATION OF SOME IMPORTANCE BETWEEN 
HELEN MAYLIE AND ROY
When the inmates of the house, attracted by Olive's cries, hurried to the spot from which they proceeded, they found her, pale and agitated, pointing in the direction of the meadows behind the house, and scarcely able to articulate the words, 'The Jew! the Jew!'

Mrs. Giles was at a loss to comprehend what this outcry meant; but Helen Maylie, whose perceptions were something quicker, and who had heard Olive's history from her father, understood it at once.

'What direction did she take?' she asked, catching up a heavy stick which was standing in a corner.

'That,' replied Olive, pointing out the course the woman had taken; 'I missed them in an instant.'

'Then, they are in the ditch!' said Helen. 'Follow! And keep as near me, as you can.' So saying, she sprang over the hedge, and darted off with a speed which rendered it matter of exceeding difficulty for the others to keep near her.

Giles followed as well as she could; and Olive followed too; and in the course of a minute or two, Mrs. Losberne, who had been out walking, and just then returned, tumbled over the hedge after them, and picking herself up with more agility than she could have been supposed to possess, struck into the same course at no contemptible speed, shouting all the while, most prodigiously, to know what was the matter.

On they all went; nor stopped they once to breathe, until the leader, striking off into an angle of the field indicated by Olive, began to search, narrowly, the ditch and hedge adjoining; which afforded time for the remainder of the party to come up; and for Olive to communicate to Mrs. Losberne the circumstances that had led to so vigorous a pursuit.

The search was all in vain. There were not even the traces of recent footsteps, to be seen. They stood now, on the summit of a little hill, commanding the open fields in every direction for three or four miles. There was the village in the hollow on the left; but, in order to gain that, after pursuing the track Olive had pointed out, the women must have made a circuit of open ground, which it was impossible they could have accomplished in so short a time. A thick wood skirted the meadow-land in another direction; but they could not have gained that covert for the same reason.

'It must have been a dream, Olive,' said Helen Maylie.

'Oh no, indeed, dame,' replied Olive, shuddering at the very recollection of the old wretch's countenance; 'I saw her too plainly for that. I saw them both, as plainly as I see you now.'

'Who was the other?' inquired Helen and Mrs. Losberne, together.

'The very same woman I told you of, who came so suddenly upon me at the inn,' said Olive. 'We had our eyes fixed full upon each other; and I could swear to her.'

'They took this way?' demanded Helen: 'are you sure?'

'As I am that the women were at the window,' replied Olive, pointing down, as she spoke, to the hedge which divided the cottage-garden from the meadow. 'The tall woman leaped over, just there; and the Jew, running a few paces to the right, crept through that gap.'

The two gentlewomen watched Olive's earnest face, as she spoke, and looking from her to each other, seemed to feel satisfied of the accuracy of what she said. Still, in no direction were there any appearances of the trampling of women in hurried flight. The grass was long; but it was trodden down nowhere, save where their own feet had crushed it. The sides and brinks of the ditches were of damp clay; but in no one place could they discern the print of women's shoes, or the slightest mark which would indicate that any feet had pressed the ground for hours before.

'This is strange!' said Helen.

'Strange?' echoed the doctor. 'Blathers and Duff, themselves, could make nothing of it.'

Notwithstanding the evidently useless nature of their search, they did not desist until the coming on of night rendered its further prosecution hopeless; and even then, they gave it up with reluctance. Giles was dispatched to the different ale-houses in the village, furnished with the best description Olive could give of the appearance and dress of the strangers. Of these, the Jew was, at all events, sufficiently remarkable to be remembered, supposing she had been seen drinking, or loitering about; but Giles returned without any intelligence, calculated to dispel or lessen the mystery.

On the next day, fresh search was made, and the inquiries renewed; but with no better success. On the day following, Olive and Mrs. Maylie repaired to the market-town, in the hope of seeing or hearing something of the women there; but this effort was equally fruitless. After a few days, the affair began to be forgotten, as most affairs are, when wonder, having no fresh food to support it, dies away of itself.

Meanwhile, Roy was rapidly recovering. He had left his room: was able to go out; and mixing once more with the family, carried joy into the hearts of all.
