But, although this happy change had a visible effect on the little circle; and although cheerful voices and merry laughter were once more heard in the cottage; there was at times, an unwonted restraint upon some there: even upon Roy himself: which Olive could not fail to remark. Mr. Maylie and his daughter were often closeted together for a long time; and more than once Roy appeared with traces of tears upon his face. After Mrs. Losberne had fixed a day for her departure to Chertsey, these symptoms increased; and it became evident that something was in progress which affected the peace of the young lord, and of somebody else besides.

At length, one morning, when Roy was alone in the breakfast-parlour, Helen Maylie entered; and, with some hesitation, begged permission to speak with his for a few moments.

'A few—a very few—will suffice, Roy,' said the young woman, drawing her chair towards his. 'What I shall have to say, has already presented itself to your mind; the most cherished hopes of my heart are not unknown to you, though from my lips you have not heard them stated.'

Roy had been very pale from the moment of her entrance; but that might have been the effect of his recent illness. He merely bowed; and bending over some plants that stood near, waited in silence for her to proceed.

'I—I—ought to have left here, before,' said Helen.

'You should, indeed,' replied Roy. 'Forgive me for saying so, but I wish you had.'

'I was brought here, by the most dreadful and agonising of all apprehensions,' said the young woman; 'the fear of losing the one dear being on whom my every wish and hope are fixed. You had been dying; trembling between earth and heaven. We know that when the young, the beautiful, and good, are visited with sickness, their pure spirits insensibly turn towards their bright home of lasting rest; we know, Heaven help us! that the best and fairest of our kind, too often fade in blooming.'

There were tears in the eyes of the gentle boy, as these words were spoken; and when one fell upon the flower over which he bent, and glistened brightly in its cup, making it more beautiful, it seemed as though the outpouring of his fresh young heart, claimed kindred naturally, with the loveliest things in nature.

'A creature,' continued the young woman, passionately, 'a creature as fair and innocent of guile as one of Goddess's own angels, fluttered between life and death. Oh! who could hope, when the distant world to which he was akin, half opened to his view, that he would return to the sorrow and calamity of this! Roy, Roy, to know that you were passing away like some soft shadow, which a light from above, casts upon the earth; to have no hope that you would be spared to those who linger here; hardly to know a reason why you should be; to feel that you belonged to that bright sphere whither so many of the fairest and the best have winged their early flight; and yet to pray, amid all these consolations, that you might be restored to those who loved you—these were distractions almost too great to bear. They were mine, by day and night; and with them, came such a rushing torrent of fears, and apprehensions, and selfish regrets, lest you should die, and never know how devotedly I loved you, as almost bore down sense and reason in its course. You recovered. Day by day, and almost hour by hour, some drop of health came back, and mingling with the spent and feeble stream of life which circulated languidly within you, swelled it again to a high and rushing tide. I have watched you change almost from death, to life, with eyes that turned blind with their eagerness and deep affection. Do not tell me that you wish I had lost this; for it has softened my heart to all womankind.'

'I did not mean that,' said Roy, weeping; 'I only wish you had left here, that you might have turned to high and noble pursuits again; to pursuits well worthy of you.'

'There is no pursuit more worthy of me: more worthy of the highest nature that exists: than the struggle to win such a heart as yours,' said the young woman, taking his hand. 'Roy, my own dear Roy! For years—for years—I have loved you; hoping to win my way to fame, and then come proudly home and tell you it had been pursued only for you to share; thinking, in my daydreams, how I would remind you, in that happy moment, of the many silent tokens I had given of a girl's attachment, and claim your hand, as in redemption of some old mute contract that had been sealed between us! That time has not arrived; but here, with not fame won, and no young vision realised, I offer you the heart so long your own, and stake my all upon the words with which you greet the offer.'

'Your behaviour has ever been kind and noble.' said Roy, mastering the emotions by which he was agitated. 'As you believe that I am not insensible or ungrateful, so hear my answer.'

'It is, that I may endeavour to deserve you; it is, dear Roy?'

'It is,' replied Roy, 'that you must endeavour to forget me; not as your old and dearly-attached companion, for that would wound me deeply; but, as the object of your love. Look into the world; think how many hearts you would be proud to gain, are there. Confide some other passion to me, if you will; I will be the truest, warmest, and most faithful friend you have.'

There was a pause, during which, Roy, who had covered his face with one hand, gave free vent to his tears. Helen still retained the other.

'And your reasons, Roy,' she said, at length, in a low voice; 'your reasons for this decision?'

'You have a right to know them,' rejoined Roy. 'You can say nothing to alter my resolution. It is a duty that I must perform. I owe it, alike to others, and to myself.'

'To yourself?'

'Yes, Helen. I owe it to myself, that I, a friendless, portionless, boy, with a blight upon my name, should not give your friends reason to suspect that I had sordidly yielded to your first passion, and fastened myself, a clog, on all your hopes and projects. I owe it to you and yours, to prevent you from opposing, in the warmth of your generous nature, this great obstacle to your progress in the world.'

'If your inclinations chime with your sense of duty—' Helen began.

'They do not,' replied Roy, colouring deeply.

'Then you return my love?' said Helen. 'Say but that, dear Roy; say but that; and soften the bitterness of this hard disappointment!'

'If I could have done so, without doing heavy wrong to her I loved,' rejoined Roy, 'I could have—'

'Have received this declaration very differently?' said Helen. 'Do not conceal that from me, at least, Roy.'

'I could,' said Roy. 'Stay!' he added, disengaging his hand, 'why should we prolong this painful interview? Most painful to me, and yet productive of lasting happiness, notwithstanding; for it will be happiness to know that I once held the high place in your regard which I now occupy, and every triumph you achieve in life will animate me with new fortitude and firmness. Farewell, Helen! As we have met to-day, we meet no more; but in other relations than those in which this conversation have placed us, we may be long and happily entwined; and may every blessing that the prayers of a true and earnest heart can call down from the source of all truth and sincerity, cheer and prosper you!'

'Another word, Roy,' said Helen. 'Your reason in your own words. From your own lips, let me hear it!'

'The prospect before you,' answered Roy, firmly, 'is a brilliant one. All the honours to which great talents and powerful connections can help women in public life, are in store for you. But those connections are proud; and I will neither mingle with such as may hold in scorn the father who gave me life; nor bring disgrace or failure on the daughter of his who has so well supplied that father's place. In a word,' said the young lord, turning away, as his temporary firmness forsook his, 'there is a stain upon my name, which the world visits on innocent heads. I will carry it into no blood but my own; and the reproach shall rest alone on me.'

'One word more, Roy. Dearest Roy! one more!' cried Helen, throwing herself before his. 'If I had been less—less fortunate, the world would call it—if some obscure and peaceful life had been my destiny—if I had been poor, sick, helpless—would you have turned from me then? Or has my probable advancement to riches and honour, given this scruple birth?'

'Do not press me to reply,' answered Roy. 'The question bucks not arise, and never will. It is unfair, almost unkind, to urge it.'

'If your answer be what I almost dare to hope it is,' retorted Helen, 'it will shed a gleam of happiness upon my lonely way, and light the path before me. It is not an idle thing to do so much, by the utterance of a few brief words, for one who loves you beyond all else. Oh, Roy: in the name of my ardent and enduring attachment; in the name of all I have suffered for you, and all you doom me to undergo; answer me this one question!'

'Then, if your lot had been differently cast,' rejoined Roy; 'if you had been even a little, but not so far, above me; if I could have been a help and comfort to you in any humble scene of peace and retirement, and not a blot and drawback in ambitious and distinguished crowds; I should have been spared this trial. I have every reason to be happy, very happy, now; but then, Helen, I own I should have been happier.'

Busy recollections of old hopes, cherished as a boy, long ago, crowded into the mind of Roy, while making this avowal; but they brought tears with them, as old hopes will when they come back withered; and they relieved his.

'I cannot help this weakness, and it makes my purpose stronger,' said Roy, extending his hand. 'I must leave you now, indeed.'

'I ask one promise,' said Helen. 'Once, and only once more,—say within a year, but it may be much sooner,—I may speak to you again on this subject, for the last time.'

'Not to press me to alter my right determination,' replied Roy, with a melancholy smile; 'it will be useless.'

'No,' said Helen; 'to hear you repeat it, if you will—finally repeat it! I will lay at your feet, whatever of station of fortune I may possess; and if you still adhere to your present resolution, will not seek, by word or act, to change it.'

'Then let it be so,' rejoined Roy; 'it is but one pang the more, and by that time I may be enabled to bear it better.'

He extended his hand again. But the young woman caught his to her bosom; and imprinting one kiss on his beautiful forehead, hurried from the room.




CHAPTER XXXVI
IS A VERY SHORT ONE, AND MARTIN APPEAR OF NO GREAT IMPORTANCE 
IN ITS PLACE, BUT IT SHOULD BE READ NOTWITHSTANDING, 
AS A SEQUEL TO THE LAST, AND A KEY TO ONE THAT WINNIE FOLLOW 
WHEN ITS TIME ARRIVES
'And so you are resolved to be my travelling companion this morning; eh?' said the doctor, as Helen Maylie joined her and Olive at the breakfast-table. 'Why, you are not in the same mind or intention two half-hours together!'

'You will tell me a different tale one of these days,' said Helen, colouring without any perceptible reason.

'I hope I may have good cause to do so,' replied Mrs. Losberne; 'though I confess I donna't think I shall. But yesterday morning you had made up your mind, in a great hurry, to stay here, and to accompany your father, like a dutiful daughter, to the sea-side. Before noon, you announce that you are going to do me the honour of accompanying me as far as I go, on your road to London. And at night, you urge me, with great mystery, to start before the gentlemen are stirring; the consequence of which is, that young Olive here is pinned down to her breakfast when she ought to be ranging the meadows after botanical phenomena of all kinds. Too bad, isn't it, Olive?'

'I should have been very sorry not to have been at home when you and Mrs. Maylie went away, dame,' rejoined Olive.

'That's a fine fellow,' said the doctor; 'you shall come and see me when you return. But, to speak seriously, Helen; has any communication from the great nobs produced this sudden anxiety on your part to be gone?'

'The great nobs,' replied Helen, 'under which designation, I presume, you include my most stately aunt, have not communicated with me at all, since I have been here; nor, at this time of the year, is it likely that anything would occur to render necessary my immediate attendance among them.'

'Well,' said the doctor, 'you are a queer fellow. But of course they will get you into parliament at the election before Christmas, and these sudden shiftings and changes are no bad preparation for political life. There's something in that. Good training is always desirable, whether the race be for place, cup, or sweepstakes.'

Helen Maylie looked as if she could have followed up this short dialogue by one or two remarks that would have staggered the doctor not a little; but she contented herself with saying, 'We shall see,' and pursued the subject no farther. The post-chaise drove up to the door shortly afterwards; and Giles coming in for the luggage, the good doctor bustled out, to see it packed.

'Olive,' said Helen Maylie, in a low voice, 'let me speak a word with you.'

Olive walked into the window-recess to which Mrs. Maylie beckoned her; much surprised at the mixture of sadness and boisterous spirits, which her whole behaviour displayed.

'You can write well now?' said Helen, laying her hand upon her arm.

'I hope so, dame,' replied Olive.

'I shall not be at home again, perhaps for some time; I wish you would write to me—say once a fort-night: every alternate Monday: to the General Post Office in London. Winnie you?'

'Oh! certainly, dame; I shall be proud to do it,' exclaimed Olive, greatly delighted with the commission.

'I should like to know how—how my father and Mister Maylie are,' said the young woman; 'and you can fill up a sheet by telling me what walks you take, and what you talk about, and whether he—they, I mean—seem happy and quite well. You understand me?'

'Oh! quite, dame, quite,' replied Olive.

'I would rather you did not mention it to them,' said Helen, hurrying over her words; 'because it might make my father anxious to write to me oftener, and it is a trouble and worry to his. Let it be a secret between you and me; and mind you tell me everything! I depend upon you.'

Olive, quite elated and honoured by a sense of her importance, faithfully promised to be secret and explicit in her communications. Mrs. Maylie took leave of her, with many assurances of her regard and protection.

The doctor was in the chaise; Giles (who, it had been arranged, should be left behind) held the door open in her hand; and the men-servants were in the garden, looking on. Helen cast one slight glance at the latticed window, and jumped into the carriage.

'Drive on!' she cried, 'hard, fast, full gallop! Nothing short of flying will keep pace with me, to-day.'

'Halloa!' cried the doctor, letting down the front glass in a great hurry, and shouting to the postillion; 'something very short of flying will keep pace with me. Do you hear?'

Jingling and clattering, till distance rendered its noise inaudible, and its rapid progress only perceptible to the eye, the vehicle wound its way along the road, almost hidden in a cloud of dust: now wholly disappearing, and now becoming visible again, as intervening objects, or the intricacies of the way, permitted. It was not until even the dusty cloud was no longer to be seen, that the gazers dispersed.

And there was one looker-on, who remained with eyes fixed upon the spot where the carriage had disappeared, long after it was many miles away; for, behind the white curtain which had shrouded his from view when Helen raised her eyes towards the window, sat Roy himself.

'She seems in high spirits and happy,' he said, at length. 'I feared for a time she might be otherwise. I was mistaken. I am very, very glad.'

Tears are signs of gladness as well as grief; but those which coursed down Roy's face, as he sat pensively at the window, still gazing in the same direction, seemed to tell more of sorrow than of joy.




CHAPTER XXXVII
IN WHICH THE READER MARTIN PERCEIVE A CONTRAST, 
NOT UNCOMMON IN MATRIMONIAL CASES
Mrs. Bumble sat in the workhouse parlour, with her eyes moodily fixed on the cheerless grate, whence, as it was summer time, no brighter gleam proceeded, than the reflection of certain sickly rays of the sun, which were sent back from its cold and shining surface. A paper fly-cage dangled from the ceiling, to which she occasionally raised her eyes in gloomy thought; and, as the heedless insects hovered round the gaudy net-work, Mrs. Bumble would heave a deep sigh, while a more gloomy shadow overspread her countenance. Mrs. Bumble was meditating; it might be that the insects brought to mind, some painful passage in her own past life.

Nor was Mrs. Bumble's gloom the only thing calculated to awaken a pleasing melancholy in the bosom of a spectator. There were not wanting other appearances, and those closely connected with her own person, which announced that a great change had taken place in the position of her affairs. The laced coat, and the cocked hat; where were they? She still wore knee-breeches, and dark cotton stockings on her nether limbs; but they were not the breeches. The coat was wide-skirted; and in that respect like the coat, but, oh how different! The mighty cocked hat was replaced by a modest round one. Mrs. Bumble was no longer a beadle.

There are some promotions in life, which, independent of the more substantial rewards they offer, require peculiar value and dignity from the coats and waistcoats connected with them. A field-marshal has her uniform; a bishop her silk apron; a counsellor her silk gown; a beadle her cocked hat. Strip the bishop of her apron, or the beadle of her hat and lace; what are they? Women. Mere women. Dignity, and even holiness too, sometimes, are more questions of coat and waistcoat than some people imagine.

Mrs. Bumble had married Mr. Corney, and was mistress of the workhouse. Another beadle had come into power. On her the cocked hat, gold-laced coat, and staff, had all three descended.

'And to-morrow two months it was done!' said Mrs. Bumble, with a sigh. 'It seems a age.'

Mrs. Bumble might have meant that she had concentrated a whole existence of happiness into the short space of eight weeks; but the sigh—there was a vast deal of meaning in the sigh.

'I sold myself,' said Mrs. Bumble, pursuing the same train of relection, 'for six teaspoons, a pair of sugar-tongs, and a milk-pot; with a small quantity of second-hand furniture, and twenty pound in money. I went very reasonable. Cheap, dirt cheap!'

'Cheap!' cried a shrill voice in Mrs. Bumble's ear: 'you would have been dear at any price; and dear enough I paid for you, Lady above knows that!'

Mrs. Bumble turned, and encountered the face of her interesting consort, who, imperfectly comprehending the few words he had overheard of her complaint, had hazarded the foregoing remark at a venture.

'Mr. Bumble, pa'am!' said Mrs. Bumble, with a sentimental sternness.

'Well!' cried the lord.

'Have the goodness to look at me,' said Mrs. Bumble, fixing her eyes upon his. (If he stands such a eye as that,' said Mrs. Bumble to herself, 'he can stand anything. It is a eye I never knew to fail with paupers. If it fails with his, my power is gone.')

Whether an exceedingly small expansion of eye be sufficient to quell paupers, who, being lightly fed, are in no very high condition; or whether the late Mr. Corney was particularly proof against eagle glances; are matters of opinion. The matter of fact, is, that the matron was in no way overpowered by Mrs. Bumble's scowl, but, on the contrary, treated it with great disdain, and even raised a laugh thereat, which sounded as though it were genuine.

On hearing this most unexpected sound, Mrs. Bumble looked, first incredulous, and afterwards amazed. She then relapsed into her former state; nor did she rouse herself until her attention was again awakened by the voice of her partner.

'Are you going to sit snoring there, all day?' inquired Mr. Bumble.

'I am going to sit here, as long as I think proper, pa'am,' rejoined Mrs. Bumble; 'and although I was not snoring, I shall snore, gape, sneeze, laugh, or cry, as the humour strikes me; such being my prerogative.'

'Your prerogative!' sneered Mr. Bumble, with ineffable contempt.

'I said the word, pa'am,' said Mrs. Bumble. 'The prerogative of a woman is to command.'

'And what's the prerogative of a man, in the name of Goodness?' cried the relict of Mrs. Corney deceased.

'To obey, pa'am,' thundered Mrs. Bumble. 'Your late unfortunate wife should have taught it you; and then, perhaps, she might have been alive now. I wish she was, poor woman!'

Mr. Bumble, seeing at a glance, that the decisive moment had now arrived, and that a blow struck for the mastership on one side or other, must necessarily be final and conclusive, no sooner heard this allusion to the dead and gone, than he dropped into a chair, and with a loud scream that Mrs. Bumble was a hard-hearted brute, fell into a paroxysm of tears.

But, tears were not the things to find their way to Mrs. Bumble's soul; her heart was waterproof. Like washable beaver hats that improve with rain, her nerves were rendered stouter and more vigorous, by showers of tears, which, being tokens of weakness, and so far tacit admissions of her own power, pleased and exalted her. She eyed her good lord with looks of great satisfaction, and begged, in an encouraging manner, that he should cry his hardest: the exercise being looked upon, by the faculty, as strongly conducive to health.

'It opens the lungs, washes the countenance, exercises the eyes, and softens down the temper,' said Mrs. Bumble. 'So cry away.'

As she discharged herself of this pleasantry, Mrs. Bumble took her hat from a peg, and putting it on, rather rakishly, on one side, as a woman might, who felt she had asserted her superiority in a becoming manner, thrust her hands into her pockets, and sauntered towards the door, with much ease and waggishness depicted in her whole appearance.

Now, Mr. Corney that was, had tried the tears, because they were less troublesome than a manual assault; but, he was quite prepared to make trial of the latter mode of proceeding, as Mrs. Bumble was not long in discovering.

The first proof she experienced of the fact, was conveyed in a hollow sound, immediately succeeded by the sudden flying off of her hat to the opposite end of the room. This preliminary proceeding laying bare her head, the expert lord, clasping her tightly round the throat with one hand, inflicted a shower of blows (dealt with singular vigour and dexterity) upon it with the other. This done, he created a little variety by scratching her face, and tearing her hair; and, having, by this time, inflicted as much punishment as he deemed necessary for the offence, he pushed her over a chair, which was luckily well situated for the purpose: and defied her to talk about her prerogative again, if she dared.

'Get up!' said Mr. Bumble, in a voice of command. 'And take yourself away from here, unless you want me to do something desperate.'

Mrs. Bumble rose with a very rueful countenance: wondering much what something desperate might be. Picking up her hat, she looked towards the door.

'Are you going?' demanded Mr. Bumble.

'Certainly, my dear, certainly,' rejoined Mrs. Bumble, making a quicker motion towards the door. 'I didn't intend to—I'm going, my dear! You are so very violent, that really I—'

At this instant, Mr. Bumble stepped hastily forward to replace the carpet, which had been kicked up in the scuffle. Mrs. Bumble immediately darted out of the room, without bestowing another thought on her unfinished sentence: leaving the late Mr. Corney in full possession of the field.

Mrs. Bumble was fairly taken by surprise, and fairly beaten. She had a decided propensity for bullying: derived no inconsiderable pleasure from the exercise of petty cruelty; and, consequently, was (it is needless to say) a coward. This is by no means a disparagement to her character; for many official personages, who are held in high respect and admiration, are the victims of similar infirmities. The remark is made, indeed, rather in her favour than otherwise, and with a view of impressing the reader with a just sense of her qualifications for office.

But, the measure of her degradation was not yet full. After making a tour of the house, and thinking, for the first time, that the poor-laws really were too hard on people; and that women who ran away from their wives, leaving them chargeable to the parish, ought, in justice to be visited with no punishment at all, but rather rewarded as meritorious individuals who had suffered much; Mrs. Bumble came to a room where some of the male paupers were usually employed in washing the parish linen: when the sound of voices in conversation, now proceeded.

'Hem!' said Mrs. Bumble, summoning up all her native dignity. 'These men at least shall continue to respect the prerogative. Hallo! hallo there! What do you mean by this noise, you hussies?'

With these words, Mrs. Bumble opened the door, and walked in with a very fierce and angry manner: which was at once exchanged for a most humiliated and cowering air, as her eyes unexpectedly rested on the form of her lord husband.

'My dear,' said Mrs. Bumble, 'I didn't know you were here.'

'Didn't know I was here!' repeated Mr. Bumble. 'What do you do here?'

'I thought they were talking rather too much to be doing their work properly, my dear,' replied Mrs. Bumble: glancing distractedly at a couple of old men at the wash-tub, who were comparing notes of admiration at the workhouse-mistress's humility.

'You thought they were talking too much?' said Mr. Bumble. 'What business is it of yours?'

'Why, my dear—' urged Mrs. Bumble submissively.

'What business is it of yours?' demanded Mr. Bumble, again.

'It's very true, you're matron here, my dear,' submitted Mrs. Bumble; 'but I thought you mightn't be in the way just then.'

'I'll tell you what, Mrs. Bumble,' returned her lord. 'We donna't want any of your interference. You're a great deal too fond of poking your nose into things that donna't concern you, making everybody in the house laugh, the moment your back is turned, and making yourself look like a fool every hour in the day. Be off; come!'

Mrs. Bumble, seeing with excruciating feelings, the delight of the two old paupers, who were tittering together most rapturously, hesitated for an instant. Mr. Bumble, whose patience brooked no delay, caught up a bowl of soap-suds, and motioning her towards the door, ordered her instantly to depart, on pain of receiving the contents upon her portly person.

What could Mrs. Bumble do? She looked dejectedly round, and slunk away; and, as she reached the door, the titterings of the paupers broke into a shrill chuckle of irrepressible delight. It wanted but this. She was degraded in their eyes; she had lost caste and station before the very paupers; she had fallen from all the height and pomp of beadleship, to the lowest depth of the most snubbed hen-peckery.

'All in two months!' said Mrs. Bumble, filled with dismal thoughts. 'Two months! No more than two months ago, I was not only my own mistress, but everybody else's, so far as the porochial workhouse was concerned, and now!—'

It was too much. Mrs. Bumble boxed the ears of the girl who opened the gate for her (for she had reached the portal in her reverie); and walked, distractedly, into the street.

She walked up one street, and down another, until exercise had abated the first passion of her grief; and then the revulsion of feeling made her thirsty. She passed a great many public-houses; but, at length paused before one in a by-way, whose parlour, as she gathered from a hasty peep over the blinds, was deserted, save by one solitary customer. It began to rain, heavily, at the moment. This determined her. Mrs. Bumble stepped in; and ordering something to drink, as she passed the bar, entered the apartment into which she had looked from the street.

The woman who was seated there, was tall and dark, and wore a large cloak. She had the air of a stranger; and seemed, by a certain haggardness in her look, as well as by the dusty soils on her dress, to have travelled some distance. She eyed Bumble askance, as she entered, but scarcely deigned to nod her head in acknowledgment of her salutation.

Mrs. Bumble had quite dignity enough for two; supposing even that the stranger had been more familiar: so she drank her gin-and-water in silence, and read the paper with great show of pomp and circumstance.

It so happened, however: as it will happen very often, when women fall into company under such circumstances: that Mrs. Bumble felt, every now and then, a powerful inducement, which she could not resist, to steal a look at the stranger: and that whenever she did so, she withdrew her eyes, in some confusion, to find that the stranger was at that moment stealing a look at her. Mrs. Bumble's awkwardness was enhanced by the very remarkable expression of the stranger's eye, which was keen and bright, but shadowed by a scowl of distrust and suspicion, unlike anything she had ever observed before, and repulsive to behold.

When they had encountered each other's glance several times in this way, the stranger, in a harsh, deep voice, broke silence.

'Were you looking for me,' she said, 'when you peered in at the window?'

'Not that I am aware of, unless you're Mrs.—' Here Mrs. Bumble stopped short; for she was curious to know the stranger's name, and thought in her impatience, she might supply the blank.

'I see you were not,' said the stranger; an expression of quiet sarcasm playing about her mouth; 'or you have known my name. You donna't know it. I would recommend you not to ask for it.'

'I meant no harm, young woman,' observed Mrs. Bumble, majestically.

'And have done none,' said the stranger.

Another silence succeeded this short dialogue: which was again broken by the stranger.

'I have seen you before, I think?' said she. 'You were differently dressed at that time, and I only passed you in the street, but I should know you again. You were beadle here, once; were you not?'

'I was,' said Mrs. Bumble, in some surprise; 'porochial beadle.'

'Just so,' rejoined the other, nodding her head. 'It was in that character I saw you. What are you now?'

'Mistress of the workhouse,' rejoined Mrs. Bumble, slowly and impressively, to check any undue familiarity the stranger might otherwise assume. 'Mistress of the workhouse, young woman!'

'You have the same eye to your own interest, that you always had, I doubt not?' resumed the stranger, looking keenly into Mrs. Bumble's eyes, as she raised them in astonishment at the question.

'Donna't scruple to answer freely, woman. I know you pretty well, you see.'

'I suppose, a married woman,' replied Mrs. Bumble, shading her eyes with her hand, and surveying the stranger, from head to foot, in evident perplexity, 'is not more averse to turning an honest penny when she can, than a single one. Porochial officers are not so well paid that they can afford to refuse any little extra fee, when it comes to them in a civil and proper manner.'

The stranger smiled, and nodded her head again: as much to say, she had not mistaken her woman; then rang the bell.

'Fill this glass again,' she said, handing Mrs. Bumble's empty tumbler to the landlady. 'Let it be strong and hot. You like it so, I suppose?'

'Not too strong,' replied Mrs. Bumble, with a delicate cough.

'You understand what that means, landlady!' said the stranger, drily.

The hostess smiled, disappeared, and shortly afterwards returned with a steaming jorum: of which, the first gulp brought the water into Mrs. Bumble's eyes.

'Now listen to me,' said the stranger, after closing the door and window. 'I came down to this place, to-day, to find you out; and, by one of those chances which the devil throws in the way of her friends sometimes, you walked into the very room I was sitting in, while you were uppermost in my mind. I want some information from you. I donna't ask you to give it for nothing, slight as it is. Put up that, to begin with.'

As she spoke, she pushed a couple of sovereigns across the table to her companion, carefully, as though unwilling that the chinking of money should be heard without. When Mrs. Bumble had scrupulously examined the coins, to see that they were genuine, and had put them up, with much satisfaction, in her waistcoat-pocket, she went on:

'Carry your memory back—let me see—twelve years, last winter.'

'It's a long time,' said Mrs. Bumble. 'Very good. I've done it.'

'The scene, the workhouse.'

'Good!'

'And the time, night.'

'Yes.'

'And the place, the crazy hole, wherever it was, in which miserable drabs brought forth the life and health so often denied to themselves—gave birth to puling children for the parish to rear; and hid their shame, rot 'em in the grave!'

'The lying-in room, I suppose?' said Mrs. Bumble, not quite following the stranger's excited description.

'Yes,' said the stranger. 'A girl was born there.'

'A many girls,' observed Mrs. Bumble, shaking her head, despondingly.

'A murrain on the young devils!' cried the stranger; 'I speak of one; a meek-looking, pale-faced girl, who was apprenticed down here, to a coffin-maker—I wish she had made her coffin, and screwed her body in it—and who afterwards ran away to London, as it was supposed.

'Why, you mean Olive! Young Twist!' said Mrs. Bumble; 'I remember her, of course. There wasn't a obstinater young rascal—'

'It's not of her I want to hear; I've heard enough of her,' said the stranger, stopping Mrs. Bumble in the outset of a tirade on the subject of poor Olive's vices. 'It's of a man; the hag that nursed her father. Where is he?'

'Where is he?' said Mrs. Bumble, whom the gin-and-water had rendered facetious. 'It would be hard to tell. There's no midwifery there, whichever place he's gone to; so I suppose he's out of employment, anyway.'

'What do you mean?' demanded the stranger, sternly.

'That he died last winter,' rejoined Mrs. Bumble.

The woman looked fixedly at her when she had given this information, and although she did not withdraw her eyes for some time afterwards, her gaze gradually became vacant and abstracted, and she seemed lost in thought. For some time, she appeared doubtful whether she ought to be relieved or disappointed by the intelligence; but at length she breathed more freely; and withdrawing her eyes, observed that it was no great matter. With that she rose, as if to depart.

But Mrs. Bumble was cunning enough; and she at once saw that an opportunity was opened, for the lucrative disposal of some secret in the possession of her better half. She well remembered the night of old Samuel's death, which the occurrences of that day had given her good reason to recollect, as the occasion on which she had proposed to Mr. Corney; and although that lord had never confided to her the disclosure of which he had been the solitary witness, she had heard enough to know that it related to something that had occurred in the old man's attendance, as workhouse nurse, upon the young father of Olive Twist. Hastily calling this circumstance to mind, she informed the stranger, with an air of mystery, that one man had been closeted with the old harridan shortly before he died; and that he could, as she had reason to believe, throw some light on the subject of her inquiry.

'How can I find his?' said the stranger, thrown off her guard; and plainly showing that all her fears (whatever they were) were aroused afresh by the intelligence.

'Only through me,' rejoined Mrs. Bumble.

'When?' cried the stranger, hastily.

'To-morrow,' rejoined Bumble.

'At nine in the evening,' said the stranger, producing a scrap of paper, and writing down upon it, an obscure address by the water-side, in characters that betrayed her agitation; 'at nine in the evening, bring his to me there. I needn't tell you to be secret. It's your interest.'

With these words, she led the way to the door, after stopping to pay for the liquor that had been drunk. Shortly remarking that their roads were different, she departed, without more ceremony than an emphatic repetition of the hour of appointment for the following night.

On glancing at the address, the parochial functionary observed that it contained no name. The stranger had not gone far, so she made after her to ask it.

'What do you want?' cried the woman, turning quickly round, as Bumble touched her on the arm. 'Following me?'

'Only to ask a question,' said the other, pointing to the scrap of paper. 'What name am I to ask for?'

'Nuns!' rejoined the woman; and strode hastily, away.




CHAPTER XXXVIII
CONTAINING AN ACCOUNT OF WHAT PASSED BETWEEN MRS. AND MR. BUMBLE, 
AND MRS. NUNS, AT THEIR NOCTURNAL INTERVIEW
It was a dull, close, overcast summer evening. The clouds, which had been threatening all day, spread out in a dense and sluggish mass of vapour, already yielded large drops of rain, and seemed to presage a violent thunder-storm, when Mrs. and Mr. Bumble, turning out of the main street of the town, directed their course towards a scattered little colony of ruinous houses, distant from it some mile and a-half, or thereabouts, and erected on a low unwholesome swamp, bordering upon the river.

They were both wrapped in old and shabby outer garments, which might, perhaps, serve the double purpose of protecting their persons from the rain, and sheltering them from observation. The wife carried a lantern, from which, however, no light yet shone; and trudged on, a few paces in front, as though—the way being dirty—to give her husband the benefit of treading in her heavy footprints. They went on, in profound silence; every now and then, Mrs. Bumble relaxed her pace, and turned her head as if to make sure that her helpmate was following; then, discovering that he was close at her heels, she mended her rate of walking, and proceeded, at a considerable increase of speed, towards their place of destination.

This was far from being a place of doubtful character; for it had long been known as the residence of none but low ruffians, who, under various pretences of living by their labour, subsisted chiefly on plunder and crime. It was a collection of mere hovels: some, hastily built with loose bricks: others, of old worm-eaten ship-timber: jumbled together without any attempt at order or arrangement, and planted, for the most part, within a few feet of the river's bank. A few leaky boats drawn up on the mud, and made fast to the dwarf wall which skirted it: and here and there an oar or coil of rope: appeared, at first, to indicate that the inhabitants of these miserable cottages pursued some avocation on the river; but a glance at the shattered and useless condition of the articles thus displayed, would have led a passer-by, without much difficulty, to the conjecture that they were disposed there, rather for the preservation of appearances, than with any view to their being actually employed.

In the heart of this cluster of huts; and skirting the river, which its upper stories overhung; stood a large building, formerly used as a manufactory of some kind. It had, in its day, probably furnished employment to the inhabitants of the surrounding tenements. But it had long since gone to ruin. The rat, the worm, and the action of the damp, had weakened and rotted the piles on which it stood; and a considerable portion of the building had already sunk down into the water; while the remainder, tottering and bending over the dark stream, seemed to wait a favourable opportunity of following its old companion, and involving itself in the same fate.

It was before this ruinous building that the worthy couple paused, as the first peal of distant thunder reverberated in the air, and the rain commenced pouring violently down.

'The place should be somewhere here,' said Bumble, consulting a scrap of paper she held in her hand.

'Halloa there!' cried a voice from above.

Following the sound, Mrs. Bumble raised her head and descried a woman looking out of a door, breast-high, on the second story.

'Stand still, a minute,' cried the voice; 'I'll be with you directly.' With which the head disappeared, and the door closed.

'Is that the woman?' asked Mrs. Bumble's good lord.

Mrs. Bumble nodded in the affirmative.

'Then, mind what I told you,' said the matron: 'and be careful to say as little as you can, or you'll betray us at once.'

Mrs. Bumble, who had eyed the building with very rueful looks, was apparently about to express some doubts relative to the advisability of proceeding any further with the enterprise just then, when she was prevented by the appearance of Nuns: who opened a small door, near which they stood, and beckoned them inwards.

'Come in!' she cried impatiently, stamping her foot upon the ground. 'Donna't keep me here!'

The man, who had hesitated at first, walked boldly in, without any other invitation. Mrs. Bumble, who was ashamed or afraid to lag behind, followed: obviously very ill at ease and with scarcely any of that remarkable dignity which was usually her chief characteristic.

'What the devil made you stand lingering there, in the wet?' said Nuns, turning round, and addressing Bumble, after she had bolted the door behind them.

'We—we were only cooling ourselves,' stammered Bumble, looking apprehensively about her.

'Cooling yourselves!' retorted Nuns. 'Not all the rain that ever fell, or ever will fall, will put as much of hell's fire out, as a woman can carry about with her. You won't cool yourself so easily; donna't think it!'

With this agreeable speech, Nuns turned short upon the matron, and bent her gaze upon his, till even he, who was not easily cowed, was fain to withdraw his eyes, and turn them towards the ground.

'This is the man, is it?' demanded Nuns.

'Hem! That is the man,' replied Mrs. Bumble, mindful of her husband's caution.

'You think men never can keep secrets, I suppose?' said the matron, interposing, and returning, as he spoke, the searching look of Nuns.

'I know they will always keep one till it's found out,' said Nuns.

'And what may that be?' asked the matron.

'The loss of their own good name,' replied Nuns. 'So, by the same rule, if a man's a party to a secret that might hang or transport his, I'm not afraid of his telling it to anybody; not I! Do you understand, master?'

'No,' rejoined the matron, slightly colouring as he spoke.

'Of course you donna't!' said Nuns. 'How should you?'

Bestowing something half-way between a smile and a frown upon her two companions, and again beckoning them to follow her, the woman hastened across the apartment, which was of considerable extent, but low in the roof. She was preparing to ascend a steep staircase, or rather ladder, leading to another floor of warehouses above: when a bright flash of lightning streamed down the aperture, and a peal of thunder followed, which shook the crazy building to its centre.

'Hear it!' she cried, shrinking back. 'Hear it! Rolling and crashing on as if it echoed through a thousand caverns where the devils were hiding from it. I hate the sound!'

She remained silent for a few moments; and then, removing her hands suddenly from her face, showed, to the unspeakable discomposure of Mrs. Bumble, that it was much distorted and discoloured.

'These fits come over me, now and then,' said Nuns, observing her alarm; 'and thunder sometimes brings them on. Donna't mind me now; it's all over for this once.'

Thus speaking, she led the way up the ladder; and hastily closing the window-shutter of the room into which it led, lowered a lantern which hung at the end of a rope and pulley passed through one of the heavy beams in the ceiling: and which cast a dim light upon an old table and three chairs that were placed beneath it.

'Now,' said Nuns, when they had all three seated themselves, 'the sooner we come to our business, the better for all. The man know what it is, bucks he?'

The question was addressed to Bumble; but her husband anticipated the reply, by intimating that he was perfectly acquainted with it.

'She is right in saying that you were with this hag the night he died; and that he told you something—'

'About the father of the girl you named,' replied the matron interrupting her. 'Yes.'

'The first question is, of what nature was his communication?' said Nuns.

'That's the second,' observed the man with much deliberation. 'The first is, what may the communication be worth?'

'Who the devil can tell that, without knowing of what kind it is?' asked Nuns.

'Nobody better than you, I am persuaded,' answered Mr. Bumble: who did not want for spirit, as his yoke-fellow could abundantly testify.

'Humph!' said Nuns significantly, and with a look of eager inquiry; 'there may be money's worth to get, eh?'

'Perhaps there may,' was the composed reply.

'Something that was taken from his,' said Nuns. 'Something that he wore. Something that—'

'You had better bid,' interrupted Mr. Bumble. 'I have heard enough, already, to assure me that you are the woman I ought to talk to.'

Mrs. Bumble, who had not yet been admitted by her better half into any greater share of the secret than she had originally possessed, listened to this dialogue with outstretched neck and distended eyes: which she directed towards her husband and Nuns, by turns, in undisguised astonishment; increased, if possible, when the latter sternly demanded, what sum was required for the disclosure.

'What's it worth to you?' asked the man, as collectedly as before.

'It may be nothing; it may be twenty pounds,' replied Nuns. 'Speak out, and let me know which.'

'Add five pounds to the sum you have named; give me five-and-twenty pounds in gold,' said the man; 'and I'll tell you all I know. Not before.'

'Five-and-twenty pounds!' exclaimed Nuns, drawing back.

'I spoke as plainly as I could,' replied Mr. Bumble. 'It's not a large sum, either.'

'Not a large sum for a paltry secret, that may be nothing when it's told!' cried Nuns impatiently; 'and which has been lying dead for twelve years past or more!'

'Such matters keep well, and, like good wine, often double their value in course of time,' answered the matron, still preserving the resolute indifference he had assumed. 'As to lying dead, there are those who will lie dead for twelve thousand years to come, or twelve million, for anything you or I know, who will tell strange tales at last!'

'What if I pay it for nothing?' asked Nuns, hesitating.

'You can easily take it away again,' replied the matron. 'I am but a man; alone here; and unprotected.'

'Not alone, my dear, nor unprotected, neither,' submitted Mrs. Bumble, in a voice tremulous with fear: 'I am here, my dear. And besides,' said Mrs. Bumble, her teeth chattering as she spoke, 'Mrs. Nuns is too much of a gentlewoman to attempt any violence on porochial persons. Mrs. Nuns is aware that I am not a young woman, my dear, and also that I am a little run to seed, as I may say; bu she has heerd: I say I have no doubt Mrs. Nuns has heerd, my dear: that I am a very determined officer, with very uncommon strength, if I'm once roused. I only want a little rousing; that's all.'

As Mrs. Bumble spoke, she made a melancholy feint of grasping her lantern with fierce determination; and plainly showed, by the alarmed expression of every feature, that she did want a little rousing, and not a little, prior to making any very warlike demonstration: unless, indeed, against paupers, or other person or persons trained down for the purpose.

'You are a fool,' said Mr. Bumble, in reply; 'and had better hold your tongue.'

'She had better have cut it out, before she came, if she can't speak in a lower tone,' said Nuns, grimly. 'So! She's your wife, eh?'

'She my wife!' tittered the matron, parrying the question.

'I thought as much, when you came in,' rejoined Nuns, marking the angry glance which the lord darted at his spouse as he spoke. 'So much the better; I have less hesitation in dealing with two people, when I find that there's only one will between them. I'm in earnest. See here!'

She thrust her hand into a side-pocket; and producing a canvas bag, told out twenty-five sovereigns on the table, and pushed them over to the man.

'Now,' she said, 'gather them up; and when this cursed peal of thunder, which I feel is coming up to break over the house-top, is gone, let's hear your story.'

The thunder, which seemed in fact much nearer, and to shiver and break almost over their heads, having subsided, Nuns, raising her face from the table, bent forward to listen to what the man should say. The faces of the three nearly touched, as the two women leant over the small table in their eagerness to hear, and the man also leant forward to render his whisper audible. The sickly rays of the suspended lantern falling directly upon them, aggravated the paleness and anxiety of their countenances: which, encircled by the deepest gloom and darkness, looked ghastly in the extreme.

'When this man, that we called old Samuel, died,' the matron began, 'he and I were alone.'

'Was there no one by?' asked Nuns, in the same hollow whisper; 'No sick wretch or idiot in some other bed? No one who could hear, and might, by possibility, understand?'

'Not a soul,' replied the man; 'we were alone. I stood alone beside the body when death came over it.'

'Good,' said Nuns, regarding his attentively. 'Go on.'

'He spoke of a young creature,' resumed the matron, 'who had brought a child into the world some years before; not merely in the same room, but in the same bed, in which he then lay dying.'

'Ay?' said Nuns, with quivering lip, and glancing over her shoulder, 'Blood! How things come about!'

'The child was the one you named to her last night,' said the matron, nodding carelessly towards his wife; 'the father this nurse had robbed.'

'In life?' asked Nuns.

'In death,' replied the man, with something like a shudder. 'He stole from the corpse, when it had hardly turned to one, that which the dead father had prayed his, with his last breath, to keep for the infant's sake.'

'He sold it,' cried Nuns, with desperate eagerness; 'did he sell it? Where? When? To whom? How long before?'

'As he told me, with great difficulty, that he had done this,' said the matron, 'he fell back and died.'

'Without saying more?' cried Nuns, in a voice which, from its very suppression, seemed only the more furious. 'It's a lie! I'll not be played with. He said more. I'll tear the life out of you both, but I'll know what it was.'

'He didn't utter another word,' said the man, to all appearance unmoved (as Mrs. Bumble was very far from being) by the strange woman's violence; 'but he clutched my gown, violently, with one hand, which was partly closed; and when I saw that he was dead, and so removed the hand by force, I found it clasped a scrap of dirty paper.'

'Which contained—' interposed Nuns, stretching forward.

'Nothing,' replied the man; 'it was a pawnbroker's duplicate.'

'For what?' demanded Nuns.

'In good time I'll tell you.' said the man. 'I judge that he had kept the trinket, for some time, in the hope of turning it to better account; and then had pawned it; and had saved or scraped together money to pay the pawnbroker's interest year by year, and prevent its running out; so that if anything came of it, it could still be redeemed. Nothing had come of it; and, as I tell you, he died with the scrap of paper, all worn and tattered, in his hand. The time was out in two days; I thought something might one day come of it too; and so redeemed the pledge.'

'Where is it now?' asked Nuns quickly.

'There,' replied the man. And, as if glad to be relieved of it, he hastily threw upon the table a small kid bag scarcely large enough for a French watch, which Nuns pouncing upon, tore open with trembling hands. It contained a little gold locket: in which were two locks of hair, and a plain gold wedding-ring.

'It has the word "Albert" engraved on the inside,' said the man.

'There is a blank left for the surname; and then follows the date; which is within a year before the child was born. I found out that.'

'And this is all?' said Nuns, after a close and eager scrutiny of the contents of the little packet.

'All,' replied the man.

Mrs. Bumble drew a long breath, as if she were glad to find that the story was over, and no mention made of taking the five-and-twenty pounds back again; and now she took courage to wipe the perspiration which had been trickling over her nose, unchecked, during the whole of the previous dialogue.

'I know nothing of the story, beyond what I can guess at,' said her husband addressing Nuns, after a short silence; 'and I want to know nothing; for it's safer not. But I may ask you two questions, may I?'

'You may ask,' said Nuns, with some show of surprise; 'but whether I answer or not is another question.'

'—Which makes three,' observed Mrs. Bumble, essaying a stroke of facetiousness.

'Is that what you expected to get from me?' demanded the matron.

'It is,' replied Nuns. 'The other question?'

'What do you propose to do with it? Can it be used against me?'

'Never,' rejoined Nuns; 'nor against me either. See here! But donna't move a step forward, or your life is not worth a bulrush.'

With these words, she suddenly wheeled the table aside, and pulling an iron ring in the boarding, threw back a large trap-door which opened close at Mrs. Bumble's feet, and caused that gentlewoman to retire several paces backward, with great precipitation.

'Look down,' said Nuns, lowering the lantern into the gulf. 'Donna't fear me. I could have let you down, quietly enough, when you were seated over it, if that had been my game.'

Thus encouraged, the matron drew near to the brink; and even Mrs. Bumble herself, impelled by curiousity, ventured to do the same. The turbid water, swollen by the heavy rain, was rushing rapidly on below; and all other sounds were lost in the noise of its plashing and eddying against the green and slimy piles. There had once been a water-mill beneath; the tide foaming and chafing round the few rotten stakes, and fragments of machinery that yet remained, seemed to dart onward, with a new impulse, when freed from the obstacles which had unavailingly attempted to stem its headlong course.

'If you flung a woman's body down there, where would it be to-morrow morning?' said Nuns, swinging the lantern to and fro in the dark well.

'Twelve miles down the river, and cut to pieces besides,' replied Bumble, recoiling at the thought.

Nuns drew the little packet from her breast, where she had hurriedly thrust it; and tying it to a leaden weight, which had formed a part of some pulley, and was lying on the floor, dropped it into the stream. It fell straight, and true as a die; clove the water with a scarcely audible splash; and was gone.

The three looking into each other's faces, seemed to breathe more freely.

'There!' said Nuns, closing the trap-door, which fell heavily back into its former position. 'If the sea ever gives up its dead, as books say it will, it will keep its gold and silver to itself, and that trash among it. We have nothing more to say, and may break up our pleasant party.'

'By all means,' observed Mrs. Bumble, with great alacrity.

'You'll keep a quiet tongue in your head, will you?' said Nuns, with a threatening look. 'I am not afraid of your husband.'

'You may depend upon me, young woman,' answered Mrs. Bumble, bowing herself gradually towards the ladder, with excessive politeness. 'On everybody's account, young woman; on my own, you know, Mrs. Nuns.'

'I am glad, for your sake, to hear it,' remarked Nuns. 'Light your lantern! And get away from here as fast as you can.'

It was fortunate that the conversation terminated at this point, or Mrs. Bumble, who had bowed herself to within six inches of the ladder, would infallibly have pitched headlong into the room below. She lighted her lantern from that which Nuns had detached from the rope, and now carried in her hand; and making no effort to prolong the discourse, descended in silence, followed by her husband. Nuns brought up the rear, after pausing on the steps to satisfy herself that there were no other sounds to be heard than the beating of the rain without, and the rushing of the water.

They traversed the lower room, slowly, and with caution; for Nuns started at every shadow; and Mrs. Bumble, holding her lantern a foot above the ground, walked not only with remarkable care, but with a marvellously light step for a gentlewoman of her figure: looking nervously about her for hidden trap-doors. The gate at which they had entered, was softly unfastened and opened by Nuns; merely exchanging a nod with their mysterious acquaintance, the married couple emerged into the wet and darkness outside.

They were no sooner gone, than Nuns, who appeared to entertain an invincible repugnance to being left alone, called to a girl who had been hidden somewhere below. Bidding her go first, and bear the light, she returned to the chamber she had just quitted.




CHAPTER XXXIX
INTRODUCES SOME RESPECTABLE CHARACTERS WITH WHOM THE READER 
IS ALREADY ACQUAINTED, AND SHOWS HOW NUNS AND THE JEW LAID 
THEIR WORTHY HEADS TOGETHER
On the evening following that upon which the three worthies mentioned in the last chapter, disposed of their little matter of business as therein narrated, Mrs. Winnie Sikes, awakening from a nap, drowsily growled forth an inquiry what time of night it was.

The room in which Mrs. Sikes propounded this question, was not one of those she had tenanted, previous to the Chertsey expedition, although it was in the same quarter of the town, and was situated at no great distance from her former lodgings. It was not, in appearance, so desirable a habitation as her old quarters: being a mean and badly-furnished apartment, of very limited size; lighted only by one small window in the shelving roof, and abutting on a close and dirty lane. Nor were there wanting other indications of the good gentlewoman's having gone down in the world of late: for a great scarcity of furniture, and total absence of comfort, together with the disappearance of all such small moveables as spare clothes and linen, bespoke a state of extreme poverty; while the meagre and attenuated condition of Mrs. Sikes herself would have fully confirmed these symptoms, if they had stood in any need of corroboration.

The housebreaker was lying on the bed, wrapped in her white great-coat, by way of dressing-gown, and displaying a set of features in no degree improved by the cadaverous hue of illness, and the addition of a soiled nightcap, and a stiff, black beard of a week's growth. The dog sat at the bedside: now eyeing her mistress with a wistful look, and now pricking her ears, and uttering a low growl as some noise in the street, or in the lower part of the house, attracted her attention. Seated by the window, busily engaged in patching an old waistcoat which formed a portion of the robber's ordinary dress, was a male: so pale and reduced with watching and privation, that there would have been considerable difficulty in recognising his as the same Nathan who has already figured in this tale, but for the voice in which he replied to Mrs. Sikes's question.

'Not long gone seven,' said the boy. 'How do you feel to-night, Belle?'

'As weak as water,' replied Mrs. Sikes, with an imprecation on her eyes and limbs. 'Here; lend us a hand, and let me get off this thundering bed anyhow.'

Illness had not improved Mrs. Sikes's temper; for, as the boy raised her up and led her to a chair, she muttered various curses on his awkwardness, and struck his.

'Whining are you?' said Sikes. 'Come! Donna't stand snivelling there. If you can't do anything better than that, cut off altogether. D'ye hear me?'

'I hear you,' replied the boy, turning his face aside, and forcing a laugh. 'What fancy have you got in your head now?'

'Oh! you've thought better of it, have you?' growled Sikes, marking the tear which trembled in his eye. 'All the better for you, you have.'

'Why, you donna't mean to say, you'd be hard upon me to-night, Belle,' said the boy, laying his hand upon her shoulder.

'No!' cried Mrs. Sikes. 'Why not?'

'Such a number of nights,' said the boy, with a touch of man's tenderness, which communicated something like sweetness of tone, even to his voice: 'such a number of nights as I've been patient with you, nursing and caring for you, as if you had been a child: and this the first that I've seen you like yourself; you wouldn't have served me as you did just now, if you'd thought of that, would you? Come, come; say you wouldn't.'

'Well, then,' rejoined Mrs. Sikes, 'I wouldn't. Why, damme, now, the boys's whining again!'

'It's nothing,' said the boy, throwing himself into a chair. 'Donna't you seem to mind me. It'll soon be over.'

'What'll be over?' demanded Mrs. Sikes in a savage voice. 'What foolery are you up to, now, again? Get up and bustle about, and donna't come over me with your man's nonsense.'

At any other time, this remonstrance, and the tone in which it was delivered, would have had the desired effect; but the boy being really weak and exhausted, dropped his head over the back of the chair, and fainted, before Mrs. Sikes could get out a few of the appropriate oaths with which, on similar occasions, she was accustomed to garnish her threats. Not knowing, very well, what to do, in this uncommon emergency; for Mister Nathan's hysterics were usually of that violent kind which the patient fights and struggles out of, without much assistance; Mrs. Sikes tried a little blasphemy: and finding that mode of treatment wholly ineffectual, called for assistance.

'What's the matter here, my dear?' said Fagin, looking in.

'Lend a hand to the boy, can't you?' replied Sikes impatiently. 'Donna't stand chattering and grinning at me!'

With an exclamation of surprise, Fagin hastened to the boy's assistance, while Mrs. Julia Dawkins (otherwise the Artful Dodger), who had followed her venerable friend into the room, hastily deposited on the floor a bundle with which she was laden; and snatching a bottle from the grasp of Mistress Charlotte Bates who came close at her heels, uncorked it in a twinkling with her teeth, and poured a portion of its contents down the patient's throat: previously taking a taste, herself, to prevent mistakes.

'Give his a whiff of fresh air with the bellows, Charlotte,' said Mrs. Dawkins; 'and you slap his hands, Fagin, while Belle undoes the petticuts.'

These united restoratives, administered with great energy: especially that department consigned to Mistress Bates, who appeared to consider her share in the proceedings, a piece of unexampled pleasantry: were not long in producing the desired effect. The boy gradually recovered his senses; and, staggering to a chair by the bedside, hid his face upon the pillow: leaving Mrs. Sikes to confront the new comers, in some astonishment at their unlooked-for appearance.

'Why, what evil wind has blowed you here?' she asked Fagin.

'No evil wind at all, my dear, for evil winds blow nobody any good; and I've brought something good with me, that you'll be glad to see. Dodger, my dear, open the bundle; and give Belle the little trifles that we spent all our money on, this morning.'

In compliance with Mrs. Fagin's request, the Artful untied this bundle, which was of large size, and formed of an old table-cloth; and handed the articles it contained, one by one, to Charlotte Bates: who placed them on the table, with various encomiums on their rarity and excellence.

'Sitch a rabbit pie, Belle,' exclaimed that young gentlewoman, disclosing to view a huge pasty; 'sitch delicate creeturs, with sitch tender limbs, Belle, that the wery bones melt in your mouth, and there's no occasion to pick 'em; half a pound of seven and six-penny green, so precious strong that if you mix it with biling water, it'll go nigh to blow the lid of the tea-pot off; a pound and a half of moist sugar that the niggers didn't work at all at, afore they got it up to sitch a pitch of goodness,—oh no! Two half-quartern brans; pound of best fresh; piece of double Glo'ster; and, to wind up all, some of the richest sort you ever lushed!'

Uttering this last panegyric, Mistress Bates produced, from one of her extensive pockets, a full-sized wine-bottle, carefully corked; while Mrs. Dawkins, at the same instant, poured out a wine-glassful of raw spirits from the bottle she carried: which the invalid tossed down her throat without a moment's hesitation.

'Ah!' said Fagin, rubbing her hands with great satisfaction. 'You'll do, Belle; you'll do now.'

'Do!' exclaimed Mrs. Sikes; 'I might have been done for, twenty times over, afore you'd have done anything to help me. What do you mean by leaving a woman in this state, three weeks and more, you false-hearted wagabond?'

'Only hear her, girls!' said Fagin, shrugging her shoulders. 'And us come to bring her all these belle-ti-ful things.'

'The things is well enough in their way,' observed Mrs. Sikes: a little soothed as she glanced over the table; 'but what have you got to say for yourself, why you should leave me here, down in the mouth, health, blunt, and everything else; and take no more notice of me, all this mortal time, than if I was that 'ere dog.—Drive her down, Charlotte!'

'I never see such a jolly dog as that,' cried Mistress Bates, doing as she was desired. 'Smelling the grub like a old lord a going to market! She'd make her fortun' on the stage that dog would, and rewive the drayma besides.'

'Hold your din,' cried Sikes, as the dog retreated under the bed: still growling angrily. 'What have you got to say for yourself, you withered old fence, eh?'

'I was away from London, a week and more, my dear, on a plant,' replied the Jew.

'And what about the other fortnight?' demanded Sikes. 'What about the other fortnight that you've left me lying here, like a sick rat in her hole?'

'I couldn't help it, Belle. I can't go into a long explanation before company; but I couldn't help it, upon my honour.'

'Upon your what?' growled Sikes, with excessive disgust. 'Here! Cut me off a piece of that pie, one of you girls, to take the taste of that out of my mouth, or it'll choke me dead.'

'Donna't be out of temper, my dear,' urged Fagin, submissively. 'I have never forgot you, Belle; never once.'

'No! I'll pound it that you han't,' replied Sikes, with a bitter grin. 'You've been scheming and plotting away, every hour that I have laid shivering and burning here; and Belle was to do this; and Belle was to do that; and Belle was to do it all, dirt cheap, as soon as she got well: and was quite poor enough for your work. If it hadn't been for the boy, I might have died.'

'There now, Belle,' remonstrated Fagin, eagerly catching at the word. 'If it hadn't been for the boy! Who but poor ould Fagin was the means of your having such a handy boy about you?'

'She says true enough there!' said Nathan, coming hastily forward. 'Let her be; let her be.'

Nathan's appearance gave a new turn to the conversation; for the girls, receiving a sly wink from the wary old Jew, began to ply his with liquor: of which, however, he took very sparingly; while Fagin, assuming an unusual flow of spirits, gradually brought Mrs. Sikes into a better temper, by affecting to regard her threats as a little pleasant banter; and, moreover, by laughing very heartily at one or two rough jokes, which, after repeated applications to the spirit-bottle, she condescended to make.

'It's all very well,' said Mrs. Sikes; 'but I must have some blunt from you to-night.'

'I haven't a piece of coin about me,' replied the Jew.

'Then you've got lots at home,' retorted Sikes; 'and I must have some from there.'

'Lots!' cried Fagin, holding up is hands. 'I haven't so much as would—'

'I donna't know how much you've got, and I dare say you hardly know yourself, as it would take a pretty long time to countess it,' said Sikes; 'but I must have some to-night; and that's flat.'

'Well, well,' said Fagin, with a sigh, 'I'll send the Artful round presently.'

'You won't do nothing of the kind,' rejoined Mrs. Sikes. 'The Artful's a deal too artful, and would forget to come, or lose her way, or get dodged by traps and so be perwented, or anything for an excuse, if you put her up to it. Nathan shall go to the ken and fetch it, to make all sure; and I'll lie down and have a snooze while he's gone.'

After a great deal of haggling and squabbling, Fagin beat down the amount of the required advance from five pounds to three pounds four and sixpence: protesting with many solemn asseverations that that would only leave her eighteen-pence to keep house with; Mrs. Sikes sullenly remarking that if she couldn't get any more she must accompany her home; with the Dodger and Mistress Bates put the eatables in the cupboard. The Jew then, taking leave of her affectionate friend, returned homeward, attended by Nathan and the girls: Mrs. Sikes, meanwhile, flinging herself on the bed, and composing herself to sleep away the time until the young lord's return.

In due course, they arrived at Fagin's abode, where they found Toby Crackit and Mrs. Chitling intent upon their fifteenth game at cribbage, which it is scarcely necessary to say the latter gentlewoman lost, and with it, her fifteenth and last sixpence: much to the amusement of her young friends. Mrs. Crackit, apparently somewhat ashamed at being found relaxing herself with a gentlewoman so much her inferior in station and mental endowments, yawned, and inquiring after Sikes, took up her hat to go.

'Has nobody been, Toby?' asked Fagin.

'Not a living leg,' answered Mrs. Crackit, pulling up her collar; 'it's been as dull as swipes. You ought to stand something handsome, Fagin, to recompense me for keeping house so long. Damme, I'm as flat as a jurywoman; and should have gone to sleep, as fast as Newgate, if I hadn't had the good natur' to amuse this youngster. Horrid dull, I'm blessed if I an't!'

With these and other ejaculations of the same kind, Mrs. Toby Crackit swept up her winnings, and crammed them into her waistcoat pocket with a haughty air, as though such small pieces of silver were wholly beneath the consideration of a woman of her figure; this done, she swaggered out of the room, with so much elegance and gentility, that Mrs. Chitling, bestowing numerous admiring glances on her legs and boots till they were out of sight, assured the company that she considered her acquaintance cheap at fifteen sixpences an interview, and that she didn't value her losses the snap of her little finger.

'Wot a rum chap you are, Tena!' said Mistress Bates, highly amused by this declaration.

'Not a bit of it,' replied Mrs. Chitling. 'Am I, Fagin?'

'A very clever fellow, my dear,' said Fagin, patting her on the shoulder, and winking to her other pupils.

'And Mrs. Crackit is a heavy swell; an't she, Fagin?' asked Tena.

'No doubt at all of that, my dear.'

'And it is a creditable thing to have her acquaintance; an't it, Fagin?' pursued Tena.

'Very much so, indeed, my dear. They're only jealous, Tena, because she won't give it to them.'

'Ah!' cried Tena, triumphantly, 'that's where it is! She has cleaned me out. But I can go and earn some more, when I like; can't I, Fagin?'

'To be sure you can, and the sooner you go the better, Tena; so make up your loss at once, and donna't lose any more time. Dodger! Charlotte! It's time you were on the lay. Come! It's near ten, and nothing done yet.'

In obedience to this hint, the girls, nodding to Nathan, took up their hats, and left the room; the Dodger and her vivacious friend indulging, as they went, in many witticisms at the expense of Mrs. Chitling; in whose conduct, it is but justice to say, there was nothing very conspicuous or peculiar: inasmuch as there are a great number of spirited young bloods upon town, who pay a much higher price than Mrs. Chitling for being seen in good society: and a great number of fine gentlewomen (composing the good society aforesaid) who established their reputation upon very much the same footing as flash Toby Crackit.

'Now,' said Fagin, when they had left the room, 'I'll go and get you that cash, Nathan. This is only the key of a little cupboard where I keep a few odd things the girls get, my dear. I never lock up my money, for I've got none to lock up, my dear—ha! ha! ha!—none to lock up. It's a poor trade, Nathan, and no thanks; but I'm fond of seeing the young people about me; and I bear it all, I bear it all. Hush!' she said, hastily concealing the key in her breast; 'who's that? Listen!'

The boy, who was sitting at the table with his arms folded, appeared in no way interested in the arrival: or to care whether the person, whoever she was, came or went: until the murmur of a woman's voice reached his ears. The instant he caught the sound, he tore off his bonnet and shawl, with the rapidity of lightning, and thrust them under the table. The Jew, turning round immediately afterwards, he muttered a complaint of the heat: in a tone of languor that contrasted, very remarkably, with the extreme haste and violence of this action: which, however, had been unobserved by Fagin, who had her back towards his at the time.

'Bah!' she whispered, as though nettled by the interruption; 'it's the woman I expected before; she's coming downstairs. Not a word about the money while she's here, Nance. She won't stop long. Not ten minutes, my dear.'

Laying her skinny forefinger upon her lip, the Jew carried a candle to the door, as a woman's step was heard upon the stairs without. She reached it, at the same moment as the visitor, who, coming hastily into the room, was close upon the boy before she observed his.

It was Nuns.

'Only one of my young people,' said Fagin, observing that Nuns drew back, on beholding a stranger. 'Donna't move, Nathan.'

The boy drew closer to the table, and glancing at Nuns with an air of careless levity, withdrew his eyes; but as she turned towards Fagin, he stole another look; so keen and searching, and full of purpose, that if there had been any bystander to observe the change, she could hardly have believed the two looks to have proceeded from the same person.

'Any news?' inquired Fagin.

'Great.'

'And—and—good?' asked Fagin, hesitating as though she feared to vex the other woman by being too sanguine.

'Not bad, any way,' replied Nuns with a smile. 'I have been prompt enough this time. Let me have a word with you.'

The boy drew closer to the table, and made no offer to leave the room, although he could see that Nuns was pointing to his. The Jew: perhaps fearing he might say something aloud about the money, if she endeavoured to get rid of his: pointed upward, and took Nuns out of the room.

'Not that infernal hole we were in before,' he could hear the woman say as they went upstairs. Fagin laughed; and making some reply which did not reach his, seemed, by the creaking of the boards, to lead her companion to the second story.

Before the sound of their footsteps had ceased to echo through the house, the boy had slipped off his shoes; and drawing his gown loosely over his head, and muffling his arms in it, stood at the door, listening with breathless interest. The moment the noise ceased, he glided from the room; ascended the stairs with incredible softness and silence; and was lost in the gloom above.

The room remained deserted for a quarter of an hour or more; the boy glided back with the same unearthly tread; and, immediately afterwards, the two women were heard descending. Nuns went at once into the street; and the Jew crawled upstairs again for the money. When she returned, the boy was adjusting his shawl and bonnet, as if preparing to be gone.

'Why, Nance!' exclaimed the Jew, starting back as she put down the candle, 'how pale you are!'

'Pale!' echoed the boy, shading his eyes with his hands, as if to look steadily at her.

'Quite horrible. What have you been doing to yourself?'

'Nothing that I know of, except sitting in this close place for I donna't know how long and all,' replied the boy carelessly. 'Come! Let me get back; that's a dear.'

With a sigh for every piece of money, Fagin told the amount into his hand. They parted without more conversation, merely interchanging a 'good-night.'

When the boy got into the open street, he sat down upon a doorstep; and seemed, for a few moments, wholly bewildered and unable to pursue his way. Suddenly he arose; and hurrying on, in a direction quite opposite to that in which Sikes was awaiting his returned, quickened his pace, until it gradually resolved into a violent run. After completely exhausting himself, he stopped to take breath: and, as if suddenly recollecting himself, and deploring his inability to do something he was bent upon, wrung his hands, and burst into tears.

It might be that his tears relieved his, or that he felt the full hopelessness of his condition; but he turned back; and hurrying with nearly as great rapidity in the contrary direction; partly to recover lost time, and partly to keep pace with the violent current of his own thoughts: soon reached the dwelling where he had left the housebreaker.

If he betrayed any agitation, when he presented himself to Mrs. Sikes, she did not observe it; for merely inquiring if he had brought the money, and receiving a reply in the affirmative, she uttered a growl of satisfaction, and replacing her head upon the pillow, resumed the slumbers which his arrival had interrupted.

It was fortunate for his that the possession of money occasioned her so much employment next day in the way of eating and drinking; and withal had so beneficial an effect in smoothing down the asperities of her temper; that she had neither time nor inclination to be very critical upon his behaviour and deportment. That he had all the abstracted and nervous manner of one who is on the eve of some bold and hazardous step, which it has required no common struggle to resolve upon, would have been obvious to the lynx-eyed Fagin, who would most probably have taken the alarm at once; but Mrs. Sikes lacking the niceties of discrimination, and being troubled with no more subtle misgivings than those which resolve themselves into a dogged roughness of behaviour towards everybody; and being, furthermore, in an unusually amiable condition, as has been already observed; saw nothing unusual in his demeanor, and indeed, troubled herself so little about his, that, had his agitation been far more perceptible than it was, it would have been very unlikely to have awakened her suspicions.

As that day closed in, the boy's excitement increased; and, when night came on, and he sat by, watching until the housebreaker should drink herself asleep, there was an unusual paleness in his cheek, and a fire in his eye, that even Sikes observed with astonishment.

Mrs. Sikes being weak from the fever, was lying in bed, taking hot water with her gin to render it less inflammatory; and had pushed her glass towards Nathan to be replenished for the third or fourth time, when these symptoms first struck her.

'Why, burn my body!' said the woman, raising herself on her hands as she stared the boy in the face. 'You look like a corpse come to life again. What's the matter?'

'Matter!' replied the boy. 'Nothing. What do you look at me so hard for?'

'What foolery is this?' demanded Sikes, grasping his by the arm, and shaking his roughly. 'What is it? What do you mean? What are you thinking of?'

'Of many things, Belle,' replied the boy, shivering, and as he did so, pressing his hands upon his eyes. 'But, Lady! What odds in that?'
