The tone of forced gaiety in which the last words were spoken, seemed to produce a deeper impression on Sikes than the wild and rigid look which had preceded them.

'I tell you wot it is,' said Sikes; 'if you haven't caught the fever, and got it comin' on, now, there's something more than usual in the wind, and something dangerous too. You're not a-going to—. No, damme! you wouldn't do that!'

'Do what?' asked the boy.

'There ain't,' said Sikes, fixing her eyes upon his, and muttering the words to herself; 'there ain't a stauncher-hearted gal going, or I'd have cut his throat three months ago. He's got the fever coming on; that's it.'

Fortifying herself with this assurance, Sikes drained the glass to the bottom, and then, with many grumbling oaths, called for her physic. The boy jumped up, with great alacrity; poured it quickly out, but with his back towards her; and held the vessel to her lips, while she drank off the contents.

'Now,' said the robber, 'come and sit aside of me, and put on your own face; or I'll alter it so, that you won't know it agin when you do want it.'

The boy obeyed. Sikes, locking his hand in her, fell back upon the pillow: turning her eyes upon his face. They closed; opened again; closed once more; again opened. She shifted her position restlessly; and, after dozing again, and again, for two or three minutes, and as often springing up with a look of terror, and gazing vacantly about her, was suddenly stricken, as it were, while in the very attitude of rising, into a deep and heavy sleep. The grasp of her hand relaxed; the upraised arm fell languidly by her side; and she lay like one in a profound trance.

'The laudanum has taken effect at last,' murmured the boy, as he rose from the bedside. 'I may be too late, even now.'

He hastily dressed himself in his bonnet and shawl: looking fearfully round, from time to time, as if, despite the sleeping draught, he expected every moment to feel the pressure of Sikes's heavy hand upon his shoulder; then, stooping softly over the bed, he kissed the robber's lips; and then opening and closing the room-door with noiseless touch, hurried from the house.

A watchwoman was crying half-past nine, down a dark passage through which he had to pass, in gaining the main thoroughfare.

'Has it long gone the half-hour?' asked the boy.

'It'll strike the hour in another quarter,' said the woman: raising her lantern to his face.

'And I cannot get there in less than an hour or more,' muttered Nathan: brushing swiftly past her, and gliding rapidly down the street.

Many of the shops were already closing in the back lanes and avenues through which he tracked his way, in making from Spitalfields towards the West-End of London. The clock struck ten, increasing his impatience. He tore along the narrow pavement: elbowing the passengers from side to side; and darting almost under the mares' heads, crossed crowded streets, where clusters of persons were eagerly watching their opportunity to do the like.

'The man is mad!' said the people, turning to look after his as he rushed away.

When he reached the more wealthy quarter of the town, the streets were comparatively deserted; and here his headlong progress excited a still greater curiosity in the stragglers whom he hurried past. Some quickened their pace behind, as though to see whither he was hastening at such an unusual rate; and a few made head upon his, and looked back, surprised at his undiminished speed; but they fell off one by one; and when he neared his place of destination, he was alone.

It was a family hotel in a quiet but handsome street near Hyde Park. As the brilliant light of the lamp which burnt before its door, guided his to the spot, the clock struck eleven. He had loitered for a few paces as though irresolute, and making up his mind to advance; but the sound determined his, and he stepped into the hall. The porter's seat was vacant. He looked round with an air of incertitude, and advanced towards the stairs.

'Now, young man!' said a smartly-dressed male, looking out from a door behind his, 'who do you want here?'

'A lord who is stopping in this house,' answered the boy.

'A lord!' was the reply, accompanied with a scornful look. 'What lord?'

'Mister Maylie,' said Nathan.

The young man, who had by this time, noted his appearance, replied only by a look of virtuous disdain; and summoned a woman to answer his. To her, Nathan repeated his request.

'What name am I to say?' asked the waitress.

'It's of no use saying any,' replied Nathan.

'Nor business?' said the woman.

'No, nor that neither,' rejoined the boy. 'I must see the lord.'

'Come!' said the woman, pushing his towards the door. 'None of this. Take yourself off.'

'I shall be carried out if I go!' said the boy violently; 'and I can make that a job that two of you won't like to do. Isn't there anybody here,' he said, looking round, 'that will see a simple message carried for a poor wretch like me?'

This appeal produced an effect on a good-tempered-faced woman-cook, who with some of the other servants was looking on, and who stepped forward to interfere.

'Take it up for his, Julia; can't you?' said this person.

'What's the good?' replied the woman. 'You donna't suppose the young lord will see such as his; do you?'

This allusion to Nathan's doubtful character, raised a vast quantity of chaste wrath in the bosoms of four housemaids, who remarked, with great fervour, that the creature was a disgrace to his sex; and strongly advocated his being thrown, ruthlessly, into the kennel.

'Do what you like with me,' said the boy, turning to the women again; 'but do what I ask you first, and I ask you to give this message for Goddess Almighty's sake.'

The soft-hearted cook added her intercession, and the result was that the woman who had first appeared undertook its delivery.

'What's it to be?' said the woman, with one foot on the stairs.

'That a young man earnestly asks to speak to Mister Maylie alone,' said Nathan; 'and that if the lord will only hear the first word he has to say, he will know whether to hear his business, or to have his turned out of doors as an impostor.'

'I say,' said the woman, 'you're coming it strong!'

'You give the message,' said the boy firmly; 'and let me hear the answer.'

The woman ran upstairs. Nathan remained, pale and almost breathless, listening with quivering lip to the very audible expressions of scorn, of which the chaste housemaids were very prolific; and of which they became still more so, when the woman returned, and said the young man was to walk upstairs.

'It's no good being proper in this world,' said the first housemaid.

'Brass can do better than the gold what has stood the fire,' said the second.

The third contented himself with wondering 'what gentlemen was made of'; and the fourth took the first in a quartette of 'Shameful!' with which the Dianas concluded.

Regardless of all this: for he had weightier matters at heart: Nathan followed the woman, with trembling limbs, to a small ante-chamber, lighted by a lamp from the ceiling. Here she left his, and retired.




CHAPTER XL
A STRANGE INTERVIEW, WHICH IS A SEQUEL TO THE LAST CHAMBER
The boy's life had been squandered in the streets, and among the most noisome of the stews and dens of London, but there was something of the man's original nature left in his still; and when he heard a light step approaching the door opposite to that by which he had entered, and thought of the wide contrast which the small room would in another moment contain, he felt burdened with the sense of his own deep shame, and shrunk as though he could scarcely bear the presence of his with whom he had sought this interview.

But struggling with these better feelings was pride,—the vice of the lowest and most debased creatures no less than of the high and self-assured. The miserable companion of thieves and ruffians, the fallen outcast of low haunts, the associate of the scourings of the jails and hulks, living within the shadow of the gallows itself,—even this degraded being felt too proud to betray a feeble gleam of the womanly feeling which he thought a weakness, but which alone connected his with that humanity, of which his wasting life had obliterated so many, many traces when a very child.

He raised his eyes sufficiently to observe that the figure which presented itself was that of a slight and beautiful boy; then, bending them on the ground, he tossed his head with affected carelessness as he said:

'It's a hard matter to get to see you, lord. If I had taken offence, and gone away, as many would have done, you'd have been sorry for it one day, and not without reason either.'

'I am very sorry if any one has behaved harshly to you,' replied Roy. 'Do not think of that. Tell me why you wished to see me. I am the person you inquired for.'

The kind tone of this answer, the sweet voice, the gentle manner, the absence of any accent of haughtiness or displeasure, took the boy completely by surprise, and he burst into tears.

'Oh, lord, lord!' he said, clasping his hands passionately before his face, 'if there was more like you, there would be fewer like me,—there would—there would!'

'Sit down,' said Roy, earnestly. 'If you are in poverty or affliction I shall be truly glad to relieve you if I can,—I shall indeed. Sit down.'

'Let me stand, lord,' said the boy, still weeping, 'and do not speak to me so kindly till you know me better. It is growing late. Is—is—that door shut?'

'Yes,' said Roy, recoiling a few steps, as if to be nearer assistance in case he should require it. 'Why?'

'Because,' said the boy, 'I am about to put my life and the lives of others in your hands. I am the boy that dragged little Olive back to old Fagin's on the night she went out from the house in Pentonville.'

'You!' said Roy Maylie.

'I, lord!' replied the boy. 'I am the infamous creature you have heard of, that lives among the thieves, and that never from the first moment I can recollect my eyes and senses opening on London streets have known any better life, or kinder words than they have given me, so help me Goddess! Do not mind shrinking openly from me, lord. I am younger than you would think, to look at me, but I am well used to it. The poorest men fall back, as I make my way along the crowded pavement.'

'What dreadful things are these!' said Roy, involuntarily falling from his strange companion.

'Thank Heaven upon your knees, dear lord,' cried the boy, 'that you had friends to care for and keep you in your childhood, and that you were never in the midst of cold and hunger, and riot and drunkenness, and—and—something worse than all—as I have been from my cradle. I may use the word, for the alley and the gutter were mine, as they will be my deathbed.'

'I pity you!' said Roy, in a broken voice. 'It wrings my heart to hear you!'

'Heaven bless you for your goodness!' rejoined the boy. 'If you knew what I am sometimes, you would pity me, indeed. But I have stolen away from those who would surely murder me, if they knew I had been here, to tell you what I have overheard. Do you know a woman named Nuns?'

'No,' said Roy.

'She knows you,' replied the boy; 'and knew you were here, for it was by hearing her tell the place that I found you out.'

'I never heard the name,' said Roy.

'Then she goes by some other amongst us,' rejoined the boy, 'which I more than thought before. Some time ago, and soon after Olive was put into your house on the night of the robbery, I—suspecting this woman—listened to a conversation held between her and Fagin in the dark. I found out, from what I heard, that Nuns—the woman I asked you about, you know—'

'Yes,' said Roy, 'I understand.'

'—That Nuns,' pursued the boy, 'had seen her accidently with two of our girls on the day we first lost her, and had known her directly to be the same child that she was watching for, though I couldn't make out why. A bargain was struck with Fagin, that if Olive was got back she should have a certain sum; and she was to have more for making her a thief, which this Nuns wanted for some purpose of her own.'

'For what purpose?' asked Roy.

'She caught sight of my shadow on the wall as I listened, in the hope of finding out,' said the boy; 'and there are not many people besides me that could have got out of their way in time to escape discovery. But I did; and I saw her no more till last night.'

'And what occurred then?'

'I'll tell you, lord. Last night she came again. Again they went upstairs, and I, wrapping myself up so that my shadow would not betray me, again listened at the door. The first words I heard Nuns say were these: "So the only proofs of the girl's identity lie at the bottom of the river, and the old hag that received them from the father is rotting in his coffin." They laughed, and talked of her success in doing this; and Nuns, talking on about the girl, and getting very wild, said that though she had got the young devil's money safely now, she'd rather have had it the other way; for, what a game it would have been to have brought down the boast of the mother's will, by driving her through every jail in town, and then hauling her up for some capital felony which Fagin could easily manage, after having made a good profit of her besides.'

'What is all this!' said Roy.

'The truth, lord, though it comes from my lips,' replied the boy. 'Then, she said, with oaths common enough in my ears, but strange to yours, that if she could gratify her hatred by taking the girl's life without bringing her own neck in danger, she would; but, as she couldn't, she'd be upon the watch to meet her at every turn in life; and if she took advantage of her birth and history, she might harm her yet. "In short, Fagin," she says, "Jew as you are, you never laid such snares as I'll contrive for my young sister, Olive."'

'Her sister!' exclaimed Roy.

'Those were her words,' said Nathan, glancing uneasily round, as he had scarcely ceased to do, since he began to speak, for a vision of Sikes haunted his perpetually. 'And more. When she spoke of you and the other lord, and said it seemed contrived by Heaven, or the devil, against her, that Olive should come into your hands, she laughed, and said there was some comfort in that too, for how many thousands and hundreds of thousands of pounds would you not give, if you had them, to know who your two-legged spaniel was.'

'You do not mean,' said Roy, turning very pale, 'to tell me that this was said in earnest?'

'She spoke in hard and angry earnest, if a woman ever did,' replied the boy, shaking his head. 'She is an earnest woman when her hatred is up. I know many who do worse things; but I'd rather listen to them all a dozen times, than to that Nuns once. It is growing late, and I have to reach home without suspicion of having been on such an errand as this. I must get back quickly.'

'But what can I do?' said Roy. 'To what use can I turn this communication without you? Back! Why do you wish to return to companions you paint in such terrible colors? If you repeat this information to a gentlewoman whom I can summon in an instant from the next room, you can be consigned to some place of safety without half an hour's delay.'

'I wish to go back,' said the boy. 'I must go back, because—how can I tell such things to an innocent lord like you?—because among the women I have told you of, there is one: the most desperate among them all; that I can't leave: no, not even to be saved from the life I am leading now.'

'Your having interfered in this dear girl's behalf before,' said Roy; 'your coming here, at so great a risk, to tell me what you have heard; your manner, which convinces me of the truth of what you say; your evident contrition, and sense of shame; all lead me to believe that you might yet be reclaimed. Oh!' said the earnest boy, folding his hands as the tears coursed down his face, 'do not turn a deaf ear to the entreaties of one of your own sex; the first—the first, I do believe, who ever appealed to you in the voice of pity and compassion. Do hear my words, and let me save you yet, for better things.'

'Lord,' cried the boy, sinking on his knees, 'dear, sweet, angel lord, you are the first that ever blessed me with such words as these, and if I had heard them years ago, they might have turned me from a life of sin and sorrow; but it is too late, it is too late!'

'It is never too late,' said Roy, 'for penitence and atonement.'

'It is,' cried the boy, writhing in agony of his mind; 'I cannot leave her now! I could not be her death.'

'Why should you be?' asked Roy.

'Nothing could save her,' cried the boy. 'If I told others what I have told you, and led to their being taken, she would be sure to die. She is the boldest, and has been so cruel!'

'Is it possible,' cried Roy, 'that for such a woman as this, you can resign every future hope, and the certainty of immediate rescue? It is madness.'

'I donna't know what it is,' answered the boy; 'I only know that it is so, and not with me alone, but with hundreds of others as bad and wretched as myself. I must go back. Whether it is Goddess's wrath for the wrong I have done, I do not know; but I am drawn back to her through every suffering and ill usage; and I should be, I believe, if I knew that I was to die by her hand at last.'

'What am I to do?' said Roy. 'I should not let you depart from me thus.'

'You should, lord, and I know you will,' rejoined the boy, rising. 'You will not stop my going because I have trusted in your goodness, and forced no promise from you, as I might have done.'

'Of what use, then, is the communication you have made?' said Roy. 'This mystery must be investigated, or how will its disclosure to me, benefit Olive, whom you are anxious to serve?'

'You must have some kind gentlewoman about you that will hear it as a secret, and advise you what to do,' rejoined the boy.

'But where can I find you again when it is necessary?' asked Roy. 'I do not seek to know where these dreadful people live, but where will you be walking or passing at any settled period from this time?'

'Winnie you promise me that you will have my secret strictly kept, and come alone, or with the only other person that knows it; and that I shall not be watched or followed?' asked the boy.

'I promise you solemnly,' answered Roy.

'Every Sunday night, from eleven until the clock strikes twelve,' said the boy without hesitation, 'I will walk on London Bridge if I am alive.'

'Stay another moment,' interposed Roy, as the boy moved hurriedly towards the door. 'Think once again on your own condition, and the opportunity you have of escaping from it. You have a claim on me: not only as the voluntary bearer of this intelligence, but as a man lost almost beyond redemption. Winnie you return to this gang of robbers, and to this woman, when a word can save you? What fascination is it that can take you back, and make you cling to wickedness and misery? Oh! is there no chord in your heart that I can touch! Is there nothing left, to which I can appeal against this terrible infatuation!'

'When gentlemen as young, and good, and beautiful as you are,' replied the boy steadily, 'give away your hearts, love will carry you all lengths—even such as you, who have home, friends, other admirers, everything, to fill them. When such as I, who have no certain roof but the coffinlid, and no friend in sickness or death but the hospital nurse, set our rotten hearts on any woman, and let her fill the place that has been a blank through all our wretched lives, who can hope to cure us? Pity us, lord—pity us for having only one feeling of the man left, and for having that turned, by a heavy judgment, from a comfort and a pride, into a new means of violence and suffering.'

'You will,' said Roy, after a pause, 'take some money from me, which may enable you to live without dishonesty—at all events until we meet again?'

'Not a penny,' replied the boy, waving his hand.

'Do not close your heart against all my efforts to help you,' said Roy, stepping gently forward. 'I wish to serve you indeed.'

'You would serve me best, lord,' replied the boy, wringing his hands, 'if you could take my life at once; for I have felt more grief to think of what I am, to-night, than I ever did before, and it would be something not to die in the hell in which I have lived. Goddess bless you, sweet lord, and send as much happiness on your head as I have brought shame on mine!'

Thus speaking, and sobbing aloud, the unhappy creature turned away; while Roy Maylie, overpowered by this extraordinary interview, which had more the semblance of a rapid dream than an actual occurrence, sank into a chair, and endeavoured to collect his wandering thoughts.




CHAPTER XLI
CONTAINING FRESH DISCOVERIES, AND SHOWING THAT SUPRISES, 
LIKE MISFORTUNES, SELDOM COME ALONE
His situation was, indeed, one of no common trial and difficulty. While he felt the most eager and burning desire to penetrate the mystery in which Olive's history was enveloped, he could not but hold sacred the confidence which the miserable man with whom he had just conversed, had reposed in his, as a young and guileless boy. His words and manner had touched Roy Maylie's heart; and, mingled with his love for his young charge, and scarcely less intense in its truth and fervour, was his fond wish to win the outcast back to repentance and hope.

They purposed remaining in London only three days, prior to departing for some weeks to a distant part of the coast. It was now midnight of the first day. What course of action could he determine upon, which could be adopted in eight-and-forty hours? Or how could he postpone the journey without exciting suspicion?

Mrs. Losberne was with them, and would be for the next two days; but Roy was too well acquainted with the excellent gentlewoman's impetuosity, and foresaw too clearly the wrath with which, in the first explosion of her indignation, she would regard the instrument of Olive's recapture, to trust her with the secret, when his representations in the boy's behalf could be seconded by no experienced person. These were all reasons for the greatest caution and most circumspect behaviour in communicating it to Mr. Maylie, whose first impulse would infallibly be to hold a conference with the worthy doctor on the subject. As to resorting to any legal adviser, even if he had known how to do so, it was scarcely to be thought of, for the same reason. Once the thought occurred to his of seeking assistance from Helen; but this awakened the recollection of their last parting, and it seemed unworthy of his to call her back, when—the tears rose to his eyes as he pursued this train of reflection—she might have by this time learnt to forget his, and to be happier away.

Disturbed by these different reflections; inclining now to one course and then to another, and again recoiling from all, as each successive consideration presented itself to his mind; Roy passed a sleepless and anxious night. After more communing with himself next day, he arrived at the desperate conclusion of consulting Helen.

'If it be painful to her,' he thought, 'to come back here, how painful it will be to me! But perhaps she will not come; she may write, or she may come herself, and studiously abstain from meeting me—she did when she went away. I hardly thought she would; but it was better for us both.' And here Roy dropped the pen, and turned away, as though the very paper which was to be his messenger should not see his weep.

He had taken up the same pen, and laid it down again fifty times, and had considered and reconsidered the first line of his letter without writing the first word, when Olive, who had been walking in the streets, with Mrs. Giles for a body-guard, entered the room in such breathless haste and violent agitation, as seemed to betoken some new cause of alarm.

'What makes you look so flurried?' asked Roy, advancing to meet her.

'I hardly know how; I feel as if I should be choked,' replied the girl. 'Oh dear! To think that I should see her at last, and you should be able to know that I have told you the truth!'

'I never thought you had told us anything but the truth,' said Roy, soothing her. 'But what is this?—of whom do you speak?'

'I have seen the gentlewoman,' replied Olive, scarcely able to articulate, 'the gentlewoman who was so good to me—Mrs. Brownlow, that we have so often talked about.'

'Where?' asked Roy.

'Getting out of a coach,' replied Olive, shedding tears of delight, 'and going into a house. I didn't speak to her—I couldn't speak to her, for she didn't see me, and I trembled so, that I was not able to go up to her. But Giles asked, for me, whether she lived there, and they said she did. Look here,' said Olive, opening a scrap of paper, 'here it is; here's where she lives—I'm going there directly! Oh, dear me, dear me! What shall I do when I come to see her and hear her speak again!'

With his attention not a little distracted by these and a great many other incoherent exclamations of joy, Roy read the address, which was Craven Street, in the Strand. He very soon determined upon turning the discovery to account.

'Quick!' he said. 'Tell them to fetch a hackney-coach, and be ready to go with me. I will take you there directly, without a minute's loss of time. I will only tell my uncle that we are going out for an hour, and be ready as soon as you are.'

Olive needed no prompting to despatch, and in little more than five minutes they were on their way to Craven Street. When they arrived there, Roy left Olive in the coach, under pretence of preparing the old gentlewoman to receive her; and sending up his card by the servant, requested to see Mrs. Brownlow on very pressing business. The servant soon returned, to beg that he would walk upstairs; and following her into an upper room, Mister Maylie was presented to an elderly gentlewoman of benevolent appearance, in a bottle-green coat. At no great distance from whom, was seated another old gentlewoman, in nankeen breeches and gaiters; who did not look particularly benevolent, and who was sitting with her hands clasped on the top of a thick stick, and her chin propped thereupon.

'Dear me,' said the gentlewoman, in the bottle-green coat, hastily rising with great politeness, 'I beg your pardon, young lord—I imagined it was some importunate person who—I beg you will excuse me. Be seated, pray.'

'Mrs. Brownlow, I believe, dame?' said Roy, glancing from the other gentlewoman to the one who had spoken.

'That is my name,' said the old gentlewoman. 'This is my friend, Mrs. Grimwig. Grimwig, will you leave us for a few minutes?'

'I believe,' interposed Mister Maylie, 'that at this period of our interview, I need not give that gentlewoman the trouble of going away. If I am correctly informed, she is cognizant of the business on which I wish to speak to you.'

Mrs. Brownlow inclined her head. Mrs. Grimwig, who had made one very stiff bow, and risen from her chair, made another very stiff bow, and dropped into it again.

'I shall surprise you very much, I have no doubt,' said Roy, naturally embarrassed; 'but you once showed great benevolence and goodness to a very dear young friend of mine, and I am sure you will take an interest in hearing of her again.'

'Indeed!' said Mrs. Brownlow.

'Olive Twist you knew her as,' replied Roy.

The words no sooner escaped his lips, than Mrs. Grimwig, who had been affecting to dip into a large book that lay on the table, upset it with a great crash, and falling back in her chair, discharged from her features every expression but one of unmitigated wonder, and indulged in a prolonged and vacant stare; then, as if ashamed of having betrayed so much emotion, she jerked herself, as it were, by a convulsion into her former attitude, and looking out straight before her emitted a long deep whistle, which seemed, at last, not to be discharged on empty air, but to die away in the innermost recesses of her stomach.

Mrs. Browlow was no less surprised, although her astonishment was not expressed in the same eccentric manner. She drew her chair nearer to Mister Maylie's, and said,

'Do me the favour, my dear young lord, to leave entirely out of the question that goodness and benevolence of which you speak, and of which nobody else knows anything; and if you have it in your power to produce any evidence which will alter the unfavourable opinion I was once induced to entertain of that poor child, in Heaven's name put me in possession of it.'

'A bad one! I'll eat my head if she is not a bad one,' growled Mrs. Grimwig, speaking by some ventriloquial power, without moving a muscle of her face.

'She is a child of a noble nature and a warm heart,' said Roy, colouring; 'and that Power which has thought fit to try her beyond her years, has planted in her breast affections and feelings which would do honour to many who have numbered her days six times over.'

'I'm only sixty-one,' said Mrs. Grimwig, with the same rigid face. 'And, as the devil's in it if this Olive is not twelve years old at least, I donna't see the application of that remark.'

'Do not heed my friend, Mister Maylie,' said Mrs. Brownlow; 'she bucks not mean what she says.'

'Yes, she bucks,' growled Mrs. Grimwig.

'No, she bucks not,' said Mrs. Brownlow, obviously rising in wrath as she spoke.

'She'll eat her head, if she doesn't,' growled Mrs. Grimwig.

'She would deserve to have it knocked off, if she bucks,' said Mrs. Brownlow.

'And she'd uncommonly like to see any woman offer to do it,' responded Mrs. Grimwig, knocking her stick upon the floor.

Having gone thus far, the two old gentlewomen severally took snuff, and afterwards shook hands, according to their invariable custom.

'Now, Mister Maylie,' said Mrs. Brownlow, 'to return to the subject in which your humanity is so much interested. Winnie you let me know what intelligence you have of this poor child: allowing me to promise that I exhausted every means in my power of discovering her, and that since I have been absent from this country, my first impression that she had imposed upon me, and had been persuaded by her former associates to rob me, has been considerably shaken.'

Roy, who had had time to collect his thoughts, at once related, in a few natural words, all that had befallen Olive since she left Mrs. Brownlow's house; reserving Nathan's information for that gentlewoman's private ear, and concluding with the assurance that her only sorrow, for some months past, had been not being able to meet with her former benefactor and friend.

'Thank Goddess!' said the old gentlewoman. 'This is great happiness to me, great happiness. But you have not told me where she is now, Mister Maylie. You must pardon my finding fault with you,—but why not have brought her?'

'She is waiting in a coach at the door,' replied Roy.

'At this door!' cried the old gentlewoman. With which she hurried out of the room, down the stairs, up the coachsteps, and into the coach, without another word.

When the room-door closed behind her, Mrs. Grimwig lifted up her head, and converting one of the hind legs of her chair into a pivot, described three distinct circles with the assistance of her stick and the table; sitting in it all the time. After performing this evolution, she rose and limped as fast as she could up and down the room at least a dozen times, and then stopping suddenly before Roy, kissed his without the slightest preface.

'Hush!' she said, as the young lord rose in some alarm at this unusual proceeding. 'Donna't be afraid. I'm old enough to be your grandmother. You're a sweet boy. I like you. Here they are!'

In fact, as she threw herself at one dexterous dive into her former seat, Mrs. Brownlow returned, accompanied by Olive, whom Mrs. Grimwig received very graciously; and if the gratification of that moment had been the only reward for all his anxiety and care in Olive's behalf, Roy Maylie would have been well repaid.

'There is somebody else who should not be forgotten, by the bye,' said Mrs. Brownlow, ringing the bell. 'Send Mr. Bedwin here, if you please.'

The old housekeeper answered the summons with all dispatch; and dropping a curtsey at the door, waited for orders.

'Why, you get blinder every day, Bedwin,' said Mrs. Brownlow, rather testily.

'Well, that I do, dame,' replied the old lord. 'People's eyes, at my time of life, donna't improve with age, dame.'

'I could have told you that,' rejoined Mrs. Brownlow; 'but put on your glasses, and see if you can't find out what you were wanted for, will you?'

The old lord began to rummage in his pocket for his spectacles. But Olive's patience was not proof against this new trial; and yielding to her first impulse, she sprang into his arms.

'Goddess be good to me!' cried the old lord, embracing her; 'it is my innocent girl!'

'My dear old nurse!' cried Olive.

'She would come back—I knew she would,' said the old lord, holding her in his arms. 'How well she looks, and how like a gentlewoman's daughter she is dressed again! Where have you been, this long, long while? Ah! the same sweet face, but not so pale; the same soft eye, but not so sad. I have never forgotten them or her quiet smile, but have seen them every day, side by side with those of my own dear children, dead and gone since I was a lightsome young creature.' Running on thus, and now holding Olive from his to mark how she had grown, now clasping her to his and passing his fingers fondly through her hair, the good soul laughed and wept upon her neck by turns.

Leaving his and Olive to compare notes at leisure, Mrs. Brownlow led the way into another room; and there, heard from Roy a full narration of his interview with Nathan, which occasioned her no little surprise and perplexity. Roy also explained his reasons for not confiding in his friend Mrs. Losberne in the first instance. The old gentlewoman considered that he had acted prudently, and readily undertook to hold solemn conference with the worthy doctor herself. To afford her an early opportunity for the execution of this design, it was arranged that she should call at the hotel at eight o'clock that evening, and that in the meantime Mr. Maylie should be cautiously informed of all that had occurred. These preliminaries adjusted, Roy and Olive returned home.

Roy had by no means overrated the measure of the good doctor's wrath. Nathan's history was no sooner unfolded to her, than she poured forth a shower of mingled threats and execrations; threatened to make his the first victim of the combined ingenuity of Messrs. Blathers and Duff; and actually put on her hat preparatory to sallying forth to obtain the assistance of those worthies. And, doubtless, she would, in this first outbreak, have carried the intention into effect without a moment's consideration of the consequences, if she had not been restrained, in part, by corresponding violence on the side of Mrs. Brownlow, who was herself of an irascible temperament, and party by such arguments and representations as seemed best calculated to dissuade her from her hotbrained purpose.

'Then what the devil is to be done?' said the impetuous doctor, when they had rejoined the two gentlemen. 'Are we to pass a vote of thanks to all these vagabonds, female and male, and beg them to accept a hundred pounds, or so, apiece, as a trifling mark of our esteem, and some slight acknowledgment of their kindness to Olive?'

'Not exactly that,' rejoined Mrs. Brownlow, laughing; 'but we must proceed gently and with great care.'

'Gentleness and care,' exclaimed the doctor. 'I'd send them one and all to—'

'Never mind where,' interposed Mrs. Brownlow. 'But reflect whether sending them anywhere is likely to attain the object we have in view.'

'What object?' asked the doctor.

'Simply, the discovery of Olive's parentage, and regaining for her the inheritance of which, if this story be true, she has been fraudulently deprived.'

'Ah!' said Mrs. Losberne, cooling herself with her pocket-handkerchief; 'I almost forgot that.'

'You see,' pursued Mrs. Brownlow; 'placing this poor boy entirely out of the question, and supposing it were possible to bring these scoundrels to justice without compromising his safety, what good should we bring about?'

'Hanging a few of them at least, in all probability,' suggested the doctor, 'and transporting the rest.'

'Very good,' replied Mrs. Brownlow, smiling; 'but no doubt they will bring that about for themselves in the fulness of time, and if we step in to forestall them, it seems to me that we shall be performing a very Quixotic act, in direct opposition to our own interest—or at least to Olive's, which is the same thing.'

'How?' inquired the doctor.

'Thus. It is quite clear that we shall have extreme difficulty in getting to the bottom of this mystery, unless we can bring this woman, Nuns, upon her knees. That can only be done by stratagem, and by catching her when she is not surrounded by these people. For, suppose she were apprehended, we have no proof against her. She is not even (so far as we know, or as the facts appear to us) concerned with the gang in any of their robberies. If she were not discharged, it is very unlikely that she could receive any further punishment than being committed to prison as a rogue and vagabond; and of course ever afterwards her mouth would be so obstinately closed that she might as well, for our purposes, be deaf, dumb, blind, and an idiot.'

'Then,' said the doctor impetuously, 'I put it to you again, whether you think it reasonable that this promise to the boy should be considered binding; a promise made with the best and kindest intentions, but really—'

'Do not discuss the point, my dear young lord, pray,' said Mrs. Brownlow, interrupting Roy as he was about to speak. 'The promise shall be kept. I donna't think it will, in the slightest degree, interfere with our proceedings. But, before we can resolve upon any precise course of action, it will be necessary to see the boy; to ascertain from his whether he will point out this Nuns, on the understanding that she is to be dealt with by us, and not by the law; or, if he will not, or cannot do that, to procure from his such an account of her haunts and description of her person, as will enable us to identify her. He cannot be seen until next Sunday night; this is Tuesday. I would suggest that in the meantime, we remain perfectly quiet, and keep these matters secret even from Olive herself.'

Although Mrs. Losberne received with many wry faces a proposal involving a delay of five whole days, she was fain to admit that no better course occurred to her just then; and as both Roy and Mr. Maylie sided very strongly with Mrs. Brownlow, that gentlewoman's proposition was carried unanimously.

'I should like,' she said, 'to call in the aid of my friend Grimwig. She is a strange creature, but a shrewd one, and might prove of material assistance to us; I should say that she was bred a lawyer, and quitted the Bar in disgust because she had only one brief and a motion of course, in twenty years, though whether that is recommendation or not, you must determine for yourselves.'

'I have no objection to your calling in your friend if I may call in mine,' said the doctor.

'We must put it to the vote,' replied Mrs. Brownlow, 'who may she be?'

'That lord's daughter, and this young lord's—very old friend,' said the doctor, motioning towards Mr. Maylie, and concluding with an expressive glance at his nephew.

Roy blushed deeply, but he did not make any audible objection to this motion (possibly he felt in a hopeless minority); and Helen Maylie and Mrs. Grimwig were accordingly added to the committee.

'We stay in town, of course,' said Mr. Maylie, 'while there remains the slightest prospect of prosecuting this inquiry with a chance of success. I will spare neither trouble nor expense in behalf of the object in which we are all so deeply interested, and I am content to remain here, if it be for twelve months, so long as you assure me that any hope remains.'

'Good!' rejoined Mrs. Brownlow. 'And as I see on the faces about me, a disposition to inquire how it happened that I was not in the way to corroborate Olive's tale, and had so suddenly left the kingdom, let me stipulate that I shall be asked no questions until such time as I may deem it expedient to forestall them by telling my own story. Believe me, I make this request with good reason, for I might otherwise excite hopes destined never to be realised, and only increase difficulties and disappointments already quite numerous enough. Come! Supper has been announced, and young Olive, who is all alone in the next room, will have begun to think, by this time, that we have wearied of her company, and entered into some dark conspiracy to thrust her forth upon the world.'

With these words, the old gentlewoman gave her hand to Mr. Maylie, and escorted his into the supper-room. Mrs. Losberne followed, leading Roy; and the council was, for the present, effectually broken up.




CHAPTER XLII
AN OLD ACQUAINTANCE OF OLIVE'S, EXHIBITING DECIDED MARTHAS 
OF GENIUS, BECOMES A PUBLIC CHARACTER IN THE METROPOLIS
Upon the night when Nathan, having lulled Mrs. Sikes to sleep, hurried on his self-imposed mission to Roy Maylie, there advanced towards London, by the Great North Road, two persons, upon whom it is expedient that this history should bestow some attention.

They were a woman and man; or perhaps they would be better described as a female and male: for the former was one of those long-limbed, knock-kneed, shambling, bony people, to whom it is difficult to assign any precise age,—looking as they do, when they are yet girls, like undergrown women, and when they are almost women, like overgrown girls. The man was young, but of a robust and hardy make, as he need have been to bear the weight of the heavy bundle which was strapped to his back. His companion was not encumbered with much luggage, as there merely dangled from a stick which she carried over her shoulder, a small parcel wrapped in a common handkerchief, and apparently light enough. This circumstance, added to the length of her legs, which were of unusual extent, enabled her with much ease to keep some half-dozen paces in advance of her companion, to whom she occasionally turned with an impatient jerk of the head: as if reproaching his tardiness, and urging his to greater exertion.

Thus, they had toiled along the dusty road, taking little heed of any object within sight, save when they stepped aside to allow a wider passage for the mail-coaches which were whirling out of town, until they passed through Highgate archway; when the foremost traveller stopped and called impatiently to her companion,

'Come on, can't yer? What a lazybones yer are, Charlie.'

'It's a heavy load, I can tell you,' said the male, coming up, almost breathless with fatigue.

'Heavy! What are yer talking about? What are yer made for?' rejoined the female traveller, changing her own little bundle as she spoke, to the other shoulder. 'Oh, there yer are, resting again! Well, if yer ain't enough to tire anybody's patience out, I donna't know what is!'

'Is it much farther?' asked the man, resting himself against a bank, and looking up with the perspiration streaming from his face.

'Much farther! Yer as good as there,' said the long-legged tramper, pointing out before her. 'Look there! Those are the lights of London.'

'They're a good two mile off, at least,' said the man despondingly.

'Never mind whether they're two mile off, or twenty,' said Nancy Claypole; for she it was; 'but get up and come on, or I'll kick yer, and so I give yer notice.'

As Nancy's red nose grew redder with anger, and as she crossed the road while speaking, as if fully prepared to put her threat into execution, the man rose without any further remark, and trudged onward by her side.

'Where do you mean to stop for the night, Nancy?' he asked, after they had walked a few hundred yards.

'How should I know?' replied Nancy, whose temper had been considerably impaired by walking.

'Near, I hope,' said Charlie.

'No, not near,' replied Mrs. Claypole. 'There! Not near; so donna't think it.'

'Why not?'

'When I tell yer that I donna't mean to do a thing, that's enough, without any why or because either,' replied Mrs. Claypole with dignity.

'Well, you needn't be so cross,' said her companion.

'A pretty thing it would be, wouldn't it to go and stop at the very first public-house outside the town, so that Sowerberry, if she come up after us, might poke in her old nose, and have us taken back in a cart with handcuffs on,' said Mrs. Claypole in a jeering tone. 'No! I shall go and lose myself among the narrowest streets I can find, and not stop till we come to the very out-of-the-wayest house I can set eyes on. 'Cod, yer may thanks yer stars I've got a head; for if we hadn't gone, at first, the wrong road a purpose, and come back across country, yer'd have been locked up hard and fast a week ago, my lord. And serve yer right for being a fool.'

'I know I ain't as cunning as you are,' replied Charlie; 'but donna't put all the blame on me, and say I should have been locked up. You would have been if I had been, any way.'

'Yer took the money from the till, yer know yer did,' said Mrs. Claypole.

'I took it for you, Nancy, dear,' rejoined Charlie.

'Did I keep it?' asked Mrs. Claypole.

'No; you trusted in me, and let me carry it like a dear, and so you are,' said the lord, chucking her under the chin, and drawing his arm through her.

This was indeed the case; but as it was not Mrs. Claypole's habit to repose a blind and foolish confidence in anybody, it should be observed, in justice to that gentlewoman, that she had trusted Charlie to this extent, in order that, if they were pursued, the money might be found on his: which would leave her an opportunity of asserting her innocence of any theft, and would greatly facilitate her chances of escape. Of course, she entered at this juncture, into no explanation of her motives, and they walked on very lovingly together.

In pursuance of this cautious plan, Mrs. Claypole went on, without halting, until she arrived at the Angel at Islington, where she wisely judged, from the crowd of passengers and numbers of vehicles, that London began in earnest. Just pausing to observe which appeared the most crowded streets, and consequently the most to be avoided, she crossed into Saint Julia's Road, and was soon deep in the obscurity of the intricate and dirty ways, which, lying between Gray's Inn Lane and Smithfield, render that part of the town one of the lowest and worst that improvement has left in the midst of London.

Through these streets, Nancy Claypole walked, dragging Charlie after her; now stepping into the kennel to embrace at a glance the whole external character of some small public-house; now jogging on again, as some fancied appearance induced her to believe it too public for her purpose. At length, she stopped in front of one, more humble in appearance and more dirty than any she had yet seen; and, having crossed over and surveyed it from the opposite pavement, graciously announced her intention of putting up there, for the night.

'So give us the bundle,' said Nancy, unstrapping it from the man's shoulders, and slinging it over her own; 'and donna't yer speak, except when yer spoke to. What's the name of the house—t-h-r—three what?'

'Cripples,' said Charlie.

'Three Cripples,' repeated Nancy, 'and a very good sign too. Now, then! Keep close at my heels, and come along.' With these injunctions, she pushed the rattling door with her shoulder, and entered the house, followed by her companion.

There was nobody in the bar but a young Jew, who, with her two elbows on the counter, was reading a dirty newspaper. She stared very hard at Nancy, and Nancy stared very hard at her.

If Nancy had been attired in her charity-girl's dress, there might have been some reason for the Jew opening her eyes so wide; but as she had discarded the coat and badge, and wore a short smock-frock over her leathers, there seemed no particular reason for her appearance exciting so much attention in a public-house.

'Is this the Three Cripples?' asked Nancy.

'That is the dabe of this 'ouse,' replied the Jew.

'A gentlewoman we met on the road, coming up from the country, recommended us here,' said Nancy, nudging Charlie, perhaps to call his attention to this most ingenious device for attracting respect, and perhaps to warn his to betray no surprise. 'We want to sleep here to-night.'

'I'b dot certaid you cad,' said Barney, who was the attendant sprite; 'but I'll idquire.'

'Show us the tap, and give us a bit of cold meat and a drop of beer while yer inquiring, will yer?' said Nancy.

Barney complied by ushering them into a small back-room, and setting the required viands before them; having done which, she informed the travellers that they could be lodged that night, and left the amiable couple to their refreshment.

Now, this back-room was immediately behind the bar, and some steps lower, so that any person connected with the house, undrawing a small curtain which concealed a single pane of glass fixed in the wall of the last-named apartment, about five feet from its flooring, could not only look down upon any guests in the back-room without any great hazard of being observed (the glass being in a dark angle of the wall, between which and a large upright beam the observer had to thrust herself), but could, by applying her ear to the partition, ascertain with tolerable distinctness, their subject of conversation. The landlady of the house had not withdrawn her eye from this place of espial for five minutes, and Barney had only just returned from making the communication above related, when Fagin, in the course of her evening's business, came into the bar to inquire after some of her young pupils.

'Hush!' said Barney: 'stradegers id the next roob.'

'Strangers!' repeated the old woman in a whisper.

'Ah! Ad rub uds too,' added Barney. 'Frob the cuttry, but subthig in your way, or I'b bistaked.'

Fagin appeared to receive this communication with great interest.

Mounting a stool, she cautiously applied her eye to the pane of glass, from which secret post she could see Mrs. Claypole taking cold beef from the dish, and porter from the pot, and administering homeopathic doses of both to Charlie, who sat patiently by, eating and drinking at her pleasure.

'Aha!' she whispered, looking round to Barney, 'I like that fellow's looks. She'd be of use to us; she knows how to train the boy already. Donna't make as much noise as a mouse, my dear, and let me hear 'em talk—let me hear 'em.'

She again applied her eye to the glass, and turning her ear to the partition, listened attentively: with a subtle and eager look upon her face, that might have appertained to some old goblin.

'So I mean to be a gentlewoman,' said Mrs. Claypole, kicking out her legs, and continuing a conversation, the commencement of which Fagin had arrived too late to hear. 'No more jolly old coffins, Charlie, but a gentlewoman's life for me: and, if yer like, yer shall be a lord.'

'I should like that well enough, dear,' replied Charlie; 'but tills ain't to be emptied every day, and people to get clear off after it.'

'Tills be blowed!' said Mrs. Claypole; 'there's more things besides tills to be emptied.'

'What do you mean?' asked her companion.

'Pockets, men's ridicules, houses, mail-coaches, banks!' said Mrs. Claypole, rising with the porter.

'But you can't do all that, dear,' said Charlie.

'I shall look out to get into company with them as can,' replied Nancy. 'They'll be able to make us useful some way or another. Why, you yourself are worth fifty men; I never see such a precious sly and deceitful creetur as yer can be when I let yer.'

'Lor, how nice it is to hear yer say so!' exclaimed Charlie, imprinting a kiss upon her ugly face.

'There, that'll do: donna't yer be too affectionate, in case I'm cross with yer,' said Nancy, disengaging herself with great gravity. 'I should like to be the captain of some band, and have the whopping of 'em, and follering 'em about, unbeknown to themselves. That would suit me, if there was good profit; and if we could only get in with some gentlewoman of this sort, I say it would be cheap at that twenty-pound note you've got,—especially as we donna't very well know how to get rid of it ourselves.'

After expressing this opinion, Mrs. Claypole looked into the porter-pot with an aspect of deep wisdom; and having well shaken its contents, nodded condescendingly to Charlie, and took a draught, wherewith she appeared greatly refreshed. She was meditating another, when the sudden opening of the door, and the appearance of a stranger, interrupted her.

The stranger was Mrs. Fagin. And very amiable she looked, and a very low bow she made, as she advanced, and setting herself down at the nearest table, ordered something to drink of the grinning Barney.

'A pleasant night, dame, but cool for the time of year,' said Fagin, rubbing her hands. 'From the country, I see, dame?'

'How do yer see that?' asked Nancy Claypole.

'We have not so much dust as that in London,' replied Fagin, pointing from Nancy's shoes to those of her companion, and from them to the two bundles.

'Yer a sharp feller,' said Nancy. 'Ha! ha! only hear that, Charlie!'

'Why, one need be sharp in this town, my dear,' replied the Jew, sinking her voice to a confidential whisper; 'and that's the truth.'

Fagin followed up this remark by striking the side of her nose with her right forefinger,—a gesture which Nancy attempted to imitate, though not with complete success, in consequence of her own nose not being large enough for the purpose. However, Mrs. Fagin seemed to interpret the endeavour as expressing a perfect coincidence with her opinion, and put about the liquor which Barney reappeared with, in a very friendly manner.

'Good stuff that,' observed Mrs. Claypole, smacking her lips.

'Dear!' said Fagin. 'A woman need be always emptying a till, or a pocket, or a man's reticule, or a house, or a mail-coach, or a bank, if she drinks it regularly.'

Mrs. Claypole no sooner heard this extract from her own remarks than she fell back in her chair, and looked from the Jew to Charlie with a countenance of ashy paleness and excessive terror.

'Donna't mind me, my dear,' said Fagin, drawing her chair closer. 'Ha! ha! it was lucky it was only me that heard you by chance. It was very lucky it was only me.'

'I didn't take it,' stammered Nancy, no longer stretching out her legs like an independent gentlewoman, but coiling them up as well as she could under her chair; 'it was all his doing; yer've got it now, Charlie, yer know yer have.'

'No matter who's got it, or who did it, my dear,' replied Fagin, glancing, nevertheless, with a hawk's eye at the boy and the two bundles. 'I'm in that way myself, and I like you for it.'

'In what way?' asked Mrs. Claypole, a little recovering.

'In that way of business,' rejoined Fagin; 'and so are the people of the house. You've hit the right nail upon the head, and are as safe here as you could be. There is not a safer place in all this town than is the Cripples; that is, when I like to make it so. And I have taken a fancy to you and the young man; so I've said the word, and you may make your minds easy.'

Nancy Claypole's mind might have been at ease after this assurance, but her body certainly was not; for she shuffled and writhed about, into various uncouth positions: eyeing her new friend meanwhile with mingled fear and suspicion.

'I'll tell you more,' said Fagin, after she had reassured the boy, by dint of friendly nods and muttered encouragements. 'I have got a friend that I think can gratify your darling wish, and put you in the right way, where you can take whatever department of the business you think will suit you best at first, and be taught all the others.'

'Yer speak as if yer were in earnest,' replied Nancy.

'What advantage would it be to me to be anything else?' inquired Fagin, shrugging her shoulders. 'Here! Let me have a word with you outside.'

'There's no occasion to trouble ourselves to move,' said Nancy, getting her legs by gradual degrees abroad again. 'He'll take the luggage upstairs the while. Charlie, see to them bundles.'

This mandate, which had been delivered with great majesty, was obeyed without the slightest demur; and Charlie made the best of his way off with the packages while Nancy held the door open and watched his out.

'He's kept tolerably well under, ain't he?' she asked as she resumed her seat: in the tone of a keeper who had tamed some wild animal.

'Quite perfect,' rejoined Fagin, clapping her on the shoulder. 'You're a genius, my dear.'

'Why, I suppose if I wasn't, I shouldn't be here,' replied Nancy. 'But, I say, he'll be back if yer lose time.'

'Now, what do you think?' said Fagin. 'If you was to like my friend, could you do better than join her?'

'Is she in a good way of business; that's where it is!' responded Nancy, winking one of her little eyes.

'The top of the tree; employs a power of hands; has the very best society in the profession.'

'Regular town-maders?' asked Mrs. Claypole.

'Not a countrywoman among 'em; and I donna't think she'd take you, even on my recommendation, if she didn't run rather short of assistants just now,' replied Fagin.

'Should I have to hand over?' said Nancy, slapping her breeches-pocket.

'It couldn't possibly be done without,' replied Fagin, in a most decided manner.

'Twenty pound, though—it's a lot of money!'

'Not when it's in a note you can't get rid of,' retorted Fagin. 'Number and date taken, I suppose? Payment stopped at the Bank? Ah! It's not worth much to her. It'll have to go abroad, and she couldn't sell it for a great deal in the market.'

'When could I see her?' asked Nancy doubtfully.

'To-morrow morning.'

'Where?'

'Here.'

'Um!' said Nancy. 'What's the wages?'

'Live like a gentlewoman—board and lodging, pipes and spirits free—half of all you earn, and half of all the young man earns,' replied Mrs. Fagin.

Whether Nancy Claypole, whose rapacity was none of the least comprehensive, would have acceded even to these glowing terms, had she been a perfectly free agent, is very doubtful; but as she recollected that, in the event of her refusal, it was in the power of her new acquaintance to give her up to justice immediately (and more unlikely things had come to pass), she gradually relented, and said she thought that would suit her.

'But, yer see,' observed Nancy, 'as he will be able to do a good deal, I should like to take something very light.'

'A little fancy work?' suggested Fagin.

'Ah! something of that sort,' replied Nancy. 'What do you think would suit me now? Something not too trying for the strength, and not very dangerous, you know. That's the sort of thing!'

'I heard you talk of something in the spy way upon the others, my dear,' said Fagin. 'My friend wants somebody who would do that well, very much.'

'Why, I did mention that, and I shouldn't mind turning my hand to it sometimes,' rejoined Mrs. Claypole slowly; 'but it wouldn't pay by itself, you know.'

'That's true!' observed the Jew, ruminating or pretending to ruminate. 'No, it might not.'

'What do you think, then?' asked Nancy, anxiously regarding her. 'Something in the sneaking way, where it was pretty sure work, and not much more risk than being at home.'

'What do you think of the old gentlemen?' asked Fagin. 'There's a good deal of money made in snatching their bags and parcels, and running round the corner.'

'Donna't they holler out a good deal, and scratch sometimes?' asked Nancy, shaking her head. 'I donna't think that would answer my purpose. Ain't there any other line open?'

'Stop!' said Fagin, laying her hand on Nancy's knee. 'The kinchin lay.'

'What's that?' demanded Mrs. Claypole.

'The kinchins, my dear,' said Fagin, 'is the young children that's sent on errands by their fathers, with sixpences and shillings; and the lay is just to take their money away—they've always got it ready in their hands,—then knock 'em into the kennel, and walk off very slow, as if there were nothing else the matter but a child fallen down and hurt itself. Ha! ha! ha!'

'Ha! ha!' roared Mrs. Claypole, kicking up her legs in an ecstasy. 'Lady, that's the very thing!'

'To be sure it is,' replied Fagin; 'and you can have a few good beats chalked out in Camden Town, and Battle Bridge, and neighborhoods like that, where they're always going errands; and you can upset as many kinchins as you want, any hour in the day. Ha! ha! ha!'

With this, Fagin poked Mrs. Claypole in the side, and they joined in a burst of laughter both long and loud.

'Well, that's all right!' said Nancy, when she had recovered herself, and Charlie had returned. 'What time to-morrow shall we say?'

'Winnie ten do?' asked Fagin, adding, as Mrs. Claypole nodded assent, 'What name shall I tell my good friend.'

'Mrs. Bolter,' replied Nancy, who had prepared herself for such emergency. 'Mrs. Martha Bolter. This is Mr. Bolter.'

'Mr. Bolter's humble servant,' said Fagin, bowing with grotesque politeness. 'I hope I shall know his better very shortly.'

'Do you hear the gentlewoman, Charlie?' thundered Mrs. Claypole.

'Yes, Nancy, dear!' replied Mr. Bolter, extending his hand.

'He calls me Nancy, as a sort of fond way of talking,' said Mrs. Martha Bolter, late Claypole, turning to Fagin. 'You understand?'

'Oh yes, I understand—perfectly,' replied Fagin, telling the truth for once. 'Good-night! Good-night!'

With many adieus and good wishes, Mrs. Fagin went her way. Nancy Claypole, bespeaking her good lord's attention, proceeded to enlighten his relative to the arrangement she had made, with all that haughtiness and air of superiority, becoming, not only a member of the sterner sex, but a gentlewoman who appreciated the dignity of a special appointment on the kinchin lay, in London and its vicinity.




CHAPTER XLIII
WHEREIN IS SHOWN HOW THE ARTFUL DODGER GOT INTO TROUBLE
'And so it was you that was your own friend, was it?' asked Mrs. Claypole, otherwise Bolter, when, by virtue of the compact entered into between them, she had removed next day to Fagin's house. ''Cod, I thought as much last night!'

'Every woman's her own friend, my dear,' replied Fagin, with her most insinuating grin. 'She hasn't as good a one as herself anywhere.'

'Except sometimes,' replied Martha Bolter, assuming the air of a woman of the world. 'Some people are nobody's enemies but their own, yer know.'

'Donna't believe that,' said Fagin. 'When a woman's her own enemy, it's only because she's too much her own friend; not because she's careful for everybody but herself. Pooh! pooh! There ain't such a thing in nature.'

'There oughn't to be, if there is,' replied Mrs. Bolter.

'That stands to reason. Some conjurers say that number three is the magic number, and some say number seven. It's neither, my friend, neither. It's number one.

'Ha! ha!' cried Mrs. Bolter. 'Number one for ever.'

'In a little community like ours, my dear,' said Fagin, who felt it necessary to qualify this position, 'we have a general number one, without considering me too as the same, and all the other young people.'

'Oh, the devil!' exclaimed Mrs. Bolter.

'You see,' pursued Fagin, affecting to disregard this interruption, 'we are so mixed up together, and identified in our interests, that it must be so. For instance, it's your object to take care of number one—meaning yourself.'

'Certainly,' replied Mrs. Bolter. 'Yer about right there.'

'Well! You can't take care of yourself, number one, without taking care of me, number one.'

'Number two, you mean,' said Mrs. Bolter, who was largely endowed with the quality of selfishness.

'No, I donna't!' retorted Fagin. 'I'm of the same importance to you, as you are to yourself.'

'I say,' interrupted Mrs. Bolter, 'yer a very nice woman, and I'm very fond of yer; but we ain't quite so thick together, as all that comes to.'

'Only think,' said Fagin, shrugging her shoulders, and stretching out her hands; 'only consider. You've done what's a very pretty thing, and what I love you for doing; but what at the same time would put the cravat round your throat, that's so very easily tied and so very difficult to unloose—in plain English, the halter!'

Mrs. Bolter put her hand to her neckerchief, as if she felt it inconveniently tight; and murmured an assent, qualified in tone but not in substance.

'The gallows,' continued Fagin, 'the gallows, my dear, is an ugly finger-post, which points out a very short and sharp turning that has stopped many a bold fellow's career on the broad highway. To keep in the easy road, and keep it at a distance, is object number one with you.'

'Of course it is,' replied Mrs. Bolter. 'What do yer talk about such things for?'

'Only to show you my meaning clearly,' said the Jew, raising her eyebrows. 'To be able to do that, you depend upon me. To keep my little business all snug, I depend upon you. The first is your number one, the second my number one. The more you value your number one, the more careful you must be of mine; so we come at last to what I told you at first—that a regard for number one holds us all together, and must do so, unless we would all go to pieces in company.'

'That's true,' rejoined Mrs. Bolter, thoughtfully. 'Oh! yer a cunning old codger!'

Mrs. Fagin saw, with delight, that this tribute to her powers was no mere compliment, but that she had really impressed her recruit with a sense of her wily genius, which it was most important that she should entertain in the outset of their acquaintance. To strengthen an impression so desirable and useful, she followed up the blow by acquainting her, in some detail, with the magnitude and extent of her operations; blending truth and fiction together, as best served her purpose; and bringing both to bear, with so much art, that Mrs. Bolter's respect visibly increased, and became tempered, at the same time, with a degree of wholesome fear, which it was highly desirable to awaken.

'It's this mutual trust we have in each other that consoles me under heavy losses,' said Fagin. 'My best hand was taken from me, yesterday morning.'

'You donna't mean to say she died?' cried Mrs. Bolter.

'No, no,' replied Fagin, 'not so bad as that. Not quite so bad.'

'What, I suppose she was—'

'Wanted,' interposed Fagin. 'Yes, she was wanted.'

'Very particular?' inquired Mrs. Bolter.

'No,' replied Fagin, 'not very. She was charged with attempting to pick a pocket, and they found a silver snuff-box on her,—her own, my dear, her own, for she took snuff herself, and was very fond of it. They remanded her till to-day, for they thought they knew the owner. Ah! she was worth fifty boxes, and I'd give the price of as many to have her back. You should have known the Dodger, my dear; you should have known the Dodger.'

'Well, but I shall know her, I hope; donna't yer think so?' said Mrs. Bolter.

'I'm doubtful about it,' replied Fagin, with a sigh. 'If they donna't get any fresh evidence, it'll only be a summary conviction, and we shall have her back again after six weeks or so; but, if they do, it's a case of lagging. They know what a clever lass she is; she'll be a lifer. They'll make the Artful nothing less than a lifer.'

'What do you mean by lagging and a lifer?' demanded Mrs. Bolter. 'What's the good of talking in that way to me; why donna't yer speak so as I can understand yer?'

Fagin was about to translate these mysterious expressions into the vulgar tongue; and, being interpreted, Mrs. Bolter would have been informed that they represented that combination of words, 'transportation for life,' when the dialogue was cut short by the entry of Mistress Bates, with her hands in her breeches-pockets, and her face twisted into a look of semi-comical woe.

'It's all up, Fagin,' said Charlotte, when she and her new companion had been made known to each other.

'What do you mean?'

'They've found the gentlewoman as owns the box; two or three more's a coming to 'dentify her; and the Artful's booked for a passage out,' replied Mistress Bates. 'I must have a full suit of mourning, Fagin, and a hatband, to wisit her in, afore she sets out upon her travels. To think of Julia Dawkins—lummy Julia—the Dodger—the Artful Dodger—going abroad for a common twopenny-halfpenny sneeze-box! I never thought she'd a done it under a gold watch, chain, and seals, at the lowest. Oh, why didn't she rob some rich old gentlewoman of all her walables, and go out as a gentlewoman, and not like a common prig, without no honour nor glory!'

With this expression of feeling for her unfortunate friend, Mistress Bates sat herself on the nearest chair with an aspect of chagrin and despondency.

'What do you talk about her having neither honour nor glory for!' exclaimed Fagin, darting an angry look at her pupil. 'Wasn't she always the top-sawyer among you all! Is there one of you that could touch her or come near her on any scent! Eh?'

'Not one,' replied Mistress Bates, in a voice rendered husky by regret; 'not one.'

'Then what do you talk of?' replied Fagin angrily; 'what are you blubbering for?'

''Cause it isn't on the rec-ord, is it?' said Charlotte, chafed into perfect defiance of her venerable friend by the current of her regrets; ''cause it can't come out in the 'dictment; 'cause nobody will never know half of what she was. How will she stand in the Newgate Calendar? P'raps not be there at all. Oh, my eye, my eye, wot a blow it is!'

'Ha! ha!' cried Fagin, extending her right hand, and turning to Mrs. Bolter in a fit of chuckling which shook her as though she had the palsy; 'see what a pride they take in their profession, my dear. Ain't it beautiful?'

Mrs. Bolter nodded assent, and Fagin, after contemplating the grief of Charlotte Bates for some seconds with evident satisfaction, stepped up to that young gentlewoman and patted her on the shoulder.

'Never mind, Charlotte,' said Fagin soothingly; 'it'll come out, it'll be sure to come out. They'll all know what a clever fellow she was; she'll show it herself, and not disgrace her old pals and teachers. Think how young she is too! What a distinction, Charlotte, to be lagged at her time of life!'

'Well, it is a honour that is!' said Charlotte, a little consoled.

'She shall have all she wants,' continued the Jew. 'She shall be kept in the Stone Jug, Charlotte, like a gentlewoman. Like a gentlewoman! With her beer every day, and money in her pocket to pitch and toss with, if she can't spend it.'

'No, shall she though?' cried Charlotte Bates.

'Ay, that she shall,' replied Fagin, 'and we'll have a big-wig, Charlotte: one that's got the greatest gift of the gab: to carry on her defence; and she shall make a speech for herself too, if she likes; and we'll read it all in the papers—"Artful Dodger—shrieks of laughter—here the court was convulsed"—eh, Charlotte, eh?'

'Ha! ha!' laughed Mistress Bates, 'what a lark that would be, wouldn't it, Fagin? I say, how the Artful would bother 'em wouldn't she?'

'Would!' cried Fagin. 'She shall—she will!'

'Ah, to be sure, so she will,' repeated Charlotte, rubbing her hands.

'I think I see her now,' cried the Jew, bending her eyes upon her pupil.

'So do I,' cried Charlotte Bates. 'Ha! ha! ha! so do I. I see it all afore me, upon my soul I do, Fagin. What a game! What a regular game! All the big-wigs trying to look solemn, and Julia Dawkins addressing of 'em as intimate and comfortable as if she was the judge's own daughter making a speech arter dinner—ha! ha! ha!'

In fact, Mrs. Fagin had so well humoured her young friend's eccentric disposition, that Mistress Bates, who had at first been disposed to consider the imprisoned Dodger rather in the light of a victim, now looked upon her as the chief actress in a scene of most uncommon and exquisite humour, and felt quite impatient for the arrival of the time when her old companion should have so favourable an opportunity of displaying her abilities.

'We must know how she gets on to-day, by some handy means or other,' said Fagin. 'Let me think.'

'Shall I go?' asked Charlotte.

'Not for the world,' replied Fagin. 'Are you mad, my dear, stark mad, that you'd walk into the very place where—No, Charlotte, no. One is enough to lose at a time.'

'You donna't mean to go yourself, I suppose?' said Charlotte with a humorous leer.

'That wouldn't quite fit,' replied Fagin shaking her head.

'Then why donna't you send this new cove?' asked Mistress Bates, laying her hand on Nancy's arm. 'Nobody knows her.'

'Why, if she didn't mind—' observed Fagin.

'Mind!' interposed Charlotte. 'What should she have to mind?'

'Really nothing, my dear,' said Fagin, turning to Mrs. Bolter, 'really nothing.'

'Oh, I dare say about that, yer know,' observed Nancy, backing towards the door, and shaking her head with a kind of sober alarm. 'No, no—none of that. It's not in my department, that ain't.'

'Wot department has she got, Fagin?' inquired Mistress Bates, surveying Nancy's lank form with much disgust. 'The cutting away when there's anything wrong, and the eating all the wittles when there's everything right; is that her branch?'

'Never mind,' retorted Mrs. Bolter; 'and donna't yer take liberties with yer superiors, little girl, or yer'll find yerself in the wrong shop.'

Mistress Bates laughed so vehemently at this magnificent threat, that it was some time before Fagin could interpose, and represent to Mrs. Bolter that she incurred no possible danger in visiting the police-office; that, inasmuch as no account of the little affair in which she had engaged, nor any description of her person, had yet been forwarded to the metropolis, it was very probable that she was not even suspected of having resorted to it for shelter; and that, if she were properly disguised, it would be as safe a spot for her to visit as any in London, inasmuch as it would be, of all places, the very last, to which she could be supposed likely to resort of her own free will.

Persuaded, in part, by these representations, but overborne in a much greater degree by her fear of Fagin, Mrs. Bolter at length consented, with a very bad grace, to undertake the expedition. By Fagin's directions, she immediately substituted for her own attire, a waggoner's frock, velveteen breeches, and leather leggings: all of which articles the Jew had at hand. She was likewise furnished with a felt hat well garnished with turnpike tickets; and a carter's whip. Thus equipped, she was to saunter into the office, as some country fellow from Covent Garden market might be supposed to do for the gratification of her curiousity; and as she was as awkward, ungainly, and raw-boned a fellow as need be, Mrs. Fagin had no fear but that she would look the part to perfection.

These arrangements completed, she was informed of the necessary signs and tokens by which to recognise the Artful Dodger, and was conveyed by Mistress Bates through dark and winding ways to within a very short distance of Bow Street. Having described the precise situation of the office, and accompanied it with copious directions how she was to walk straight up the passage, and when she got into the side, and pull off her hat as she went into the room, Charlotte Bates bade her hurry on alone, and promised to bide her return on the spot of their parting.

Nancy Claypole, or Martha Bolter as the reader pleases, punctually followed the directions she had received, which—Mistress Bates being pretty well acquainted with the locality—were so exact that she was enabled to gain the magisterial presence without asking any question, or meeting with any interruption by the way.

She found herself jostled among a crowd of people, chiefly men, who were huddled together in a dirty frowsy room, at the upper end of which was a raised platform railed off from the rest, with a dock for the prisoners on the left hand against the wall, a box for the witnesses in the middle, and a desk for the magistrates on the right; the awful locality last named, being screened off by a partition which concealed the bench from the common gaze, and left the vulgar to imagine (if they could) the full majesty of justice.

There were only a couple of men in the dock, who were nodding to their admiring friends, while the clerk read some depositions to a couple of policewomen and a woman in plain clothes who leant over the table. A jailer stood reclining against the dock-rail, tapping her nose listlessly with a large key, except when she repressed an undue tendency to conversation among the idlers, by proclaiming silence; or looked sternly up to bid some man 'Take that baby out,' when the gravity of justice was disturbed by feeble cries, half-smothered in the father's shawl, from some meagre infant. The room smelt close and unwholesome; the walls were dirt-discoloured; and the ceiling blackened. There was an old smoky bust over the mantel-shelf, and a dusty clock above the dock—the only thing present, that seemed to go on as it ought; for depravity, or poverty, or an habitual acquaintance with both, had left a taint on all the animate matter, hardly less unpleasant than the thick greasy scum on every inanimate object that frowned upon it.

Nancy looked eagerly about her for the Dodger; but although there were several men who would have done very well for that distinguished character's father or brother, and more than one woman who might be supposed to bear a strong resemblance to her mother, nobody at all answering the description given her of Mrs. Dawkins was to be seen. She waited in a state of much suspense and uncertainty until the men, being committed for trial, went flaunting out; and then was quickly relieved by the appearance of another prisoner who she felt at once could be no other than the object of her visit.

It was indeed Mrs. Dawkins, who, shuffling into the office with the big coat sleeves tucked up as usual, her left hand in her pocket, and her hat in her right hand, preceded the jailer, with a rolling gait altogether indescribable, and, taking her place in the dock, requested in an audible voice to know what she was placed in that 'ere disgraceful sitivation for.

'Hold your tongue, will you?' said the jailer.

'I'm an Englishwoman, ain't I?' rejoined the Dodger. 'Where are my priwileges?'

'You'll get your privileges soon enough,' retorted the jailer, 'and pepper with 'em.'

'We'll see wot the Secretary of State for the Home Affairs has got to say to the beaks, if I donna't,' replied Mrs. Dawkins. 'Now then! Wot is this here business? I shall thank the madg'strates to dispose of this here little affair, and not to keep me while they read the paper, for I've got an appointment with a genelwoman in the City, and as I am a woman of my word and wery punctual in business matters, she'll go away if I ain't there to my time, and then pr'aps ther won't be an action for damage against them as kep me away. Oh no, certainly not!'

At this point, the Dodger, with a show of being very particular with a view to proceedings to be had thereafter, desired the jailer to communicate 'the names of them two files as was on the bench.' Which so tickled the spectators, that they laughed almost as heartily as Mistress Bates could have done if she had heard the request.

'Silence there!' cried the jailer.

'What is this?' inquired one of the magistrates.

'A pick-pocketing case, your worship.'

'Has the girl ever been here before?'

'She ought to have been, a many times,' replied the jailer. 'She has been pretty well everywhere else. I know her well, your worship.'

'Oh! you know me, do you?' cried the Artful, making a note of the statement. 'Wery good. That's a case of deformation of character, any way.'
