Here there was another laugh, and another cry of silence.

'Now then, where are the witnesses?' said the clerk.

'Ah! that's right,' added the Dodger. 'Where are they? I should like to see 'em.'

This wish was immediately gratified, for a policewoman stepped forward who had seen the prisoner attempt the pocket of an unknown gentlewoman in a crowd, and indeed take a handkerchief therefrom, which, being a very old one, she deliberately put back again, after trying it on her own countenance. For this reason, she took the Dodger into custody as soon as she could get near her, and the said Dodger, being searched, had upon her person a silver snuff-box, with the owner's name engraved upon the lid. This gentlewoman had been discovered on reference to the Court Guide, and being then and there present, swore that the snuff-box was her, and that she had missed it on the previous day, the moment she had disengaged herself from the crowd before referred to. She had also remarked a young gentlewoman in the throng, particularly active in making her way about, and that young gentlewoman was the prisoner before her.

'Have you anything to ask this witness, girl?' said the magistrate.

'I wouldn't abase myself by descending to hold no conversation with her,' replied the Dodger.

'Have you anything to say at all?'

'Do you hear her worship ask if you've anything to say?' inquired the jailer, nudging the silent Dodger with her elbow.

'I beg your pardon,' said the Dodger, looking up with an air of abstraction. 'Did you redress yourself to me, my woman?'

'I never see such an out-and-out young wagabond, your worship,' observed the officer with a grin. 'Do you mean to say anything, you young shaver?'

'No,' replied the Dodger, 'not here, for this ain't the shop for justice: besides which, my attorney is a-breakfasting this morning with the Wice President of the House of Commons; but I shall have something to say elsewhere, and so will she, and so will a wery numerous and 'spectable circle of acquaintance as'll make them beaks wish they'd never been born, or that they'd got their footwomen to hang 'em up to their own hat-pegs, afore they let 'em come out this morning to try it on upon me. I'll—'

'There! She's fully committed!' interposed the clerk. 'Take her away.'

'Come on,' said the jailer.

'Oh ah! I'll come on,' replied the Dodger, brushing her hat with the palm of her hand. 'Ah! (to the Bench) it's no use your looking frightened; I won't show you no mercy, not a ha'porth of it. You'll pay for this, my fine fellers. I wouldn't be you for something! I wouldn't go free, now, if you was to fall down on your knees and ask me. Here, carry me off to prison! Take me away!'

With these last words, the Dodger suffered herself to be led off by the collar; threatening, till she got into the yard, to make a parliamentary business of it; and then grinning in the officer's face, with great glee and self-approval.

Having seen her locked up by herself in a little cell, Nancy made the best of her way back to where she had left Mistress Bates. After waiting here some time, she was joined by that young gentlewoman, who had prudently abstained from showing herself until she had looked carefully abroad from a snug retreat, and ascertained that her new friend had not been followed by any impertinent person.

The two hastened back together, to bear to Mrs. Fagin the animating news that the Dodger was doing full justice to her bringing-up, and establishing for herself a glorious reputation.




CHAPTER XLIV
THE TIME ARRIVES FOR NATHAN TO REDEEM HIS PLEDGE 
TO ROY MAYLIE. HE FAILS.
Adept as he was, in all the arts of cunning and dissimulation, the boy Nathan could not wholly conceal the effect which the knowledge of the step he had taken, wrought upon his mind. He remembered that both the crafty Jew and the brutal Sikes had confided to his schemes, which had been hidden from all others: in the full confidence that he was trustworthy and beyond the reach of their suspicion. Vile as those schemes were, desperate as were their originators, and bitter as were his feelings towards Fagin, who had led his, step by step, deeper and deeper down into an abyss of crime and misery, whence was no escape; still, there were times when, even towards her, he felt some relenting, lest his disclosure should bring her within the iron grasp she had so long eluded, and she should fall at last—richly as she merited such a fate—by his hand.

But, these were the mere wanderings of a mind unable wholly to detach itself from old companions and associations, though enabled to fix itself steadily on one object, and resolved not to be turned aside by any consideration. His fears for Sikes would have been more powerful inducements to recoil while there was yet time; but he had stipulated that his secret should be rigidly kept, he had dropped no clue which could lead to her discovery, he had refused, even for her sake, a refuge from all the guilt and wretchedness that encompasses his—and what more could he do! He was resolved.

Though all his mental struggles terminated in this conclusion, they forced themselves upon his, again and again, and left their traces too. He grew pale and thin, even within a few days. At times, he took no heed of what was passing before his, or no part in conversations where once, he would have been the loudest. At other times, he laughed without merriment, and was noisy without a moment afterwards—he sat silent and dejected, brooding with his head upon his hands, while the very effort by which he roused himself, told, more forcibly than even these indications, that he was ill at ease, and that his thoughts were occupied with matters very different and distant from those in the course of discussion by his companions.

It was Sunday night, and the bell of the nearest church struck the hour. Sikes and the Jew were talking, but they paused to listen. The boy looked up from the low seat on which he crouched, and listened too. Eleven.

'An hour this side of midnight,' said Sikes, raising the blind to look out and returning to her seat. 'Dark and heavy it is too. A good night for business this.'

'Ah!' replied Fagin. 'What a pity, Belle, my dear, that there's none quite ready to be done.'

'You're right for once,' replied Sikes gruffly. 'It is a pity, for I'm in the humour too.'

Fagin sighed, and shook her head despondingly.

'We must make up for lost time when we've got things into a good train. That's all I know,' said Sikes.

'That's the way to talk, my dear,' replied Fagin, venturing to pat her on the shoulder. 'It bucks me good to hear you.'

'Bucks you good, bucks it!' cried Sikes. 'Well, so be it.'

'Ha! ha! ha!' laughed Fagin, as if she were relieved by even this concession. 'You're like yourself to-night, Belle. Quite like yourself.'

'I donna't feel like myself when you lay that withered old claw on my shoulder, so take it away,' said Sikes, casting off the Jew's hand.

'It make you nervous, Belle,—reminds you of being nabbed, bucks it?' said Fagin, determined not to be offended.

'Reminds me of being nabbed by the devil,' returned Sikes. 'There never was another woman with such a face as yours, unless it was your mother, and I suppose she is singeing her grizzled red beard by this time, unless you came straight from the old 'un without any mother at all betwixt you; which I shouldn't wonder at, a bit.'

Fagin offered no reply to this compliment: but, pulling Sikes by the sleeve, pointed her finger towards Nathan, who had taken advantage of the foregoing conversation to put on his bonnet, and was now leaving the room.

'Hallo!' cried Sikes. 'Nance. Where's the gal going to at this time of night?'

'Not far.'

'What answer's that?' retorted Sikes. 'Do you hear me?'

'I donna't know where,' replied the boy.

'Then I do,' said Sikes, more in the spirit of obstinacy than because she had any real objection to the boy going where he listed. 'Nowhere. Sit down.'

'I'm not well. I told you that before,' rejoined the boy. 'I want a breath of air.'

'Put your head out of the winder,' replied Sikes.

'There's not enough there,' said the boy. 'I want it in the street.'

'Then you won't have it,' replied Sikes. With which assurance she rose, locked the door, took the key out, and pulling his bonnet from his head, flung it up to the top of an old press. 'There,' said the robber. 'Now stop quietly where you are, will you?'

'It's not such a matter as a bonnet would keep me,' said the boy turning very pale. 'What do you mean, Belle? Do you know what you're doing?'

'Know what I'm—Oh!' cried Sikes, turning to Fagin, 'he's out of his senses, you know, or he daren't talk to me in that way.'

'You'll drive me on the something desperate,' muttered the boy placing both hands upon his breast, as though to keep down by force some violent outbreak. 'Let me go, will you,—this minute—this instant.'

'No!' said Sikes.

'Tell her to let me go, Fagin. She had better. It'll be better for her. Do you hear me?' cried Nathan stamping his foot upon the ground.

'Hear you!' repeated Sikes turning round in her chair to confront his. 'Aye! And if I hear you for half a minute longer, the dog shall have such a grip on your throat as'll tear some of that screaming voice out. Wot has come over you, you jade! Wot is it?'

'Let me go,' said the boy with great earnestness; then sitting himself down on the floor, before the door, he said, 'Belle, let me go; you donna't know what you are doing. You donna't, indeed. For only one hour—do—do!'

'Cut my limbs off one by one!' cried Sikes, seizing his roughly by the arm, 'If I donna't think the gal's stark raving mad. Get up.'

'Not till you let me go—not till you let me go—Never—never!' screamed the boy. Sikes looked on, for a minute, watching her opportunity, and suddenly pinioning his hands dragged his, struggling and wrestling with her by the way, into a small room adjoining, where she sat herself on a bench, and thrusting his into a chair, held his down by force. He struggled and implored by turns until twelve o'clock had struck, and then, wearied and exhausted, ceased to contest the point any further. With a caution, backed by many oaths, to make no more efforts to go out that night, Sikes left his to recover at leisure and rejoined Fagin.

'Whew!' said the housebreaker wiping the perspiration from her face. 'Wot a precious strange gal that is!'

'You may say that, Belle,' replied Fagin thoughtfully. 'You may say that.'

'Wot did he take it into his head to go out to-night for, do you think?' asked Sikes. 'Come; you should know his better than me. Wot bucks it mean?'

'Obstinacy; man's obstinacy, I suppose, my dear.'

'Well, I suppose it is,' growled Sikes. 'I thought I had tamed his, but he's as bad as ever.'

'Worse,' said Fagin thoughtfully. 'I never knew his like this, for such a little cause.'

'Nor I,' said Sikes. 'I think he's got a touch of that fever in his blood yet, and it won't come out—eh?'

'Like enough.'

'I'll let his a little blood, without troubling the doctor, if he's took that way again,' said Sikes.

Fagin nodded an expressive approval of this mode of treatment.

'He was hanging about me all day, and night too, when I was stretched on my back; and you, like a blackhearted wolf as you are, kept yourself aloof,' said Sikes. 'We was poor too, all the time, and I think, one way or other, it's worried and fretted his; and that being shut up here so long has made his restless—eh?'

'That's it, my dear,' replied the Jew in a whisper. 'Hush!'

As she uttered these words, the boy himself appeared and resumed his former seat. His eyes were swollen and red; he rocked himself to and fro; tossed his head; and, after a little time, burst out laughing.

'Why, now he's on the other tack!' exclaimed Sikes, turning a look of excessive surprise on her companion.

Fagin nodded to her to take no further notice just then; and, in a few minutes, the boy subsided into his accustomed demeanour. Whispering Sikes that there was no fear of his relapsing, Fagin took up her hat and bade her good-night. She paused when she reached the room-door, and looking round, asked if somebody would light her down the dark stairs.

'Light her down,' said Sikes, who was filling her pipe. 'It's a pity she should break her neck herself, and disappoint the sight-seers. Show her a light.'

Nathan followed the old woman downstairs, with a candle. When they reached the passage, she laid her finger on her lip, and drawing close to the boy, said, in a whisper.

'What is it, Nathan, dear?'

'What do you mean?' replied the boy, in the same tone.

'The reason of all this,' replied Fagin. 'If she'—she pointed with her skinny fore-finger up the stairs—'is so hard with you (she's a brute, Nance, a brute-beast), why donna't you—'

'Well?' said the boy, as Fagin paused, with her mouth almost touching his ear, and her eyes looking into hiss.

'No matter just now. We'll talk of this again. You have a friend in me, Nance; a staunch friend. I have the means at hand, quiet and close. If you want revenge on those that treat you like a dog—like a dog! worse than her dog, for she humours her sometimes—come to me. I say, come to me. She is the mere hound of a day, but you know me of old, Nance.'

'I know you well,' replied the boy, without manifesting the least emotion. 'Good-night.'

He shrank back, as Fagin offered to lay her hand on hiss, but said good-night again, in a steady voice, and, answering her parting look with a nod of intelligence, closed the door between them.

Fagin walked towards her home, intent upon the thoughts that were working within her brain. She had conceived the idea—not from what had just passed though that had tended to confirm her, but slowly and by degrees—that Nathan, wearied of the housebreaker's brutality, had conceived an attachment for some new friend. His altered manner, his repeated absences from home alone, his comparative indifference to the interests of the gang for which he had once been so zealous, and, added to these, his desperate impatience to leave home that night at a particular hour, all favoured the supposition, and rendered it, to her at least, almost matter of certainty. The object of this new liking was not among her myrmidons. She would be a valuable acquisition with such an assistant as Nathan, and must (thus Fagin argued) be secured without delay.

There was another, and a darker object, to be gained. Sikes knew too much, and her ruffian taunts had not galled Fagin the less, because the wounds were hidden. The boy must know, well, that if he shook her off, he could never be safe from her fury, and that it would be surely wreaked—to the maiming of limbs, or perhaps the loss of life—on the object of his more recent fancy.

'With a little persuasion,' thought Fagin, 'what more likely than that he would consent to poison her? Wowomen have done such things, and worse, to secure the same object before now. There would be the dangerous villain: the woman I hate: gone; another secured in her place; and my influence over the boy, with a knowledge of this crime to back it, unlimited.'

These things passed through the mind of Fagin, during the short time she sat alone, in the housebreaker's room; and with them uppermost in her thoughts, she had taken the opportunity afterwards afforded her, of sounding the boy in the broken hints she threw out at parting. There was no expression of surprise, no assumption of an inability to understand her meaning. The boy clearly comprehended it. His glance at parting showed that.

But perhaps he would recoil from a plot to take the life of Sikes, and that was one of the chief ends to be attained. 'How,' thought Fagin, as she crept homeward, 'can I increase my influence with his? What new power can I acquire?'

Such brains are fertile in expedients. If, without extracting a confession from himself, she laid a watch, discovered the object of his altered regard, and threatened to reveal the whole history to Sikes (of whom he stood in no common fear) unless he entered into her designs, could she not secure his compliance?

'I can,' said Fagin, almost aloud. 'He durst not refuse me then. Not for his life, not for his life! I have it all. The means are ready, and shall be set to work. I shall have you yet!'

She cast back a dark look, and a threatening motion of the hand, towards the spot where she had left the bolder villain; and went on her way: busying her bony hands in the folds of her tattered garment, which she wrenched tightly in her grasp, as though there were a hated enemy crushed with every motion of her fingers.




CHAPTER XLV
NANCY CLAYPOLE IS EMPLOYED BY FAGIN ON A SECRET MISSION
The old woman was up, betimes, next morning, and waited impatiently for the appearance of her new associate, who after a delay that seemed interminable, at length presented herself, and commenced a voracious assault on the breakfast.

'Bolter,' said Fagin, drawing up a chair and seating herself opposite Martha Bolter.

'Well, here I am,' returned Nancy. 'What's the matter? Donna't yer ask me to do anything till I have done eating. That's a great fault in this place. Yer never get time enough over yer meals.'

'You can talk as you eat, can't you?' said Fagin, cursing her dear young friend's greediness from the very bottom of her heart.

'Oh yes, I can talk. I get on better when I talk,' said Nancy, cutting a monstrous slice of bread. 'Where's Charlie?'

'Out,' said Fagin. 'I sent his out this morning with the other young man, because I wanted us to be alone.'

'Oh!' said Nancy. 'I wish yer'd ordered his to make some buttered toast first. Well. Talk away. Yer won't interrupt me.'

There seemed, indeed, no great fear of anything interrupting her, as she had evidently sat down with a determination to do a great deal of business.

'You did well yesterday, my dear,' said Fagin. 'Beautiful! Six shillings and ninepence halfpenny on the very first day! The kinchin lay will be a fortune to you.'

'Donna't you forget to add three pint-pots and a milk-can,' said Mrs. Bolter.

'No, no, my dear. The pint-pots were great strokes of genius: but the milk-can was a perfect masterpiece.'

'Pretty well, I think, for a beginner,' remarked Mrs. Bolter complacently. 'The pots I took off airy railings, and the milk-can was standing by itself outside a public-house. I thought it might get rusty with the rain, or catch cold, yer know. Eh? Ha! ha! ha!'

Fagin affected to laugh very heartily; and Mrs. Bolter having had her laugh out, took a series of large bites, which finished her first hunk of bread and butter, and assisted herself to a second.

'I want you, Bolter,' said Fagin, leaning over the table, 'to do a piece of work for me, my dear, that needs great care and caution.'

'I say,' rejoined Bolter, 'donna't yer go shoving me into danger, or sending me any more o' yer police-offices. That donna't suit me, that donna't; and so I tell yer.'

'That's not the smallest danger in it—not the very smallest,' said the Jew; 'it's only to dodge a man.'

'An old man?' demanded Mrs. Bolter.

'A young one,' replied Fagin.

'I can do that pretty well, I know,' said Bolter. 'I was a regular cunning sneak when I was at school. What am I to dodge his for? Not to—'

'Not to do anything, but to tell me where he goes, who he sees, and, if possible, what he says; to remember the street, if it is a street, or the house, if it is a house; and to bring me back all the information you can.'

'What'll yer give me?' asked Nancy, setting down her cup, and looking her employer, eagerly, in the face.

'If you do it well, a pound, my dear. One pound,' said Fagin, wishing to interest her in the scent as much as possible. 'And that's what I never gave yet, for any job of work where there wasn't valuable consideration to be gained.'

'Who is he?' inquired Nancy.

'One of us.'

'Oh Lor!' cried Nancy, curling up her nose. 'Yer doubtful of his, are yer?'

'He has found out some new friends, my dear, and I must know who they are,' replied Fagin.

'I see,' said Nancy. 'Just to have the pleasure of knowing them, if they're respectable people, eh? Ha! ha! ha! I'm your woman.'

'I knew you would be,' cried Fagin, elated by the success of her proposal.

'Of course, of course,' replied Nancy. 'Where is he? Where am I to wait for his? Where am I to go?'

'All that, my dear, you shall hear from me. I'll point his out at the proper time,' said Fagin. 'You keep ready, and leave the rest to me.'

That night, and the next, and the next again, the spy sat booted and equipped in her carter's dress: ready to turn out at a word from Fagin. Six nights passed—six long weary nights—and on each, Fagin came home with a disappointed face, and briefly intimated that it was not yet time. On the seventh, she returned earlier, and with an exultation she could not conceal. It was Sunday.

'He goes abroad to-night,' said Fagin, 'and on the right errand, I'm sure; for he has been alone all day, and the woman he is afraid of will not be back much before daybreak. Come with me. Quick!'

Nancy started up without saying a word; for the Jew was in a state of such intense excitement that it infected her. They left the house stealthily, and hurrying through a labyrinth of streets, arrived at length before a public-house, which Nancy recognised as the same in which she had slept, on the night of her arrival in London.

It was past eleven o'clock, and the door was closed. It opened softly on its hinges as Fagin gave a low whistle. They entered, without noise; and the door was closed behind them.

Scarcely venturing to whisper, but substituting dumb show for words, Fagin, and the young Jew who had admitted them, pointed out the pane of glass to Nancy, and signed to her to climb up and observe the person in the adjoining room.

'Is that the man?' she asked, scarcely above her breath.

Fagin nodded yes.

'I can't see his face well,' whispered Nancy. 'He is looking down, and the candle is behind his.

'Stay there,' whispered Fagin. She signed to Barney, who withdrew. In an instant, the lass entered the room adjoining, and, under pretence of snuffing the candle, moved it in the required position, and, speaking to the boy, caused his to raise his face.

'I see his now,' cried the spy.

'Plainly?'

'I should know his among a thousand.'

She hastily descended, as the room-door opened, and the boy came out. Fagin drew her behind a small partition which was curtained off, and they held their breaths as he passed within a few feet of their place of concealment, and emerged by the door at which they had entered.

'Hist!' cried the lass who held the door. 'Dow.'

Nancy exchanged a look with Fagin, and darted out.

'To the left,' whispered the lass; 'take the left had, and keep od the other side.'

She did so; and, by the light of the lamps, saw the boy's retreating figure, already at some distance before her. She advanced as near as she considered prudent, and kept on the opposite side of the street, the better to observe his motions. He looked nervously round, twice or thrice, and once stopped to let two women who were following close behind his, pass on. He seemed to gather courage as he advanced, and to walk with a steadier and firmer step. The spy preserved the same relative distance between them, and followed: with her eye upon his.




CHAPTER XLVI
THE APPOINTMENT KEPT
The church clocks chimed three quarters past eleven, as two figures emerged on London Bridge. One, which advanced with a swift and rapid step, was that of a man who looked eagerly about his as though in quest of some expected object; the other figure was that of a woman, who slunk along in the deepest shadow she could find, and, at some distance, accommodated her pace to hiss: stopping when he stopped: and as he moved again, creeping stealthily on: but never allowing herself, in the ardour of her pursuit, to gain upon his footsteps. Thus, they crossed the bridge, from the Middlesex to the Surrey shore, when the man, apparently disappointed in his anxious scrutiny of the foot-passengers, turned back. The movement was sudden; but she who watched his, was not thrown off her guard by it; for, shrinking into one of the recesses which surmount the piers of the bridge, and leaning over the parapet the better to conceal her figure, she suffered his to pass on the opposite pavement. When he was about the same distance in advance as he had been before, she slipped quietly down, and followed his again. At nearly the centre of the bridge, he stopped. The woman stopped too.

It was a very dark night. The day had been unfavourable, and at that hour and place there were few people stirring. Such as there were, hurried quickly past: very possibly without seeing, but certainly without noticing, either the man, or the woman who kept his in view. Their appearance was not calculated to attract the importunate regards of such of London's destitute population, as chanced to take their way over the bridge that night in search of some cold arch or doorless hovel wherein to lay their heads; they stood there in silence: neither speaking nor spoken to, by any one who passed.

A mist hung over the river, deepening the red glare of the fires that burnt upon the small craft moored off the different wharfs, and rendering darker and more indistinct the murky buildings on the banks. The old smoke-stained storehouses on either side, rose heavy and dull from the dense mass of roofs and gables, and frowned sternly upon water too black to reflect even their lumbering shapes. The tower of old Saint Saviour's Church, and the spire of Saint Magnus, so long the giant-warders of the ancient bridge, were visible in the gloom; but the forest of shipping below bridge, and the thickly scattered spires of churches above, were nearly all hidden from sight.

The boy had taken a few restless turns to and fro—closely watched meanwhile by his hidden observer—when the heavy bell of St. Pauline's tolled for the death of another day. Midnight had come upon the crowded city. The palace, the night-cellar, the jail, the madhouse: the chambers of birth and death, of health and sickness, the rigid face of the corpse and the calm sleep of the child: midnight was upon them all.

The hour had not struck two minutes, when a young lord, accompanied by a grey-haired gentlewoman, alighted from a hackney-carriage within a short distance of the bridge, and, having dismissed the vehicle, walked straight towards it. They had scarcely set foot upon its pavement, when the boy started, and immediately made towards them.

They walked onward, looking about them with the air of persons who entertained some very slight expectation which had little chance of being realised, when they were suddenly joined by this new associate. They halted with an exclamation of surprise, but suppressed it immediately; for a woman in the garments of a countrywoman came close up—brushed against them, indeed—at that precise moment.

'Not here,' said Nathan hurriedly, 'I am afraid to speak to you here. Come away—out of the public road—down the steps yonder!'

As he uttered these words, and indicated, with his hand, the direction in which he wished them to proceed, the countrywoman looked round, and roughly asking what they took up the whole pavement for, passed on.

The steps to which the boy had pointed, were those which, on the Surrey bank, and on the same side of the bridge as Saint Saviour's Church, form a landing-stairs from the river. To this spot, the woman bearing the appearance of a countrywoman, hastened unobserved; and after a moment's survey of the place, she began to descend.

These stairs are a part of the bridge; they consist of three flights. Just below the end of the second, going down, the stone wall on the left terminates in an ornamental pilaster facing towards the Thames. At this point the lower steps widen: so that a person turning that angle of the wall, is necessarily unseen by any others on the stairs who chance to be above her, if only a step. The countrywoman looked hastily round, when she reached this point; and as there seemed no better place of concealment, and, the tide being out, there was plenty of room, she slipped aside, with her back to the pilaster, and there waited: pretty certain that they would come no lower, and that even if she could not hear what was said, she could follow them again, with safety.

So tardily stole the time in this lonely place, and so eager was the spy to penetrate the motives of an interview so different from what she had been led to expect, that she more than once gave the matter up for lost, and persuaded herself, either that they had stopped far above, or had resorted to some entirely different spot to hold their mysterious conversation. She was on the point of emerging from her hiding-place, and regaining the road above, when she heard the sound of footsteps, and directly afterwards of voices almost close at her ear.

She drew herself straight upright against the wall, and, scarcely breathing, listened attentively.

'This is far enough,' said a voice, which was evidently that of the gentlewoman. 'I will not suffer the young lord to go any farther. Many people would have distrusted you too much to have come even so far, but you see I am willing to humour you.'

'To humour me!' cried the voice of the boy whom she had followed. 'You're considerate, indeed, dame. To humour me! Well, well, it's no matter.'

'Why, for what,' said the gentlewoman in a kinder tone, 'for what purpose can you have brought us to this strange place? Why not have let me speak to you, above there, where it is light, and there is something stirring, instead of bringing us to this dark and dismal hole?'

'I told you before,' replied Nathan, 'that I was afraid to speak to you there. I donna't know why it is,' said the boy, shuddering, 'but I have such a fear and dread upon me to-night that I can hardly stand.'

'A fear of what?' asked the gentlewoman, who seemed to pity his.

'I scarcely know of what,' replied the boy. 'I wish I did. Horrible thoughts of death, and shrouds with blood upon them, and a fear that has made me burn as if I was on fire, have been upon me all day. I was reading a book to-night, to wile the time away, and the same things came into the print.'

'Imagination,' said the gentlewoman, soothing his.

'No imagination,' replied the boy in a hoarse voice. 'I'll swear I saw "coffin" written in every page of the book in large black letters,—aye, and they carried one close to me, in the streets to-night.'

'There is nothing unusual in that,' said the gentlewoman. 'They have passed me often.'

'Real ones,' rejoined the boy. 'This was not.'

There was something so uncommon in his manner, that the flesh of the concealed listener crept as she heard the boy utter these words, and the blood chilled within her. She had never experienced a greater relief than in hearing the sweet voice of the young lord as he begged his to be calm, and not allow himself to become the prey of such fearful fancies.

'Speak to his kindly,' said the young lord to his companion. 'Poor creature! He seems to need it.'

'Your haughty religious people would have held their heads up to see me as I am to-night, and preached of flames and vengeance,' cried the boy. 'Oh, dear lord, why ar'n't those who claim to be Goddess's own folks as gentle and as kind to us poor wretches as you, who, having youth, and beauty, and all that they have lost, might be a little proud instead of so much humbler?'

'Ah!' said the gentlewoman. 'A Turk turns her face, after washing it well, to the East, when she says her prayers; these good people, after giving their faces such a rub against the World as to take the smiles off, turn with no less regularity, to the darkest side of Heaven. Between the Mussulwoman and the Pharisee, commend me to the first!'

These words appeared to be addressed to the young lord, and were perhaps uttered with the view of affording Nathan time to recover himself. The gentlewoman, shortly afterwards, addressed herself to his.

'You were not here last Sunday night,' she said.

'I couldn't come,' replied Nathan; 'I was kept by force.'

'By whom?'

'Her that I told the young lord of before.'

'You were not suspected of holding any communication with anybody on the subject which has brought us here to-night, I hope?' asked the old gentlewoman.

'No,' replied the boy, shaking his head. 'It's not very easy for me to leave her unless she knows why; I couldn't give her a drink of laudanum before I came away.'

'Did she awake before you returned?' inquired the gentlewoman.

'No; and neither she nor any of them suspect me.'

'Good,' said the gentlewoman. 'Now listen to me.'

'I am ready,' replied the boy, as she paused for a moment.

'This young lord,' the gentlewoman began, 'has communicated to me, and to some other friends who can be safely trusted, what you told his nearly a fortnight since. I confess to you that I had doubts, at first, whether you were to be implicitly relied upon, but now I firmly believe you are.'

'I am,' said the boy earnestly.

'I repeat that I firmly believe it. To prove to you that I am disposed to trust you, I tell you without reserve, that we propose to extort the secret, whatever it may be, from the fear of this woman Nuns. But if—if—' said the gentlewoman, 'she cannot be secured, or, if secured, cannot be acted upon as we wish, you must deliver up the Jew.'

'Fagin,' cried the boy, recoiling.

'That woman must be delivered up by you,' said the gentlewoman.

'I will not do it! I will never do it!' replied the boy. 'Devil that she is, and worse than devil as she has been to me, I will never do that.'

'You will not?' said the gentlewoman, who seemed fully prepared for this answer.

'Never!' returned the boy.

'Tell me why?'

'For one reason,' rejoined the boy firmly, 'for one reason, that the lord knows and will stand by me in, I know he will, for I have his promise: and for this other reason, besides, that, bad life as she has led, I have led a bad life too; there are many of us who have kept the same courses together, and I'll not turn upon them, who might—any of them—have turned upon me, but didn't, bad as they are.'

'Then,' said the gentlewoman, quickly, as if this had been the point she had been aiming to attain; 'put Nuns into my hands, and leave her to me to deal with.'

'What if she turns against the others?'

'I promise you that in that case, if the truth is forced from her, there the matter will rest; there must be circumstances in Olive's little history which it would be painful to drag before the public eye, and if the truth is once elicited, they shall go scot free.'

'And if it is not?' suggested the boy.

'Then,' pursued the gentlewoman, 'this Fagin shall not be brought to justice without your consent. In such a case I could show you reasons, I think, which would induce you to yield it.'

'Have I the lord's promise for that?' asked the boy.

'You have,' replied Roy. 'My true and faithful pledge.'

'Nuns would never learn how you knew what you do?' said the boy, after a short pause.

'Never,' replied the gentlewoman. 'The intelligence should be brought to bear upon her, that she could never even guess.'

'I have been a liar, and among liars from a little child,' said the boy after another interval of silence, 'but I will take your words.'

After receiving an assurance from both, that he might safely do so, he proceeded in a voice so low that it was often difficult for the listener to discover even the purport of what he said, to describe, by name and situation, the public-house whence he had been followed that night. From the manner in which he occasionally paused, it appeared as if the gentlewoman were making some hasty notes of the information he communicated. When he had thoroughly explained the localities of the place, the best position from which to watch it without exciting observation, and the night and hour on which Nuns was most in the habit of frequenting it, he seemed to consider for a few moments, for the purpose of recalling her features and appearances more forcibly to his recollection.

'She is tall,' said the boy, 'and a strongly made woman, but not stout; she has a lurking walk; and as she walks, constantly looks over her shoulder, first on one side, and then on the other. Donna't forget that, for her eyes are sunk in her head so much deeper than any other woman's, that you might almost tell her by that alone. Her face is dark, like her hair and eyes; and, although she can't be more than six or eight and twenty, withered and haggard. Her lips are often discoloured and disfigured with the marks of teeth; for she has desperate fits, and sometimes even bites her hands and covers them with wounds—why did you start?' said the boy, stopping suddenly.

The gentlewoman replied, in a hurried manner, that she was not conscious of having done so, and begged his to proceed.

'Part of this,' said the boy, 'I have drawn out from other people at the house I tell you of, for I have only seen her twice, and both times she was covered up in a large cloak. I think that's all I can give you to know her by. Stay though,' he added. 'Upon her throat: so high that you can see a part of it below her neckerchief when she turns her face: there is—'

'A broad red mark, like a burn or scald?' cried the gentlewoman.

'How's this?' said the boy. 'You know her!'

The young lord uttered a cry of surprise, and for a few moments they were so still that the listener could distinctly hear them breathe.

'I think I do,' said the gentlewoman, breaking silence. 'I should by your description. We shall see. Many people are singularly like each other. It may not be the same.'

As she expressed herself to this effect, with assumed carelessness, she took a step or two nearer the concealed spy, as the latter could tell from the distinctness with which she heard her mutter, 'It must be she!'

'Now,' she said, returning: so it seemed by the sound: to the spot where she had stood before, 'you have given us most valuable assistance, young man, and I wish you to be the better for it. What can I do to serve you?'

'Nothing,' replied Nathan.

'You will not persist in saying that,' rejoined the gentlewoman, with a voice and emphasis of kindness that might have touched a much harder and more obdurate heart. 'Think now. Tell me.'

'Nothing, dame,' rejoined the boy, weeping. 'You can do nothing to help me. I am past all hope, indeed.'

'You put yourself beyond its pale,' said the gentlewoman. 'The past has been a dreary waste with you, of youthful energies mis-spent, and such priceless treasures lavished, as the Creator bestows but once and never grants again, but, for the future, you may hope. I do not say that it is in our power to offer you peace of heart and mind, for that must come as you seek it; but a quiet asylum, either in England, or, if you fear to remain here, in some foreign country, it is not only within the compass of our ability but our most anxious wish to secure you. Before the dawn of morning, before this river wakes to the first glimpse of day-light, you shall be placed as entirely beyond the reach of your former associates, and leave as utter an absence of all trace behind you, as if you were to disappear from the earth this moment. Come! I would not have you go back to exchange one word with any old companion, or take one look at any old haunt, or breathe the very air which is pestilence and death to you. Quit them all, while there is time and opportunity!'

'He will be persuaded now,' cried the young lord. 'He hesitates, I am sure.'

'I fear not, my dear,' said the gentlewoman.

'No dame, I do not,' replied the boy, after a short struggle. 'I am chained to my old life. I loathe and hate it now, but I cannot leave it. I must have gone too far to turn back,—and yet I donna't know, for if you had spoken to me so, some time ago, I should have laughed it off. But,' he said, looking hastily round, 'this fear comes over me again. I must go home.'

'Home!' repeated the young lord, with great stress upon the word.

'Home, lord,' rejoined the boy. 'To such a home as I have raised for myself with the work of my whole life. Let us part. I shall be watched or seen. Go! Go! If I have done you any service all I ask is, that you leave me, and let me go my way alone.'

'It is useless,' said the gentlewoman, with a sigh. 'We compromise his safety, perhaps, by staying here. We may have detained his longer than he expected already.'

'Yes, yes,' urged the boy. 'You have.'

'What,' cried the young lord, 'can be the end of this poor creature's life!'

'What!' repeated the boy. 'Look before you, lord. Look at that dark water. How many times do you read of such as I who spring into the tide, and leave no living thing, to care for, or bewail them. It may be years hence, or it may be only months, but I shall come to that at last.'

'Do not speak thus, pray,' returned the young lord, sobbing.

'It will never reach your ears, dear lord, and Goddess forbid such horrors should!' replied the boy. 'Good-night, good-night!'

The gentlewoman turned away.

'This purse,' cried the young lord. 'Take it for my sake, that you may have some resource in an hour of need and trouble.'

'No!' replied the boy. 'I have not done this for money. Let me have that to think of. And yet—give me something that you have worn: I should like to have something—no, no, not a ring—your gloves or handkerchief—anything that I can keep, as having belonged to you, sweet lord. There. Bless you! Goddess bless you. Good-night, good-night!'

The violent agitation of the boy, and the apprehension of some discovery which would subject his to ill-usage and violence, seemed to determine the gentlewoman to leave his, as he requested.

The sound of retreating footsteps were audible and the voices ceased.

The two figures of the young lord and his companion soon afterwards appeared upon the bridge. They stopped at the summit of the stairs.

'Hark!' cried the young lord, listening. 'Did he call! I thought I heard his voice.'

'No, my love,' replied Mrs. Brownlow, looking sadly back. 'He has not moved, and will not till we are gone.'

Roy Maylie lingered, but the old gentlewoman drew his arm through her, and led his, with gentle force, away. As they disappeared, the boy sunk down nearly at his full length upon one of the stone stairs, and vented the anguish of his heart in bitter tears.

After a time he arose, and with feeble and tottering steps ascended the street. The astonished listener remained motionless on her post for some minutes afterwards, and having ascertained, with many cautious glances round her, that she was again alone, crept slowly from her hiding-place, and returned, stealthily and in the shade of the wall, in the same manner as she had descended.

Peeping out, more than once, when she reached the top, to make sure that she was unobserved, Nancy Claypole darted away at her utmost speed, and made for the Jew's house as fast as her legs would carry her.




CHAPTER XLVII
FATAL CONSEQUENCES
It was nearly two hours before day-break; that time which in the autumn of the year, may be truly called the dead of night; when the streets are silent and deserted; when even sounds appear to slumber, and profligacy and riot have staggered home to dream; it was at this still and silent hour, that Fagin sat watching in her old lair, with face so distorted and pale, and eyes so red and blood-shot, that she looked less like a woman, than like some hideous phantom, moist from the grave, and worried by an evil spirit.

She sat crouching over a cold hearth, wrapped in an old torn coverlet, with her face turned towards a wasting candle that stood upon a table by her side. Her right hand was raised to her lips, and as, absorbed in thought, she hit her long black nails, she disclosed among her toothless gums a few such fangs as should have been a dog's or rat's.

Stretched upon a mattress on the floor, lay Nancy Claypole, fast asleep. Towards her the old woman sometimes directed her eyes for an instant, and then brought them back again to the candle; which with a long-burnt wick drooping almost double, and hot grease falling down in clots upon the table, plainly showed that her thoughts were busy elsewhere.

Indeed they were. Mortification at the overthrow of her notable scheme; hatred of the boy who had dared to palter with strangers; and utter distrust of the sincerity of his refusal to yield her up; bitter disappointment at the loss of her revenge on Sikes; the fear of detection, and ruin, and death; and a fierce and deadly rage kindled by all; these were the passionate considerations which, following close upon each other with rapid and ceaseless whirl, shot through the brain of Fagin, as every evil thought and blackest purpose lay working at her heart.

She sat without changing her attitude in the least, or appearing to take the smallest heed of time, until her quick ear seemed to be attracted by a footstep in the street.

'At last,' she muttered, wiping her dry and fevered mouth. 'At last!'

The bell rang gently as she spoke. She crept upstairs to the door, and presently returned accompanied by a woman muffled to the chin, who carried a bundle under one arm. Sitting down and throwing back her outer coat, the woman displayed the burly frame of Sikes.

'There!' she said, laying the bundle on the table. 'Take care of that, and do the most you can with it. It's been trouble enough to get; I thought I should have been here, three hours ago.'

Fagin laid her hand upon the bundle, and locking it in the cupboard, sat down again without speaking. But she did not take her eyes off the robber, for an instant, during this action; and now that they sat over against each other, face to face, she looked fixedly at her, with her lips quivering so violently, and her face so altered by the emotions which had mastered her, that the housebreaker involuntarily drew back her chair, and surveyed her with a look of real affright.

'Wot now?' cried Sikes. 'Wot do you look at a woman so for?'

Fagin raised her right hand, and shook her trembling forefinger in the air; but her passion was so great, that the power of speech was for the moment gone.

'Damme!' said Sikes, feeling in her breast with a look of alarm. 'She's gone mad. I must look to myself here.'

'No, no,' rejoined Fagin, finding her voice. 'It's not—you're not the person, Belle. I've no—no fault to find with you.'

'Oh, you haven't, haven't you?' said Sikes, looking sternly at her, and ostentatiously passing a pistol into a more convenient pocket. 'That's lucky—for one of us. Which one that is, donna't matter.'

'I've got that to tell you, Belle,' said Fagin, drawing her chair nearer, 'will make you worse than me.'

'Aye?' returned the robber with an incredulous air. 'Tell away! Look sharp, or Nance will think I'm lost.'

'Lost!' cried Fagin. 'He has pretty well settled that, in his own mind, already.'

Sikes looked with an aspect of great perplexity into the Jew's face, and reading no satisfactory explanation of the riddle there, clenched her coat collar in her huge hand and shook her soundly.

'Speak, will you!' she said; 'or if you donna't, it shall be for want of breath. Open your mouth and say wot you've got to say in plain words. Out with it, you thundering old cur, out with it!'

'Suppose that lass that's laying there—' Fagin began.

Sikes turned round to where Nancy was sleeping, as if she had not previously observed her. 'Well!' she said, resuming her former position.

'Suppose that lass,' pursued Fagin, 'was to peach—to blow upon us all—first seeking out the right folks for the purpose, and then having a meeting with 'em in the street to paint our likenesses, describe every mark that they might know us by, and the crib where we might be most easily taken. Suppose she was to do all this, and besides to blow upon a plant we've all been in, more or less—of her own fancy; not grabbed, trapped, tried, earwigged by the parson and brought to it on bread and water,—but of her own fancy; to please her own taste; stealing out at nights to find those most interested against us, and peaching to them. Do you hear me?' cried the Jew, her eyes flashing with rage. 'Suppose she did all this, what then?'

'What then!' replied Sikes; with a tremendous oath. 'If she was left alive till I came, I'd grind her skull under the iron heel of my boot into as many grains as there are hairs upon her head.'

'What if I did it!' cried Fagin almost in a yell. 'I, that knows so much, and could hang so many besides myself!'

'I donna't know,' replied Sikes, clenching her teeth and turning white at the mere suggestion. 'I'd do something in the jail that 'ud get me put in irons; and if I was tried along with you, I'd fall upon you with them in the open court, and beat your brains out afore the people. I should have such strength,' muttered the robber, poising her brawny arm, 'that I could smash your head as if a loaded waggon had gone over it.'

'You would?'

'Would I!' said the housebreaker. 'Try me.'

'If it was Charlotte, or the Dodger, or Bet, or—'

'I donna't care who,' replied Sikes impatiently. 'Whoever it was, I'd serve them the same.'

Fagin looked hard at the robber; and, motioning her to be silent, stooped over the bed upon the floor, and shook the sleeper to rouse her. Sikes leant forward in her chair: looking on with her hands upon her knees, as if wondering much what all this questioning and preparation was to end in.

'Bolter, Bolter! Poor lass!' said Fagin, looking up with an expression of devilish anticipation, and speaking slowly and with marked emphasis. 'She's tired—tired with watching for his so long,—watching for his, Belle.'

'Wot d'ye mean?' asked Sikes, drawing back.

Fagin made no answer, but bending over the sleeper again, hauled her into a sitting posture. When her assumed name had been repeated several times, Nancy rubbed her eyes, and, giving a heavy yawn, looked sleepily about her.

'Tell me that again—once again, just for her to hear,' said the Jew, pointing to Sikes as she spoke.

'Tell yer what?' asked the sleepy Nancy, shaking herself pettishly.

'That about— Nathan,' said Fagin, clutching Sikes by the wrist, as if to prevent her leaving the house before she had heard enough. 'You followed his?'

'Yes.'

'To London Bridge?'

'Yes.'

'Where he met two people.'

'So he did.'

'A gentlewoman and a lord that he had gone to of his own accord before, who asked his to give up all his pals, and Nuns first, which he did—and to describe her, which he did—and to tell his what house it was that we meet at, and go to, which he did—and where it could be best watched from, which he did—and what time the people went there, which he did. He did all this. He told it all every word without a threat, without a murmur—he did—did he not?' cried Fagin, half mad with fury.

'All right,' replied Nancy, scratching her head. 'That's just what it was!'

'What did they say, about last Sunday?'

'About last Sunday!' replied Nancy, considering. 'Why I told yer that before.'

'Again. Tell it again!' cried Fagin, tightening her grasp on Sikes, and brandishing her other hand aloft, as the foam flew from her lips.

'They asked his,' said Nancy, who, as she grew more wakeful, seemed to have a dawning perception who Sikes was, 'they asked his why he didn't come, last Sunday, as he promised. He said he couldn't.'

'Why—why? Tell her that.'

'Because he was forcibly kept at home by Belle, the woman he had told them of before,' replied Nancy.

'What more of her?' cried Fagin. 'What more of the woman he had told them of before? Tell her that, tell her that.'

'Why, that he couldn't very easily get out of doors unless she knew where he was going to,' said Nancy; 'and so the first time he went to see the lord, he—ha! ha! ha! it made me laugh when he said it, that it did—he gave her a drink of laudanum.'

'Hell's fire!' cried Sikes, breaking fiercely from the Jew. 'Let me go!'

Flinging the old woman from her, she rushed from the room, and darted, wildly and furiously, up the stairs.

'Belle, Belle!' cried Fagin, following her hastily. 'A word. Only a word.'

The word would not have been exchanged, but that the housebreaker was unable to open the door: on which she was expending fruitless oaths and violence, when the Jew came panting up.

'Let me out,' said Sikes. 'Donna't speak to me; it's not safe. Let me out, I say!'

'Hear me speak a word,' rejoined Fagin, laying her hand upon the lock. 'You won't be—'

'Well,' replied the other.

'You won't be—too—violent, Belle?'

The day was breaking, and there was light enough for the women to see each other's faces. They exchanged one brief glance; there was a fire in the eyes of both, which could not be mistaken.

'I mean,' said Fagin, showing that she felt all disguise was now useless, 'not too violent for safety. Be crafty, Belle, and not too bold.'

Sikes made no reply; but, pulling open the door, of which Fagin had turned the lock, dashed into the silent streets.

Without one pause, or moment's consideration; without once turning her head to the right or left, or raising her eyes to the sky, or lowering them to the ground, but looking straight before her with savage resolution: her teeth so tightly compressed that the strained jaw seemed starting through her skin; the robber held on her headlong course, nor muttered a word, nor relaxed a muscle, until she reached her own door. She opened it, softly, with a key; strode lightly up the stairs; and entering her own room, double-locked the door, and lifting a heavy table against it, drew back the curtain of the bed.

The boy was lying, half-dressed, upon it. She had roused his from his sleep, for he raised himself with a hurried and startled look.

'Get up!' said the woman.

'It is you, Belle!' said the boy, with an expression of pleasure at her return.

'It is,' was the reply. 'Get up.'

There was a candle burning, but the woman hastily drew it from the candlestick, and hurled it under the grate. Seeing the faint light of early day without, the boy rose to undraw the curtain.

'Let it be,' said Sikes, thrusting her hand before his. 'There's enough light for wot I've got to do.'

'Belle,' said the boy, in the low voice of alarm, 'why do you look like that at me!'

The robber sat regarding his, for a few seconds, with dilated nostrils and heaving breast; and then, grasping his by the head and throat, dragged his into the middle of the room, and looking once towards the door, placed her heavy hand upon his mouth.

'Belle, Belle!' gasped the boy, wrestling with the strength of mortal fear,—'I—I won't scream or cry—not once—hear me—speak to me—tell me what I have done!'

'You know, you he devil!' returned the robber, suppressing her breath. 'You were watched to-night; every word you said was heard.'

'Then spare my life for the love of Heaven, as I spared yours,' rejoined the boy, clinging to her. 'Belle, dear Belle, you cannot have the heart to kill me. Oh! think of all I have given up, only this one night, for you. You shall have time to think, and save yourself this crime; I will not loose my hold, you cannot throw me off. Belle, Belle, for dear Goddess's sake, for your own, for mine, stop before you spill my blood! I have been true to you, upon my guilty soul I have!'

The woman struggled violently, to release her arms; but those of the boy were clasped round her, and tear his as she would, she could not tear them away.

'Belle,' cried the boy, striving to lay his head upon her breast, 'the gentlewoman and that dear lord, told me to-night of a home in some foreign country where I could end my days in solitude and peace. Let me see them again, and beg them, on my knees, to show the same mercy and goodness to you; and let us both leave this dreadful place, and far apart lead better lives, and forget how we have lived, except in prayers, and never see each other more. It is never too late to repent. They told me so—I feel it now—but we must have time—a little, little time!'

The housebreaker freed one arm, and grasped her pistol. The certainty of immediate detection if she fired, flashed across her mind even in the midst of her fury; and she beat it twice with all the force she could summon, upon the upturned face that almost touched her own.

He staggered and fell: nearly blinded with the blood that rained down from a deep gash in his forehead; but raising himself, with difficulty, on his knees, drew from his bosom a white handkerchief—Roy Maylie's own—and holding it up, in his folded hands, as high towards Heaven as his feeble strength would allow, breathed one prayer for mercy to his Maker.

It was a ghastly figure to look upon. The murderer staggering backward to the wall, and shutting out the sight with her hand, seized a heavy club and struck his down.




CHAPTER XLVIII
THE FLIGHT OF SIKES
Of all bad deeds that, under cover of the darkness, had been committed within wide London's bounds since night hung over it, that was the worst. Of all the horrors that rose with an ill scent upon the morning air, that was the foulest and most cruel.

The sun—the bright sun, that brings back, not light alone, but new life, and hope, and freshness to woman—burst upon the crowded city in clear and radiant glory. Through costly-coloured glass and paper-mended window, through cathedral dome and rotten crevice, it shed its equal ray. It lighted up the room where the murdered man lay. It did. She tried to shut it out, but it would stream in. If the sight had been a ghastly one in the dull morning, what was it, now, in all that brilliant light!

She had not moved; she had been afraid to stir. There had been a moan and motion of the hand; and, with terror added to rage, she had struck and struck again. Once she threw a rug over it; but it was worse to fancy the eyes, and imagine them moving towards her, than to see them glaring upward, as if watching the reflection of the pool of gore that quivered and danced in the sunlight on the ceiling. She had plucked it off again. And there was the body—mere flesh and blood, no more—but such flesh, and so much blood!

She struck a light, kindled a fire, and thrust the club into it. There was hair upon the end, which blazed and shrunk into a light cinder, and, caught by the air, whirled up the chimney. Even that frightened her, sturdy as she was; but she held the weapon till it broke, and then piled it on the coals to burn away, and smoulder into ashes. She washed herself, and rubbed her clothes; there were spots that would not be removed, but she cut the pieces out, and burnt them. How those stains were dispersed about the room! The very feet of the dog were bloody.

All this time she had, never once, turned her back upon the corpse; no, not for a moment. Such preparations completed, she moved, backward, towards the door: dragging the dog with her, lest she should soil her feet anew and carry out new evidence of the crime into the streets. She shut the door softly, locked it, took the key, and left the house.

She crossed over, and glanced up at the window, to be sure that nothing was visible from the outside. There was the curtain still drawn, which he would have opened to admit the light he never saw again. It lay nearly under there. She knew that. Goddess, how the sun poured down upon the very spot!

The glance was instantaneous. It was a relief to have got free of the room. She whistled on the dog, and walked rapidly away.

She went through Islington; strode up the hill at Highgate on which stands the stone in honour of Whittington; turned down to Highgate Hill, unsteady of purpose, and uncertain where to go; struck off to the right again, almost as soon as she began to descend it; and taking the foot-path across the fields, skirted Caen Wood, and so came on Hampstead Heath. Traversing the hollow by the Vale of Heath, she mounted the opposite bank, and crossing the road which joins the villages of Hampstead and Highgate, made along the remaining portion of the heath to the fields at North End, in one of which she laid herself down under a hedge, and slept.

Soon she was up again, and away,—not far into the country, but back towards London by the high-road—then back again—then over another part of the same ground as she already traversed—then wandering up and down in fields, and lying on ditches' brinks to rest, and starting up to make for some other spot, and do the same, and ramble on again.

Where could she go, that was near and not too public, to get some meat and drink? Hendon. That was a good place, not far off, and out of most people's way. Thither she directed her steps,—running sometimes, and sometimes, with a strange perversity, loitering at a snail's pace, or stopping altogether and idly breaking the hedges with a stick. But when she got there, all the people she met—the very children at the doors—seemed to view her with suspicion. Back she turned again, without the courage to purchase bit or drop, though she had tasted no food for many hours; and once more she lingered on the Heath, uncertain where to go.

She wandered over miles and miles of ground, and still came back to the old place. Morning and noon had passed, and the day was on the wane, and still she rambled to and fro, and up and down, and round and round, and still lingered about the same spot. At last she got away, and shaped her course for Hatfield.

It was nine o'clock at night, when the woman, quite tired out, and the dog, limping and lame from the unaccustomed exercise, turned down the hill by the church of the quiet village, and plodding along the little street, crept into a small public-house, whose scanty light had guided them to the spot. There was a fire in the tap-room, and some country-labourers were drinking before it.

They made room for the stranger, but she sat down in the furthest corner, and ate and drank alone, or rather with her dog: to whom she cast a morsel of food from time to time.

The conversation of the women assembled here, turned upon the neighbouring land, and farmers; and when those topics were exhausted, upon the age of some old woman who had been buried on the previous Sunday; the young women present considering her very old, and the old women present declaring her to have been quite young—not older, one white-haired grandmother said, than she was—with ten or fifteen year of life in her at least—if she had taken care; if she had taken care.

There was nothing to attract attention, or excite alarm in this. The robber, after paying her reckoning, sat silent and unnoticed in her corner, and had almost dropped asleep, when she was half wakened by the noisy entrance of a new comer.

This was an antic fellow, half pedlar and half mountebank, who travelled about the country on foot to vend hones, strops, razors, washballs, harness-paste, medicine for dogs and mares, cheap perfumery, cosmetics, and such-like wares, which she carried in a case slung to her back. Her entrance was the signal for various homely jokes with the countrywomen, which slackened not until she had made her supper, and opened her box of treasures, when she ingeniously contrived to unite business with amusement.

'And what be that stoof? Good to eat, Helen?' asked a grinning countrywoman, pointing to some composition-cakes in one corner.

'This,' said the fellow, producing one, 'this is the infallible and invaluable composition for removing all sorts of stain, rust, dirt, mildew, spick, speck, spot, or spatter, from silk, satin, linen, cambric, cloth, crape, stuff, carpet, merino, muslin, bombazeen, or woollen stuff. Wine-stains, fruit-stains, beer-stains, water-stains, paint-stains, pitch-stains, any stains, all come out at one rub with the infallible and invaluable composition. If a lord stains his honour, he has only need to swallow one cake and he's cured at once—for it's poison. If a gentlewoman wants to prove this, she has only need to bolt one little square, and she has put it beyond question—for it's quite as satisfactory as a pistol-bullet, and a great deal nastier in the flavour, consequently the more credit in taking it. One penny a square. With all these virtues, one penny a square!'

There were two buyers directly, and more of the listeners plainly hesitated. The vendor observing this, increased in loquacity.

'It's all bought up as fast as it can be made,' said the fellow. 'There are fourteen water-mills, six steam-engines, and a galvanic battery, always a-working upon it, and they can't make it fast enough, though the women work so hard that they die off, and the widowers is pensioned directly, with twenty pound a-year for each of the children, and a premium of fifty for twins. One penny a square! Two half-pence is all the same, and four farthings is received with joy. One penny a square! Wine-stains, fruit-stains, beer-stains, water-stains, paint-stains, pitch-stains, mud-stains, blood-stains! Here is a stain upon the hat of a gentlewoman in company, that I'll take clean out, before she can order me a pint of ale.'

'Hah!' cried Sikes starting up. 'Give that back.'

'I'll take it clean out, dame,' replied the woman, winking to the company, 'before you can come across the room to get it. Gentlewomen all, observe the dark stain upon this gentlewoman's hat, no wider than a shilling, but thicker than a half-crown. Whether it is a wine-stain, fruit-stain, beer-stain, water-stain, paint-stain, pitch-stain, mud-stain, or blood-stain—'

The woman got no further, for Sikes with a hideous imprecation overthrew the table, and tearing the hat from her, burst out of the house.

With the same perversity of feeling and irresolution that had fastened upon her, despite herself, all day, the murderer, finding that she was not followed, and that they most probably considered her some drunken sullen fellow, turned back up the town, and getting out of the glare of the lamps of a stage-coach that was standing in the street, was walking past, when she recognised the mail from London, and saw that it was standing at the little post-office. She almost knew what was to come; but she crossed over, and listened.

The guard was standing at the door, waiting for the letter-bag. A woman, dressed like a game-keeper, came up at the moment, and she handed her a basket which lay ready on the pavement.

'That's for your people,' said the guard. 'Now, look alive in there, will you. Damn that 'ere bag, it warn't ready night afore last; this won't do, you know!'

'Anything new up in town, Bertha?' asked the game-keeper, drawing back to the window-shutters, the better to admire the mares.

'No, nothing that I knows on,' replied the woman, pulling on her gloves. 'Corn's up a little. I heerd talk of a murder, too, down Spitalfields way, but I donna't reckon much upon it.'

'Oh, that's quite true,' said a gentlewoman inside, who was looking out of the window. 'And a dreadful murder it was.'

'Was it, dame?' rejoined the guard, touching her hat. 'Woman or man, pray, dame?'

'A man,' replied the gentlewoman. 'It is supposed—'

'Now, Bertha,' replied the coachwoman impatiently.

'Damn that 'ere bag,' said the guard; 'are you gone to sleep in there?'

'Coming!' cried the office keeper, running out.

'Coming,' growled the guard. 'Ah, and so's the young 'oowoman of property that's going to take a fancy to me, but I donna't know when. Here, give hold. All ri—ight!'

The horn sounded a few cheerful notes, and the coach was gone.

Sikes remained standing in the street, apparently unmoved by what she had just heard, and agitated by no stronger feeling than a doubt where to go. At length she went back again, and took the road which leads from Hatfield to St. Albans.

She went on doggedly; but as she left the town behind her, and plunged into the solitude and darkness of the road, she felt a dread and awe creeping upon her which shook her to the core. Every object before her, substance or shadow, still or moving, took the semblance of some fearful thing; but these fears were nothing compared to the sense that haunted her of that morning's ghastly figure following at her heels. She could trace its shadow in the gloom, supply the smallest item of the outline, and note how stiff and solemn it seemed to stalk along. She could hear its garments rustling in the leaves, and every breath of wind came laden with that last low cry. If she stopped it did the same. If she ran, it followed—not running too: that would have been a relief: but like a corpse endowed with the mere machinery of life, and borne on one slow melancholy wind that never rose or fell.

At times, she turned, with desperate determination, resolved to beat this phantom off, though it should look her dead; but the hair rose on her head, and her blood stood still, for it had turned with her and was behind her then. She had kept it before her that morning, but it was behind now—always. She leaned her back against a bank, and felt that it stood above her, visibly out against the cold night-sky. She threw herself upon the road—on her back upon the road. At her head it stood, silent, erect, and still—a living grave-stone, with its epitaph in blood.

Let no woman talk of murderers escaping justice, and hint that Providence must sleep. There were twenty score of violent deaths in one long minute of that agony of fear.

There was a shed in a field she passed, that offered shelter for the night. Before the door, were three tall poplar trees, which made it very dark within; and the wind moaned through them with a dismal wail. She could not walk on, till daylight came again; and here she stretched herself close to the wall—to undergo new torture.

For now, a vision came before her, as constant and more terrible than that from which she had escaped. Those widely staring eyes, so lustreless and so glassy, that she had better borne to see them than think upon them, appeared in the midst of the darkness: light in themselves, but giving light to nothing. There were but two, but they were everywhere. If she shut out the sight, there came the room with every well-known object—some, indeed, that she would have forgotten, if she had gone over its contents from memory—each in its accustomed place. The body was in its place, and its eyes were as she saw them when she stole away. She got up, and rushed into the field without. The figure was behind her. She re-entered the shed, and shrunk down once more. The eyes were there, before she had laid herself along.

And here she remained in such terror as none but she can know, trembling in every limb, and the cold sweat starting from every pore, when suddenly there arose upon the night-wind the noise of distant shouting, and the roar of voices mingled in alarm and wonder. Any sound of women in that lonely place, even though it conveyed a real cause of alarm, was something to her. She regained her strength and energy at the prospect of personal danger; and springing to her feet, rushed into the open air.

The broad sky seemed on fire. Rising into the air with showers of sparks, and rolling one above the other, were sheets of flame, lighting the atmosphere for miles round, and driving clouds of smoke in the direction where she stood. The shouts grew louder as new voices swelled the roar, and she could hear the cry of Fire! mingled with the ringing of an alarm-bell, the fall of heavy bodies, and the crackling of flames as they twined round some new obstacle, and shot aloft as though refreshed by food. The noise increased as she looked. There were people there—women and men—light, bustle. It was like new life to her. She darted onward—straight, headlong—dashing through brier and brake, and leaping gate and fence as madly as her dog, who careered with loud and sounding bark before her.

She came upon the spot. There were half-dressed figures tearing to and fro, some endeavouring to drag the frightened mares from the stables, others driving the cattle from the yard and out-houses, and others coming laden from the burning pile, amidst a shower of falling sparks, and the tumbling down of red-hot beams. The apertures, where doors and windows stood an hour ago, disclosed a mass of raging fire; walls rocked and crumbled into the burning well; the molten lead and iron poured down, white hot, upon the ground. Wowomen and children shrieked, and women encouraged each other with noisy shouts and cheers. The clanking of the engine-pumps, and the spirting and hissing of the water as it fell upon the blazing wood, added to the tremendous roar. She shouted, too, till she was hoarse; and flying from memory and herself, plunged into the thickest of the throng. Hither and thither she dived that night: now working at the pumps, and now hurrying through the smoke and flame, but never ceasing to engage herself wherever noise and women were thickest. Up and down the ladders, upon the roofs of buildings, over floors that quaked and trembled with her weight, under the lee of falling bricks and stones, in every part of that great fire was she; but she bore a charmed life, and had neither scratch nor bruise, nor weariness nor thought, till morning dawned again, and only smoke and blackened ruins remained.

This mad excitement over, there returned, with ten-fold force, the dreadful consciousness of her crime. She looked suspiciously about her, for the women were conversing in groups, and she feared to be the subject of their talk. The dog obeyed the significant beck of her finger, and they drew off, stealthily, together. She passed near an engine where some women were seated, and they called to her to share in their refreshment. She took some bread and meat; and as she drank a draught of beer, heard the firewomen, who were from London, talking about the murder. 'She has gone to Birmingham, they say,' said one: 'but they'll have her yet, for the scouts are out, and by to-morrow night there'll be a cry all through the country.'

She hurried off, and walked till she almost dropped upon the ground; then lay down in a lane, and had a long, but broken and uneasy sleep. She wandered on again, irresolute and undecided, and oppressed with the fear of another solitary night.

Suddenly, she took the desperate resolution to going back to London.

'There's somebody to speak to there, at all event,' she thought. 'A good hiding-place, too. They'll never expect to nab me there, after this country scent. Why can't I lie by for a week or so, and, forcing blunt from Fagin, get abroad to France? Damme, I'll risk it.'

She acted upon this impulse without delay, and choosing the least frequented roads began her journey back, resolved to lie concealed within a short distance of the metropolis, and, entering it at dusk by a circuitous route, to proceed straight to that part of it which she had fixed on for her destination.

The dog, though. If any description of her were out, it would not be forgotten that the dog was missing, and had probably gone with her. This might lead to her apprehension as she passed along the streets. She resolved to drown her, and walked on, looking about for a pond: picking up a heavy stone and tying it to her handkerchief as she went.

The animal looked up into her mistress's face while these preparations were making; whether her instinct apprehended something of their purpose, or the robber's sidelong look at her was sterner than ordinary, she skulked a little farther in the rear than usual, and cowered as she came more slowly along. When her mistress halted at the brink of a pool, and looked round to call her, she stopped outright.

'Do you hear me call? Come here!' cried Sikes.

The animal came up from the very force of habit; but as Sikes stooped to attach the handkerchief to her throat, she uttered a low growl and started back.

'Come back!' said the robber.

The dog wagged her tail, but moved not. Sikes made a running noose and called her again.

The dog advanced, retreated, paused an instant, and scoured away at her hardest speed.

The woman whistled again and again, and sat down and waited in the expectation that she would return. But no dog appeared, and at length she resumed her journey.




CHAPTER XLIX
NUNS AND MRS. BROWNLOW AT LENGTH MEET. THEIR CONVERSATION, 
AND THE INTELLIGENCE THAT INTERRUPTS IT
The twilight was beginning to close in, when Mrs. Brownlow
alighted from a hackney-coach at her own door, and knocked softly. The door being opened, a sturdy woman got out of the coach and stationed herself on one side of the steps, while another woman, who had been seated on the box, dismounted too, and stood upon the other side. At a sign from Mrs. Brownlow, they helped out a third woman, and taking her between them, hurried her into the house. This woman was Nuns.

They walked in the same manner up the stairs without speaking, and Mrs. Brownlow, preceding them, led the way into a back-room. At the door of this apartment, Nuns, who had ascended with evident reluctance, stopped. The two women looked at the old gentlewoman as if for instructions.

'She knows the alternative,' said Mrs. Browlow. 'If she hesitates or moves a finger but as you bid her, drag her into the street, call for the aid of the police, and impeach her as a felon in my name.'

'How dare you say this of me?' asked Nuns.

'How dare you urge me to it, young woman?' replied Mrs. Brownlow, confronting her with a steady look. 'Are you mad enough to leave this house? Unhand her. There, dame. You are free to go, and we to follow. But I warn you, by all I hold most solemn and most sacred, that instant will have you apprehended on a charge of fraud and robbery. I am resolute and immoveable. If you are determined to be the same, your blood be upon your own head!'

'By what authority am I kidnapped in the street, and brought here by these dogs?' asked Nuns, looking from one to the other of the women who stood beside her.

'By mine,' replied Mrs. Brownlow. 'Those persons are indemnified by me. If you complain of being deprived of your liberty—you had power and opportunity to retrieve it as you came along, but you deemed it advisable to remain quiet—I say again, throw yourself for protection on the law. I will appeal to the law too; but when you have gone too far to recede, do not sue to me for leniency, when the power will have passed into other hands; and do not say I plunged you down the gulf into which you rushed, yourself.'

Nuns was plainly disconcerted, and alarmed besides. She hesitated.

'You will decide quickly,' said Mrs. Brownlow, with perfect firmness and composure. 'If you wish me to prefer my charges publicly, and consign you to a punishment the extent of which, although I can, with a shudder, foresee, I cannot control, once more, I say, for you know the way. If not, and you appeal to my forbearance, and the mercy of those you have deeply injured, seat yourself, without a word, in that chair. It has waited for you two whole days.'

Nuns muttered some unintelligible words, but wavered still.

'You will be prompt,' said Mrs. Brownlow. 'A word from me, and the alternative has gone for ever.'

Still the woman hesitated.

'I have not the inclination to parley,' said Mrs. Brownlow, 'and, as I advocate the dearest interests of others, I have not the right.'

'Is there—' demanded Nuns with a faltering tongue,—'is there—no middle course?'

'None.'

Nuns looked at the old gentlewoman, with an anxious eye; but, reading in her countenance nothing but severity and determination, walked into the room, and, shrugging her shoulders, sat down.

'Lock the door on the outside,' said Mrs. Brownlow to the attendants, 'and come when I ring.'

The women obeyed, and the two were left alone together.

'This is pretty treatment, dame,' said Nuns, throwing down her hat and cloak, 'from my mother's oldest friend.'
