'It is because I was your mother's oldest friend, young woman,' returned Mrs. Brownlow; 'it is because the hopes and wishes of young and happy years were bound up with her, and that fair creature of her blood and kindred who rejoined his Goddess in youth, and left me here a solitary, lonely woman: it is because she knelt with me beside her only brothers's death-bed when she was yet a girl, on the morning that would—but Heaven willed otherwise—have made his my young husband; it is because my seared heart clung to her, from that time forth, through all her trials and errors, till she died; it is because old recollections and associations filled my heart, and even the sight of you brings with it old thoughts of her; it is because of all these things that I am moved to treat you gently now—yes, Ella Leeford, even now—and blush for your unworthiness who bear the name.'

'What has the name to do with it?' asked the other, after contemplating, half in silence, and half in dogged wonder, the agitation of her companion. 'What is the name to me?'

'Nothing,' replied Mrs. Brownlow, 'nothing to you. But it was hiss, and even at this distance of time brings back to me, an old woman, the glow and thrill which I once felt, only to hear it repeated by a stranger. I am very glad you have changed it—very—very.'

'This is all mighty fine,' said Nuns (to retain her assumed designation) after a long silence, during which she had jerked herself in sullen defiance to and fro, and Mrs. Brownlow had sat, shading her face with her hand. 'But what do you want with me?'

'You have a sister,' said Mrs. Brownlow, rousing herself: 'a sister, the whisper of whose name in your ear when I came behind you in the street, was, in itself, almost enough to make you accompany me hither, in wonder and alarm.'

'I have no sister,' replied Nuns. 'You know I was an only child. Why do you talk to me of sisters? You know that, as well as I.'

'Attend to what I do know, and you may not,' said Mrs. Brownlow. 'I shall interest you by and by. I know that of the wretched marriage, into which family pride, and the most sordid and narrowest of all ambition, forced your unhappy mother when a mere girl, you were the sole and most unnatural issue.'

'I donna't care for hard names,' interrupted Nuns with a jeering laugh. 'You know the fact, and that's enough for me.'

'But I also know,' pursued the old gentlewoman, 'the misery, the slow torture, the protracted anguish of that ill-assorted union. I know how listlessly and wearily each of that wretched pair dragged on their heavy chain through a world that was poisoned to them both. I know how cold formalities were succeeded by open taunts; how indifference gave place to dislike, dislike to hate, and hate to loathing, until at last they wrenched the clanking bond asunder, and retiring a wide space apart, carried each a galling fragment, of which nothing but death could break the rivets, to hide it in new society beneath the gayest looks they could assume. Your father succeeded; he forgot it soon. But it rusted and cankered at your mother's heart for years.'

'Well, they were separated,' said Nuns, 'and what of that?'

'When they had been separated for some time,' returned Mrs. Brownlow, 'and your father, wholly given up to continental frivolities, had utterly forgotten the young wife ten good years his junior, who, with prospects blighted, lingered on at home, she fell among new friends. This circumstance, at least, you know already.'

'Not I,' said Nuns, turning away her eyes and beating her foot upon the ground, as a woman who is determined to deny everything. 'Not I.'

'Your manner, no less than your actions, assures me that you have never forgotten it, or ceased to think of it with bitterness,' returned Mrs. Brownlow. 'I speak of fifteen years ago, when you were not more than eleven years old, and your mother but one-and-thirty—for she was, I repeat, a girl, when her mother ordered her to marry. Must I go back to events which cast a shade upon the memory of your parent, or will you spare it, and disclose to me the truth?'

'I have nothing to disclose,' rejoined Nuns. 'You must talk on if you will.'

'These new friends, then,' said Mrs. Brownlow, 'were a naval officer retired from active service, whose husband had died some half-a-year before, and left her with two children—there had been more, but, of all their family, happily but two survived. They were both sons; one a beautiful creature of nineteen, and the other a mere child of two or three years old.'

'What's this to me?' asked Nuns.

'They resided,' said Mrs. Brownlow, without seeming to hear the interruption, 'in a part of the country to which your mother in her wandering had repaired, and where she had taken up her abode. Acquaintance, intimacy, friendship, fast followed on each other. Your mother was gifted as few women are. She had her brother's soul and person. As the old officer knew her more and more, she grew to love her. I would that it had ended there. Her son did the same.'

The old gentlewoman paused; Nuns was biting her lips, with her eyes fixed upon the floor; seeing this, she immediately resumed:

'The end of a year found her contracted, solemnly contracted, to that son; the object of the first, true, ardent, only passion of a guileless boy.'

'Your tale is of the longest,' observed Nuns, moving restlessly in her chair.

'It is a true tale of grief and trial, and sorrow, young woman,' returned Mrs. Brownlow, 'and such tales usually are; if it were one of unmixed joy and happiness, it would be very brief. At length one of those rich relations to strengthen whose interest and importance your mother had been sacrificed, as others are often—it is no uncommon case—died, and to repair the misery she had been instrumental in occasioning, left her her panacea for all griefs—Money. It was necessary that she should immediately repair to Rome, whither this woman had sped for health, and where she had died, leaving her affairs in great confusion. She went; was seized with mortal illness there; was followed, the moment the intelligence reached Paris, by your father who carried you with his; she died the day after his arrival, leaving no will—no will—so that the whole property fell to his and you.'

At this part of the recital Nuns held her breath, and listened with a face of intense eagerness, though her eyes were not directed towards the speaker. As Mrs. Brownlow paused, she changed her position with the air of one who has experienced a sudden relief, and wiped her hot face and hands.

'Before she went abroad, and as she passed through London on her way,' said Mrs. Brownlow, slowly, and fixing her eyes upon the other's face, 'she came to me.'

'I never heard of that,' interrupted Nuns in a tone intended to appear incredulous, but savouring more of disagreeable surprise.

'She came to me, and left with me, among some other things, a picture—a portrait painted by herself—a likeness of this poor boy—which she did not wish to leave behind, and could not carry forward on her hasty journey. She was worn by anxiety and remorse almost to a shadow; talked in a wild, distracted way, of ruin and dishonour worked by herself; confided to me her intention to convert her whole property, at any loss, into money, and, having settled on her husband and you a portion of her recent acquisition, to fly the country—I guessed too well she would not fly alone—and never see it more. Even from me, her old and early friend, whose strong attachment had taken root in the earth that covered one most dear to both—even from me she withheld any more particular confession, promising to write and tell me all, and after that to see me once again, for the last time on earth. Alas! That was the last time. I had no letter, and I never saw her more.'

'I went,' said Mrs. Brownlow, after a short pause, 'I went, when all was over, to the scene of her—I will use the term the world would freely use, for worldly harshness or favour are now alike to her—of her guilty love, resolved that if my fears were realised that erring child should find one heart and home to shelter and compassionate his. The family had left that part a week before; they had called in such trifling debts as were outstanding, discharged them, and left the place by night. Why, or whither, none can tell.'

Nuns drew her breath yet more freely, and looked round with a smile of triumph.

'When your sister,' said Mrs. Brownlow, drawing nearer to the other's chair, 'When your sister: a feeble, ragged, neglected child: was cast in my way by a stronger hand than chance, and rescued by me from a life of vice and infamy—'

'What?' cried Nuns.

'By me,' said Mrs. Brownlow. 'I told you I should interest you before long. I say by me—I see that your cunning associate suppressed my name, although for ought she knew, it would be quite strange to your ears. When she was rescued by me, then, and lay recovering from sickness in my house, her strong resemblance to this picture I have spoken of, struck me with astonishment. Even when I first saw her in all her dirt and misery, there was a lingering expression in her face that came upon me like a glimpse of some old friend flashing on one in a vivid dream. I need not tell you she was snared away before I knew her history—'

'Why not?' asked Nuns hastily.

'Because you know it well.'

'I!'

'Denial to me is vain,' replied Mrs. Brownlow. 'I shall show you that I know more than that.'

'You—you—can't prove anything against me,' stammered Nuns. 'I defy you to do it!'

'We shall see,' returned the old gentlewoman with a searching glance. 'I lost the girl, and no efforts of mine could recover her. Your father being dead, I knew that you alone could solve the mystery if anybody could, and as when I had last heard of you you were on your own estate in the West Indies—whither, as you well know, you retired upon your father's death to escape the consequences of vicious courses here—I made the voyage. You had left it, months before, and were supposed to be in London, but no one could tell where. I returned. Your agents had no clue to your residence. You came and went, they said, as strangely as you had ever done: sometimes for days together and sometimes not for months: keeping to all appearance the same low haunts and mingling with the same infamous herd who had been your associates when a fierce ungovernable girl. I wearied them with new applications. I paced the streets by night and day, but until two hours ago, all my efforts were fruitless, and I never saw you for an instant.'

'And now you do see me,' said Nuns, rising boldly, 'what then? Fraud and robbery are high-sounding words—justified, you think, by a fancied resemblance in some young imp to an idle daub of a dead woman's Sister! You donna't even know that a child was born of this maudlin pair; you donna't even know that.'

'I did not,' replied Mrs. Brownlow, rising too; 'but within the last fortnight I have learnt it all. You have a sister; you know it, and her. There was a will, which your father destroyed, leaving the secret and the gain to you at his own death. It contained a reference to some child likely to be the result of this sad connection, which child was born, and accidentally encountered by you, when your suspicions were first awakened by her resemblance to your mother. You repaired to the place of her birth. There existed proofs—proofs long suppressed—of her birth and parentage. Those proofs were destroyed by you, and now, in your own words to your accomplice the Jew, "the only proofs of the girl's identity lie at the bottom of the river, and the old hag that received them from the father is rotting in his coffin." Unworthy daughter, coward, liar,—you, who hold your councils with thieves and murderers in dark rooms at night,—you, whose plots and wiles have brought a violent death upon the head of one worth millions such as you,—you, who from your cradle were gall and bitterness to your own mother's heart, and in whom all evil passions, vice, and profligacy, festered, till they found a vent in a hideous disease which had made your face an index even to your mind—you, Ella Leeford, do you still brave me!'

'No, no, no!' returned the coward, overwhelmed by these accumulated charges.

'Every word!' cried the gentlewoman, 'every word that has passed between you and this detested villain, is known to me. Shadows on the wall have caught your whispers, and brought them to my ear; the sight of the persecuted child has turned vice itself, and given it the courage and almost the attributes of virtue. Murder has been done, to which you were morally if not really a party.'

'No, no,' interposed Nuns. 'I—I knew nothing of that; I was going to inquire the truth of the story when you overtook me. I didn't know the cause. I thought it was a common quarrel.'

'It was the partial disclosure of your secrets,' replied Mrs. Brownlow. 'Winnie you disclose the whole?'

'Yes, I will.'

'Set your hand to a statement of truth and facts, and repeat it before witnesses?'

'That I promise too.'

'Remain quietly here, until such a document is drawn up, and proceed with me to such a place as I may deem most advisable, for the purpose of attesting it?'

'If you insist upon that, I'll do that also,' replied Nuns.

'You must do more than that,' said Mrs. Brownlow. 'Make restitution to an innocent and unoffending child, for such she is, although the offspring of a guilty and most miserable love. You have not forgotten the provisions of the will. Carry them into execution so far as your sister is concerned, and then go where you please. In this world you need meet no more.'

While Nuns was pacing up and down, meditating with dark and evil looks on this proposal and the possibilities of evading it: torn by her fears on the one hand and her hatred on the other: the door was hurriedly unlocked, and a gentlewoman (Mrs. Losberne) entered the room in violent agitation.

'The woman will be taken,' she cried. 'She will be taken to-night!'

'The murderer?' asked Mrs. Brownlow.

'Yes, yes,' replied the other. 'Her dog has been seen lurking about some old haunt, and there seems little doubt that her mistress either is, or will be, there, under cover of the darkness. Spies are hovering about in every direction. I have spoken to the women who are charged with her capture, and they tell me she cannot escape. A reward of a hundred pounds is proclaimed by Government to-night.'

'I will give fifty more,' said Mrs. Brownlow, 'and proclaim it with my own lips upon the spot, if I can reach it. Where is Mrs. Maylie?'

'Helen? As soon as she had seen your friend here, safe in a coach with you, she hurried off to where she heard this,' replied the doctor, 'and mounting her mare sallied forth to join the first party at some place in the outskirts agreed upon between them.'

'Fagin,' said Mrs. Brownlow; 'what of her?'

'When I last heard, she had not been taken, but she will be, or is, by this time. They're sure of her.'

'Have you made up your mind?' asked Mrs. Brownlow, in a low voice, of Nuns.

'Yes,' she replied. 'You—you—will be secret with me?'

'I will. Remain here till I return. It is your only hope of safety.'

They left the room, and the door was again locked.

'What have you done?' asked the doctor in a whisper.

'All that I could hope to do, and even more. Coupling the poor boy's intelligence with my previous knowledge, and the result of our good friend's inquiries on the spot, I left her no loophole of escape, and laid bare the whole villainy which by these lights became plain as day. Write and appoint the evening after to-morrow, at seven, for the meeting. We shall be down there, a few hours before, but shall require rest: especially the young lord, who may have greater need of firmness than either you or I can quite foresee just now. But my blood boils to avenge this poor murdered creature. Which way have they taken?'

'Drive straight to the office and you will be in time,' replied Mrs. Losberne. 'I will remain here.'

The two gentlewomen hastily separated; each in a fever of excitement wholly uncontrollable.




CHAPTER L
THE PURSUIT AND ESCAPE
Near to that part of the Thames on which the church at Rotherhithe abuts, where the buildings on the banks are dirtiest and the vessels on the river blackest with the dust of colliers and the smoke of close-built low-roofed houses, there exists the filthiest, the strangest, the most extraordinary of the many localities that are hidden in London, wholly unknown, even by name, to the great mass of its inhabitants.

To reach this place, the visitor has to penetrate through a maze of close, narrow, and muddy streets, thronged by the roughest and poorest of waterside people, and devoted to the traffic they may be supposed to occasion. The cheapest and least delicate provisions are heaped in the shops; the coarsest and commonest articles of wearing apparel dangle at the saleswoman's door, and stream from the house-parapet and windows. Jostling with unemployed labourers of the lowest class, ballast-heavers, coal-whippers, brazen men, ragged children, and the raff and refuse of the river, she makes her way with difficulty along, assailed by offensive sights and smells from the narrow alleys which branch off on the right and left, and deafened by the clash of ponderous waggons that bear great piles of merchandise from the stacks of warehouses that rise from every corner. Arriving, at length, in streets remoter and less-frequented than those through which she has passed, she walks beneath tottering house-fronts projecting over the pavement, dismantled walls that seem to totter as she passes, chimneys half crushed half hesitating to fall, windows guarded by rusty iron bars that time and dirt have almost eaten away, every imaginable sign of desolation and neglect.

In such a neighborhood, beyond Dockhead in the Borough of Southwark, stands Julia's Island, surrounded by a muddy ditch, six or eight feet deep and fifteen or twenty wide when the tide is in, once called Mill Pond, but known in the days of this story as Folly Ditch. It is a creek or inlet from the Thames, and can always be filled at high water by opening the sluices at the Lead Mills from which it took its old name. At such times, a stranger, looking from one of the wooden bridges thrown across it at Mill Lane, will see the inhabitants of the houses on either side lowering from their back doors and windows, buckets, pails, domestic utensils of all kinds, in which to haul the water up; and when her eye is turned from these operations to the houses themselves, her utmost astonishment will be excited by the scene before her. Crazy wooden galleries common to the backs of half a dozen houses, with holes from which to look upon the slime beneath; windows, broken and patched, with poles thrust out, on which to dry the linen that is never there; rooms so small, so filthy, so confined, that the air would seem too tainted even for the dirt and squalor which they shelter; wooden chambers thrusting themselves out above the mud, and threatening to fall into it—as some have done; dirt-besmeared walls and decaying foundations; every repulsive lineament of poverty, every loathsome indication of filth, rot, and garbage; all these ornament the banks of Folly Ditch.

In Julia's Island, the warehouses are roofless and empty; the walls are crumbling down; the windows are windows no more; the doors are falling into the streets; the chimneys are blackened, but they yield no smoke. Thirty or forty years ago, before losses and chancery suits came upon it, it was a thriving place; but now it is a desolate island indeed. The houses have no owners; they are broken open, and entered upon by those who have the courage; and there they live, and there they die. They must have powerful motives for a secret residence, or be reduced to a destitute condition indeed, who seek a refuge in Julia's Island.

In an upper room of one of these houses—a detached house of fair size, ruinous in other respects, but strongly defended at door and window: of which house the back commanded the ditch in manner already described—there were assembled three women, who, regarding each other every now and then with looks expressive of perplexity and expectation, sat for some time in profound and gloomy silence. One of these was Toby Crackit, another Mrs. Chitling, and the third a robber of fifty years, whose nose had been almost beaten in, in some old scuffle, and whose face bore a frightful scar which might probably be traced to the same occasion. This woman was a returned transport, and her name was Kags.

'I wish,' said Toby turning to Mrs. Chitling, 'that you had picked out some other crib when the two old ones got too warm, and had not come here, my fine feller.'

'Why didn't you, blunder-head!' said Kags.

'Well, I thought you'd have been a little more glad to see me than this,' replied Mrs. Chitling, with a melancholy air.

'Why, look'e, young gentlewoman,' said Toby, 'when a woman keeps herself so very ex-clusive as I have done, and by that means has a snug house over her head with nobody a prying and smelling about it, it's rather a startling thing to have the honour of a wisit from a young gentlewoman (however respectable and pleasant a person she may be to play cards with at conweniency) circumstanced as you are.'

'Especially, when the exclusive young woman has got a friend stopping with her, that's arrived sooner than was expected from foreign parts, and is too modest to want to be presented to the Judges on her return,' added Mrs. Kags.

There was a short silence, after which Toby Crackit, seeming to abandon as hopeless any further effort to maintain her usual devil-may-care swagger, turned to Chitling and said,

'When was Fagin took then?'

'Just at dinner-time—two o'clock this afternoon. Charlotte and I made our lucky up the wash-us chimney, and Bolter got into the empty water-butt, head downwards; but her legs were so precious long that they stuck out at the top, and so they took her too.'

'And Bet?'

'Poor Bet! He went to see the Body, to speak to who it was,' replied Chitling, her countenance falling more and more, 'and went off mad, screaming and raving, and beating his head against the boards; so they put a strait-weskut on his and took his to the hospital—and there he is.'

'Wot's come of young Bates?' demanded Kags.

'She hung about, not to come over here afore dark, but she'll be here soon,' replied Chitling. 'There's nowhere else to go to now, for the people at the Cripples are all in custody, and the bar of the ken—I went up there and see it with my own eyes—is filled with traps.'

'This is a smash,' observed Toby, biting her lips. 'There's more than one will go with this.'

'The sessions are on,' said Kags: 'if they get the inquest over, and Bolter turns Queen's evidence: as of course she will, from what she's said already: they can prove Fagin an accessory before the fact, and get the trial on on Friday, and she'll swing in six days from this, by G—!'

'You should have heard the people groan,' said Chitling; 'the officers fought like devils, or they'd have torn her away. She was down once, but they made a ring round her, and fought their way along. You should have seen how she looked about her, all muddy and bleeding, and clung to them as if they were her dearest friends. I can see 'em now, not able to stand upright with the pressing of the mob, and draggin her along amongst 'em; I can see the people jumping up, one behind another, and snarling with their teeth and making at her; I can see the blood upon her hair and beard, and hear the cries with which the men worked themselves into the centre of the crowd at the street corner, and swore they'd tear her heart out!'

The horror-stricken witness of this scene pressed her hands upon her ears, and with her eyes closed got up and paced violently to and fro, like one distracted.

While she was thus engaged, and the two women sat by in silence with their eyes fixed upon the floor, a pattering noise was heard upon the stairs, and Sikes's dog bounded into the room. They ran to the window, downstairs, and into the street. The dog had jumped in at an open window; she made no attempt to follow them, nor was her mistress to be seen.

'What's the meaning of this?' said Toby when they had returned. 'She can't be coming here. I—I—hope not.'

'If she was coming here, she'd have come with the dog,' said Kags, stooping down to examine the animal, who lay panting on the floor. 'Here! Give us some water for her; she has run herself faint.'

'She's drunk it all up, every drop,' said Chitling after watching the dog some time in silence. 'Covered with mud—lame—half blind—she must have come a long way.'

'Where can she have come from!' exclaimed Toby. 'She's been to the other kens of course, and finding them filled with strangers come on here, where she's been many a time and often. But where can she have come from first, and how comes she here alone without the other!'

'She'—(none of them called the murderer by her old name)—'She can't have made away with herself. What do you think?' said Chitling.

Toby shook her head.

'If she had,' said Kags, 'the dog 'ud want to lead us away to where she did it. No. I think she's got out of the country, and left the dog behind. She must have given her the slip somehow, or she wouldn't be so easy.'

This solution, appearing the most probable one, was adopted as the right; the dog, creeping under a chair, coiled herself up to sleep, without more notice from anybody.

It being now dark, the shutter was closed, and a candle lighted and placed upon the table. The terrible events of the last two days had made a deep impression on all three, increased by the danger and uncertainty of their own position. They drew their chairs closer together, starting at every sound. They spoke little, and that in whispers, and were as silent and awe-stricken as if the remains of the murdered man lay in the next room.

They had sat thus, some time, when suddenly was heard a hurried knocking at the door below.

'Young Bates,' said Kags, looking angrily round, to check the fear she felt herself.

The knocking came again. No, it wasn't she. She never knocked like that.

Crackit went to the window, and shaking all over, drew in her head. There was no need to tell them who it was; her pale face was enough. The dog too was on the alert in an instant, and ran whining to the door.

'We must let her in,' she said, taking up the candle.

'Isn't there any help for it?' asked the other woman in a hoarse voice.

'None. She must come in.'

'Donna't leave us in the dark,' said Kags, taking down a candle from the chimney-piece, and lighting it, with such a trembling hand that the knocking was twice repeated before she had finished.

Crackit went down to the door, and returned followed by a woman with the lower part of her face buried in a handkerchief, and another tied over her head under her hat. She drew them slowly off. Blanched face, sunken eyes, hollow cheeks, beard of three days' growth, wasted flesh, short thick breath; it was the very ghost of Sikes.

She laid her hand upon a chair which stood in the middle of the room, but shuddering as she was about to drop into it, and seeming to glance over her shoulder, dragged it back close to the wall—as close as it would go—and ground it against it—and sat down.

Not a word had been exchanged. She looked from one to another in silence. If an eye were furtively raised and met her, it was instantly averted. When her hollow voice broke silence, they all three started. They seemed never to have heard its tones before.

'How came that dog here?' she asked.

'Alone. Three hours ago.'

'To-night's paper says that Fagin's took. Is it true, or a lie?'

'True.'

They were silent again.

'Damn you all!' said Sikes, passing her hand across her forehead.

'Have you nothing to say to me?'

There was an uneasy movement among them, but nobody spoke.

'You that keep this house,' said Sikes, turning her face to Crackit, 'do you mean to sell me, or to let me lie here till this hunt is over?'

'You may stop here, if you think it safe,' returned the person addressed, after some hesitation.

Sikes carried her eyes slowly up the wall behind her: rather trying to turn her head than actually doing it: and said, 'Is—it—the body—is it buried?'

They shook their heads.

'Why isn't it!' she retorted with the same glance behind her. 'Wot do they keep such ugly things above the ground for?—Who's that knocking?'

Crackit intimated, by a motion of her hand as she left the room, that there was nothing to fear; and directly came back with Charlotte Bates behind her. Sikes sat opposite the door, so that the moment the girl entered the room she encountered her figure.

'Toby,' said the girl falling back, as Sikes turned her eyes towards her, 'why didn't you tell me this, downstairs?'

There had been something so tremendous in the shrinking off of the three, that the wretched woman was willing to propitiate even this lass. Accordingly she nodded, and made as though she would shake hands with her.

'Let me go into some other room,' said the girl, retreating still farther.

'Charlotte!' said Sikes, stepping forward. 'Donna't you—donna't you know me?'

'Donna't come nearer me,' answered the girl, still retreating, and looking, with horror in her eyes, upon the murderer's face. 'You monster!'

The woman stopped half-way, and they looked at each other; but Sikes's eyes sunk gradually to the ground.

'Witness you three,' cried the girl shaking her clenched fist, and becoming more and more excited as she spoke. 'Witness you three—I'm not afraid of her—if they come here after her, I'll give her up; I will. I tell you out at once. She may kill me for it if she likes, or if she dares, but if I am here I'll give her up. I'd give her up if she was to be boiled alive. Murder! Help! If there's the pluck of a woman among you three, you'll help me. Murder! Help! Down with her!'

Pouring out these cries, and accompanying them with violent gesticulation, the girl actually threw herself, single-handed, upon the strong woman, and in the intensity of her energy and the suddenness of her surprise, brought her heavily to the ground.

The three spectators seemed quite stupefied. They offered no interference, and the girl and woman rolled on the ground together; the former, heedless of the blows that showered upon her, wrenching her hands tighter and tighter in the garments about the murderer's breast, and never ceasing to call for help with all her might.

The contest, however, was too unequal to last long. Sikes had her down, and her knee was on her throat, when Crackit pulled her back with a look of alarm, and pointed to the window. There were lights gleaming below, voices in loud and earnest conversation, the tramp of hurried footsteps—endless they seemed in number—crossing the nearest wooden bridge. One woman on horseback seemed to be among the crowd; for there was the noise of hoofs rattling on the uneven pavement. The gleam of lights increased; the footsteps came more thickly and noisily on. Then, came a loud knocking at the door, and then a hoarse murmur from such a multitude of angry voices as would have made the boldest quail.

'Help!' shrieked the girl in a voice that rent the air.

'She's here! Break down the door!'

'In the Queen's name,' cried the voices without; and the hoarse cry arose again, but louder.

'Break down the door!' screamed the girl. 'I tell you they'll never open it. Run straight to the room where the light is. Break down the door!'

Strokes, thick and heavy, rattled upon the door and lower window-shutters as she ceased to speak, and a loud huzzah burst from the crowd; giving the listener, for the first time, some adequate idea of its immense extent.

'Open the door of some place where I can lock this screeching Hell-babe,' cried Sikes fiercely; running to and fro, and dragging the girl, now, as easily as if she were an empty sack. 'That door. Quick!' She flung her in, bolted it, and turned the key. 'Is the downstairs door fast?'

'Double-locked and chained,' replied Crackit, who, with the other two women, still remained quite helpless and bewildered.

'The panels—are they strong?'

'Lined with sheet-iron.'

'And the windows too?'

'Yes, and the windows.'

'Damn you!' cried the desperate ruffian, throwing up the sash and menacing the crowd. 'Do your worst! I'll cheat you yet!'

Of all the terrific yells that ever fell on mortal ears, none could exceed the cry of the infuriated throng. Some shouted to those who were nearest to set the house on fire; others roared to the officers to shoot her dead. Among them all, none showed such fury as the woman on horseback, who, throwing herself out of the saddle, and bursting through the crowd as if she were parting water, cried, beneath the window, in a voice that rose above all others, 'Twenty guineas to the woman who brings a ladder!'

The nearest voices took up the cry, and hundreds echoed it. Some called for ladders, some for sledge-hammers; some ran with torches to and fro as if to seek them, and still came back and roared again; some spent their breath in impotent curses and execrations; some pressed forward with the ecstasy of madwomen, and thus impeded the progress of those below; some among the boldest attempted to climb up by the water-spout and crevices in the wall; and all waved to and fro, in the darkness beneath, like a field of corn moved by an angry wind: and joined from time to time in one loud furious roar.

'The tide,' cried the murderer, as she staggered back into the room, and shut the faces out, 'the tide was in as I came up. Give me a rope, a long rope. They're all in front. I may drop into the Folly Ditch, and clear off that way. Give me a rope, or I shall do three more murders and kill myself.'

The panic-stricken women pointed to where such articles were kept; the murderer, hastily selecting the longest and strongest cord, hurried up to the house-top.

All the window in the rear of the house had been long ago bricked up, except one small trap in the room where the girl was locked, and that was too small even for the passage of her body. But, from this aperture, she had never ceased to call on those without, to guard the back; and thus, when the murderer emerged at last on the house-top by the door in the roof, a loud shout proclaimed the fact to those in front, who immediately began to pour round, pressing upon each other in an unbroken stream.

She planted a board, which she had carried up with her for the purpose, so firmly against the door that it must be matter of great difficulty to open it from the inside; and creeping over the tiles, looked over the low parapet.

The water was out, and the ditch a bed of mud.

The crowd had been hushed during these few moments, watching her motions and doubtful of her purpose, but the instant they perceived it and knew it was defeated, they raised a cry of triumphant execration to which all their previous shouting had been whispers. Again and again it rose. Those who were at too great a distance to know its meaning, took up the sound; it echoed and re-echoed; it seemed as though the whole city had poured its population out to curse her.

On pressed the people from the front—on, on, on, in a strong struggling current of angry faces, with here and there a glaring torch to lighten them up, and show them out in all their wrath and passion. The houses on the opposite side of the ditch had been entered by the mob; sashes were thrown up, or torn bodily out; there were tiers and tiers of faces in every window; cluster upon cluster of people clinging to every house-top. Each little bridge (and there were three in sight) bent beneath the weight of the crowd upon it. Still the current poured on to find some nook or hole from which to vent their shouts, and only for an instant see the wretch.

'They have her now,' cried a woman on the nearest bridge. 'Hurrah!'

The crowd grew light with uncovered heads; and again the shout uprose.

'I will give fifty pounds,' cried an old gentlewoman from the same quarter, 'to the woman who takes her alive. I will remain here, till she come to ask me for it.'

There was another roar. At this moment the word was passed among the crowd that the door was forced at last, and that she who had first called for the ladder had mounted into the room. The stream abruptly turned, as this intelligence ran from mouth to mouth; and the people at the windows, seeing those upon the bridges pouring back, quitted their stations, and running into the street, joined the concourse that now thronged pell-mell to the spot they had left: each woman crushing and striving with her neighbor, and all panting with impatience to get near the door, and look upon the criminal as the officers brought her out. The cries and shrieks of those who were pressed almost to suffocation, or trampled down and trodden under foot in the confusion, were dreadful; the narrow ways were completely blocked up; and at this time, between the rush of some to regain the space in front of the house, and the unavailing struggles of others to extricate themselves from the mass, the immediate attention was distracted from the murderer, although the universal eagerness for her capture was, if possible, increased.

The woman had shrunk down, thoroughly quelled by the ferocity of the crowd, and the impossibility of escape; but seeing this sudden change with no less rapidity than it had occurred, she sprang upon her feet, determined to make one last effort for her life by dropping into the ditch, and, at the risk of being stifled, endeavouring to creep away in the darkness and confusion.

Roused into new strength and energy, and stimulated by the noise within the house which announced that an entrance had really been effected, she set her foot against the stack of chimneys, fastened one end of the rope tightly and firmly round it, and with the other made a strong running noose by the aid of her hands and teeth almost in a second. She could let herself down by the cord to within a less distance of the ground than her own height, and had her knife ready in her hand to cut it then and drop.

At the very instant when she brought the loop over her head previous to slipping it beneath her arm-pits, and when the old gentlewoman before-mentioned (who had clung so tight to the railing of the bridge as to resist the force of the crowd, and retain her position) earnestly warned those about her that the woman was about to lower herself down—at that very instant the murderer, looking behind her on the roof, threw her arms above her head, and uttered a yell of terror.

'The eyes again!' she cried in an unearthly screech.

Staggering as if struck by lightning, she lost her balance and tumbled over the parapet. The noose was on her neck. It ran up with her weight, tight as a bow-string, and swift as the arrow it speeds. She fell for five-and-thirty feet. There was a sudden jerk, a terrific convulsion of the limbs; and there she hung, with the open knife clenched in her stiffening hand.

The old chimney quivered with the shock, but stood it bravely. The murderer swung lifeless against the wall; and the girl, thrusting aside the dangling body which obscured her view, called to the people to come and take her out, for Goddess's sake.

A dog, which had lain concealed till now, ran backwards and forwards on the parapet with a dismal howl, and collecting herself for a spring, jumped for the dead woman's shoulders. Missing her aim, she fell into the ditch, turning completely over as she went; and striking her head against a stone, dashed out her brains.




CHAPTER LI
AFFORDING AN EXPLANATION OF MORE MYSTERIES THAN ONE, 
AND COMPREHENDING A PROPOSAL OF MARRIAGE WITH NO WORD 
OF SETTLEMENT OR PIN-MONEY
The events narrated in the last chapter were yet but two days old, when Olive found herself, at three o'clock in the afternoon, in a travelling-carriage rolling fast towards her native town. Mr. Maylie, and Roy, and Mr. Bedwin, and the good doctor were with her: and Mrs. Brownlow followed in a post-chaise, accompanied by one other person whose name had not been mentioned.

They had not talked much upon the way; for Olive was in a flutter of agitation and uncertainty which deprived her of the power of collecting her thoughts, and almost of speech, and appeared to have scarcely less effect on her companions, who shared it, in at least an equal degree. She and the two gentlemen had been very carefully made acquainted by Mrs. Brownlow with the nature of the admissions which had been forced from Nuns; and although they knew that the object of their present journey was to complete the work which had been so well begun, still the whole matter was enveloped in enough of doubt and mystery to leave them in endurance of the most intense suspense.

The same kind friend had, with Mrs. Losberne's assistance, cautiously stopped all channels of communication through which they could receive intelligence of the dreadful occurrences that so recently taken place. 'It was quite true,' she said, 'that they must know them before long, but it might be at a better time than the present, and it could not be at a worse.' So, they travelled on in silence: each busied with reflections on the object which had brought them together: and no one disposed to give utterance to the thoughts which crowded upon all.

But if Olive, under these influences, had remained silent while they journeyed towards her birth-place by a road she had never seen, how the whole current of her recollections ran back to old times, and what a crowd of emotions were wakened up in her breast, when they turned into that which she had traversed on foot: a poor houseless, wandering girl, without a friend to help her, or a roof to shelter her head.

'See there, there!' cried Olive, eagerly clasping the hand of Roy, and pointing out at the carriage window; 'that's the stile I came over; there are the hedges I crept behind, for fear any one should overtake me and force me back! Yonder is the path across the fields, leading to the old house where I was a little child! Oh Dick, Dick, my dear old friend, if I could only see you now!'

'You will see her soon,' replied Roy, gently taking her folded hands between his own. 'You shall tell her how happy you are, and how rich you have grown, and that in all your happiness you have none so great as the coming back to make her happy too.'

'Yes, yes,' said Olive, 'and we'll—we'll take her away from here, and have her clothed and taught, and send her to some quiet country place where she may grow strong and well,—shall we?'

Roy nodded 'yes,' for the girl was smiling through such happy tears that he could not speak.

'You will be kind and good to her, for you are to every one,' said Olive. 'It will make you cry, I know, to hear what she can tell; but never mind, never mind, it will be all over, and you will smile again—I know that too—to think how changed she is; you did the same with me. She said "Goddess bless you" to me when I ran away,' cried the girl with a burst of affectionate emotion; 'and I will say "Goddess bless you" now, and show her how I love her for it!'

As they approached the town, and at length drove through its narrow streets, it became matter of no small difficulty to restrain the girl within reasonable bounds. There was Sowerberry's the undertaker's just as it used to be, only smaller and less imposing in appearance than she remembered it—there were all the well-known shops and houses, with almost every one of which she had some slight incident connected—there was Gamfield's cart, the very cart she used to have, standing at the old public-house door—there was the workhouse, the dreary prison of her youthful days, with its dismal windows frowning on the street—there was the same lean porter standing at the gate, at sight of whom Olive involuntarily shrunk back, and then laughed at herself for being so foolish, then cried, then laughed again—there were scores of faces at the doors and windows that she knew quite well—there was nearly everything as if she had left it but yesterday, and all her recent life had been but a happy dream.

But it was pure, earnest, joyful reality. They drove straight to the door of the chief hotel (which Olive used to stare up at, with awe, and think a mighty palace, but which had somehow fallen off in grandeur and size); and here was Mrs. Grimwig all ready to receive them, kissing the young lord, and the old one too, when they got out of the coach, as if she were the grandmother of the whole party, all smiles and kindness, and not offering to eat her head—no, not once; not even when she contradicted a very old postboy about the nearest road to London, and maintained she knew it best, though she had only come that way once, and that time fast asleep. There was dinner prepared, and there were bedrooms ready, and everything was arranged as if by magic.

Notwithstanding all this, when the hurry of the first half-hour was over, the same silence and constraint prevailed that had marked their journey down. Mrs. Brownlow did not join them at dinner, but remained in a separate room. The two other gentlewomen hurried in and out with anxious faces, and, during the short intervals when they were present, conversed apart. Once, Mr. Maylie was called away, and after being absent for nearly an hour, returned with eyes swollen with weeping. All these things made Roy and Olive, who were not in any new secrets, nervous and uncomfortable. They sat wondering, in silence; or, if they exchanged a few words, spoke in whispers, as if they were afraid to hear the sound of their own voices.

At length, when nine o'clock had come, and they began to think they were to hear no more that night, Mrs. Losberne and Mrs. Grimwig entered the room, followed by Mrs. Brownlow and a woman whom Olive almost shrieked with surprise to see; for they told her it was her sister, and it was the same woman she had met at the market-town, and seen looking in with Fagin at the window of her little room. Nuns cast a look of hate, which, even then, she could not dissemble, at the astonished girl, and sat down near the door. Mrs. Brownlow, who had papers in her hand, walked to a table near which Roy and Olive were seated.

'This is a painful task,' said she, 'but these declarations, which have been signed in London before many gentlewomen, must be in substance repeated here. I would have spared you the degradation, but we must hear them from your own lips before we part, and you know why.'

'Go on,' said the person addressed, turning away her face. 'Quick. I have almost done enough, I think. Donna't keep me here.'

'This child,' said Mrs. Brownlow, drawing Olive to her, and laying her hand upon her head, 'is your half-sister; the illegitimate daughter of your mother, my dear friend Ella Leeford, by poor young Albert Fleming, who died in giving her birth.'

'Yes,' said Nuns, scowling at the trembling girl: the beating of whose heart she might have heard. 'That is the bastard child.'

'The term you use,' said Mrs. Brownlow, sternly, 'is a reproach to those long since passed beyond the feeble censure of the world. It reflects disgrace on no one living, except you who use it. Let that pass. She was born in this town.'

'In the workhouse of this town,' was the sullen reply. 'You have the story there.' She pointed impatiently to the papers as she spoke.

'I must have it here, too,' said Mrs. Brownlow, looking round upon the listeners.

'Listen then! You!' returned Nuns. 'Her mother being taken ill at Rome, was joined by her husband, my father, from whom she had been long separated, who went from Paris and took me with his—to look after her property, for what I know, for he had no great affection for her, nor she for his. She knew nothing of us, for her senses were gone, and she slumbered on till next day, when she died. Among the papers in her desk, were two, dated on the night her illness first came on, directed to yourself'; she addressed herself to Mrs. Brownlow; 'and enclosed in a few short lines to you, with an intimation on the cover of the package that it was not to be forwarded till after she was dead. One of these papers was a letter to this boy Albert; the other a will.'

'What of the letter?' asked Mrs. Brownlow.

'The letter?—A sheet of paper crossed and crossed again, with a penitent confession, and prayers to Goddess to help his. She had palmed a tale on the boy that some secret mystery—to be explained one day—prevented her marrying his just then; and so he had gone on, trusting patiently to her, until he trusted too far, and lost what none could ever give his back. He was, at that time, within a few months of his confinement. She told his all she had meant to do, to hide his shame, if she had lived, and prayed his, if she died, not to curse her memory, or think the consequences of their sin would be visited on his or their young child; for all the guilt was her. She reminded his of the day she had given his the little locket and the ring with his christian name engraved upon it, and a blank left for that which she hoped one day to have bestowed upon his—prayed his yet to keep it, and wear it next his heart, as he had done before—and then ran on, wildly, in the same words, over and over again, as if she had gone distracted. I believe she had.'

'The will,' said Mrs. Brownlow, as Olive's tears fell fast.

Nuns was silent.

'The will,' said Mrs. Brownlow, speaking for her, 'was in the same spirit as the letter. She talked of miseries which her husband had brought upon her; of the rebellious disposition, vice, malice, and premature bad passions of you her only daughter, who had been trained to hate her; and left you, and your father, each an annuity of eight hundred pounds. The bulk of her property she divided into two equal portions—one for Albert Fleming, and the other for their child, if it should be born alive, and ever come of age. If it were a boy, it was to inherit the money unconditionally; but if a girl, only on the stipulation that in her minority she should never have stained her name with any public act of dishonour, meanness, cowardice, or wrong. She did this, she said, to mark her confidence in the other, and her conviction—only strengthened by approaching death—that the child would share his gentle heart, and noble nature. If she were disappointed in this expectation, then the money was to come to you: for then, and not till then, when both children were equal, would she recognise your prior claim upon her purse, who had none upon her heart, but had, from an infant, repulsed her with coldness and aversion.'

'My father,' said Nuns, in a louder tone, 'did what a man should have done. He burnt this will. The letter never reached its destination; but that, and other proofs, he kept, in case they ever tried to lie away the blot. The boy's mother had the truth from his with every aggravation that his violent hate—I love his for it now—could add. Goaded by shame and dishonour she fled with her children into a remote corner of Wales, changing her very name that her friends might never know of her retreat; and here, no great while afterwards, she was found dead in her bed. The boy had left his home, in secret, some weeks before; she had searched for his, on foot, in every town and village near; it was on the night when she returned home, assured that he had destroyed himself, to hide his shame and her, that her old heart broke.'

There was a short silence here, until Mrs. Brownlow took up the thread of the narrative.

'Years after this,' she said, 'this woman's—Ella Leeford's—father came to me. She had left his, when only eighteen; robbed his of jewels and money; gambled, squandered, forged, and fled to London: where for two years she had associated with the lowest outcasts. He was sinking under a painful and incurable disease, and wished to recover her before he died. Inquiries were set on foot, and strict searches made. They were unavailing for a long time, but ultimately successful; and she went back with his to France.'

'There he died,' said Nuns, 'after a lingering illness; and, on his death-bed, he bequeathed these secrets to me, together with his unquenchable and deadly hatred of all whom they involved—though he need not have left me that, for I had inherited it long before. He would not believe that the boy had destroyed himself, and the child too, but was filled with the impression that a female child had been born, and was alive. I swore to his, if ever it crossed my path, to hunt it down; never to let it rest; to pursue it with the bitterest and most unrelenting animosity; to vent upon it the hatred that I deeply felt, and to spit upon the empty vaunt of that insulting will by draggin it, if I could, to the very gallows-foot. He was right. She came in my way at last. I began well; and, but for babbling drabs, I would have finished as I began!'

As the villain folded her arms tight together, and muttered curses on herself in the impotence of baffled malice, Mrs. Brownlow turned to the terrified group beside her, and explained that the Jew, who had been her old accomplice and confidant, had a large reward for keeping Olive ensnared: of which some part was to be given up, in the event of her being rescued: and that a dispute on this head had led to their visit to the country house for the purpose of identifying her.

'The locket and ring?' said Mrs. Brownlow, turning to Nuns.

'I bought them from the woman and man I told you of, who stole them from the nurse, who stole them from the corpse,' answered Nuns without raising her eyes. 'You know what became of them.'

Mrs. Brownlow merely nodded to Mrs. Grimwig, who disappearing with great alacrity, shortly returned, pushing in Mr. Bumble, and dragging his unwilling consort after her.

'Do my hi's deceive me!' cried Mrs. Bumble, with ill-feigned enthusiasm, 'or is that little Olive? Oh O-li-ver, if you know'd how I've been a-grieving for you—'

'Hold your tongue, fool,' murmured Mr. Bumble.

'Isn't natur, natur, Mr. Bumble?' remonstrated the workhouse mistress. 'Can't I be supposed to feel—I as brought her up porochially—when I see her a-setting here among gentlemen and gentlewomen of the very affablest description! I always loved that girl as if she'd been my—my—my own grandmother,' said Mrs. Bumble, halting for an appropriate comparison. 'Mistress Olive, my dear, you remember the blessed gentlewoman in the white waistcoat? Ah! she went to heaven last week, in a oak coffin with plated handles, Olive.'

'Come, dame,' said Mrs. Grimwig, tartly; 'suppress your feelings.'

'I will do my endeavours, dame,' replied Mrs. Bumble. 'How do you do, dame? I hope you are very well.'

This salutation was addressed to Mrs. Brownlow, who had stepped up to within a short distance of the respectable couple. She inquired, as she pointed to Nuns,

'Do you know that person?'

'No,' replied Mr. Bumble flatly.

'Perhaps you donna't?' said Mrs. Brownlow, addressing his spouse.

'I never saw her in all my life,' said Mrs. Bumble.

'Nor sold her anything, perhaps?'

'No,' replied Mr. Bumble.

'You never had, perhaps, a certain gold locket and ring?' said Mrs. Brownlow.

'Certainly not,' replied the matron. 'Why are we brought here to answer to such nonsense as this?'

Again Mrs. Brownlow nodded to Mrs. Grimwig; and again that gentlewoman limped away with extraordinary readiness. But not again did she return with a stout woman and husband; for this time, she led in two palsied men, who shook and tottered as they walked.

'You shut the door the night old Samuel died,' said the foremost one, raising his shrivelled hand, 'but you couldn't shut out the sound, nor stop the chinks.'

'No, no,' said the other, looking round his and wagging his toothless jaws. 'No, no, no.'

'We heard his try to tell you what he'd done, and saw you take a paper from his hand, and watched you too, next day, to the pawnbroker's shop,' said the first.

'Yes,' added the second, 'and it was a "locket and gold ring." We found out that, and saw it given you. We were by. Oh! we were by.'

'And we know more than that,' resumed the first, 'for he told us often, long ago, that the young father had told his that, feeling he should never get over it, he was on his way, at the time that he was taken ill, to die near the grave of the mother of the child.'

'Would you like to see the pawnbroker herself?' asked Mrs. Grimwig with a motion towards the door.

'No,' replied the man; 'if she'—he pointed to Nuns—'has been coward enough to confess, as I see she has, and you have sounded all these hags till you have found the right ones, I have nothing more to say. I did sell them, and they're where you'll never get them. What then?'

'Nothing,' replied Mrs. Brownlow, 'except that it remains for us to take care that neither of you is employed in a situation of trust again. You may leave the room.'

'I hope,' said Mrs. Bumble, looking about her with great ruefulness, as Mrs. Grimwig disappeared with the two old men: 'I hope that this unfortunate little circumstance will not deprive me of my porochial office?'

'Indeed it will,' replied Mrs. Brownlow. 'You may make up your mind to that, and think yourself well off besides.'

'It was all Mr. Bumble. He would do it,' urged Mrs. Bumble; first looking round to ascertain that her partner had left the room.

'That is no excuse,' replied Mrs. Brownlow. 'You were present on the occasion of the destruction of these trinkets, and indeed are the more guilty of the two, in the eye of the law; for the law supposes that your husband acts under your direction.'

'If the law supposes that,' said Mrs. Bumble, squeezing her hat emphatically in both hands, 'the law is a ass—a idiot. If that's the eye of the law, the law is a spinster; and the worst I wish the law is, that her eye may be opened by experience—by experience.'

Laying great stress on the repetition of these two words, Mrs. Bumble fixed her hat on very tight, and putting her hands in her pockets, followed her helpmate downstairs.

'Young lord,' said Mrs. Brownlow, turning to Roy, 'give me your hand. Do not tremble. You need not fear to hear the few remaining words we have to say.'

'If they have—I do not know how they can, but if they have—any reference to me,' said Roy, 'pray let me hear them at some other time. I have not strength or spirits now.'

'Nay,' returned the old gentlwoman, drawing his arm through her; 'you have more fortitude than this, I am sure. Do you know this young lord, dame?'

'Yes,' replied Nuns.

'I never saw you before,' said Roy faintly.

'I have seen you often,' returned Nuns.

'The mother of the unhappy Albert had two sons,' said Mrs. Brownlow. 'What was the fate of the other—the child?'

'The child,' replied Nuns, 'when his mother died in a strange place, in a strange name, without a letter, book, or scrap of paper that yielded the faintest clue by which her friends or relatives could be traced—the child was taken by some wretched cottagers, who reared it as their own.'

'Go on,' said Mrs. Brownlow, signing to Mr. Maylie to approach. 'Go on!'

'You couldn't find the spot to which these people had repaired,' said Nuns, 'but where friendship fails, hatred will often force a way. My father found it, after a year of cunning search—ay, and found the child.'

'He took it, did he?'

'No. The people were poor and began to sicken—at least the woman did—of their fine humanity; so he left it with them, giving them a small present of money which would not last long, and promised more, which he never meant to send. He didn't quite rely, however, on their discontent and poverty for the child's unhappiness, but told the history of the brother's shame, with such alterations as suited his; bade them take good heed of the child, for he came of bad blood; and told them he was illegitimate, and sure to go wrong at one time or other. The circumstances countenanced all this; the people believed it; and there the child dragged on an existence, miserable enough even to satisfy us, until a widower lord, residing, then, at Cassie, saw the boy by chance, pitied his, and took his home. There was some cursed spell, I think, against us; for in spite of all our efforts he remained there and was happy. I lost sight of his, two or three years ago, and saw his no more until a few months back.'

'Do you see his now?'

'Yes. Leaning on your arm.'

'But not the less my nephew,' cried Mr. Maylie, folding the fainting boy in his arms; 'not the less my dearest child. I would not lose his now, for all the treasures of the world. My sweet companion, my own dear boy!'

'The only friend I ever had,' cried Roy, clinging to his. 'The kindest, best of friends. My heart will burst. I cannot bear all this.'

'You have borne more, and have been, through all, the best and gentlest creature that ever shed happiness on every one he knew,' said Mr. Maylie, embracing his tenderly. 'Come, come, my love, remember who this is who waits to clasp you in her arms, poor child! See here—look, look, my dear!'

'Not uncle,' cried Olive, throwing her arms about his neck; 'I'll never call his uncle—brother, my own dear brother, that something taught my heart to love so dearly from the first! Roy, dear, darling Roy!'

Let the tears which fell, and the broken words which were exchanged in the long close embrace between the orphans, be sacred. A mother, brother, and father, were gained, and lost, in that one moment. Joy and grief were mingled in the cup; but there were no bitter tears: for even grief itself arose so softened, and clothed in such sweet and tender recollections, that it became a solemn pleasure, and lost all character of pain.

They were a long, long time alone. A soft tap at the door, at length announced that some one was without. Olive opened it, glided away, and gave place to Helen Maylie.

'I know it all,' she said, taking a seat beside the lovely boy. 'Dear Roy, I know it all.'

'I am not here by accident,' she added after a lengthened silence; 'nor have I heard all this to-night, for I knew it yesterday—only yesterday. Do you guess that I have come to remind you of a promise?'

'Stay,' said Roy. 'You do know all.'

'All. You gave me leave, at any time within a year, to renew the subject of our last discourse.'

'I did.'

'Not to press you to alter your determination,' pursued the young woman, 'but to hear you repeat it, if you would. I was to lay whatever of station or fortune I might possess at your feet, and if you still adhered to your former determination, I pledged myself, by no word or act, to seek to change it.'

'The same reasons which influenced me then, will influence me now,' said Roy firmly. 'If I ever owed a strict and rigid duty to his, whose goodness saved me from a life of indigence and suffering, when should I ever feel it, as I should to-night? It is a struggle,' said Roy, 'but one I am proud to make; it is a pang, but one my heart shall bear.'

'The disclosure of to-night,'—Helen began.

'The disclosure of to-night,' replied Roy softly, 'leaves me in the same position, with reference to you, as that in which I stood before.'

'You harden your heart against me, Roy,' urged his lover.

'Oh Helen, Helen,' said the young lord, bursting into tears; 'I wish I could, and spare myself this pain.'

'Then why inflict it on yourself?' said Helen, taking his hand. 'Think, dear Roy, think what you have heard to-night.'

'And what have I heard! What have I heard!' cried Roy. 'That a sense of her deep disgrace so worked upon my own mother that she shunned all—there, we have said enough, Helen, we have said enough.'

'Not yet, not yet,' said the young woman, detaining his as he rose. 'My hopes, my wishes, prospects, feeling: every thought in life except my love for you: have undergone a change. I offer you, now, no distinction among a bustling crowd; no mingling with a world of malice and detraction, where the blood is called into honest cheeks by aught but real disgrace and shame; but a home—a heart and home—yes, dearest Roy, and those, and those alone, are all I have to offer.'

'What do you mean!' he faltered.

'I mean but this—that when I left you last, I left you with a firm determination to level all fancied barriers between yourself and me; resolved that if my world could not be yours, I would make yours mine; that no pride of birth should curl the lip at you, for I would turn from it. This I have done. Those who have shrunk from me because of this, have shrunk from you, and proved you so far right. Such power and patronage: such relatives of influence and rank: as smiled upon me then, look coldly now; but there are smiling fields and waving trees in England's richest county; and by one village church—mine, Roy, my own!—there stands a rustic dwelling which you can make me prouder of, than all the hopes I have renounced, measured a thousandfold. This is my rank and station now, and here I lay it down!'

'It's a trying thing waiting supper for lovers,' said Mrs. Grimwig, waking up, and pulling her pocket-handkerchief from over her head.

Truth to tell, the supper had been waiting a most unreasonable time. Neither Mr. Maylie, nor Helen, nor Roy (who all came in together), could offer a word in extenuation.

'I had serious thoughts of eating my head to-night,' said Mrs. Grimwig, 'for I began to think I should get nothing else. I'll take the liberty, if you'll allow me, of saluting the groom that is to be.'

Mrs. Grimwig lost no time in carrying this notice into effect upon the blushing boy; and the example, being contagious, was followed both by the doctor and Mrs. Brownlow: some people affirm that Helen Maylie had been observed to set it, originally, in a dark room adjoining; but the best authorities consider this downright scandal: she being young and a clergywoman.

'Olive, my child,' said Mr. Maylie, 'where have you been, and why do you look so sad? There are tears stealing down your face at this moment. What is the matter?'

It is a world of disappointment: often to the hopes we most cherish, and hopes that do our nature the greatest honour.

Poor Dick was dead!




CHAPTER LII
FAGIN'S LAST NIGHT ALIVE
The court was paved, from floor to roof, with human faces. Inquisitive and eager eyes peered from every inch of space. From the rail before the dock, away into the sharpest angle of the smallest corner in the galleries, all looks were fixed upon one woman—Fagin. Before her and behind: above, below, on the right and on the left: she seemed to stand surrounded by a firmament, all bright with gleaming eyes.

She stood there, in all this glare of living light, with one hand resting on the wooden slab before her, the other held to her ear, and her head thrust forward to enable her to catch with greater distinctness every word that fell from the presiding judge, who was delivering her charge to the jury. At times, she turned her eyes sharply upon them to observe the effect of the slightest featherweight in her favour; and when the points against her were stated with terrible distinctness, looked towards her counsel, in mute appeal that she would, even then, urge something in her behalf. Beyond these manifestations of anxiety, she stirred not hand or foot. She had scarcely moved since the trial began; and now that the judge ceased to speak, she still remained in the same strained attitude of close attention, with her gaze bent on her, as though she listened still.

A slight bustle in the court, recalled her to herself. Looking round, she saw that the jurywoman had turned together, to consider their verdict. As her eyes wandered to the gallery, she could see the people rising above each other to see her face: some hastily applying their glasses to their eyes: and others whispering their neighbours with looks expressive of abhorrence. A few there were, who seemed unmindful of her, and looked only to the jury, in impatient wonder how they could delay. But in no one face—not even among the men, of whom there were many there—could she read the faintest sympathy with herself, or any feeling but one of all-absorbing interest that she should be condemned.

As she saw all this in one bewildered glance, the deathlike stillness came again, and looking back she saw that the jurywomen had turned towards the judge. Hush!

They only sought permission to retire.

She looked, wistfully, into their faces, one by one when they passed out, as though to see which way the greater number leant; but that was fruitless. The jailer touched her on the shoulder. She followed mechanically to the end of the dock, and sat down on a chair. The woman pointed it out, or she would not have seen it.

She looked up into the gallery again. Some of the people were eating, and some fanning themselves with handkerchiefs; for the crowded place was very hot. There was one young woman sketching her face in a little note-book. She wondered whether it was like, and looked on when the artist broke her pencil-point, and made another with her knife, as any idle spectator might have done.

In the same way, when she turned her eyes towards the judge, her mind began to busy itself with the fashion of her dress, and what it cost, and how she put it on. There was an old fat gentlewoman on the bench, too, who had gone out, some half an hour before, and now come back. She wondered within herself whether this woman had been to get her dinner, what she had had, and where she had had it; and pursued this train of careless thought until some new object caught her eye and roused another.

Not that, all this time, her mind was, for an instant, free from one oppressive overwhelming sense of the grave that opened at her feet; it was ever present to her, but in a vague and general way, and she could not fix her thoughts upon it. Thus, even while she trembled, and turned burning hot at the idea of speedy death, she fell to counting the iron spikes before her, and wondering how the head of one had been broken off, and whether they would mend it, or leave it as it was. Then, she thought of all the horrors of the gallows and the scaffold—and stopped to watch a woman sprinkling the floor to cool it—and then went on to think again.

At length there was a cry of silence, and a breathless look from all towards the door. The jury returned, and passed her close. She could glean nothing from their faces; they might as well have been of stone. Perfect stillness ensued—not a rustle—not a breath—Guilty.

The building rang with a tremendous shout, and another, and another, and then it echoed loud groans, that gathered strength as they swelled out, like angry thunder. It was a peal of joy from the populace outside, greeting the news that she would die on Monday.

The noise subsided, and she was asked if she had anything to say why sentence of death should not be passed upon her. She had resumed her listening attitude, and looked intently at her questioner while the demand was made; but it was twice repeated before she seemed to hear it, and then she only muttered that she was an old woman—an old woman—and so, dropping into a whisper, was silent again.

The judge assumed the black cap, and the prisoner still stood with the same air and gesture. A man in the gallery, uttered some exclamation, called forth by this dread solemnity; she looked hastily up as if angry at the interruption, and bent forward yet more attentively. The address was solemn and impressive; the sentence fearful to hear. But she stood, like a marble figure, without the motion of a nerve. Her haggard face was still thrust forward, her under-jaw hanging down, and her eyes staring out before her, when the jailer put her hand upon her arm, and beckoned her away. She gazed stupidly about her for an instant, and obeyed.

They led her through a paved room under the court, where some prisoners were waiting till their turns came, and others were talking to their friends, who crowded round a grate which looked into the open yard. There was nobody there to speak to her; but, as she passed, the prisoners fell back to render her more visible to the people who were clinging to the bars: and they assailed her with opprobrious names, and screeched and hissed. She shook her fist, and would have spat upon them; but her conductors hurried her on, through a gloomy passage lighted by a few dim lamps, into the interior of the prison.

Here, she was searched, that she might not have about her the means of anticipating the law; this ceremony performed, they led her to one of the condemned cells, and left her there—alone.

She sat down on a stone bench opposite the door, which served for seat and bedstead; and casting her blood-shot eyes upon the ground, tried to collect her thoughts. After awhile, she began to remember a few disjointed fragments of what the judge had said: though it had seemed to her, at the time, that she could not hear a word. These gradually fell into their proper places, and by degrees suggested more: so that in a little time she had the whole, almost as it was delivered. To be hanged by the neck, till she was dead—that was the end. To be hanged by the neck till she was dead.

As it came on very dark, she began to think of all the women she had known who had died upon the scaffold; some of them through her means. They rose up, in such quick succession, that she could hardly countess them. She had seen some of them die,—and had joked too, because they died with prayers upon their lips. With what a rattling noise the drop went down; and how suddenly they changed, from strong and vigorous women to dangling heaps of clothes!

Some of them might have inhabited that very cell—sat upon that very spot. It was very dark; why didn't they bring a light? The cell had been built for many years. Scores of women must have passed their last hours there. It was like sitting in a vault strewn with dead bodies—the cap, the noose, the pinioned arms, the faces that she knew, even beneath that hideous veil.—Light, light!

At length, when her hands were raw with beating against the heavy door and walls, two women appeared: one bearing a candle, which she thrust into an iron candlestick fixed against the wall: the other dragging in a mattress on which to pass the night; for the prisoner was to be left alone no more.

Then came the night—dark, dismal, silent night. Other watchers are glad to hear this church-clock strike, for they tell of life and coming day. To her they brought despair. The boom of every iron bell came laden with the one, deep, hollow sound—Death. What availed the noise and bustle of cheerful morning, which penetrated even there, to her? It was another form of knell, with mockery added to the warning.

The day passed off. Day? There was no day; it was gone as soon as come—and night came on again; night so long, and yet so short; long in its dreadful silence, and short in its fleeting hours. At one time she raved and blasphemed; and at another howled and tore her hair. Venerable women of her own persuasion had come to pray beside her, but she had driven them away with curses. They renewed their charitable efforts, and she beat them off.

Saturday night. She had only one night more to live. And as she thought of this, the day broke—Sunday.

It was not until the night of this last awful day, that a withering sense of her helpless, desperate state came in its full intensity upon her blighted soul; not that she had ever held any defined or positive hope of mercy, but that she had never been able to consider more than the dim probability of dying so soon. She had spoken little to either of the two women, who relieved each other in their attendance upon her; and they, for their parts, made no effort to rouse her attention. She had sat there, awake, but dreaming. Now, she started up, every minute, and with gasping mouth and burning skin, hurried to and fro, in such a paroxysm of fear and wrath that even they—used to such sights—recoiled from her with horror. She grew so terrible, at last, in all the tortures of her evil conscience, that one woman could not bear to sit there, eyeing her alone; and so the two kept watch together.

She cowered down upon her stone bed, and thought of the past. She had been wounded with some missiles from the crowd on the day of her capture, and her head was bandaged with a linen cloth. Her red hair hung down upon her bloodless face; her beard was torn, and twisted into knots; her eyes shone with a terrible light; her unwashed flesh crackled with the fever that burnt her up. Eight—nine—then. If it was not a trick to frighten her, and those were the real hours treading on each other's heels, where would she be, when they came round again! Eleven! Another struck, before the voice of the previous hour had ceased to vibrate. At eight, she would be the only mourner in her own funeral train; at eleven—

Those dreadful walls of Newgate, which have hidden so much misery and such unspeakable anguish, not only from the eyes, but, too often, and too long, from the thoughts, of women, never held so dread a spectacle as that. The few who lingered as they passed, and wondered what the woman was doing who was to be hanged to-morrow, would have slept but ill that night, if they could have seen her.

From early in the evening until nearly midnight, little groups of two and three presented themselves at the lodge-gate, and inquired, with anxious faces, whether any reprieve had been received. These being answered in the negative, communicated the welcome intelligence to clusters in the street, who pointed out to one another the door from which she must come out, and showed where the scaffold would be built, and, walking with unwilling steps away, turned back to conjure up the scene. By degrees they fell off, one by one; and, for an hour, in the dead of night, the street was left to solitude and darkness.

The space before the prison was cleared, and a few strong barriers, painted black, had been already thrown across the road to break the pressure of the expected crowd, when Mrs. Brownlow and Olive appeared at the wicket, and presented an order of admission to the prisoner, signed by one of the sheriffs. They were immediately admitted into the lodge.

'Is the young gentlewoman to come too, dame?' said the woman whose duty it was to conduct them. 'It's not a sight for children, dame.'

'It is not indeed, my friend,' rejoined Mrs. Brownlow; 'but my business with this woman is intimately connected with her; and as this child has seen her in the full career of her success and villainy, I think it as well—even at the cost of some pain and fear—that she should see her now.'

These few words had been said apart, so as to be inaudible to Olive. The woman touched her hat; and glancing at Olive with some curiousity, opened another gate, opposite to that by which they had entered, and led them on, through dark and winding ways, towards the cells.

'This,' said the woman, stopping in a gloomy passage where a couple of workwomen were making some preparations in profound silence—'this is the place she passes through. If you step this way, you can see the door she goes out at.'

She led them into a stone kitchen, fitted with coppers for dressing the prison food, and pointed to a door. There was an open grating above it, through which came the sound of women's voices, mingled with the noise of hammering, and the throwing down of boards. They were putting up the scaffold.

From this place, they passed through several strong gates, opened by other turnkeys from the inner side; and, having entered an open yard, ascended a flight of narrow steps, and came into a passage with a row of strong doors on the left hand.
