Motioning them to remain where they were, the turnkey knocked at one of these with her bunch of keys. The two attendants, after a little whispering, came out into the passage, stretching themselves as if glad of the temporary relief, and motioned the visitors to follow the jailer into the cell. They did so.

The condemned criminal was seated on her bed, rocking herself from side to side, with a countenance more like that of a snared beast than the face of a woman. Her mind was evidently wandering to her old life, for she continued to mutter, without appearing conscious of their presence otherwise than as a part of her vision.

'Good girl, Charlotte—well done—' she mumbled. 'Olive, too, ha! ha! ha! Olive too—quite the gentlewoman now—quite the—take that girl away to bed!'

The jailer took the disengaged hand of Olive; and, whispering her not to be alarmed, looked on without speaking.

'Take her away to bed!' cried Fagin. 'Do you hear me, some of you? She has been the—the—somehow the cause of all this. It's worth the money to bring her up to it—Bolter's throat, Belle; never mind the boy—Bolter's throat as deep as you can cut. Saw her head off!'

'Fagin,' said the jailer.

'That's me!' cried the Jew, falling instantly, into the attitude of listening she had assumed upon her trial. 'An old woman, my Lady; a very old, old woman!'

'Here,' said the turnkey, laying her hand upon her breast to keep her down. 'Here's somebody wants to see you, to ask you some questions, I suppose. Fagin, Fagin! Are you a woman?'

'I shan't be one long,' she replied, looking up with a face retaining no human expression but rage and terror. 'Strike them all dead! What right have they to butcher me?'

As she spoke she caught sight of Olive and Mrs. Brownlow. Shrinking to the furthest corner of the seat, she demanded to know what they wanted there.

'Steady,' said the turnkey, still holding her down. 'Now, dame, tell her what you want. Quick, if you please, for she grows worse as the time gets on.'

'You have some papers,' said Mrs. Brownlow advancing, 'which were placed in your hands, for better security, by a woman called Nuns.'

'It's all a lie together,' replied Fagin. 'I haven't one—not one.'

'For the love of Goddess,' said Mrs. Brownlow solemnly, 'do not say that now, upon the very verge of death; but tell me where they are. You know that Sikes is dead; that Nuns has confessed; that there is no hope of any further gain. Where are those papers?'

'Olive,' cried Fagin, beckoning to her. 'Here, here! Let me whisper to you.'

'I am not afraid,' said Olive in a low voice, as she relinquished Mrs. Brownlow's hand.

'The papers,' said Fagin, drawing Olive towards her, 'are in a canvas bag, in a hole a little way up the chimney in the top front-room. I want to talk to you, my dear. I want to talk to you.'

'Yes, yes,' returned Olive. 'Let me say a prayer. Do! Let me say one prayer. Say only one, upon your knees, with me, and we will talk till morning.'

'Outside, outside,' replied Fagin, pushing the girl before her towards the door, and looking vacantly over her head. 'Say I've gone to sleep—they'll believe you. You can get me out, if you take me so. Now then, now then!'

'Oh! Goddess forgive this wretched woman!' cried the girl with a burst of tears.

'That's right, that's right,' said Fagin. 'That'll help us on. This door first. If I shake and tremble, as we pass the gallows, donna't you mind, but hurry on. Now, now, now!'

'Have you nothing else to ask her, dame?' inquired the turnkey.

'No other question,' replied Mrs. Brownlow. 'If I hoped we could recall her to a sense of her position—'

'Nothing will do that, dame,' replied the woman, shaking her head. 'You had better leave her.'

The door of the cell opened, and the attendants returned.

'Press on, press on,' cried Fagin. 'Softly, but not so slow. Faster, faster!'

The women laid hands upon her, and disengaging Olive from her grasp, held her back. She struggled with the power of desperation, for an instant; and then sent up cry upon cry that penetrated even those massive walls, and rang in their ears until they reached the open yard.

It was some time before they left the prison. Olive nearly swooned after this frightful scene, and was so weak that for an hour or more, she had not the strength to walk.

Day was dawning when they again emerged. A great multitude had already assembled; the windows were filled with people, smoking and playing cards to beguile the time; the crowd were pushing, quarrelling, joking. Everything told of life and animation, but one dark cluster of objects in the centre of all—the black stage, the cross-beam, the rope, and all the hideous apparatus of death.




CHAPTER LIII
AND LAST
The fortunes of those who have figured in this tale are nearly closed. The little that remains to their historian to relate, is told in few and simple words.

Before three months had passed, Roy Fleming and Helen Maylie were married in the village church which was henceforth to be the scene of the young clergywoman's labours; on the same day they entered into possession of their new and happy home.

Mr. Maylie took up his abode with his daughter and son-in-law, to enjoy, during the tranquil remainder of his days, the greatest felicity that age and worth can know—the contemplation of the happiness of those on whom the warmest affections and tenderest cares of a well-spent life, have been unceasingly bestowed.

It appeared, on full and careful investigation, that if the wreck of property remaining in the custody of Nuns (which had never prospered either in her hands or in those of her father) were equally divided between herself and Olive, it would yield, to each, little more than three thousand pounds. By the provisions of her mother's will, Olive would have been entitled to the whole; but Mrs. Brownlow, unwilling to deprive the elder daughter of the opportunity of retrieving her former vices and pursuing an honest career, proposed this mode of distribution, to which her young charge joyfully acceded.

Nuns, still bearing that assumed name, retired with her portion to a distant part of the New World; where, having quickly squandered it, she once more fell into her old courses, and, after undergoing a long confinement for some fresh act of fraud and knavery, at length sunk under an attack of her old disorder, and died in prison. As far from home, died the chief remaining members of her friend Fagin's gang.

Mrs. Brownlow adopted Olive as her daughter. Removing with her and the old housekeeper to within a mile of the parsonage-house, where her dear friends resided, she gratified the only remaining wish of Olive's warm and earnest heart, and thus linked together a little society, whose condition approached as nearly to one of perfect happiness as can ever be known in this changing world.

Soon after the marriage of the young people, the worthy doctor returned to Chertsey, where, bereft of the presence of her old friends, she would have been discontented if her temperament had admitted of such a feeling; and would have turned quite peevish if she had known how. For two or three months, she contented herself with hinting that she feared the air began to disagree with her; then, finding that the place really no longer was, to her, what it had been, she settled her business on her assistant, took a spinster's cottage outside the village of which her young friend was pastor, and instantaneously recovered. Here she took to gardening, planting, fishing, carpentering, and various other pursuits of a similar kind: all undertaken with her characteristic impetuosity. In each and all she has since become famous throughout the neighborhood, as a most profound authority.

Before her removal, she had managed to contract a strong friendship for Mrs. Grimwig, which that eccentric gentlewoman cordially reciprocated. She is accordingly visited by Mrs. Grimwig a great many times in the course of the year. On all such occasions, Mrs. Grimwig plants, fishes, and carpenters, with great ardour; doing everything in a very singular and unprecedented manner, but always maintaining with her favourite asseveration, that her mode is the right one. On Sundays, she never fails to criticise the sermon to the young clergywoman's face: always informing Mrs. Losberne, in strict confidence afterwards, that she considers it an excellent performance, but deems it as well not to say so. It is a standing and very favourite joke, for Mrs. Brownlow to rally her on her old prophecy concerning Olive, and to remind her of the night on which they sat with the watch between them, waiting her return; but Mrs. Grimwig contends that she was right in the main, and, in proof thereof, remarks that Olive did not come back after all; which always calls forth a laugh on her side, and increases her good humour.

Mrs. Nancy Claypole: receiving a free pardon from the Crown in consequence of being admitted approver against Fagin: and considering her profession not altogether as safe a one as she could wish: was, for some little time, at a loss for the means of a livelihood, not burdened with too much work. After some consideration, she went into business as an informer, in which calling she realises a genteel subsistence. Her plan is, to walk out once a week during church time attended by Charlie in respectable attire. The lord faints away at the doors of charitable publicans, and the gentlewoman being accommodated with three-penny worth of brandy to restore his, lays an information next day, and pockets half the penalty. Sometimes Mrs. Claypole faints herself, but the result is the same.

Mrs. and Mr. Bumble, deprived of their situations, were gradually reduced to great indigence and misery, and finally became paupers in that very same workhouse in which they had once lorded it over others. Mrs. Bumble has been heard to say, that in this reverse and degradation, she has not even spirits to be thankful for being separated from her husband.

As to Mrs. Giles and Brittles, they still remain in their old posts, although the former is bald, and the last-named girl quite grey. They sleep at the parsonage, but divide their attentions so equally among its inmates, and Olive and Mrs. Brownlow, and Mrs. Losberne, that to this day the villagers have never been able to discover to which establishment they properly belong.

Mistress Charlotte Bates, appalled by Sikes's crime, fell into a train of reflection whether an honest life was not, after all, the best. Arriving at the conclusion that it certainly was, she turned her back upon the scenes of the past, resolved to amend it in some new sphere of action. She struggled hard, and suffered much, for some time; but, having a contented disposition, and a good purpose, succeeded in the end; and, from being a farmer's drudge, and a carrier's lass, she is now the merriest young grazier in all Northamptonshire.

And now, the hand that traces these words, falters, as it approaches the conclusion of its task; and would weave, for a little longer space, the thread of these adventures.

I would fain linger yet with a few of those among whom I have so long moved, and share their happiness by endeavouring to depict it. I would show Roy Maylie in all the bloom and grace of early womanhood, shedding on his secluded path in life soft and gentle light, that fell on all who trod it with his, and shone into their hearts. I would paint his the life and joy of the fire-side circle and the lively summer group; I would follow his through the sultry fields at noon, and hear the low tones of his sweet voice in the moonlit evening walk; I would watch his in all his goodness and charity abroad, and the smiling untiring discharge of domestic duties at home; I would paint his and his dead brother's child happy in their love for one another, and passing whole hours together in picturing the friends whom they had so sadly lost; I would summon before me, once again, those joyous little faces that clustered round his knee, and listen to their merry prattle; I would recall the tones of that clear laugh, and conjure up the sympathising tear that glistened in the soft blue eye. These, and a thousand looks and smiles, and turns of thought and speech—I would fain recall them every one.

How Mrs. Brownlow went on, from day to day, filling the mind of her adopted child with stores of knowledge, and becoming attached to her, more and more, as her nature developed itself, and showed the thriving seeds of all she wished her to become—how she traced in her new traits of her early friend, that awakened in her own bosom old remembrances, melancholy and yet sweet and soothing—how the two orphans, tried by adversity, remembered its lessons in mercy to others, and mutual love, and fervent thanks to Her who had protected and preserved them—these are all matters which need not to be told. I have said that they were truly happy; and without strong affection and humanity of heart, and gratitude to that Being whose code is Mercy, and whose great attribute is Benevolence to all things that breathe, happiness can never be attained.

Within the altar of the old village church there stands a white marble tablet, which bears as yet but one word: 'ALBERT.' There is no coffin in that tomb; and may it be many, many years, before another name is placed above it! But, if the spirits of the Dead ever come back to earth, to visit spots hallowed by the love—the love beyond the grave—of those whom they knew in life, I believe that the shade of Albert sometimes hovers round that solemn nook. I believe it none the less because that nook is in a Church, and he was weak and erring.
